Smith, Dave Bruchie\r\n\r\n*** START OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK KIM ***\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nKim\r\n\r\nby Rudyard Kipling\r\n\r\n\r\nContents\r\n\r\n CHAPTER I\r\n CHAPTER II\r\n CHAPTER III\r\n CHAPTER IV\r\n CHAPTER V\r\n CHAPTER VI\r\n CHAPTER VII\r\n CHAPTER VIII\r\n CHAPTER IX\r\n CHAPTER X\r\n CHAPTER XI\r\n CHAPTER XII\r\n CHAPTER XIII\r\n CHAPTER XIV\r\n CHAPTER XV\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I\r\n\r\n\r\nO ye who tread the Narrow Way\r\nBy Tophet-flare to Judgment Day,\r\nBe gentle when \xe2\x80\x9cthe heathen\xe2\x80\x9d pray\r\n    To Buddha at Kamakura!\r\n\r\nHe sat, in defiance of municipal orders, astride the gun Zam Zammah on\r\nher brick platform opposite the old Ajaib-Gher\xe2\x80\x94the Wonder House, as the\r\nnatives call the Lahore Museum. Who hold Zam-Zammah, that\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cfire-breathing dragon\xe2\x80\x9d, hold the Punjab, for the great green-bronze\r\npiece is always first of the conqueror\xe2\x80\x99s loot.\r\n\r\nThere was some justification for Kim\xe2\x80\x94he had kicked Lala Dinanath\xe2\x80\x99s boy\r\noff the trunnions\xe2\x80\x94since the English held the Punjab and Kim was\r\nEnglish. Though he was burned black as any native; though he spoke the\r\nvernacular by preference, and his mother-tongue in a clipped uncertain\r\nsing-song; though he consorted on terms of perfect equality with the\r\nsmall boys of the bazar; Kim was white\xe2\x80\x94a poor white of the very\r\npoorest. The half-caste woman who looked after him (she smoked opium,\r\nand pretended to keep a second-hand furniture shop by the square where\r\nthe cheap cabs wait) told the missionaries that she was Kim\xe2\x80\x99s mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsister; but his mother had been nursemaid in a Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s family and had\r\nmarried Kimball O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, a young colour-sergeant of the Mavericks, an\r\nIrish regiment. He afterwards took a post on the Sind, Punjab, and\r\nDelhi Railway, and his Regiment went home without him. The wife died of\r\ncholera in Ferozepore, and O\xe2\x80\x99Hara fell to drink and loafing up and down\r\nthe line with the keen-eyed three-year-old baby. Societies and\r\nchaplains, anxious for the child, tried to catch him, but O\xe2\x80\x99Hara\r\ndrifted away, till he came across the woman who took opium and learned\r\nthe taste from her, and died as poor whites die in India. His estate at\r\ndeath consisted of three papers\xe2\x80\x94one he called his \xe2\x80\x9c_ne varietur_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nbecause those words were written below his signature thereon, and\r\nanother his \xe2\x80\x9cclearance-certificate\xe2\x80\x9d. The third was Kim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbirth-certificate. Those things, he was used to say, in his glorious\r\nopium-hours, would yet make little Kimball a man. On no account was Kim\r\nto part with them, for they belonged to a great piece of magic\xe2\x80\x94such\r\nmagic as men practised over yonder behind the Museum, in the big\r\nblue-and-white Jadoo-Gher\xe2\x80\x94the Magic House, as we name the Masonic\r\nLodge. It would, he said, all come right some day, and Kim\xe2\x80\x99s horn would\r\nbe exalted between pillars\xe2\x80\x94monstrous pillars\xe2\x80\x94of beauty and strength.\r\nThe Colonel himself, riding on a horse, at the head of the finest\r\nRegiment in the world, would attend to Kim\xe2\x80\x94little Kim that should have\r\nbeen better off than his father. Nine hundred first-class devils, whose\r\nGod was a Red Bull on a green field, would attend to Kim, if they had\r\nnot forgotten O\xe2\x80\x99Hara\xe2\x80\x94poor O\xe2\x80\x99Hara that was gang-foreman on the\r\nFerozepore line. Then he would weep bitterly in the broken rush chair\r\non the veranda. So it came about after his death that the woman sewed\r\nparchment, paper, and birth-certificate into a leather amulet-case\r\nwhich she strung round Kim\xe2\x80\x99s neck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd some day,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, confusedly remembering O\xe2\x80\x99Hara\xe2\x80\x99s prophecies,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthere will come for you a great Red Bull on a green field, and the\r\nColonel riding on his tall horse, yes, and\xe2\x80\x9d dropping into English\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cnine\r\nhundred devils.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall remember. A Red Bull and a Colonel on a horse\r\nwill come, but first, my father said, will come the two men making\r\nready the ground for these matters. That is how my father said they\r\nalways did; and it is always so when men work magic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf the woman had sent Kim up to the local Jadoo-Gher with those papers,\r\nhe would, of course, have been taken over by the Provincial Lodge, and\r\nsent to the Masonic Orphanage in the Hills; but what she had heard of\r\nmagic she distrusted. Kim, too, held views of his own. As he reached\r\nthe years of indiscretion, he learned to avoid missionaries and white\r\nmen of serious aspect who asked who he was, and what he did. For Kim\r\ndid nothing with an immense success. True, he knew the wonderful walled\r\ncity of Lahore from the Delhi Gate to the outer Fort Ditch; was hand in\r\nglove with men who led lives stranger than anything Haroun al Raschid\r\ndreamed of; and he lived in a life wild as that of the Arabian Nights,\r\nbut missionaries and secretaries of charitable societies could not see\r\nthe beauty of it. His nickname through the wards was \xe2\x80\x9cLittle Friend of\r\nall the World\xe2\x80\x9d; and very often, being lithe and inconspicuous, he\r\nexecuted commissions by night on the crowded housetops for sleek and\r\nshiny young men of fashion. It was intrigue,\xe2\x80\x94of course he knew that\r\nmuch, as he had known all evil since he could speak,\xe2\x80\x94but what he loved\r\nwas the game for its own sake\xe2\x80\x94the stealthy prowl through the dark\r\ngullies and lanes, the crawl up a waterpipe, the sights and sounds of\r\nthe women\xe2\x80\x99s world on the flat roofs, and the headlong flight from\r\nhousetop to housetop under cover of the hot dark. Then there were holy\r\nmen, ash-smeared _faquirs_ by their brick shrines under the trees at\r\nthe riverside, with whom he was quite familiar\xe2\x80\x94greeting them as they\r\nreturned from begging-tours, and, when no one was by, eating from the\r\nsame dish. The woman who looked after him insisted with tears that he\r\nshould wear European clothes\xe2\x80\x94trousers, a shirt and a battered hat. Kim\r\nfound it easier to slip into Hindu or Mohammedan garb when engaged on\r\ncertain businesses. One of the young men of fashion\xe2\x80\x94he who was found\r\ndead at the bottom of a well on the night of the earthquake\xe2\x80\x94had once\r\ngiven him a complete suit of Hindu kit, the costume of a lowcaste\r\nstreet boy, and Kim stored it in a secret place under some baulks in\r\nNila Ram\xe2\x80\x99s timber-yard, beyond the Punjab High Court, where the\r\nfragrant deodar logs lie seasoning after they have driven down the\r\nRavi. When there was business or frolic afoot, Kim would use his\r\nproperties, returning at dawn to the veranda, all tired out from\r\nshouting at the heels of a marriage procession, or yelling at a Hindu\r\nfestival. Sometimes there was food in the house, more often there was\r\nnot, and then Kim went out again to eat with his native friends.\r\n\r\nAs he drummed his heels against Zam-Zammah he turned now and again from\r\nhis king-of-the-castle game with little Chota Lal and Abdullah the\r\nsweetmeat-seller\xe2\x80\x99s son, to make a rude remark to the native policeman\r\non guard over rows of shoes at the Museum door. The big Punjabi grinned\r\ntolerantly: he knew Kim of old. So did the water-carrier, sluicing\r\nwater on the dry road from his goat-skin bag. So did Jawahir Singh, the\r\nMuseum carpenter, bent over new packing-cases. So did everybody in\r\nsight except the peasants from the country, hurrying up to the Wonder\r\nHouse to view the things that men made in their own province and\r\nelsewhere. The Museum was given up to Indian arts and manufactures, and\r\nanybody who sought wisdom could ask the Curator to explain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOff! Off! Let me up!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Abdullah, climbing up Zam-Zammah\xe2\x80\x99s wheel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy father was a pastry-cook, Thy mother stole the _ghi_,\xe2\x80\x9d sang Kim.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll Mussalmans fell off Zam-Zammah long ago!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet _me_ up!\xe2\x80\x9d shrilled little Chota Lal in his gilt-embroidered cap.\r\nHis father was worth perhaps half a million sterling, but India is the\r\nonly democratic land in the world.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Hindus fell off Zam-Zammah too. The Mussalmans pushed them off.\r\nThy father was a pastry-cook\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped; for there shuffled round the corner, from the roaring Motee\r\nBazar, such a man as Kim, who thought he knew all castes, had never\r\nseen. He was nearly six feet high, dressed in fold upon fold of dingy\r\nstuff like horse-blanketing, and not one fold of it could Kim refer to\r\nany known trade or profession. At his belt hung a long open-work iron\r\npencase and a wooden rosary such as holy men wear. On his head was a\r\ngigantic sort of tam-o\xe2\x80\x99-shanter. His face was yellow and wrinkled, like\r\nthat of Fook Shing, the Chinese bootmaker in the bazar. His eyes turned\r\nup at the corners and looked like little slits of onyx.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim to his companions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it is a man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Abdullah, finger in mouth, staring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithout doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Kim; \xe2\x80\x9cbut he is no man of India that _I_ have\r\never seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA priest, perhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chota Lal, spying the rosary. \xe2\x80\x9cSee! He goes\r\ninto the Wonder House!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, nay,\xe2\x80\x9d said the policeman, shaking his head. \xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand\r\nyour talk.\xe2\x80\x9d The constable spoke Punjabi. \xe2\x80\x9cO Friend of all the World,\r\nwhat does he say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSend him hither,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, dropping from Zam-Zammah, flourishing his\r\nbare heels. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is a foreigner, and thou art a buffalo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man turned helplessly and drifted towards the boys. He was old, and\r\nhis woollen gaberdine still reeked of the stinking artemisia of the\r\nmountain passes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Children, what is that big house?\xe2\x80\x9d he said in very fair Urdu.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Ajaib-Gher, the Wonder House!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim gave him no title\xe2\x80\x94such as Lala\r\nor Mian. He could not divine the man\xe2\x80\x99s creed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! The Wonder House! Can any enter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is written above the door\xe2\x80\x94all can enter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithout payment?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI go in and out. _I_ am no banker,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas! I am an old man. I did not know.\xe2\x80\x9d Then, fingering his rosary, he\r\nhalf turned to the Museum.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is your caste? Where is your house? Have you come far?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came by Kulu\xe2\x80\x94from beyond the Kailas\xe2\x80\x94but what know you? From the\r\nHills where\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he sighed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe air and water are fresh and cool.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha! Khitai [a Chinaman],\xe2\x80\x9d said Abdullah proudly. Fook Shing had once\r\nchased him out of his shop for spitting at the joss above the boots.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPahari [a hillman],\xe2\x80\x9d said little Chota Lal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, child\xe2\x80\x94a hillman from hills thou\xe2\x80\x99lt never see. Didst hear of\r\nBhotiyal [Tibet]? I am no Khitai, but a Bhotiya [Tibetan], since you\r\nmust know\xe2\x80\x94a lama\xe2\x80\x94or, say, a _guru_ in your tongue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA _guru_ from Tibet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cI have not seen such a man. They be\r\nHindus in Tibet, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe be followers of the Middle Way, living in peace in our lamasseries,\r\nand I go to see the Four Holy Places before I die. Now do you, who are\r\nchildren, know as much as I do who am old.\xe2\x80\x9d He smiled benignantly on\r\nthe boys.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou eaten?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe fumbled in his bosom and drew forth a worn, wooden begging-bowl. The\r\nboys nodded. All priests of their acquaintance begged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not wish to eat yet.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned his head like an old tortoise in\r\nthe sunlight. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it true that there are many images in the Wonder\r\nHouse of Lahore?\xe2\x80\x9d He repeated the last words as one making sure of an\r\naddress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true,\xe2\x80\x9d said Abdullah. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is full of heathen _b\xc5\xabts_. Thou also\r\nart an idolater.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind _him_,\xe2\x80\x9d said. Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is the Government\xe2\x80\x99s house and\r\nthere is no idolatry in it, but only a Sahib with a white beard. Come\r\nwith me and I will show.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrange priests eat boys,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Chota Lal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he is a stranger and a _b\xc5\xabt-parast_ (idolater),\xe2\x80\x9d said Abdullah,\r\nthe Mohammedan.\r\n\r\nKim laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is new. Run to your mothers\xe2\x80\x99 laps, and be safe. Come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim clicked round the self-registering turnstile; the old man followed\r\nand halted amazed. In the entrance-hall stood the larger figures of the\r\nGreco-Buddhist sculptures done, savants know how long since, by\r\nforgotten workmen whose hands were feeling, and not unskilfully, for\r\nthe mysteriously transmitted Grecian touch. There were hundreds of\r\npieces, friezes of figures in relief, fragments of statues and slabs\r\ncrowded with figures that had encrusted the brick walls of the Buddhist\r\n_stupas_ and _viharas_ of the North Country and now, dug up and\r\nlabelled, made the pride of the Museum. In open-mouthed wonder the lama\r\nturned to this and that, and finally checked in rapt attention before a\r\nlarge alto-relief representing a coronation or apotheosis of the Lord\r\nBuddha. The Master was represented seated on a lotus the petals of\r\nwhich were so deeply undercut as to show almost detached. Round Him was\r\nan adoring hierarchy of kings, elders, and old-time Buddhas. Below were\r\nlotus-covered waters with fishes and water-birds. Two butterfly-winged\r\ndevas held a wreath over His head; above them another pair supported an\r\numbrella surmounted by the jewelled headdress of the Bodhisat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Lord! The Lord! It is Sakya Muni himself,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama half sobbed;\r\nand under his breath began the wonderful Buddhist invocation:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Him the Way,\xe2\x80\x94the Law,\xe2\x80\x94Apart\xe2\x80\x94\r\nWhom Maya held beneath her heart,\r\nAnanda\xe2\x80\x99s Lord\xe2\x80\x94the Bodhisat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd He is here! The Most Excellent Law is here also. My pilgrimage is\r\nwell begun. And what work! What work!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYonder is the Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, and dodged sideways among the cases of\r\nthe arts and manufacturers wing. A white-bearded Englishman was looking\r\nat the lama, who gravely turned and saluted him and after some fumbling\r\ndrew forth a note-book and a scrap of paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is my name,\xe2\x80\x9d smiling at the clumsy, childish print.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of us who had made pilgrimage to the Holy Places\xe2\x80\x94he is now Abbot\r\nof the Lung-Cho Monastery\xe2\x80\x94gave it me,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cHe spoke of\r\nthese.\xe2\x80\x9d His lean hand moved tremulously round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWelcome, then, O lama from Tibet. Here be the images, and I am\r\nhere\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he glanced at the lama\xe2\x80\x99s face\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cto gather knowledge. Come to my\r\noffice awhile.\xe2\x80\x9d The old man was trembling with excitement.\r\n\r\nThe office was but a little wooden cubicle partitioned off from the\r\nsculpture-lined gallery. Kim laid himself down, his ear against a crack\r\nin the heat-split cedar door, and, following his instinct, stretched\r\nout to listen and watch.\r\n\r\nMost of the talk was altogether above his head. The lama, haltingly at\r\nfirst, spoke to the Curator of his own lamassery, the Such-zen,\r\nopposite the Painted Rocks, four months\xe2\x80\x99 march away. The Curator\r\nbrought out a huge book of photos and showed him that very place,\r\nperched on its crag, overlooking the gigantic valley of many-hued\r\nstrata.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, ay!\xe2\x80\x9d The lama mounted a pair of horn-rimmed spectacles of Chinese\r\nwork. \xe2\x80\x9cHere is the little door through which we bring wood before\r\nwinter. And thou\xe2\x80\x94the English know of these things? He who is now Abbot\r\nof Lung-Cho told me, but I did not believe. The Lord\xe2\x80\x94the Excellent\r\nOne\xe2\x80\x94He has honour here too? And His life is known?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all carven upon the stones. Come and see, if thou art rested.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOut shuffled the lama to the main hall, and, the Curator beside him,\r\nwent through the collection with the reverence of a devotee and the\r\nappreciative instinct of a craftsman.\r\n\r\nIncident by incident in the beautiful story he identified on the\r\nblurred stone, puzzled here and there by the unfamiliar Greek\r\nconvention, but delighted as a child at each new trove. Where the\r\nsequence failed, as in the Annunciation, the Curator supplied it from\r\nhis mound of books\xe2\x80\x94French and German, with photographs and\r\nreproductions.\r\n\r\nHere was the devout Asita, the pendant of Simeon in the Christian\r\nstory, holding the Holy Child on his knee while mother and father\r\nlistened; and here were incidents in the legend of the cousin\r\nDevadatta. Here was the wicked woman who accused the Master of\r\nimpurity, all confounded; here was the teaching in the Deer-park; the\r\nmiracle that stunned the fire-worshippers; here was the Bodhisat in\r\nroyal state as a prince; the miraculous birth; the death at Kusinagara,\r\nwhere the weak disciple fainted; while there were almost countless\r\nrepetitions of the meditation under the Bodhi tree; and the adoration\r\nof the alms-bowl was everywhere. In a few minutes the Curator saw that\r\nhis guest was no mere bead-telling mendicant, but a scholar of parts.\r\nAnd they went at it all over again, the lama taking snuff, wiping his\r\nspectacles, and talking at railway speed in a bewildering mixture of\r\nUrdu and Tibetan. He had heard of the travels of the Chinese pilgrims,\r\nFu-Hiouen and Hwen-Tsiang, and was anxious to know if there was any\r\ntranslation of their record. He drew in his breath as he turned\r\nhelplessly over the pages of Beal and Stanislas Julien. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis all here.\r\nA treasure locked.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he composed himself reverently to listen to\r\nfragments hastily rendered into Urdu. For the first time he heard of\r\nthe labours of European scholars, who by the help of these and a\r\nhundred other documents have identified the Holy Places of Buddhism.\r\nThen he was shown a mighty map, spotted and traced with yellow. The\r\nbrown finger followed the Curator\xe2\x80\x99s pencil from point to point. Here\r\nwas Kapilavastu, here the Middle Kingdom, and here Mahabodhi, the Mecca\r\nof Buddhism; and here was Kusinagara, sad place of the Holy One\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath. The old man bowed his head over the sheets in silence for a\r\nwhile, and the Curator lit another pipe. Kim had fallen asleep. When he\r\nwaked, the talk, still in spate, was more within his comprehension.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd thus it was, O Fountain of Wisdom, that I decided to go to the\r\nHoly Places which His foot had trod\xe2\x80\x94to the Birthplace, even to Kapila;\r\nthen to Mahabodhi, which is Buddh Gaya\xe2\x80\x94to the Monastery\xe2\x80\x94to the\r\nDeer-park\xe2\x80\x94to the place of His death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama lowered his voice. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I come here alone. For\r\nfive\xe2\x80\x94seven\xe2\x80\x94eighteen\xe2\x80\x94forty years it was in my mind that the Old Law was\r\nnot well followed; being overlaid, as thou knowest, with devildom,\r\ncharms, and idolatry. Even as the child outside said but now. Ay, even\r\nas the child said, with _b\xc5\xabt-parasti_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it comes with all faiths.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThinkest thou? The books of my lamassery I read, and they were dried\r\npith; and the later ritual with which we of the Reformed Law have\r\ncumbered ourselves\xe2\x80\x94that, too, had no worth to these old eyes. Even the\r\nfollowers of the Excellent One are at feud on feud with one another. It\r\nis all illusion. Ay, _maya_, illusion. But I have another desire\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nseamed yellow face drew within three inches of the Curator, and the\r\nlong forefinger-nail tapped on the table. \xe2\x80\x9cYour scholars, by these\r\nbooks, have followed the Blessed Feet in all their wanderings; but\r\nthere are things which they have not sought out. I know nothing\xe2\x80\x94nothing\r\ndo I know\xe2\x80\x94but I go to free myself from the Wheel of Things by a broad\r\nand open road.\xe2\x80\x9d He smiled with most simple triumph. \xe2\x80\x9cAs a pilgrim to\r\nthe Holy Places I acquire merit. But there is more. Listen to a true\r\nthing. When our gracious Lord, being as yet a youth, sought a mate, men\r\nsaid, in His father\xe2\x80\x99s Court, that He was too tender for marriage. Thou\r\nknowest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Curator nodded, wondering what would come next.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo they made the triple trial of strength against all comers. And at\r\nthe test of the Bow, our Lord first breaking that which they gave Him,\r\ncalled for such a bow as none might bend. Thou knowest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is written. I have read.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, overshooting all other marks, the arrow passed far and far beyond\r\nsight. At the last it fell; and, where it touched earth, there broke\r\nout a stream which presently became a River, whose nature, by our\r\nLord\xe2\x80\x99s beneficence, and that merit He acquired ere He freed himself, is\r\nthat whoso bathes in it washes away all taint and speckle of sin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it is written,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Curator sadly.\r\n\r\nThe lama drew a long breath. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is that River? Fountain of Wisdom,\r\nwhere fell the arrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas, my brother, I do not know,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Curator.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, if it please thee to forget\xe2\x80\x94the one thing only that thou hast not\r\ntold me. Surely thou must know? See, I am an old man! I ask with my\r\nhead between thy feet, O Fountain of Wisdom. We _know_ He drew the bow!\r\nWe _know_ the arrow fell! We know the stream gushed! Where, then, is\r\nthe River? My dream told me to find it. So I came. I am here. But where\r\nis the River?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I knew, think you I would not cry it aloud?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy it one attains freedom from the Wheel of Things,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama went on,\r\nunheeding. \xe2\x80\x9cThe River of the Arrow! Think again! Some little stream,\r\nmaybe\xe2\x80\x94dried in the heats? But the Holy One would never so cheat an old\r\nman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know. I do not know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama brought his thousand-wrinkled face once more a handsbreadth\r\nfrom the Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s. \xe2\x80\x9cI see thou dost not know. Not being of the Law,\r\nthe matter is hid from thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy\xe2\x80\x94hidden\xe2\x80\x94hidden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are both bound, thou and I, my brother. But I\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he rose with a sweep\r\nof the soft thick drapery\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI go to cut myself free. Come also!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am bound,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Curator. \xe2\x80\x9cBut whither goest thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst to Kashi [Benares]: where else? There I shall meet one of the\r\npure faith in a Jain temple of that city. He also is a Seeker in\r\nsecret, and from him haply I may learn. Maybe he will go with me to\r\nBuddh Gaya. Thence north and west to Kapilavastu, and there will I seek\r\nfor the River. Nay, I will seek everywhere as I go\xe2\x80\x94for the place is not\r\nknown where the arrow fell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how wilt thou go? It is a far cry to Delhi, and farther to\r\nBenares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy road and the trains. From Path\xc3\xa2nkot, having left the Hills, I came\r\nhither in a _te-rain_. It goes swiftly. At first I was amazed to see\r\nthose tall poles by the side of the road snatching up and snatching up\r\ntheir threads,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he illustrated the stoop and whirl of a telegraph-pole\r\nflashing past the train. \xe2\x80\x9cBut later, I was cramped and desired to walk,\r\nas I am used.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd thou art sure of thy road?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Curator.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, for that one but asks a question and pays money, and the appointed\r\npersons despatch all to the appointed place. That much I knew in my\r\nlamassery from sure report,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama proudly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when dost thou go?\xe2\x80\x9d The Curator smiled at the mixture of old-world\r\npiety and modern progress that is the note of India today.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs soon as may be. I follow the places of His life till I come to the\r\nRiver of the Arrow. There is, moreover, a written paper of the hours of\r\nthe trains that go south.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd for food?\xe2\x80\x9d Lamas, as a rule, have good store of money somewhere\r\nabout them, but the Curator wished to make sure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the journey, I take up the Master\xe2\x80\x99s begging-bowl. Yes. Even as He\r\nwent so go I, forsaking the ease of my monastery. There was with me\r\nwhen I left the hills a _chela_ (disciple) who begged for me as the\r\nRule demands, but halting in Kulu awhile a fever took him and he died.\r\nI have now no _chela_, but I will take the alms-bowl and thus enable\r\nthe charitable to acquire merit.\xe2\x80\x9d He nodded his head valiantly. Learned\r\ndoctors of a lamassery do not beg, but the lama was an enthusiast in\r\nthis quest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe it so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Curator, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cSuffer me now to acquire merit.\r\nWe be craftsmen together, thou and I. Here is a new book of white\r\nEnglish paper: here be sharpened pencils two and three\xe2\x80\x94thick and thin,\r\nall good for a scribe. Now lend me thy spectacles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Curator looked through them. They were heavily scratched, but the\r\npower was almost exactly that of his own pair, which he slid into the\r\nlama\xe2\x80\x99s hand, saying: \xe2\x80\x9cTry these.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA feather! A very feather upon the face.\xe2\x80\x9d The old man turned his head\r\ndelightedly and wrinkled up his nose. \xe2\x80\x9cHow scarcely do I feel them! How\r\nclearly do I see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey be _bilaur_\xe2\x80\x94crystal\xe2\x80\x94and will never scratch. May they help thee to\r\nthy River, for they are thine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will take them and the pencils and the white note-book,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nlama, \xe2\x80\x9cas a sign of friendship between priest and priest\xe2\x80\x94and now\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nfumbled at his belt, detached the open-work iron pincers, and laid it\r\non the Curator\xe2\x80\x99s table. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is for a memory between thee and me\xe2\x80\x94my\r\npencase. It is something old\xe2\x80\x94even as I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a piece of ancient design, Chinese, of an iron that is not\r\nsmelted these days; and the collector\xe2\x80\x99s heart in the Curator\xe2\x80\x99s bosom\r\nhad gone out to it from the first. For no persuasion would the lama\r\nresume his gift.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I return, having found the River, I will bring thee a written\r\npicture of the Padma Samthora such as I used to make on silk at the\r\nlamassery. Yes\xe2\x80\x94and of the Wheel of Life,\xe2\x80\x9d he chuckled, \xe2\x80\x9cfor we be\r\ncraftsmen together, thou and I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Curator would have detained him: they are few in the world who\r\nstill have the secret of the conventional brush-pen Buddhist pictures\r\nwhich are, as it were, half written and half drawn. But the lama strode\r\nout, head high in air, and pausing an instant before the great statue\r\nof a Bodhisat in meditation, brushed through the turnstiles.\r\n\r\nKim followed like a shadow. What he had overheard excited him wildly.\r\nThis man was entirely new to all his experience, and he meant to\r\ninvestigate further, precisely as he would have investigated a new\r\nbuilding or a strange festival in Lahore city. The lama was his trove,\r\nand he purposed to take possession. Kim\xe2\x80\x99s mother had been Irish, too.\r\n\r\nThe old man halted by Zam-Zammah and looked round till his eye fell on\r\nKim. The inspiration of his pilgrimage had left him for awhile, and he\r\nfelt old, forlorn, and very empty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not sit under that gun,\xe2\x80\x9d said the policeman loftily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh! Owl!\xe2\x80\x9d was Kim\xe2\x80\x99s retort on the lama\xe2\x80\x99s behalf. \xe2\x80\x9cSit under that gun\r\nif it please thee. When didst thou steal the milkwoman\xe2\x80\x99s slippers,\r\nDunnoo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was an utterly unfounded charge sprung on the spur of the moment,\r\nbut it silenced Dunnoo, who knew that Kim\xe2\x80\x99s clear yell could call up\r\nlegions of bad bazaar boys if need arose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd whom didst thou worship within?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim affably, squatting in\r\nthe shade beside the lama.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI worshipped none, child. I bowed before the Excellent Law.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim accepted this new God without emotion. He knew already a few score.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what dost thou do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg. I remember now it is long since I have eaten or drunk. What is\r\nthe custom of charity in this town? In silence, as we do of Tibet, or\r\nspeaking aloud?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose who beg in silence starve in silence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, quoting a\r\nnative proverb. The lama tried to rise, but sank back again, sighing\r\nfor his disciple, dead in far-away Kulu. Kim watched head to one side,\r\nconsidering and interested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me the bowl. I know the people of this city\xe2\x80\x94all who are\r\ncharitable. Give, and I will bring it back filled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSimply as a child the old man handed him the bowl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRest, thou. _I_ know the people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe trotted off to the open shop of a _kunjri_, a low-caste\r\nvegetable-seller, which lay opposite the belt-tramway line down the\r\nMotee Bazar. She knew Kim of old.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho, hast thou turned _yogi_ with thy begging-bowl?\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay.\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim proudly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is a new priest in the city\xe2\x80\x94a man such\r\nas I have never seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld priest\xe2\x80\x94young tiger,\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cI am tired of new\r\npriests! They settle on our wares like flies. Is the father of my son a\r\nwell of charity to give to all who ask?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cThy man is rather _yagi_ (bad-tempered) than _yogi_ (a\r\nholy man). But this priest is new. The Sahib in the Wonder House has\r\ntalked to him like a brother. O my mother, fill me this bowl. He\r\nwaits.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat bowl indeed! That cow-bellied basket! Thou hast as much grace as\r\nthe holy bull of Shiv. He has taken the best of a basket of onions\r\nalready, this morn; and forsooth, I must fill thy bowl. He comes here\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe huge, mouse-coloured Brahmini bull of the ward was shouldering his\r\nway through the many-coloured crowd, a stolen plantain hanging out of\r\nhis mouth. He headed straight for the shop, well knowing his privileges\r\nas a sacred beast, lowered his head, and puffed heavily along the line\r\nof baskets ere making his choice. Up flew Kim\xe2\x80\x99s hard little heel and\r\ncaught him on his moist blue nose. He snorted indignantly, and walked\r\naway across the tram-rails, his hump quivering with rage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee! I have saved more than the bowl will cost thrice over. Now,\r\nmother, a little rice and some dried fish atop\xe2\x80\x94yes, and some vegetable\r\ncurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA growl came out of the back of the shop, where a man lay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe drove away the bull,\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman in an undertone. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is good\r\nto give to the poor.\xe2\x80\x9d She took the bowl and returned it full of hot\r\nrice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my _yogi_ is not a cow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim gravely, making a hole with his\r\nfingers in the top of the mound. \xe2\x80\x9cA little curry is good, and a fried\r\ncake, and a morsel of conserve would please him, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a hole as big as thy head,\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman fretfully. But she\r\nfilled it, none the less, with good, steaming vegetable curry, clapped\r\na fried cake atop, and a morsel of clarified butter on the cake, dabbed\r\na lump of sour tamarind conserve at the side; and Kim looked at the\r\nload lovingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is good. When I am in the bazar the bull shall not come to this\r\nhouse. He is a bold beggar-man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd thou?\xe2\x80\x9d laughed the woman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut speak well of bulls. Hast thou not\r\ntold me that some day a Red Bull will come out of a field to help thee?\r\nNow hold all straight and ask for the holy man\xe2\x80\x99s blessing upon me.\r\nPerhaps, too, he knows a cure for my daughter\xe2\x80\x99s sore eyes. Ask. him\r\nthat also, O thou Little Friend of all the World.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Kim had danced off ere the end of the sentence, dodging pariah dogs\r\nand hungry acquaintances.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThus do we beg who know the way of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said he proudly to the lama,\r\nwho opened his eyes at the contents of the bowl. \xe2\x80\x9cEat now and\xe2\x80\x94I will\r\neat with thee. Oh\xc3\xa9 _bhistie!_\xe2\x80\x9d he called to the water-carrier, sluicing\r\nthe crotons by the Museum. \xe2\x80\x9cGive water here. We men are thirsty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe men!\xe2\x80\x9d said the _bhistie_, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cIs one skinful enough for such\r\na pair? Drink, then, in the name of the Compassionate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe loosed a thin stream into Kim\xe2\x80\x99s hands, who drank native fashion; but\r\nthe lama must needs pull out a cup from his inexhaustible upper\r\ndraperies and drink ceremonially.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Pardesi_ (a foreigner),\xe2\x80\x9d Kim explained, as the old man delivered in\r\nan unknown tongue what was evidently a blessing.\r\n\r\nThey ate together in great content, clearing the beggingbowl. Then the\r\nlama took snuff from a portentous wooden snuff-gourd, fingered his\r\nrosary awhile, and so dropped into the easy sleep of age, as the shadow\r\nof Zam-Zammah grew long.\r\n\r\nKim loafed over to the nearest tobacco-seller, a rather lively young\r\nMohammedan woman, and begged a rank cigar of the brand that they sell\r\nto students of the Punjab University who copy English customs. Then he\r\nsmoked and thought, knees to chin, under the belly of the gun, and the\r\noutcome of his thoughts was a sudden and stealthy departure in the\r\ndirection of Nila Ram\xe2\x80\x99s timber-yard.\r\n\r\nThe lama did not wake till the evening life of the city had begun with\r\nlamp-lighting and the return of white-robed clerks and subordinates\r\nfrom the Government offices. He stared dizzily in all directions, but\r\nnone looked at him save a Hindu urchin in a dirty turban and\r\nIsabella-coloured clothes. Suddenly he bowed his head on his knees and\r\nwailed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this?\xe2\x80\x9d said the boy, standing before him. \xe2\x80\x9cHast thou been\r\nrobbed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my new _chela_ (disciple) that is gone away from me, and I know\r\nnot where he is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what like of man was thy disciple?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a boy who came to me in place of him who died, on account of\r\nthe merit which I had gained when I bowed before the Law within there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe pointed towards the Museum. \xe2\x80\x9cHe came upon me to show me a road which\r\nI had lost. He led me into the Wonder House, and by his talk emboldened\r\nme to speak to the Keeper of the Images, so that I was cheered and made\r\nstrong. And when I was faint with hunger he begged for me, as would a\r\n_chela_ for his teacher. Suddenly was he sent. Suddenly has he gone\r\naway. It was in my mind to have taught him the Law upon the road to\r\nBenares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim stood amazed at this, because he had overheard the talk in the\r\nMuseum, and knew that the old man was speaking the truth, which is a\r\nthing a native on the road seldom presents to a stranger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I see now that he was but sent for a purpose. By this I know that\r\nI shall find a certain River for which I seek.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe River of the Arrow?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, with a superior smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this yet another Sending?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cTo none have I spoken\r\nof my search, save to the Priest of the Images. Who art thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy _chela_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim simply, sitting on his heels. \xe2\x80\x9cI have never\r\nseen anyone like to thee in all this my life. I go with thee to\r\nBenares. And, too, I think that so old a man as thou, speaking the\r\ntruth to chance-met people at dusk, is in great need of a disciple.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the River\xe2\x80\x94the River of the Arrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that I heard when thou wast speaking to the Englishman. I lay\r\nagainst the door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought thou hadst been a guide permitted. Such\r\nthings fall sometimes\xe2\x80\x94but I am not worthy. Thou dost not, then, know\r\nthe River?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot I,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim laughed uneasily. \xe2\x80\x9cI go to look for\xe2\x80\x94for a bull\xe2\x80\x94a Red. Bull\r\non a green field who shall help me.\xe2\x80\x9d Boylike, if an acquaintance had a\r\nscheme, Kim was quite ready with one of his own; and, boylike, he had\r\nreally thought for as much as twenty minutes at a time of his father\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nprophecy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo what, child?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod knows, but so my father told me\xe2\x80\x9d. I heard thy talk in the Wonder\r\nHouse of all those new strange places in the Hills, and if one so old\r\nand so little\xe2\x80\x94so used to truth-telling\xe2\x80\x94may go out for the small matter\r\nof a river, it seemed to me that I too must go a-travelling. If it is\r\nour fate to find those things we shall find them\xe2\x80\x94thou, thy River; and\r\nI, my Bull, and the Strong Pillars and some other matters that I\r\nforget.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not pillars but a Wheel from which I would be free,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nlama.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all one. Perhaps they will make me a king,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, serenely\r\nprepared for anything.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will teach thee other and better desires upon the road,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama\r\nreplied in the voice of authority. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us go to Benares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot by night. Thieves are abroad. Wait till the day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there is no place to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d The old man was used to the order of\r\nhis monastery, and though he slept on the ground, as the Rule decrees,\r\npreferred a decency in these things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall get good lodging at the Kashmir Serai,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, laughing at\r\nhis perplexity. \xe2\x80\x9cI have a friend there. Come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe hot and crowded bazars blazed with light as they made their way\r\nthrough the press of all the races in Upper India, and the lama mooned\r\nthrough it like a man in a dream. It was his first experience of a\r\nlarge manufacturing city, and the crowded tram-car with its continually\r\nsquealing brakes frightened him. Half pushed, half towed, he arrived at\r\nthe high gate of the Kashmir Serai: that huge open square over against\r\nthe railway station, surrounded with arched cloisters, where the camel\r\nand horse caravans put up on their return from Central Asia. Here were\r\nall manner of Northern folk, tending tethered ponies and kneeling\r\ncamels; loading and unloading bales and bundles; drawing water for the\r\nevening meal at the creaking well-windlasses; piling grass before the\r\nshrieking, wild-eyed stallions; cuffing the surly caravan dogs; paying\r\noff camel-drivers; taking on new grooms; swearing, shouting, arguing,\r\nand chaffering in the packed square. The cloisters, reached by three or\r\nfour masonry steps, made a haven of refuge around this turbulent sea.\r\nMost of them were rented to traders, as we rent the arches of a\r\nviaduct; the space between pillar and pillar being bricked or boarded\r\noff into rooms, which were guarded by heavy wooden doors and cumbrous\r\nnative padlocks. Locked doors showed that the owner was away, and a few\r\nrude\xe2\x80\x94sometimes very rude\xe2\x80\x94chalk or paint scratches told where he had\r\ngone. Thus: \xe2\x80\x9cLutuf Ullah is gone to Kurdistan.\xe2\x80\x9d Below, in coarse verse:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Allah, who sufferest lice to live on the coat of a Kabuli, why hast\r\nthou allowed this louse Lutuf to live so long?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim, fending the lama between excited men and excited beasts, sidled\r\nalong the cloisters to the far end, nearest therailway station, where\r\nMahbub Ali, the horse-trader, lived when he came in from that\r\nmysterious land beyond the Passes of the North.\r\n\r\nKim had had many dealings with Mahbub in his little life, especially\r\nbetween his tenth and his thirteenth year\xe2\x80\x94and the big burly Afghan, his\r\nbeard dyed scarlet with lime (for he was elderly and did not wish his\r\ngrey hairs to show), knew the boy\xe2\x80\x99s value as a gossip. Sometimes he\r\nwould tell Kim to watch a man who had nothing whatever to do with\r\nhorses: to follow him for one whole day and report every soul with whom\r\nhe talked. Kim would deliver himself of his tale at evening, and Mahbub\r\nwould listen without a word or gesture. It was intrigue of some kind,\r\nKim knew; but its worth lay in saying nothing whatever to anyone except\r\nMahbub, who gave him beautiful meals all hot from the cookshop at the\r\nhead of the serai, and once as much as eight annas in money.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, hitting a bad-tempered camel on the nose. \xe2\x80\x9cOh\xc3\xa9,\r\nMahbub Ali!\xe2\x80\x9d He halted at a dark arch and slipped behind the bewildered\r\nlama.\r\n\r\nThe horse-trader, his deep, embroidered Bokhariot belt unloosed, was\r\nlying on a pair of silk carpet saddle-bags, pulling lazily at an\r\nimmense silver hookah. He turned his head very slightly at the cry; and\r\nseeing only the tall silent figure, chuckled in his deep chest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah! A lama! A Red Lama! It is far from Lahore to the Passes. What\r\ndost thou do here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama held out the begging-bowl mechanically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod\xe2\x80\x99s curse on all unbelievers!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub. \xe2\x80\x9cI do not give to a\r\nlousy Tibetan; but ask my Baltis over yonder behind the camels. They\r\nmay value your blessings. Oh, horseboys, here is a countryman of yours.\r\nSee if he be hungry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA shaven, crouching Balti, who had come down with the horses, and who\r\nwas nominally some sort of degraded Buddhist, fawned upon the priest,\r\nand in thick gutturals besought the Holy One to sit at the horseboys\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, pushing him lightly, and the lama strode away, leaving\r\nKim at the edge of the cloister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub Ali, returning to his hookah. \xe2\x80\x9cLittle Hindu, run\r\naway. God\xe2\x80\x99s curse on all unbelievers! Beg from those of my tail who are\r\nof thy faith.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaharaj,\xe2\x80\x9d whined Kim, using the Hindu form of address, and thoroughly\r\nenjoying the situation; \xe2\x80\x9cmy father is dead\xe2\x80\x94my mother is dead\xe2\x80\x94my stomach\r\nis empty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeg from my men among the horses, I say. There must be some Hindus in\r\nmy tail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mahbub Ali, but am _I_ a Hindu?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim in English.\r\n\r\nThe trader gave no sign of astonishment, but looked under shaggy\r\neyebrows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle Friend of all the World,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing. I am now that holy man\xe2\x80\x99s disciple; and we go a pilgrimage\r\ntogether\xe2\x80\x94to Benares, he says. He is quite mad, and I am tired of Lahore\r\ncity. I wish new air and water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut for whom dost thou work? Why come to me?\xe2\x80\x9d The voice was harsh with\r\nsuspicion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo whom else should I come? I have no money. It is not good to go\r\nabout without money. Thou wilt sell many horses to the officers. They\r\nare very fine horses, these new ones: I have seen them. Give me a\r\nrupee, Mahbub Ali, and when I come to my wealth I will give thee a bond\r\nand pay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUm!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub Ali, thinking swiftly. \xe2\x80\x9cThou hast never before lied\r\nto me. Call that lama\xe2\x80\x94stand back in the dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, our tales will agree,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe go to Benares,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, as soon as he understood the drift\r\nof Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s questions. \xe2\x80\x9cThe boy and I, I go to seek for a certain\r\nRiver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe\xe2\x80\x94but the boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is my disciple. He was sent, I think, to guide me to that River.\r\nSitting under a gun was I when he came suddenly. Such things have\r\nbefallen the fortunate to whom guidance was allowed. But I remember\r\nnow, he said he was of this world\xe2\x80\x94a Hindu.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd his name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I did not ask. Is he not my disciple?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis country\xe2\x80\x94his race\xe2\x80\x94his village? Mussalman\xe2\x80\x94Sikh Hindu\xe2\x80\x94Jain\xe2\x80\x94low caste\r\nor high?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I ask? There is neither high nor low in the Middle Way. If\r\nhe is my _chela_\xe2\x80\x94does\xe2\x80\x94will\xe2\x80\x94can anyone take him from me? for, look you,\r\nwithout him I shall not find my River.\xe2\x80\x9d He wagged his head solemnly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone shall take him from thee. Go, sit among my Baltis,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub\r\nAli, and the lama drifted off, soothed by the promise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he not quite mad?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, coming forward to the light again.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I lie to thee, Hajji?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMahbub puffed his hookah in silence. Then he began, almost whispering:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmballa is on the road to Benares\xe2\x80\x94if indeed ye two go there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTck! Tck! I tell thee he does not know how to lie\xe2\x80\x94as we two know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if thou wilt carry a message for me as far as Umballa, I will give\r\nthee money. It concerns a horse\xe2\x80\x94a white stallion which I have sold to\r\nan officer upon the last time I returned from the Passes. But\r\nthen\xe2\x80\x94stand nearer and hold up hands as begging\xe2\x80\x94the pedigree of the\r\nwhite stallion was not fully established, and that officer, who is now\r\nat Umballa, bade me make it clear.\xe2\x80\x9d (Mahbub here described the horse\r\nand the appearance of the officer.) \xe2\x80\x9cSo the message to that officer\r\nwill be: \xe2\x80\x98The pedigree of the white stallion is fully established.\xe2\x80\x99 By\r\nthis will he know that thou comest from me. He will then say \xe2\x80\x98What\r\nproof hast thou?\xe2\x80\x99 and thou wilt answer: \xe2\x80\x98Mahbub Ali has given me the\r\nproof.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd all for the sake of a white stallion,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, with a giggle,\r\nhis eyes aflame.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat pedigree I will give thee now\xe2\x80\x94in my own fashion and some hard\r\nwords as well.\xe2\x80\x9d A shadow passed behind Kim, and a feeding camel. Mahbub\r\nAli raised his voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah! Art thou the only beggar in the city? Thy mother is dead. Thy\r\nfather is dead. So is it with all of them. Well, well\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned as feeling on the floor beside him and tossed a flap of soft,\r\ngreasy Mussalman bread to the boy. \xe2\x80\x9cGo and lie down among my horseboys\r\nfor tonight\xe2\x80\x94thou and the lama. Tomorrow I may give thee service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim slunk away, his teeth in the bread, and, as he expected, he found a\r\nsmall wad of folded tissue-paper wrapped in oilskin, with three silver\r\nrupees\xe2\x80\x94enormous largesse. He smiled and thrust money and paper into his\r\nleather amulet-case. The lama, sumptuously fed by Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s Baltis, was\r\nalready asleep in a corner of one of the stalls. Kim lay down beside\r\nhim and laughed. He knew he had rendered a service to Mahbub Ali, and\r\nnot for one little minute did he believe the tale of the stallion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npedigree.\r\n\r\nBut Kim did not suspect that Mahbub Ali, known as one of the best\r\nhorse-dealers in the Punjab, a wealthy and enterprising trader, whose\r\ncaravans penetrated far and far into the Back of Beyond, was registered\r\nin one of the locked books of the Indian Survey Department as C25 IB.\r\nTwice or thrice yearly C25 would send in a little story, baldly told\r\nbut most interesting, and generally\xe2\x80\x94it was checked by the statements of\r\nR17 and M4\xe2\x80\x94quite true. It concerned all manner of out-of-the-way\r\nmountain principalities, explorers of nationalities other than English,\r\nand the guntrade\xe2\x80\x94was, in brief, a small portion of that vast mass of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cinformation received\xe2\x80\x9d on which the Indian Government acts. But,\r\nrecently, five confederated Kings, who had no business to confederate,\r\nhad been informed by a kindly Northern Power that there was a leakage\r\nof news from their territories into British India. So those Kings\xe2\x80\x99\r\nPrime Ministers were seriously annoyed and took steps, after the\r\nOriental fashion. They suspected, among many others, the bullying,\r\nred-bearded horsedealer whose caravans ploughed through their\r\nfastnesses belly-deep in snow. At least, his caravan that season had\r\nbeen ambushed and shot at twice on the way down, when Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s men\r\naccounted for three strange ruffians who might, or might not, have been\r\nhired for the job. Therefore Mahbub had avoided halting at the\r\ninsalubrious city of Peshawur, and had come through without stop to\r\nLahore, where, knowing his country-people, he anticipated curious\r\ndevelopments.\r\n\r\nAnd there was that on Mahbub Ali which he did not wish to keep an hour\r\nlonger than was necessary\xe2\x80\x94a wad of closely folded tissue-paper, wrapped\r\nin oilskin\xe2\x80\x94an impersonal, unaddressed statement, with five microscopic\r\npin-holes in one corner, that most scandalously betrayed the five\r\nconfederated Kings, the sympathetic Northern Power, a Hindu banker in\r\nPeshawur, a firm of gun-makers in Belgium, and an important,\r\nsemi-independent Mohammedan ruler to the south. This last was R17\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwork, which Mahbub had picked up beyond the Dora Pass and was carrying\r\nin for R17, who, owing to circumstances over which he had no control,\r\ncould not leave his post of observation. Dynamite was milky and\r\ninnocuous beside that report of C25; and even an Oriental, with an\r\nOriental\xe2\x80\x99s views of the value of time, could see that the sooner it was\r\nin the proper hands the better. Mahbub had no particular desire to die\r\nby violence, because two or three family blood-feuds across the Border\r\nhung unfinished on his hands, and when these scores were cleared he\r\nintended to settle down as a more or less virtuous citizen. He had\r\nnever passed the serai gate since his arrival two days ago, but had\r\nbeen ostentatious in sending telegrams to Bombay, where he banked some\r\nof his money; to Delhi, where a sub-partner of his own clan was selling\r\nhorses to the agent of a Rajputana state; and to Umballa, where an\r\nEnglishman was excitedly demanding the pedigree of a white stallion.\r\nThe public letter-writer, who knew English, composed excellent\r\ntelegrams, such as: \xe2\x80\x9c_Creighton, Laurel Bank, Umballa. Horse is Arabian\r\nas already advised. Sorrowful delayed pedigree which am translating._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd later to the same address: \xe2\x80\x9c_Much sorrowful delay. Will forward\r\npedigree._\xe2\x80\x9d To his sub-partner at Delhi he wired: \xe2\x80\x9c_Lutuf Ullah. Have\r\nwired two thousand rupees your credit Luchman Narain\xe2\x80\x99s bank._\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d This\r\nwas entirely in the way of trade, but every one of those telegrams was\r\ndiscussed and rediscussed, by parties who conceived themselves to be\r\ninterested, before they went over to the railway station in charge of a\r\nfoolish Balti, who allowed all sorts of people to read them on the\r\nroad.\r\n\r\nWhen, in Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s own picturesque language, he had muddied the wells of\r\ninquiry with the stick of precaution, Kim had dropped on him, sent from\r\nHeaven; and, being as prompt as he was unscrupulous, Mahbub Ali used to\r\ntaking all sorts of gusty chances, pressed him into service on the\r\nspot.\r\n\r\nA wandering lama with a low-caste boy-servant might attract a moment\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninterest as they wandered about India, the land of pilgrims; but no one\r\nwould suspect them or, what was more to the point, rob.\r\n\r\nHe called for a new light-ball to his hookah, and considered the case.\r\nIf the worst came to the worst, and the boy came to harm, the paper\r\nwould incriminate nobody. And he would go up to Umballa leisurely\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94at a certain risk of exciting fresh suspicion\xe2\x80\x94repeat his tale by\r\nword of mouth to the people concerned.\r\n\r\nBut R17\xe2\x80\x99s report was the kernel of the whole affair, and it would be\r\ndistinctly inconvenient if that failed to come to hand. However, God\r\nwas great, and Mahbub Ali felt he had done all he could for the time\r\nbeing. Kim was the one soul in the world who had never told him a lie.\r\nThat would have been a fatal blot on Kim\xe2\x80\x99s character if Mahbub had not\r\nknown that to others, for his own ends or Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s business, Kim could\r\nlie like an Oriental.\r\n\r\nThen Mahbub Ali rolled across the serai to the Gate of the Harpies who\r\npaint their eyes and trap the stranger, and was at some pains to call\r\non the one girl who, he had reason to believe, was a particular friend\r\nof a smooth-faced Kashmiri pundit who had waylaid his simple Balti in\r\nthe matter of the telegrams. It was an utterly foolish thing to do;\r\nbecause they fell to drinking perfumed brandy against the Law of the\r\nProphet, and Mahbub grew wonderfully drunk, and the gates of his mouth\r\nwere loosened, and he pursued the Flower of Delight with the feet of\r\nintoxication till he fell flat among the cushions, where the Flower of\r\nDelight, aided by a smooth-faced Kashmiri pundit, searched him from\r\nhead to foot most thoroughly.\r\n\r\nAbout the same hour Kim heard soft feet in Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s deserted stall. The\r\nhorse-trader, curiously enough, had left his door unlocked, and his men\r\nwere busy celebrating their return to India with a whole sheep of\r\nMahbub\xe2\x80\x99s bounty. A sleek young gentleman from Delhi, armed with a bunch\r\nof keys which the Flower had unshackled from the senseless one\xe2\x80\x99s belt,\r\nwent through every single box, bundle, mat, and saddle-bag in Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npossession even more systematically than the Flower and the pundit were\r\nsearching the owner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I think.\xe2\x80\x9d said the Flower scornfully an hour later, one rounded\r\nelbow on the snoring carcass, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he is no more than a pig of an\r\nAfghan horse-dealer, with no thought except women and horses. Moreover,\r\nhe may have sent it away by now\xe2\x80\x94if ever there were such a thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay\xe2\x80\x94in a matter touching Five Kings it would be next his black heart,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the pundit. \xe2\x80\x9cWas there nothing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Delhi man laughed and resettled his turban as he entered. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsearched between the soles of his slippers as the Flower searched his\r\nclothes. This is not the man but another. I leave little unseen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey did not say he was the very man,\xe2\x80\x9d said the pundit thoughtfully.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey said, \xe2\x80\x98Look if he be the man, since our counsels are troubled.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat North country is full of horse-dealers as an old coat of lice.\r\nThere is Sikandar Khan, Nur Ali Beg, and Farrukh Shah all heads of\r\nkafilas [caravans]\xe2\x80\x94who deal there,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Flower.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have not yet come in,\xe2\x80\x9d said the pundit. \xe2\x80\x9cThou must ensnare them\r\nlater.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhew!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Flower with deep disgust, rolling Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s head from\r\nher lap. \xe2\x80\x9cI earn my money. Farrukh Shah is a bear, Ali Beg a\r\nswashbuckler, and old Sikandar Khan\xe2\x80\x94yaie! Go! I sleep now. This swine\r\nwill not stir till dawn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Mahbub woke, the Flower talked to him severely on the sin of\r\ndrunkenness. Asiatics do not wink when they have outmanoeuvred an\r\nenemy, but as Mahbub Ali cleared his throat, tightened his belt, and\r\nstaggered forth under the early morning stars, he came very near to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a colt\xe2\x80\x99s trick!\xe2\x80\x9d said he to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cAs if every girl in\r\nPeshawur did not use it! But \xe2\x80\x99twas prettily done. Now God He knows how\r\nmany more there be upon the Road who have orders to test me\xe2\x80\x94perhaps\r\nwith the knife. So it stands that the boy must go to Umballa\xe2\x80\x94and by\r\nrail\xe2\x80\x94for the writing is something urgent. I abide here, following the\r\nFlower and drinking wine as an Afghan coper should.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe halted at the stall next but one to his own. His men lay there heavy\r\nwith sleep. There was no sign of Kim or the lama.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUp!\xe2\x80\x9d He stirred a sleeper. \xe2\x80\x9cWhither went those who lay here last\r\neven\xe2\x80\x94the lama and the boy? Is aught missing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay,\xe2\x80\x9d grunted the man, \xe2\x80\x9cthe old madman rose at second cockcrow saying\r\nhe would go to Benares, and the young one led him away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe curse of Allah on all unbelievers!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub heartily, and\r\nclimbed into his own stall, growling in his beard.\r\n\r\nBut it was Kim who had wakened the lama\xe2\x80\x94Kim with one eye laid against a\r\nknot-hole in the planking, who had seen the Delhi man\xe2\x80\x99s search through\r\nthe boxes. This was no common thief that turned over letters, bills,\r\nand saddles\xe2\x80\x94no mere burglar who ran a little knife sideways into the\r\nsoles of Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s slippers, or picked the seams of the saddle-bags so\r\ndeftly. At first Kim had been minded to give the alarm\xe2\x80\x94the long-drawn\r\n_cho-or\xe2\x80\x94choor!_ (thief! thief!) that sets the serai ablaze of nights;\r\nbut he looked more carefully, and, hand on amulet, drew his own\r\nconclusions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be the pedigree of that made-up horse-lie,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nthing that I carry to Umballa. Better that we go now. Those who search\r\nbags with knives may presently search bellies with knives. Surely there\r\nis a woman behind this. Hai! Hai! in a whisper to the light-sleeping\r\nold man. \xe2\x80\x9cCome. It is time\xe2\x80\x94time to go to Benares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama rose obediently, and they passed out of the serai like\r\nshadows.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II\r\n\r\n\r\nAnd whoso will, from Pride released;\r\nContemning neither creed nor priest,\r\nMay feel the Soul of all the East.\r\n    About him at Kamakura.\r\n\r\nThey entered the fort-like railway station, black in the end of night;\r\nthe electrics sizzling over the goods-yard where they handle the heavy\r\nNorthern grain-traffic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the work of devils!\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, recoiling from the hollow\r\nechoing darkness, the glimmer of rails between the masonry platforms,\r\nand the maze of girders above. He stood in a gigantic stone hall paved,\r\nit seemed, with the sheeted dead third-class passengers who had taken\r\ntheir tickets overnight and were sleeping in the waiting-rooms. All\r\nhours of the twenty-four are alike to Orientals, and their passenger\r\ntraffic is regulated accordingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is where the fire-carriages come. One stands behind that\r\nhole\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim pointed to the ticket-office\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwho will give thee a paper to\r\ntake thee to Umballa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we go to Benares,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied petulantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll one. Benares then. Quick: she comes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake thou the purse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama, not so well used to trains as he had pretended, started as\r\nthe 3.25 a.m. south-bound roared in. The sleepers sprang to life, and\r\nthe station filled with clamour and shoutings, cries of water and\r\nsweetmeat vendors, shouts of native policemen, and shrill yells of\r\nwomen gathering up their baskets, their families, and their husbands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the train\xe2\x80\x94only the _te-rain_. It will not come here. Wait!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAmazed at the lama\xe2\x80\x99s immense simplicity (he had handed him a small bag\r\nfull of rupees), Kim asked and paid for a ticket to Umballa. A sleepy\r\nclerk grunted and flung out a ticket to the next station, just six\r\nmiles distant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, scanning it with a grin. \xe2\x80\x9cThis may serve for farmers,\r\nbut I live in the city of Lahore. It was cleverly done, Babu. Now give\r\nthe ticket to Umballa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Babu scowled and dealt the proper ticket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow another to Amritzar,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, who had no notion of spending\r\nMahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s money on anything so crude as a paid ride to Umballa. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nprice is so much. The small money in return is just so much. I know the\r\nways of the _te-rain_ ... Never did _yogi_ need _chela_ as thou dost,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe went on merrily to the bewildered lama. \xe2\x80\x9cThey would have flung thee\r\nout at Mian Mir but for me. This way! Come!\xe2\x80\x9d He returned the money,\r\nkeeping only one anna in each rupee of the price of the Umballa ticket\r\nas his commission\xe2\x80\x94the immemorial commission of Asia.\r\n\r\nThe lama jibbed at the open door of a crowded third-class carriage.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere it not better to walk?\xe2\x80\x9d said he weakly.\r\n\r\nA burly Sikh artisan thrust forth his bearded head. \xe2\x80\x9cIs he afraid? Do\r\nnot be afraid. I remember the time when I was afraid of the train.\r\nEnter! This thing is the work of the Government.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not fear,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cHave ye room within for two?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no room even for a mouse,\xe2\x80\x9d shrilled the wife of a well-to-do\r\ncultivator\xe2\x80\x94a Hindu Jat from the rich Jullundur, district. Our night\r\ntrains are not as well looked after as the day ones, where the sexes\r\nare very strictly kept to separate carriages.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, mother of my son, we can make space,\xe2\x80\x9d said the blueturbaned\r\nhusband. \xe2\x80\x9cPick up the child. It is a holy man, see\xe2\x80\x99st thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd my lap full of seventy times seven bundles! Why not bid him sit on\r\nmy knee, Shameless? But men are ever thus!\xe2\x80\x9d She looked round for\r\napproval. An Amritzar courtesan near the window sniffed behind her head\r\ndrapery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnter! Enter!\xe2\x80\x9d cried a fat Hindu money-lender, his folded account-book\r\nin a cloth under his arm. With an oily smirk: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is well to be kind to\r\nthe poor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, at seven per cent a month with a mortgage on the unborn calf,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid a young Dogra soldier going south on leave; and they all laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill it travel to Benares?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly. Else why should we come? Enter, or we are left,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee!\xe2\x80\x9d shrilled the Amritzar girl. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has never entered a train. Oh,\r\nsee!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, help,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cultivator, putting out a large brown hand and\r\nhauling him in. \xe2\x80\x9cThus is it done, father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94I sit on the floor. It is against the Rule to sit on a bench,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cMoreover, it cramps me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say,\xe2\x80\x9d began the money-lender, pursing his lips, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there is not\r\none rule of right living which these _te-rains_ do not cause us to\r\nbreak. We sit, for example, side by side with all castes and peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYea, and with most outrageously shameless ones,\xe2\x80\x9d said the wife,\r\nscowling at the Amritzar girl making eyes at the young sepoy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said we might have gone by cart along the road,\xe2\x80\x9d said the husband,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand thus have saved some money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94and spent twice over what we saved on food by the way. That was\r\ntalked out ten thousand times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, by ten thousand tongues,\xe2\x80\x9d grunted he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gods help us poor women if we may not speak. Oho! He is of that\r\nsort which may not look at or reply to a woman.\xe2\x80\x9d For the lama,\r\nconstrained by his Rule, took not the faintest notice of her. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd his\r\ndisciple is like him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim most promptly. \xe2\x80\x9cNot when the woman is\r\nwell-looking and above all charitable to the hungry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA beggar\xe2\x80\x99s answer,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Sikh, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cThou hast brought it on\r\nthyself, sister!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\xe2\x80\x99s hands were crooked in supplication.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd whither goest thou?\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman, handing him the half of a\r\ncake from a greasy package.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven to Benares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJugglers belike?\xe2\x80\x9d the young soldier suggested. \xe2\x80\x9cHave ye any tricks to\r\npass the time? Why does not that yellow man answer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim stoutly, \xe2\x80\x9che is holy, and thinks upon matters\r\nhidden from thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be well. We of the Ludhiana Sikhs\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he rolled it out\r\nsonorously\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cdo not trouble our heads with doctrine. We fight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy sister\xe2\x80\x99s brother\xe2\x80\x99s son is _naik_ (corporal) in that regiment,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Sikh craftsman quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are also some Dogra companies\r\nthere.\xe2\x80\x9d The soldier glared, for a Dogra is of other caste than a Sikh,\r\nand the banker tittered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are all one to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Amritzar girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat we believe,\xe2\x80\x9d snorted the cultivator\xe2\x80\x99s wife malignantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, but all who serve the Sirkar with weapons in their hands are, as\r\nit were, one brotherhood. There is one brotherhood of the caste, but\r\nbeyond that again\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she looked round timidly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe bond of the\r\n_Pulton_\xe2\x80\x94the Regiment\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother is in a Jat regiment,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cultivator. \xe2\x80\x9cDogras be good\r\nmen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy Sikhs at least were of that opinion,\xe2\x80\x9d said the soldier, with a\r\nscowl at the placid old man in the corner. \xe2\x80\x9c_Thy_ Sikhs thought so when\r\nour two companies came to help them at the Pirzai Kotal in the face of\r\neight Afridi standards on the ridge not three months gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe told the story of a Border action in which the Dogra companies of\r\nthe Ludhiana Sikhs had acquitted themselves well. The Amritzar girl\r\nsmiled; for she knew the talk was to win her approval.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas!\xe2\x80\x9d said the cultivator\xe2\x80\x99s wife at the end. \xe2\x80\x9cSo their villages were\r\nburnt and their little children made homeless?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey had marked our dead. They paid a great payment after we of the\r\nSikhs had schooled them. So it was. Is this Amritzar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, and here they cut our tickets,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, fumbling at his\r\nbelt.\r\n\r\nThe lamps were paling in the dawn when the half-caste guard came round.\r\nTicket-collecting is a slow business in the East, where people secrete\r\ntheir tickets in all sorts of curious places. Kim produced his and was\r\ntold to get out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I go to Umballa,\xe2\x80\x9d he protested. \xe2\x80\x9cI go with this holy man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou canst go to Jehannum for aught I care. This ticket is only\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim burst into a flood of tears, protesting that the lama was his\r\nfather and his mother, that he was the prop of the lama\xe2\x80\x99s declining\r\nyears, and that the lama would die without his care. All the carriage\r\nbade the guard be merciful\xe2\x80\x94the banker was specially eloquent here\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nthe guard hauled Kim on to the platform. The lama blinked\xe2\x80\x94he could not\r\novertake the situation and Kim lifted up his voice and wept outside the\r\ncarriage window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very poor. My father is dead\xe2\x80\x94my mother is dead. O charitable\r\nones, if I am left here, who shall tend that old man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94what is this?\xe2\x80\x9d the lama repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cHe must go to Benares. He must\r\ncome with me. He is my _chela_. If there is money to be paid\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, be silent,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Kim; \xe2\x80\x9care we Rajahs to throw away good\r\nsilver when the world is so charitable?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Amritzar girl stepped out with her bundles, and it was on her that\r\nKim kept his watchful eye. Ladies of that persuasion, he knew, were\r\ngenerous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA ticket\xe2\x80\x94a little _tikkut_ to Umballa\xe2\x80\x94O Breaker of Hearts!\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nlaughed. \xe2\x80\x9cHast thou no charity?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes the holy man come from the North?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom far and far in the North he comes,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cFrom among the\r\nhills.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is snow among the pine-trees in the North\xe2\x80\x94in the hills there is\r\nsnow. My mother was from Kulu. Get thee a ticket. Ask him for a\r\nblessing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen thousand blessings,\xe2\x80\x9d shrilled Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cO Holy One, a woman has given\r\nus in charity so that I can come with thee\xe2\x80\x94a woman with a golden heart.\r\nI run for the _tikkut_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl looked up at the lama, who had mechanically followed Kim to\r\nthe platform. He bowed his head that he might not see her, and muttered\r\nin Tibetan as she passed on with the crowd.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLight come\xe2\x80\x94light go,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cultivator\xe2\x80\x99s wife viciously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has acquired merit,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cBeyond doubt it was a\r\nnun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere be ten thousand such nuns in Amritzar alone. Return, old man, or\r\nthe _te-rain_ may depart without thee,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the banker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot only was it sufficient for the ticket, but for a little food\r\nalso,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, leaping to his place. \xe2\x80\x9cNow eat, Holy One. Look. Day\r\ncomes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGolden, rose, saffron, and pink, the morning mists smoked away across\r\nthe flat green levels. All the rich Punjab lay out in the splendour of\r\nthe keen sun. The lama flinched a little as the telegraph-posts swung\r\nby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGreat is the speed of the _te-rain_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, with a\r\npatronizing grin. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have gone farther since Lahore than thou couldst\r\nwalk in two days: at even, we shall enter Umballa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is still far from Benares,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama wearily, mumbling\r\nover the cakes that Kim offered. They all unloosed their bundles and\r\nmade their morning meal. Then the banker, the cultivator, and the\r\nsoldier prepared their pipes and wrapped the compartment in choking,\r\nacrid smoke, spitting and coughing and enjoying themselves. The Sikh\r\nand the cultivator\xe2\x80\x99s wife chewed _pan;_ the lama took snuff and told\r\nhis beads, while Kim, cross-legged, smiled over the comfort of a full\r\nstomach.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat rivers have ye by Benares?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama of a sudden to the\r\ncarriage at large.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have Gunga,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the banker, when the little titter had\r\nsubsided.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat others?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat other than Gunga?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, but in my mind was the thought of a certain River of healing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is Gunga. Who bathes in her is made clean and goes to the Gods.\r\nThrice have I made pilgrimage to Gunga.\xe2\x80\x9d He looked round proudly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was need,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young sepoy drily, and the travellers\xe2\x80\x99 laugh\r\nturned against the banker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClean\xe2\x80\x94to return again to the Gods,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama muttered. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd to go\r\nforth on the round of lives anew\xe2\x80\x94still tied to the Wheel.\xe2\x80\x9d He shook his\r\nhead testily. \xe2\x80\x9cBut maybe there is a mistake. Who, then, made Gunga in\r\nthe beginning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gods. Of what known faith art thou?\xe2\x80\x9d the banker said, appalled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI follow the Law\xe2\x80\x94the Most Excellent Law. So it was the Gods that made\r\nGunga. What like of Gods were they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe carriage looked at him in amazement. It was inconceivable that\r\nanyone should be ignorant of Gunga.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94what is thy God?\xe2\x80\x9d said the money-lender at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear!\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, shifting the rosary to his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cHear: for I\r\nspeak of Him now! O people of Hind, listen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe began in Urdu the tale of the Lord Buddha, but, borne by his own\r\nthoughts, slid into Tibetan and long-droned texts from a Chinese book\r\nof the Buddha\xe2\x80\x99s life. The gentle, tolerant folk looked on reverently.\r\nAll India is full of holy men stammering gospels in strange tongues;\r\nshaken and consumed in the fires of their own zeal; dreamers, babblers,\r\nand visionaries: as it has been from the beginning and will continue to\r\nthe end.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUm!\xe2\x80\x9d said the soldier of the Ludhiana Sikhs. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a Mohammedan\r\nregiment lay next to us at the Pirzai Kotal, and a priest of theirs\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nwas, as I remember, a _naik_\xe2\x80\x94when the fit was on him, spake prophecies.\r\nBut the mad all are in God\xe2\x80\x99s keeping. His officers overlooked much in\r\nthat man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama fell back on Urdu, remembering that he was in a strange land.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear the tale of the Arrow which our Lord loosed from the bow,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\nThis was much more to their taste, and they listened curiously while he\r\ntold it. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, O people of Hind, I go to seek that River. Know ye aught\r\nthat may guide me, for we be all men and women in evil case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is Gunga\xe2\x80\x94and Gunga alone\xe2\x80\x94who washes away sin.\xe2\x80\x9d ran the murmur\r\nround the carriage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThough past question we have good Gods Jullundur-way,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\ncultivator\xe2\x80\x99s wife, looking out of the window. \xe2\x80\x9cSee how they have\r\nblessed the crops.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo search every river in the Punjab is no small matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said her\r\nhusband. \xe2\x80\x9cFor me, a stream that leaves good silt on my land suffices,\r\nand I thank Bhumia, the God of the Home-stead.\xe2\x80\x9d He shrugged one\r\nknotted, bronzed shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink you our Lord came so far North?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, turning to Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may be,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim replied soothingly, as he spat red _pan_-juice on the\r\nfloor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe last of the Great Ones,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Sikh with authority, \xe2\x80\x9cwas\r\nSikander Julkarn [Alexander the Great]. He paved the streets of\r\nJullundur and built a great tank near Umballa. That pavement holds to\r\nthis day; and the tank is there also. I never heard of thy God.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet thy hair grow long and talk Punjabi,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young soldier\r\njestingly to Kim, quoting a Northern proverb. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is all that makes a\r\nSikh.\xe2\x80\x9d But he did not say this very loud.\r\n\r\nThe lama sighed and shrank into himself, a dingy, shapeless mass. In\r\nthe pauses of their talk they could hear the low droning \xe2\x80\x9c_Om mane\r\npudme hum!
