Om mane pudme hum!_\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and the thick click of the wooden\r\nrosary beads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt irks me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cThe speed and the clatter irk me.\r\nMoreover, my _chela_, I think that maybe we have over-passed that\r\nRiver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace, peace,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cWas not the River near Benares? We are yet\r\nfar from the place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94if our Lord came North, it may be any one of these little ones\r\nthat we have run across.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou wast sent to me\xe2\x80\x94wast thou sent to me?\xe2\x80\x94for the merit I had\r\nacquired over yonder at Such-zen. It was\r\ntoo dark to see his face, so Kim, beggar-wise, tried an old experiment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProtector of the Poor!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man backed towards the voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMahbub Ali says\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHah! When the servant passed\r\nhe picked it up, dropped a rupee\xe2\x80\x94Kim could hear the clink\xe2\x80\x94and strode\r\ninto the house, never turning round. Swiftly Kim took up the money; but\r\nfor all his training, he was Irish enough by birth to reckon silver the\r\nleast part of any game. What he desired was the visible effect of\r\naction; so, instead of slinking away, he lay close in the grass and\r\nwormed nearer to the house.\r\n\r\nHe saw\xe2\x80\x94Indian bungalows are open through and through\xe2\x80\x94the Englishman\r\nreturn to a small dressing-room, in a comer of the veranda, that was\r\nhalf office, littered with papers and despatch-boxes, and sit down to\r\nstudy Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s message. It\xe2\x80\x99s punishment\xe2\x80\x94not\r\nwar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs the trooper cantered off, Kim crawled round to the back of the\r\nhouse, where, going on his Lahore experiences, he judged there would be\r\nfood\xe2\x80\x94and information. The kitchen was crowded with excited scullions,\r\none of whom kicked him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, feigning tears. \xe2\x80\x9cI came only to wash dishes in return\r\nfor a bellyful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll Umballa is on the same errand. He had\r\nlearned what he wanted, and when the scullion turned he was gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd all that trouble,\xe2\x80\x9d said he to himself, thinking as usual in\r\nHindustani, \xe2\x80\x9cfor a horse\xe2\x80\x99s pedigree! Mahbub Ali should have come to me\r\nto learn a little lying. Every time before that I have borne a message\r\nit concerned a woman. Now it is men. Better. The tall man said that\r\nthey will loose a great army to punish someone\xe2\x80\x94somewhere\xe2\x80\x94the news goes\r\nto Pindi and Peshawur. There are also guns. Would I had crept nearer.\r\nIt is big news!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe returned to find the cultivator\xe2\x80\x99s cousin\xe2\x80\x99s younger brother\r\ndiscussing the family law-suit in all its bearings with the cultivator\r\nand his wife and a few friends, while the lama dozed. After the evening\r\nmeal some one passed him a water-pipe; and Kim felt very much of a man\r\nas he pulled at the smooth coconut-shell, his legs spread abroad in the\r\nmoonlight, his tongue clicking in remarks from time to time. His hosts\r\nwere most polite; for the cultivator\xe2\x80\x99s wife had told them of his vision\r\nof the Red Bull, and of his probable descent from another world.\r\nMoreover, the lama was a great and venerable curiosity.\r\n\r\nThe family priest, an old, tolerant Sarsut Brahmin, dropped in later,\r\nand naturally started a theological argument to impress the family. By\r\ncreed, of course, they were all on their priest\xe2\x80\x99s side, but the lama\r\nwas the guest and the novelty. His gentle kindliness, and his\r\nimpressive Chinese quotations, that sounded like spells, delighted them\r\nhugely; and in this sympathetic, simple air, he expanded like the\r\nBodhisat\xe2\x80\x99s own lotus, speaking of his life in the great hills of\r\nSuch-zen, before, as he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI rose up to seek enlightenment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen it came out that in those worldly days he had been a master-hand\r\nat casting horoscopes and nativities; and the family priest led him on\r\nto describe his methods; each giving the planets names that the other\r\ncould not understand, and pointing upwards as the big stars sailed\r\nacross the dark. The children of the house tugged unrebuked at his\r\nrosary; and he clean forgot the Rule which forbids looking at women as\r\nhe talked of enduring snows, landslips, blocked passes, the remote\r\ncliffs where men find sapphires and turquoise, and that wonderful\r\nupland road that leads at last into Great China itself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow thinkest thou of this one?\xe2\x80\x9d said the cultivator aside to the\r\npriest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA holy man\xe2\x80\x94a holy man indeed. His Gods are not the Gods, but his feet\r\nare upon the Way,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd his methods of nativities,\r\nthough that is beyond thee, are wise and sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim lazily, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether I find my Red Bull on a green\r\nfield, as was promised me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat knowledge hast thou of thy birth-hour?\xe2\x80\x9d the priest asked,\r\nswelling with importance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetween first and second cockcrow of the first night in May.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf what year?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know; but upon the hour that I cried first fell the great\r\nearthquake in Srinagar which is in Kashmir.\xe2\x80\x9d This Kim had from the\r\nwoman who took care of him, and she again from Kimball O\xe2\x80\x99Hara. The\r\nearthquake had been felt in India, and for long stood a leading date in\r\nthe Punjab.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAi!\xe2\x80\x9d said a woman excitedly. This seemed to make Kim\xe2\x80\x99s supernatural\r\norigin more certain. \xe2\x80\x9cWas not such an one\xe2\x80\x99s daughter born then\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd her mother bore her husband four sons in four years all likely\r\nboys,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the cultivator\xe2\x80\x99s wife, sitting outside the circle in the\r\nshadow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone reared in the knowledge,\xe2\x80\x9d said the family priest, \xe2\x80\x9cforget how the\r\nplanets stood in their Houses upon that night.\xe2\x80\x9d He began to draw in the\r\ndust of the courtyard. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least thou hast good claim to a half of the\r\nHouse of the Bull. How runs thy prophecy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon a day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, delighted at the sensation he was creating, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshall be made great by means of a Red Bull on a green field, but first\r\nthere will enter two men making all things ready.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes: thus ever at the opening of a vision. A thick darkness that\r\nclears slowly; anon one enters with a broom making ready the place.\r\nThen begins the Sight. Two men\xe2\x80\x94thou sayest? Ay, ay. The Sun, leaving\r\nthe House of the Bull, enters that of the Twins. Hence the two men of\r\nthe prophecy. Let us now consider. Fetch me a twig, little one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe knitted his brows, scratched, smoothed out, and scratched again in\r\nthe dust mysterious signs\xe2\x80\x94to the wonder of all save the lama, who, with\r\nfine instinct, forbore to interfere.\r\n\r\nAt the end of half an hour, he tossed the twig from him with a grunt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHm! Thus say the stars. Within three days come the two men to make all\r\nthings ready. After them follows the Bull; but the sign over against\r\nhim is the sign of War and armed men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was indeed a man of the Ludhiana Sikhs in the carriage from\r\nLahore,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cultivator\xe2\x80\x99s wife hopefully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTck! What concern hast thou with war?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe priest to Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cThine is a red and an angry sign of War to be\r\nloosed very soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone\xe2\x80\x94none.\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cWe seek only peace and our\r\nRiver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim smiled, remembering what he had overheard in the dressing-room.\r\nDecidedly he was a favourite of the stars.\r\n\r\nThe priest brushed his foot over the rude horoscope. \xe2\x80\x9cMore than this I\r\ncannot see. In three days comes the Bull to thee, boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd my River, my River,\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cI had hoped his Bull would\r\nlead us both to the River.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas, for that wondrous River, my brother,\xe2\x80\x9d the priest replied. \xe2\x80\x9cSuch\r\nthings are not common.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNext morning, though they were pressed to stay, the lama insisted on\r\ndeparture. They gave Kim a large bundle of good food and nearly three\r\nannas in copper money for the needs of the road, and with many\r\nblessings watched the two go southward in the dawn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPity it is that these and such as these could not be freed from\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, then would only evil people be left on the earth, and who would\r\ngive us meat and shelter?\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Kim, stepping merrily under his\r\nburden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYonder is a small stream. Let us look,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, and he led from\r\nthe white road across the fields; walking into a very hornets\xe2\x80\x99 nest of\r\npariah dogs.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III\r\n\r\n\r\nYea, voice of every Soul that clung\r\nTo life that strove from rung to rung\r\nWhen Devadatta\xe2\x80\x99s rule was young,\r\n    The warm wind brings Kamakura.\r\n\r\nBehind them an angry farmer brandished a bamboo pole. He was a\r\nmarket-gardener, Arain by caste, growing vegetables and flowers for\r\nUmballa city, and well Kim knew the breed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch an one,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, disregarding the dogs, \xe2\x80\x9cis impolite to\r\nstrangers, intemperate of speech and uncharitable. Be warned by his\r\ndemeanour, my disciple.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHo, shameless beggars!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the farmer. \xe2\x80\x9cBegone! Get hence!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe go,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama returned, with quiet dignity. \xe2\x80\x9cWe go from these\r\nunblessed fields.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, sucking in his breath. \xe2\x80\x9cIf the next crops fail, thou\r\ncanst only blame thine own tongue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man shuffled uneasily in his slippers. \xe2\x80\x9cThe land is full of\r\nbeggars,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, half apologetically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd by what sign didst thou know that we would beg from thee, O Mali?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Kim tartly, using the name that a market-gardener least likes.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll we sought was to look at that river beyond the field there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRiver, forsooth!\xe2\x80\x9d the man snorted. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat city do ye hail from not to\r\nknow a canal-cut? It runs as straight as an arrow, and I pay for the\r\nwater as though it were molten silver. There is a branch of a river\r\nbeyond. But if ye need water I can give that\xe2\x80\x94and milk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, we will go to the river,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, striding out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMilk and a meal.\xe2\x80\x9d the man stammered, as he looked at the strange tall\r\nfigure. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I would not draw evil upon myself\xe2\x80\x94or my crops. But beggars\r\nare so many in these hard days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake notice.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama turned to Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was led to speak harshly by\r\nthe Red Mist of anger. That clearing from his eyes, he becomes\r\ncourteous and of an affable heart. May his fields be blessed! Beware\r\nnot to judge men too hastily, O farmer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have met holy ones who would have cursed thee from hearthstone to\r\nbyre,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim to the abashed man. \xe2\x80\x9cIs he not wise and holy? I am his\r\ndisciple.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe cocked his nose in the air loftily and stepped across the narrow\r\nfield-borders with great dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no pride,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, after a pause, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no pride\r\namong such as follow the Middle Way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou hast said he was low-caste and discourteous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLow-caste I did not say, for how can that be which is not? Afterwards\r\nhe amended his discourtesy, and I forgot the offence. Moreover, he is\r\nas we are, bound upon the Wheel of Things; but he does not tread the\r\nway of deliverance.\xe2\x80\x9d He halted at a little runlet among the fields, and\r\nconsidered the hoof-pitted bank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, how wilt thou know thy River?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, squatting in the shade\r\nof some tall sugar-cane.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I find it, an enlightenment will surely be given. This, I feel,\r\nis not the place. O littlest among the waters, if only thou couldst\r\ntell me where runs my River! But be thou blessed to make the fields\r\nbear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook! Look!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim sprang to his side and dragged him back. A\r\nyellow-and-brown streak glided from the purple rustling stems to the\r\nbank, stretched its neck to the water, drank, and lay still\xe2\x80\x94a big cobra\r\nwith fixed, lidless eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no stick\xe2\x80\x94I have no stick,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cI will get me one and\r\nbreak his back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? He is upon the Wheel as we are\xe2\x80\x94a life ascending or\r\ndescending\xe2\x80\x94very far from deliverance. Great evil must the soul have\r\ndone that is cast into this shape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hate all snakes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. No native training can quench the white\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s horror of the Serpent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him live out his life.\xe2\x80\x9d The coiled thing hissed and half opened\r\nits hood. \xe2\x80\x9cMay thy release come soon, brother!\xe2\x80\x9d the lama continued\r\nplacidly. \xe2\x80\x9cHast _thou_ knowledge, by chance, of my River?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever have I seen such a man as thou art,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim whispered, overwhelmed.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo the very snakes understand thy talk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho knows?\xe2\x80\x9d He passed within a foot of the cobra\xe2\x80\x99s poised head. It\r\nflattened itself among the dusty coils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, thou!\xe2\x80\x9d he called over his shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim\xe2\x80\x9d. \xe2\x80\x9cI go round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome. He does no hurt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim hesitated for a moment. The lama backed his order by some droned\r\nChinese quotation which Kim took for a charm. He obeyed and bounded\r\nacross the rivulet, and the snake, indeed, made no sign.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever have I seen such a man.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim wiped the sweat from his forehead.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, whither go we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is for thee to say. I am old, and a stranger\xe2\x80\x94far from my own\r\nplace. But that the _r\xc3\xaal_-carriage fills my head with noises of\r\ndevil-drums I would go in it to Benares now ... Yet by so going we may\r\nmiss the River. Let us find another river.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhere the hard-worked soil gives three and even four crops a year\r\nthrough patches of sugar-cane, tobacco, long white radishes, and\r\nnol-kol, all that day they strolled on, turning aside to every glimpse\r\nof water; rousing village dogs and sleeping villages at noonday; the\r\nlama replying to the volleyed questions with an unswerving simplicity.\r\nThey sought a River: a River of miraculous healing. Had any one\r\nknowledge of such a stream?\r\n\r\nSometimes men laughed, but more often heard the story out to the end\r\nand offered them a place in the shade, a drink of milk, and a meal. The\r\nwomen were always kind, and the little children as children are the\r\nworld over, alternately shy and venturesome.\r\n\r\nEvening found them at rest under the village tree of a mud-walled,\r\nmud-roofed hamlet, talking to the headman as the cattle came in from\r\nthe grazing-grounds and the women prepared the day\xe2\x80\x99s last meal. They\r\nhad passed beyond the belt of market-gardens round hungry Umballa, and\r\nwere among the mile-wide green of the staple crops.\r\n\r\nHe was a white-bearded and affable elder, used to entertaining\r\nstrangers. He dragged out a string bedstead for the lama, set warm\r\ncooked food before him, prepared him a pipe, and, the evening\r\nceremonies being finished in the village temple, sent for the village\r\npriest.\r\n\r\nKim told the older children tales of the size and beauty of Lahore, of\r\nrailway travel, and such-like city things, while the men talked, slowly\r\nas their cattle chew the cud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot fathom it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the headman at last to the priest. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\nreadest thou this talk?\xe2\x80\x9d The lama, his tale told, was silently telling\r\nhis beads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a Seeker.\xe2\x80\x9d the priest answered. \xe2\x80\x9cThe land is full of such.\r\nRemember him who came only last, month\xe2\x80\x94the _faquir_ with the tortoise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, but that man had right and reason, for Krishna Himself appeared in\r\na vision promising him Paradise without the burning-pyre if he\r\njourneyed to Prayag. This man seeks no God who is within my knowledge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace, he is old: he comes from far off, and he is mad,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nsmooth-shaven priest replied. \xe2\x80\x9cHear me.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned to the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cThree\r\n_kos_ (six miles) to the westward runs the great road to Calcutta.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I would go to Benares\xe2\x80\x94to Benares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to Benares also. It crosses all streams on this side of Hind. Now\r\nmy word to thee, Holy One, is rest here till tomorrow. Then take the\r\nroad\xe2\x80\x9d (it was the Grand Trunk Road he meant) \xe2\x80\x9cand test each stream that\r\nit overpasses; for, as I understand, the virtue of thy River lies\r\nneither in one pool nor place, but throughout its length. Then, if thy\r\nGods will, be assured that thou wilt come upon thy freedom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is well said.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama was much impressed by the plan. \xe2\x80\x9cWe will\r\nbegin tomorrow, and a blessing on thee for showing old feet such a near\r\nroad.\xe2\x80\x9d A deep, sing-song Chinese half-chant closed the sentence. Even\r\nthe priest was impressed, and the headman feared an evil spell: but\r\nnone could look at the lama\xe2\x80\x99s simple, eager face and doubt him long.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeest thou my _chela?_\xe2\x80\x9d he said, diving into his snuff-gourd with an\r\nimportant sniff. It was his duty to repay courtesy with courtesy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see\xe2\x80\x94and hear.\xe2\x80\x9d The headman rolled his eye where Kim was chatting to\r\na girl in blue as she laid crackling thorns on a fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe also has a Search of his own. No river, but a Bull. Yea, a Red Bull\r\non a green field will some day raise him to honour. He is, I think, not\r\naltogether of this world. He was sent of a sudden to aid me in this\r\nsearch, and his name is Friend of all the World.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe priest smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cHo, there, Friend of all the World,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried\r\nacross the sharp-smelling smoke, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat art thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis Holy One\xe2\x80\x99s disciple,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says thou are a _b\xc5\xabt_ (a spirit).\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan _b\xc5\xabts_ eat?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, with a twinkle. \xe2\x80\x9cFor I am hungry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no jest,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cA certain astrologer of that city\r\nwhose name I have forgotten\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is no more than the city of Umballa where we slept last night,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nKim whispered to the priest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, Umballa was it? He cast a horoscope and declared that my _chela_\r\nshould find his desire within two days. But what said he of the meaning\r\nof the stars, Friend of all the World?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim cleared his throat and looked around at the village greybeards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe meaning of my Star is War,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied pompously.\r\n\r\nSomebody laughed at the little tattered figure strutting on the\r\nbrickwork plinth under the great tree. Where a native would have lain\r\ndown, Kim\xe2\x80\x99s white blood set him upon his feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, War,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a sure prophecy,\xe2\x80\x9d rumbled a deep voice. \xe2\x80\x9cFor there is always\r\nwar along the Border\xe2\x80\x94as I know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was an old, withered man, who had served the Government in the days\r\nof the Mutiny as a native officer in a newly raised cavalry regiment.\r\nThe Government had given him a good holding in the village, and though\r\nthe demands of his sons, now grey-bearded officers on their own\r\naccount, had impoverished him, he was still a person of consequence.\r\nEnglish officials\xe2\x80\x94Deputy Commissioners even\xe2\x80\x94turned aside from the main\r\nroad to visit him, and on those occasions he dressed himself in the\r\nuniform of ancient days, and stood up like a ramrod.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this shall be a great war\xe2\x80\x94a war of eight thousand.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\xe2\x80\x99s voice\r\nshrilled across the quick-gathering crowd, astonishing himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRedcoats or our own regiments?\xe2\x80\x9d the old man snapped, as though he were\r\nasking an equal. His tone made men respect Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRedcoats,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim at a venture. \xe2\x80\x9cRedcoats and guns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94but the astrologer said no word of this,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the lama, snuffing\r\nprodigiously in his excitement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut _I_ know. The word has come to me, who am this Holy One\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndisciple. There will rise a war\xe2\x80\x94a war of eight thousand redcoats. From\r\nPindi and Peshawur they will be drawn. This is sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe boy has heard bazar-talk,\xe2\x80\x9d said the priest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he was always by my side,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cHow should he know? I\r\ndid not know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will make a clever juggler when the old man is dead,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the\r\npriest to the headman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat new trick is this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA sign. Give me a sign,\xe2\x80\x9d thundered the old soldier suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cIf there\r\nwere war my sons would have told me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen all is ready, thy sons, doubt not, will be told. But it is a long\r\nroad from thy sons to the man in whose hands these things lie.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\r\nwarmed to the game, for it reminded him of experiences in the\r\nletter-carrying line, when, for the sake of a few pice, he pretended to\r\nknow more than he knew. But now he was playing for larger things\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nsheer excitement and the sense of power. He drew a new breath and went\r\non.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld man, give _me_ a sign. Do underlings order the goings of eight\r\nthousand redcoats\xe2\x80\x94with guns?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d Still the old man answered as though Kim were an equal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDost thou know who He is, then, that gives the order?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen Him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo know again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have known Him since he was a lieutenant in the _top-khana_ (the\r\nArtillery).\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA tall man. A tall man with black hair, walking thus?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim took a few\r\npaces in a stiff, wooden style.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy. But that anyone may have seen.\xe2\x80\x9d The crowd were breathless\xe2\x80\x94still\r\nthrough all this talk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I will say more. Look now. First the\r\ngreat man walks thus. Then He thinks thus.\xe2\x80\x9d (Kim drew a forefinger over\r\nhis forehead and downwards till it came to rest by the angle of the\r\njaw.) \xe2\x80\x9cAnon He twitches his fingers thus. Anon He thrusts his hat under\r\nhis left armpit.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim illustrated the motion and stood like a stork.\r\n\r\nThe old man groaned, inarticulate with amazement; and the crowd\r\nshivered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo\xe2\x80\x94so\xe2\x80\x94so. But what does He when He is about to give an order?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe rubs the skin at the back of his neck\xe2\x80\x94thus. Then falls one finger\r\non the table and He makes a small sniffing noise through his nose. Then\r\nHe speaks, saying: \xe2\x80\x98Loose such and such a regiment. Call out such\r\nguns.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man rose stiffly and saluted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98For\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim translated into the vernacular the clinching sentences he\r\nhad heard in the dressing-room at Umballa\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98For,\xe2\x80\x99 says He, \xe2\x80\x98we should\r\nhave done this long ago. It is not war\xe2\x80\x94it is a chastisement. Snff!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnough. I believe. I have seen Him thus in the smoke of battles. Seen\r\nand heard. It is He!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw no smoke\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim\xe2\x80\x99s voice shifted to the rapt sing-song of the\r\nwayside fortune-teller. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw this in darkness. First came a man to\r\nmake things clear. Then came horsemen. Then came He standing in a ring\r\nof light. The rest followed as I have said. Old man, have I spoken\r\ntruth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is He. Past all doubt it is He.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe crowd drew a long, quavering breath, staring alternately at the old\r\nman, still at attention, and ragged Kim against the purple twilight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSaid I not\xe2\x80\x94said I not he was from the other world?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the lama\r\nproudly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is the Friend of all the World. He is the Friend of the\r\nStars!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least it does not concern us,\xe2\x80\x9d a man cried. \xe2\x80\x9cO thou young\r\nsoothsayer, if the gift abides with thee at all seasons, I have a\r\nred-spotted cow. She may be sister to thy Bull for aught I know\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr I care,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cMy Stars do not concern themselves with thy\r\ncattle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, but she is very sick,\xe2\x80\x9d a woman struck in. \xe2\x80\x9cMy man is a buffalo,\r\nor he would have chosen his words better. Tell me if she recover?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHad Kim been at all an ordinary boy, he would have carried on the play;\r\nbut one does not know Lahore city, and least of all the _faquirs_ by\r\nthe Taksali Gate, for thirteen years without also knowing human nature.\r\n\r\nThe priest looked at him sideways, something bitterly\xe2\x80\x94a dry and\r\nblighting smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there no priest, then, in the village? I thought I had seen a great\r\none even now,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d the woman began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou and thy husband hoped to get the cow cured for a handful of\r\nthanks.\xe2\x80\x9d The shot told: they were notoriously the closest-fisted couple\r\nin the village. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not well to cheat the temples. Give a young calf\r\nto thine own priest, and, unless thy Gods are angry past recall, she\r\nwill give milk within a month.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA master-beggar art thou,\xe2\x80\x9d purred the priest approvingly. \xe2\x80\x9cNot the\r\ncunning of forty years could have done better. Surely thou hast made\r\nthe old man rich?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little flour, a little butter and a mouthful of cardamoms,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\r\nretorted, flushed with the praise, but still cautious\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cDoes one grow\r\nrich on that? And, as thou canst see, he is mad. But it serves me while\r\nI learn the road at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe knew what the _faquirs_ of the Taksali Gate were like when they\r\ntalked among themselves, and copied the very inflection of their lewd\r\ndisciples.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs his Search, then, truth or a cloak to other ends? It may be\r\ntreasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is mad\xe2\x80\x94many times mad. There is nothing else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the old soldier bobbled up and asked if Kim would accept his\r\nhospitality for the night. The priest recommended him to do so, but\r\ninsisted that the honour of entertaining the lama belonged to the\r\ntemple\xe2\x80\x94at which the lama smiled guilelessly. Kim glanced from one face\r\nto the other, and drew his own conclusions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the money?\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, beckoning the old man off into the\r\ndarkness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn my bosom. Where else?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive it me. Quietly and swiftly give it me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why? Here is no ticket to buy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I thy _chela_, or am I not? Do I not safeguard thy old feet about\r\nthe ways? Give me the money and at dawn I will return it.\xe2\x80\x9d He slipped\r\nhis hand above the lama\xe2\x80\x99s girdle and brought away the purse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe it so\xe2\x80\x94be it so.\xe2\x80\x9d The old man nodded his head. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a great and\r\nterrible world. I never knew there were so many men alive in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNext morning the priest was in a very bad temper, but the lama was\r\nquite happy; and Kim had enjoyed a most interesting evening with the\r\nold man, who brought out his cavalry sabre and, balancing it on his dry\r\nknees, told tales of the Mutiny and young captains thirty years in\r\ntheir graves, till Kim dropped off to sleep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly the air of this country is good,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cI sleep\r\nlightly, as do all old men; but last night I slept unwaking till broad\r\nday. Even now I am heavy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrink a draught of hot milk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, who had carried not a few such\r\nremedies to opium-smokers of his acquaintance. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is time to take the\r\nRoad again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe long Road that overpasses all the rivers of Hind,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama\r\ngaily. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us go. But how thinkest thou, _chela_, to recompense these\r\npeople, and especially the priest, for their great kindness? Truly they\r\nare _b\xc5\xabt-parast_, but in other lives, maybe, they will receive\r\nenlightenment. A rupee to the temple? The thing within is no more than\r\nstone and red paint, but the heart of man we must acknowledge when and\r\nwhere it is good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoly One, hast thou ever taken the Road alone?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim looked up sharply,\r\nlike the Indian crows so busy about the fields.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely, child: from Kulu to Path\xc3\xa2nkot\xe2\x80\x94from Kulu, where my first\r\n_chela_ died. When men were kind to us we made offerings, and all men\r\nwere well-disposed throughout all the Hills.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is otherwise in Hind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim drily. \xe2\x80\x9cTheir Gods are many-armed\r\nand malignant. Let them alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would set thee on thy road for a little, Friend of all the World,\r\nthou and thy yellow man.\xe2\x80\x9d The old soldier ambled up the village street,\r\nall shadowy in the dawn, on a punt, scissor-hocked pony. \xe2\x80\x9cLast night\r\nbroke up the fountains of remembrance in my so-dried heart, and it was\r\nas a blessing to me. Truly there is war abroad in the air. I smell it.\r\nSee! I have brought my sword.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sat long-legged on the little beast, with the big sword at his\r\nside\xe2\x80\x94hand dropped on the pommel\xe2\x80\x94staring fiercely over the flat lands\r\ntowards the North. \xe2\x80\x9cTell me again how He showed in thy vision. Come up\r\nand sit behind me. The beast will carry two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am this Holy One\xe2\x80\x99s disciple,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, as they cleared the\r\nvillage-gate. The villagers seemed almost sorry to be rid of them, but\r\nthe priest\xe2\x80\x99s farewell was cold and distant. He had wasted some opium on\r\na man who carried no money.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is well spoken. I am not much used to holy men, but respect is\r\nalways good. There is no respect in these days\xe2\x80\x94not even when a\r\nCommissioner Sahib comes to see me. But why should one whose Star leads\r\nhim to war follow a holy man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he _is_ a holy man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cIn truth, and in talk\r\nand in act, holy. He is not like the others. I have never seen such an\r\none. We be not fortune-tellers, or jugglers, or beggars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art not. That I can see. But I do not know that other. He marches\r\nwell, though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe first freshness of the day carried the lama forward with long,\r\neasy, camel-like strides. He was deep in meditation, mechanically\r\nclicking his rosary.\r\n\r\nThey followed the rutted and worn country road that wound across the\r\nflat between the great dark-green mango-groves, the line of the\r\nsnowcapped Himalayas faint to the eastward. All India was at work in\r\nthe fields, to the creaking of well-wheels, the shouting of ploughmen\r\nbehind their cattle, and the clamour of the crows. Even the pony felt\r\nthe good influence and almost broke into a trot as Kim laid a hand on\r\nthe stirrup-leather.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt repents me that I did not give a rupee to the shrine,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nlama on the last bead of his eighty-one.\r\n\r\nThe old soldier growled in his beard, so that the lama for the first\r\ntime was aware of him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeekest thou the River also?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, turning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe day is new,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat need of a river save to water at\r\nbefore sundown? I come to show thee a short lane to the Big Road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a courtesy to be remembered, O man of good will. But why the\r\nsword?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old soldier looked as abashed as a child interrupted in his game of\r\nmake-believe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sword,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, fumbling it. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, that was a fancy of mine an old\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s fancy. Truly the police orders are that no man must bear weapons\r\nthroughout Hind, but\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he cheered up and slapped the hilt\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9call the\r\nconstabeels hereabout know me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not a good fancy,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat profit to kill men?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery little\xe2\x80\x94as I know; but if evil men were not now and then slain it\r\nwould not be a good world for weaponless dreamers. I do not speak\r\nwithout knowledge who have seen the land from Delhi south awash with\r\nblood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat madness was that, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gods, who sent it for a plague, alone know. A madness ate into all\r\nthe Army, and they turned against their officers. That was the first\r\nevil, but not past remedy if they had then held their hands. But they\r\nchose to kill the Sahibs\xe2\x80\x99 wives and children. Then came the Sahibs from\r\nover the sea and called them to most strict account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome such rumour, I believe, reached me once long ago. They called it\r\nthe Black Year, as I remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat manner of life hast thou led, not to know The Year? A rumour\r\nindeed! All earth knew, and trembled!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur earth never shook but once\xe2\x80\x94upon the day that the Excellent One\r\nreceived Enlightenment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmph! I saw Delhi shake at least\xe2\x80\x94and Delhi is the navel of the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo they turned against women and children? That was a bad deed, for\r\nwhich the punishment cannot be avoided.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMany strove to do so, but with very small profit. I was then in a\r\nregiment of cavalry. It broke. Of six hundred and eighty sabres stood\r\nfast to their salt\xe2\x80\x94how many, think you? Three. Of whom I was one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe greater merit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMerit! We did not consider it merit in those days. My people, my\r\nfriends, my brothers fell from me. They said: \xe2\x80\x98The time of the English\r\nis accomplished. Let each strike out a little holding for himself.\xe2\x80\x99 But\r\nI had talked with the men of Sobraon, of Chilianwallah, of Moodkee and\r\nFerozeshah. I said: \xe2\x80\x98Abide a little and the wind turns. There is no\r\nblessing in this work.\xe2\x80\x99 In those days I rode seventy miles with an\r\nEnglish Memsahib and her babe on my saddle-bow. (Wow! That was a horse\r\nfit for a man!) I placed them in safety, and back came I to my\r\nofficer\xe2\x80\x94the one that was not killed of our five. \xe2\x80\x98Give me work,\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nI, \xe2\x80\x98for I am an outcast among my own kind, and my cousin\xe2\x80\x99s blood is wet\r\non my sabre.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Be content,\xe2\x80\x99 said he. \xe2\x80\x98There is great work forward. When\r\nthis madness is over there is a recompense.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, there is a recompense when the madness is over, surely?\xe2\x80\x9d the lama\r\nmuttered half to himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey did not hang medals in those days on all who by accident had\r\nheard a gun fired. No! In nineteen pitched battles was I; in\r\nsix-and-forty skirmishes of horse; and in small affairs without number.\r\nNine wounds I bear; a medal and four clasps and the medal of an Order,\r\nfor my captains, who are now generals, remembered me when the\r\nKaisar-i-Hind had accomplished fifty years of her reign, and all the\r\nland rejoiced. They said: \xe2\x80\x98Give him the Order of Berittish India.\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\ncarry it upon my neck now. I have also my _jaghir_ (holding) from the\r\nhands of the State\xe2\x80\x94a free gift to me and mine. The men of the old\r\ndays\xe2\x80\x94they are now Commissioners\xe2\x80\x94come riding to me through the\r\ncrops\xe2\x80\x94high upon horses so that all the village sees\xe2\x80\x94and we talk out the\r\nold skirmishes, one dead man\xe2\x80\x99s name leading to another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd after?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, afterwards they go away, but not before my village has seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd at the last what wilt thou do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the last I shall die.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd after?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet the Gods order it. I have never pestered Them with prayers. I do\r\nnot think They will pester me. Look you, I have noticed in my long life\r\nthat those who eternally break in upon Those Above with complaints and\r\nreports and bellowings and weepings are presently sent for in haste, as\r\nour Colonel used to send for slack-jawed down-country men who talked\r\ntoo much. No, I have never wearied the Gods. They will remember this,\r\nand give me a quiet place where I can drive my lance in the shade, and\r\nwait to welcome my sons: I have no less than three Rissaldar\xe2\x80\x94majors\r\nall\xe2\x80\x94in the regiments.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they likewise, bound upon the Wheel, go forth from life to\r\nlife\xe2\x80\x94from despair to despair,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama below his breath, \xe2\x80\x9chot,\r\nuneasy, snatching.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy,\xe2\x80\x9d the old soldier chuckled. \xe2\x80\x9cThree Rissaldar\xe2\x80\x94majors in three\r\nregiments. Gamblers a little, but so am I. They must be well mounted;\r\nand one cannot take the horses as in the old days one took women. Well,\r\nwell, my holding can pay for all. How thinkest thou? It is a\r\nwell-watered strip, but my men cheat me. I do not know how to ask save\r\nat the lance\xe2\x80\x99s point. Ugh! I grow angry and I curse them, and they\r\nfeign penitence, but behind my back I know they call me a toothless old\r\nape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou never desired any other thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94yes\xe2\x80\x94a thousand times! A straight back and a close-clinging knee\r\nonce more; a quick wrist and a keen eye; and the marrow that makes a\r\nman. Oh, the old days\xe2\x80\x94the good days of my strength!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat strength is weakness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has turned so; but fifty years since I could have proved it\r\notherwise,\xe2\x80\x9d the old soldier retorted, driving his stirrup-edge into the\r\npony\xe2\x80\x99s lean flank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I know a River of great healing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have drank Gunga-water to the edge of dropsy. All she gave me was a\r\nflux, and no sort of strength.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not Gunga. The River that I know washes from all taint of sin.\r\nAscending the far bank one is assured of Freedom. I do not know thy\r\nlife, but thy face is the face of the honourable and courteous. Thou\r\nhast clung to thy Way, rendering fidelity when it was hard to give, in\r\nthat Black Year of which I now remember other tales. Enter now upon the\r\nMiddle Way which is the path to Freedom. Hear the Most Excellent Law,\r\nand do not follow dreams.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak, then, old man,\xe2\x80\x9d the soldier smiled, half saluting. \xe2\x80\x9cWe be all\r\nbabblers at our age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama squatted under the shade of a mango, whose shadow played\r\ncheckerwise over his face; the soldier sat stiffly on the pony; and\r\nKim, making sure that there were no snakes, lay down in the crotch of\r\nthe twisted roots.\r\n\r\nThere was a drowsy buzz of small life in hot sunshine, a cooing of\r\ndoves, and a sleepy drone of well-wheels across the fields. Slowly and\r\nimpressively the lama began. At the end of ten minutes the old soldier\r\nslid from his pony, to hear better as he said, and sat with the reins\r\nround his wrist. The lama\xe2\x80\x99s voice faltered, the periods lengthened. Kim\r\nwas busy watching a grey squirrel. When the little scolding bunch of\r\nfur, close pressed to the branch, disappeared, preacher and audience\r\nwere fast asleep, the old officer\xe2\x80\x99s strong-cut head pillowed on his\r\narm, the lama\xe2\x80\x99s thrown back against the tree-bole, where it showed like\r\nyellow ivory. A naked child toddled up, stared, and, moved by some\r\nquick impulse of reverence, made a solemn little obeisance before the\r\nlama\xe2\x80\x94only the child was so short and fat that it toppled over sideways,\r\nand Kim laughed at the sprawling, chubby legs. The child, scared and\r\nindignant, yelled aloud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHai! Hai!\xe2\x80\x9d said the soldier, leaping to his feet. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? What\r\norders? ... It is ... a child! I dreamed it was an alarm. Little\r\none\xe2\x80\x94little one\xe2\x80\x94do not cry. Have I slept? That was discourteous indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fear! I am afraid!\xe2\x80\x9d roared the child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it to fear? Two old men and a boy? How wilt thou ever make a\r\nsoldier, Princeling?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama had waked too, but, taking no direct notice of the child,\r\nclicked his rosary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that?\xe2\x80\x9d said the child, stopping a yell midway. \xe2\x80\x9cI have never\r\nseen such things. Give them me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha.\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, smiling, and trailing a loop of it on the grass:\r\n\r\nThis is a handful of cardamoms, This is a lump of _ghi:_\r\nThis is millet and chillies and rice, A supper for thee and me!\r\n\r\nThe child shrieked with joy, and snatched at the dark, glancing beads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old soldier. \xe2\x80\x9cWhence hadst thou that song, despiser of\r\nthis world?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI learned it in Path\xc3\xa2nkot\xe2\x80\x94sitting on a doorstep,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama shyly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is good to be kind to babes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I remember, before the sleep came on us, thou hadst told me that\r\nmarriage and bearing were darkeners of the true light, stumbling-blocks\r\nupon the Way. Do children drop from Heaven in thy country? Is it the\r\nWay to sing them songs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo man is all perfect,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama gravely, recoiling the rosary.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRun now to thy mother, little one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear him!\xe2\x80\x9d said the soldier to Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is ashamed for that he has\r\nmade a child happy. There was a very good householder lost in thee, my\r\nbrother. Hai, child!\xe2\x80\x9d He threw it a pice. \xe2\x80\x9cSweetmeats are always\r\nsweet.\xe2\x80\x9d And as the little figure capered away into the sunshine: \xe2\x80\x9cThey\r\ngrow up and become men. Holy One, I grieve that I slept in the midst of\r\nthy preaching. Forgive me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe be two old men,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cThe fault is mine. I listened to\r\nthy talk of the world and its madness, and one fault led to the next.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear him! What harm do thy Gods suffer from play with a babe? And that\r\nsong was very well sung. Let us go on and I will sing thee the song of\r\nNikal Seyn before Delhi\xe2\x80\x94the old song.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd they fared out from the gloom of the mango-tope, the old man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhigh, shrill voice ringing across the field, as wail by long-drawn wail\r\nhe unfolded the story of Nikal Seyn [Nicholson]\xe2\x80\x94the song that men sing\r\nin the Punjab to this day. Kim was delighted, and the lama listened\r\nwith deep interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ahi! Nikal Seyn is dead\xe2\x80\x94he died before Delhi! Lances of the North,\r\ntake vengeance for Nikal Seyn_.\xe2\x80\x9d He quavered it out to the end, marking\r\nthe trills with the flat of his sword on the pony\xe2\x80\x99s rump.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now we come to the Big Road,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, after receiving the\r\ncompliments of Kim; for the lama was markedly silent. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is long since\r\nI have ridden this way, but thy boy\xe2\x80\x99s talk stirred me. See, Holy\r\nOne\xe2\x80\x94the Great Road which is the backbone of all Hind. For the most part\r\nit is shaded, as here, with four lines of trees; the middle road\xe2\x80\x94all\r\nhard\xe2\x80\x94takes the quick traffic. In the days before rail-carriages the\r\nSahibs travelled up and down here in hundreds. Now there are only\r\ncountry-carts and such like. Left and right is the rougher road for the\r\nheavy carts\xe2\x80\x94grain and cotton and timber, fodder, lime and hides. A man\r\ngoes in safety here for at every few _kos_ is a police-station. The\r\npolice are thieves and extortioners (I myself would patrol it with\r\ncavalry\xe2\x80\x94young recruits under a strong captain), but at least they do\r\nnot suffer any rivals. All castes and kinds of men move here.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook! Brahmins and chumars, bankers and tinkers, barbers and bunnias,\r\npilgrims and potters\xe2\x80\x94all the world going and coming. It is to me as a\r\nriver from which I am withdrawn like a log after a flood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd truly the Grand Trunk Road is a wonderful spectacle. It runs\r\nstraight, bearing without crowding India\xe2\x80\x99s traffic for fifteen hundred\r\nmiles\xe2\x80\x94such a river of life as nowhere else exists in the world. They\r\nlooked at the green-arched, shade-flecked length of it, the white\r\nbreadth speckled with slow-pacing folk; and the two-roomed\r\npolice-station opposite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho bears arms against the law?\xe2\x80\x9d a constable called out laughingly, as\r\nhe caught sight of the soldier\xe2\x80\x99s sword. \xe2\x80\x9cAre not the police enough to\r\ndestroy evil-doers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was because of the police I bought it,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes all\r\ngo well in Hind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRissaldar Sahib, all goes well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am like an old tortoise, look you, who puts his head out from the\r\nbank and draws it in again. Ay, this is the Road of Hindustan. All men\r\ncome by this way...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSon of a swine, is the soft part of the road meant for thee to scratch\r\nthy back upon? Father of all the daughters of shame and husband of ten\r\nthousand virtueless ones, thy mother was devoted to a devil, being led\r\nthereto by her mother. Thy aunts have never had a nose for seven\r\ngenerations! Thy sister\xe2\x80\x94What Owl\xe2\x80\x99s folly told thee to draw thy carts\r\nacross the road? A broken wheel? Then take a broken head and put the\r\ntwo together at leisure!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe voice and a venomous whip-cracking came out of a pillar of dust\r\nfifty yards away, where a cart had broken down. A thin, high Kathiawar\r\nmare, with eyes and nostrils aflame, rocketed out of the jam, snorting\r\nand wincing as her rider bent her across the road in chase of a\r\nshouting man. He was tall and grey-bearded, sitting the almost mad\r\nbeast as a piece of her, and scientifically lashing his victim between\r\nplunges.\r\n\r\nThe old man\xe2\x80\x99s face lit with pride. \xe2\x80\x9cMy child!\xe2\x80\x9d said he briefly, and\r\nstrove to rein the pony\xe2\x80\x99s neck to a fitting arch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I to be beaten before the police?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the carter. \xe2\x80\x9cJustice! I\r\nwill have Justice\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I to be blocked by a shouting ape who upsets ten thousand sacks\r\nunder a young horse\xe2\x80\x99s nose? That is the way to ruin a mare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe speaks truth. He speaks truth. But she follows her man close,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe old man. The carter ran under the wheels of his cart and thence\r\nthreatened all sorts of vengeance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are strong men, thy sons,\xe2\x80\x9d said the policeman serenely, picking\r\nhis teeth.\r\n\r\nThe horseman delivered one last vicious cut with his whip and came on\r\nat a canter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father!\xe2\x80\x9d He reigned back ten yards and dismounted.\r\n\r\nThe old man was off his pony in an instant, and they embraced as do\r\nfather and son in the East.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\n\r\n\r\nGood Luck, she is never a lady,\r\nBut the cursedest quean alive,\r\nTricksy, wincing, and jady\xe2\x80\x94\r\nKittle to lead or drive.\r\nGreet her\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s hailing a stranger!\r\nMeet her\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s busking to leave!\r\nLet her alone for a shrew to the bone\r\nAnd the hussy comes plucking your sleeve!\r\n    Largesse! Largesse, O Fortune!\r\n    Give or hold at your will.\r\n    If I\xe2\x80\x99ve no care for Fortune,\r\n    Fortune must follow me still!\r\n\r\n_The Wishing-Caps_.\r\n\r\nThen, lowering their voices, they spoke together. Kim came to rest\r\nunder a tree, but the lama tugged impatiently at his elbow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go on. The River is not here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHai mai! Have we not walked enough for a little? Our River will not\r\nrun away. Patience, and he will give us a dole.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis.\xe2\x80\x9d said the old soldier suddenly, \xe2\x80\x9cis the Friend of the Stars. He\r\nbrought me the news yesterday. Having seen the very man Himself, in a\r\nvision, giving orders for the war.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHm!\xe2\x80\x9d said his son, all deep in his broad chest. \xe2\x80\x9cHe came by a\r\nbazar-rumour and made profit of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis father laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least he did not ride to me begging for a new\r\ncharger, and the Gods know how many rupees. Are thy brothers\xe2\x80\x99 regiments\r\nalso under orders?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know. I took leave and came swiftly to thee in case\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn case they ran before thee to beg. O gamblers and spendthrifts all!\r\nBut thou hast never yet ridden in a charge. A good horse is needed\r\nthere, truly. A good follower and a good pony also for the marching.\r\nLet us see\xe2\x80\x94let us see.\xe2\x80\x9d He thrummed on the pommel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is no place to cast accounts in, my father. Let us go to thy\r\nhouse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least pay the boy, then: I have no pice with me, and he brought\r\nauspicious news. Ho! Friend of all the World, a war is toward as thou\r\nhast said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, as I know, _the_ war,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Kim composedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, fingering his beads, all eager for the road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy master does not trouble the Stars for hire. We brought the news\r\nbear witness, we brought the news, and now we go.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim half-crooked his\r\nhand at his side.\r\n\r\nThe son tossed a silver coin through the sunlight, grumbling something\r\nabout beggars and jugglers. It was a four-anna piece, and would feed\r\nthem well for days. The lama, seeing the flash of the metal, droned a\r\nblessing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo thy way, Friend of all the World,\xe2\x80\x9d piped the old soldier, wheeling\r\nhis scrawny mount. \xe2\x80\x9cFor once in all my days I have met a true\r\nprophet\xe2\x80\x94who was not in the Army.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFather and son swung round together: the old man sitting as erect as\r\nthe younger.\r\n\r\nA Punjabi constable in yellow linen trousers slouched across the road.\r\nHe had seen the money pass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalt!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried in impressive English. \xe2\x80\x9cKnow ye not that there is a\r\n_takkus_ of two annas a head, which is four annas, on those who enter\r\nthe Road from this side-road? It is the order of the Sirkar, and the\r\nmoney is spent for the planting of trees and the beautification of the\r\nways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the bellies of the police,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, slipping out of arm\xe2\x80\x99s reach.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConsider for a while, man with a mud head. Think you we came from the\r\nnearest pond like the frog, thy father-in-law? Hast thou ever heard the\r\nname of thy brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who was he? Leave the boy alone,\xe2\x80\x9d cried a senior constable,\r\nimmensely delighted, as he squatted down to smoke his pipe in the\r\nveranda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe took a label from a bottle of _belaitee-pani_ (soda-water), and,\r\naffixing it to a bridge, collected taxes for a month from those who\r\npassed, saying that it was the Sirkar\xe2\x80\x99s order. Then came an Englishman\r\nand broke his head. Ah, brother, I am a town-crow, not a village-crow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe policeman drew back abashed, and Kim hooted at him all down the\r\nroad.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas there ever such a disciple as I?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried merrily to the lama.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll earth would have picked thy bones within ten mile of Lahore city\r\nif I had not guarded thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI consider in my own mind whether thou art a spirit, sometimes, or\r\nsometimes an evil imp,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, smiling slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am thy _chela_.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim dropped into step at his side\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nindescribable gait of the long-distance tramp all the world over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow let us walk,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the lama, and to the click of his rosary\r\nthey walked in silence mile upon mile. The lama as usual, was deep in\r\nmeditation, but Kim\xe2\x80\x99s bright eyes were open wide. This broad, smiling\r\nriver of life, he considered, was a vast improvement on the cramped and\r\ncrowded Lahore streets. There were new people and new sights at every\r\nstride\xe2\x80\x94castes he knew and castes that were altogether out of his\r\nexperience.\r\n\r\nThey met a troop of long-haired, strong-scented Sansis with baskets of\r\nlizards and other unclean food on their backs, their lean dogs sniffing\r\nat their heels. These people kept their own side of the road, moving at\r\na quick, furtive jog-trot, and all other castes gave them ample room;\r\nfor the Sansi is deep pollution. Behind them, walking wide and stiffly\r\nacross the strong shadows, the memory of his leg-irons still on him,\r\nstrode one newly released from the jail; his full stomach and shiny\r\nskin to prove that the Government fed its prisoners better than most\r\nhonest men could feed themselves. Kim knew that walk well, and made\r\nbroad jest of it as they passed. Then an Akali, a wild-eyed,\r\nwild-haired Sikh devotee in the blue-checked clothes of his faith, with\r\npolished-steel quoits glistening on the cone of his tall blue turban,\r\nstalked past, returning from a visit to one of the independent Sikh\r\nStates, where he had been singing the ancient glories of the Khalsa to\r\nCollege-trained princelings in top-boots and white-cord breeches. Kim\r\nwas careful not to irritate that man; for the Akali\xe2\x80\x99s temper is short\r\nand his arm quick. Here and there they met or were overtaken by the\r\ngaily dressed crowds of whole villages turning out to some local fair;\r\nthe women, with their babes on their hips, walking behind the men, the\r\nolder boys prancing on sticks of sugar-cane, dragging rude brass models\r\nof locomotives such as they sell for a halfpenny, or flashing the sun\r\ninto the eyes of their betters from cheap toy mirrors. One could see at\r\na glance what each had bought; and if there were any doubt it needed\r\nonly to watch the wives comparing, brown arm against brown arm, the\r\nnewly purchased dull glass bracelets that come from the North-West.\r\nThese merry-makers stepped slowly, calling one to the other and\r\nstopping to haggle with sweetmeat-sellers, or to make a prayer before\r\none of the wayside shrines\xe2\x80\x94sometimes Hindu, sometimes Mussalman\xe2\x80\x94which\r\nthe low-caste of both creeds share with beautiful impartiality. A solid\r\nline of blue, rising and falling like the back of a caterpillar in\r\nhaste, would swing up through the quivering dust and trot past to a\r\nchorus of quick cackling. That was a gang of _changars_\xe2\x80\x94the women who\r\nhave taken all the embankments of all the Northern railways under their\r\ncharge\xe2\x80\x94a flat-footed, big-bosomed, strong-limbed, blue-petticoated clan\r\nof earth-carriers, hurrying north on news of a job, and wasting no time\r\nby the road. They belong to the caste whose men do not count, and they\r\nwalked with squared elbows, swinging hips, and heads on high, as suits\r\nwomen who carry heavy weights. A little later a marriage procession\r\nwould strike into the Grand Trunk with music and shoutings, and a smell\r\nof marigold and jasmine stronger even than the reek of the dust. One\r\ncould see the bride\xe2\x80\x99s litter, a blur of red and tinsel, staggering\r\nthrough the haze, while the bridegroom\xe2\x80\x99s bewreathed pony turned aside\r\nto snatch a mouthful from a passing fodder-cart. Then Kim would join\r\nthe Kentish-fire of good wishes and bad jokes, wishing the couple a\r\nhundred sons and no daughters, as the saying is. Still more interesting\r\nand more to be shouted over it was when a strolling juggler with some\r\nhalf-trained monkeys, or a panting, feeble bear, or a woman who tied\r\ngoats\xe2\x80\x99 horns to her feet, and with these danced on a slack-rope, set\r\nthe horses to shying and the women to shrill, long-drawn quavers of\r\namazement.\r\n\r\nThe lama never raised his eyes. He did not note the money-lender on his\r\ngoose-rumped pony, hastening along to collect the cruel interest; or\r\nthe long-shouting, deep-voiced little mob\xe2\x80\x94still in military\r\nformation\xe2\x80\x94of native soldiers on leave, rejoicing to be rid of their\r\nbreeches and puttees, and saying the most outrageous things to the most\r\nrespectable women in sight. Even the seller of Ganges-water he did not\r\nsee, and Kim expected that he would at least buy a bottle of that\r\nprecious stuff. He looked steadily at the ground, and strode as\r\nsteadily hour after hour, his soul busied elsewhere. But Kim was in the\r\nseventh heaven of joy. The Grand Trunk at this point was built on an\r\nembankment to guard against winter floods from the foothills, so that\r\none walked, as it were, a little above the country, along a stately\r\ncorridor, seeing all India spread out to left and right. It was\r\nbeautiful to behold the many-yoked grain and cotton wagons crawling\r\nover the country roads: one could hear their axles, complaining a mile\r\naway, coming nearer, till with shouts and yells and bad words they\r\nclimbed up the steep incline and plunged on to the hard main road,\r\ncarter reviling carter. It was equally beautiful to watch the people,\r\nlittle clumps of red and blue and pink and white and saffron, turning\r\naside to go to their own villages, dispersing and growing small by twos\r\nand threes across the level plain. Kim felt these things, though he\r\ncould not give tongue to his feelings, and so contented himself with\r\nbuying peeled sugar-cane and spitting the pith generously about his\r\npath. From time to time the lama took snuff, and at last Kim could\r\nendure the silence no longer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a good land\xe2\x80\x94the land of the South!\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cThe air is good;\r\nthe water is good. Eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they are all bound upon the Wheel,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cBound from\r\nlife after life. To none of these has the Way been shown.\xe2\x80\x9d He shook\r\nhimself back to this world.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now we have walked a weary way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cSurely we shall soon\r\ncome to a _parao_ (a resting-place). Shall we stay there? Look, the sun\r\nis sloping.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho will receive us this evening?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all one. This country is full of good folk. Besides\xe2\x80\x9d he sunk\r\nhis voice beneath a whisper\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwe have money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe crowd thickened as they neared the resting-place which marked the\r\nend of their day\xe2\x80\x99s journey. A line of stalls selling very simple food\r\nand tobacco, a stack of firewood, a police-station, a well, a\r\nhorse-trough, a few trees, and, under them, some trampled ground dotted\r\nwith the black ashes of old fires, are all that mark a _parao_ on the\r\nGrand Trunk; if you except the beggars and the crows\xe2\x80\x94both hungry.\r\n\r\nBy this time the sun was driving broad golden spokes through the lower\r\nbranches of the mango-trees; the parakeets and doves were coming home\r\nin their hundreds; the chattering, grey-backed Seven Sisters, talking\r\nover the day\xe2\x80\x99s adventures, walked back and forth in twos and threes\r\nalmost under the feet of the travellers; and shufflings and scufflings\r\nin the branches showed that the bats were ready to go out on the\r\nnight-picket. Swiftly the light gathered itself together, painted for\r\nan instant the faces and the cartwheels and the bullocks\xe2\x80\x99 horns as red\r\nas blood. Then the night fell, changing the touch of the air, drawing a\r\nlow, even haze, like a gossamer veil of blue, across the face of the\r\ncountry, and bringing out, keen and distinct, the smell of wood-smoke\r\nand cattle and the good scent of wheaten cakes cooked on ashes. The\r\nevening patrol hurried out of the police-station with important\r\ncoughings and reiterated orders; and a live charcoal ball in the cup of\r\na wayside carter\xe2\x80\x99s hookah glowed red while Kim\xe2\x80\x99s eye mechanically\r\nwatched the last flicker of the sun on the brass tweezers.\r\n\r\nThe life of the _parao_ was very like that of the Kashmir Serai on a\r\nsmall scale. Kim dived into the happy Asiatic disorder which, if you\r\nonly allow time, will bring you everything that a simple man needs.\r\n\r\nHis wants were few, because, since the lama had no caste scruples,\r\ncooked food from the nearest stall would serve; but, for luxury\xe2\x80\x99s sake,\r\nKim bought a handful of dung-cakes to build a fire. All about, coming\r\nand going round the little flames, men cried for oil, or grain, or\r\nsweetmeats, or tobacco, jostling one another while they waited their\r\nturn at the well; and under the men\xe2\x80\x99s voices you heard from halted,\r\nshuttered carts the high squeals and giggles of women whose faces\r\nshould not be seen in public.\r\n\r\nNowadays, well-educated natives are of opinion that when their\r\nwomenfolk travel\xe2\x80\x94and they visit a good deal\xe2\x80\x94it is better to take them\r\nquickly by rail in a properly screened compartment; and that custom is\r\nspreading. But there are always those of the old rock who hold by the\r\nuse of their forefathers; and, above all, there are always the old\r\nwomen\xe2\x80\x94more conservative than the men\xe2\x80\x94who toward the end of their days\r\ngo on a pilgrimage. They, being withered and undesirable, do not, under\r\ncertain circumstances, object to unveiling. After their long seclusion,\r\nduring which they have always been in business touch with a thousand\r\noutside interests, they love the bustle and stir of the open road, the\r\ngatherings at the shrines, and the infinite possibilities of gossip\r\nwith like-minded dowagers. Very often it suits a longsuffering family\r\nthat a strong-tongued, iron-willed old lady should disport herself\r\nabout India in this fashion; for certainly pilgrimage is grateful to\r\nthe Gods. So all about India, in the most remote places, as in the most\r\npublic, you find some knot of grizzled servitors in nominal charge of\r\nan old lady who is more or less curtained and hid away in a\r\nbullock-cart. Such men are staid and discreet, and when a European or a\r\nhigh-caste native is near will net their charge with most elaborate\r\nprecautions; but in the ordinary haphazard chances of pilgrimage the\r\nprecautions are not taken. The old lady is, after all, intensely human,\r\nand lives to look upon life.\r\n\r\nKim marked down a gaily ornamented _ruth_ or family bullock-cart, with\r\na broidered canopy of two domes, like a double-humped camel, which had\r\njust been drawn into the _parao_. Eight men made its retinue, and two\r\nof the eight were armed with rusty sabres\xe2\x80\x94sure signs that they followed\r\na person of distinction, for the common folk do not bear arms. An\r\nincreasing cackle of complaints, orders, and jests, and what to a\r\nEuropean would have been bad language, came from behind the curtains.\r\nHere was evidently a woman used to command.\r\n\r\nKim looked over the retinue critically. Half of them were thin-legged,\r\ngrey-bearded Ooryas from down country. The other half were duffle-clad,\r\nfelt-hatted hillmen of the North; and that mixture told its own tale,\r\neven if he had not overheard the incessant sparring between the two\r\ndivisions. The old lady was going south on a visit\xe2\x80\x94probably to a rich\r\nrelative, most probably to a son-in-law, who had sent up an escort as a\r\nmark of respect. The hillmen would be of her own people\xe2\x80\x94Kulu or Kangra\r\nfolk. It was quite clear that she was not taking her daughter down to\r\nbe wedded, or the curtains would have been laced home and the guard\r\nwould have allowed no one near the car. A merry and a high-spirited\r\ndame, thought Kim, balancing the dung-cake in one hand, the cooked food\r\nin the other, and piloting the lama with a nudging shoulder. Something\r\nmight be made out of the meeting. The lama would give him no help, but,\r\nas a conscientious _chela_, Kim was delighted to beg for two.\r\n\r\nHe built his fire as close to the cart as he dared, waiting for one of\r\nthe escort to order him away. The lama dropped wearily to the ground,\r\nmuch as a heavy fruit-eating bat cowers, and returned to his rosary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStand farther off, beggar!\xe2\x80\x9d The order was shouted in broken Hindustani\r\nby one of the hillmen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh! It is only a _pahari_ (a hillman)\xe2\x80\x9d, said Kim over his shoulder.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince when have the hill-asses owned all Hindustan?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe retort was a swift and brilliant sketch of Kim\xe2\x80\x99s pedigree for three\r\ngenerations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\xe2\x80\x99s voice was sweeter than ever, as he broke the dung-cake into\r\nfit pieces. \xe2\x80\x9cIn_ my_ country we call that the beginning of love-talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA harsh, thin cackle behind the curtains put the hillman on his mettle\r\nfor a second shot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so bad\xe2\x80\x94not so bad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim with calm. \xe2\x80\x9cBut have a care, my\r\nbrother, lest we\xe2\x80\x94_we_, I say\xe2\x80\x94be minded to give a curse or so in return.\r\nAnd our curses have the knack of biting home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Ooryas laughed; the hillman sprang forward threateningly. The lama\r\nsuddenly raised his head, bringing his huge tam-o\xe2\x80\x99-shanter hat into the\r\nfull light of Kim\xe2\x80\x99s new-started fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nThe man halted as though struck to stone. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94am saved from a great\r\nsin,\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe foreigner has found him a priest at last,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered one of the\r\nOoryas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHai! Why is that beggar-brat not well beaten?\xe2\x80\x9d the old woman cried.\r\n\r\nThe hillman drew back to the cart and whispered something to the\r\ncurtain. There was dead silence, then a muttering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis goes well,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Kim, pretending neither to see nor hear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen\xe2\x80\x94when\xe2\x80\x94he has eaten\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the hillman fawned on Kim\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x94it is requested\r\nthat the Holy One will do the honour to talk to one who would speak to\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter he has eaten he will sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim returned loftily. He could not\r\nquite see what new turn the game had taken, but stood resolute to\r\nprofit by it. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I will get him his food.\xe2\x80\x9d The last sentence, spoken\r\nloudly, ended with a sigh as of faintness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I myself and the others of my people will look to that\xe2\x80\x94if it is\r\npermitted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is permitted,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, more loftily than ever. \xe2\x80\x9cHoly One, these\r\npeople will bring us food.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe land is good. All the country of the South is good\xe2\x80\x94a great and a\r\nterrible world,\xe2\x80\x9d mumbled the lama drowsily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, \xe2\x80\x9cbut look to it that we are well fed when he\r\nwakes. He is a very holy man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain one of the Ooryas said something contemptuously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is not a _faquir_. He is not a down-country beggar,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim went on\r\nseverely, addressing the stars. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is the most holy of holy men. He is\r\nabove all castes. I am his _chela_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here!\xe2\x80\x9d said the flat thin voice behind the curtain; and Kim came,\r\nconscious that eyes he could not see were staring at him. One skinny\r\nbrown finger heavy with rings lay on the edge of the cart, and the talk\r\nwent this way:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is that one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn exceedingly holy one. He comes from far off. He comes from Tibet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere in Tibet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom behind the snows\xe2\x80\x94from a very far place. He knows the stars; he\r\nmakes horoscopes; he reads nativities. But he does not do this for\r\nmoney. He does it for kindness and great charity. I am his disciple. I\r\nam called also the Friend of the Stars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art no hillman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk him. He will tell thee I was sent to him from the Stars to show\r\nhim an end to his pilgrimage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHumph! Consider, brat, that I am an old woman and not altogether a\r\nfool. Lamas I know, and to these I give reverence, but thou art no more\r\na lawful _chela_ than this my finger is the pole of this wagon. Thou\r\nart a casteless Hindu\xe2\x80\x94a bold and unblushing beggar, attached, belike,\r\nto the Holy One for the sake of gain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo we not all work for gain?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim changed his tone promptly to match\r\nthat altered voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI have heard\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94this was a bow drawn at a venture\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave heard\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat hast thou heard?\xe2\x80\x9d she snapped, rapping with the finger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing that I well remember, but some talk in the bazars, which is\r\ndoubtless a lie, that even Rajahs\xe2\x80\x94small Hill Rajahs\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut none the less of good Rajput blood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly of good blood. That these even sell the more comely of their\r\nwomenfolk for gain. Down south they sell them\xe2\x80\x94to zemindars and such\xe2\x80\x94all\r\nof Oudh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf there be one thing in the world that the small Hill Rajahs deny it\r\nis just this charge; but it happens to be one thing that the bazars\r\nbelieve, when they discuss the mysterious slave-traffics of India. The\r\nold lady explained to Kim, in a tense, indignant whisper, precisely\r\nwhat manner and fashion of malignant liar he was. Had Kim hinted this\r\nwhen she was a girl, he would have been pommelled to death that same\r\nevening by an elephant. This was perfectly true.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAhai! I am only a beggar\xe2\x80\x99s brat, as the Eye of Beauty has said,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nwailed in extravagant terror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEye of Beauty, forsooth! Who am I that thou shouldst fling\r\nbeggar-endearments at me?\xe2\x80\x9d And yet she laughed at the long-forgotten\r\nword. \xe2\x80\x9cForty years ago that might have been said, and not without\r\ntruth. Ay. thirty years ago. But it is the fault of this gadding up and\r\ndown Hind that a king\xe2\x80\x99s widow must jostle all the scum of the land, and\r\nbe made a mock by beggars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGreat Queen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim promptly, for he heard her shaking with\r\nindignation, \xe2\x80\x9cI am even what the Great Queen says I am; but none the\r\nless is my master holy. He has not yet heard the Great Queen\xe2\x80\x99s order\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOrder? I order a Holy One\xe2\x80\x94a Teacher of the Law\xe2\x80\x94to come and speak to a\r\nwoman? Never!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPity my stupidity. I thought it was given as an order\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not. It was a petition. Does this make all clear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA silver coin clicked on the edge of the cart. Kim took it and salaamed\r\nprofoundly. The old lady recognized that, as the eyes and the ears of\r\nthe lama, he was to be propitiated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am but the Holy One\xe2\x80\x99s disciple. When he has eaten perhaps he will\r\ncome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, villain and shameless rogue!\xe2\x80\x9d The jewelled forefinger shook itself\r\nat him reprovingly; but he could hear the old lady\xe2\x80\x99s chuckle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, dropping into his most caressing and\r\nconfidential tone\xe2\x80\x94the one, he well knew, that few could resist. \xe2\x80\x9cIs\xe2\x80\x94is\r\nthere any need of a son in thy family? Speak freely, for we priests\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThat last was a direct plagiarism from a _faquir_ by the Taksali Gate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe priests! Thou art not yet old enough to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She checked the joke with\r\nanother laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cBelieve me, now and again, we women, O priest, think of\r\nother matters than sons. Moreover, my daughter has borne her\r\nman-child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo arrows in the quiver are better than one; and three are better\r\nstill.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim quoted the proverb with a meditative cough, looking\r\ndiscreetly earthward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue\xe2\x80\x94oh, true. But perhaps that will come. Certainly those\r\ndown-country Brahmins are utterly useless. I sent gifts and monies and\r\ngifts again to them, and they prophesied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d drawled Kim, with infinite contempt, \xe2\x80\x9cthey prophesied!\xe2\x80\x9d A\r\nprofessional could have done no better.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it was not till I remembered my own Gods that my prayers were\r\nheard. I chose an auspicious hour, and\xe2\x80\x94perhaps thy Holy One has heard\r\nof the Abbot of the Lung-Cho lamassery. It was to him I put the matter,\r\nand behold in the due time all came about as I desired. The Brahmin in\r\nthe house of the father of my daughter\xe2\x80\x99s son has since said that it was\r\nthrough _his_ prayers\xe2\x80\x94which is a little error that I will explain to\r\nhim when we reach our journey\xe2\x80\x99s end. And so afterwards I go to Buddh\r\nGaya, to make _shraddha_ for the father of my children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThither go we.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoubly auspicious,\xe2\x80\x9d chirruped the old lady. \xe2\x80\x9cA second son at least!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Friend of all the World!\xe2\x80\x9d The lama had waked, and, simply as a child\r\nbewildered in a strange bed, called for Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI come! I come, Holy One!\xe2\x80\x9d He dashed to the fire, where he found the\r\nlama already surrounded by dishes of food, the hillmen visibly adoring\r\nhim and the Southerners looking sourly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo back! Withdraw!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim cried. \xe2\x80\x9cDo we eat publicly like dogs?\xe2\x80\x9d They\r\nfinished the meal in silence, each turned a little from the other, and\r\nKim topped it with a native-made cigarette.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave I not said an hundred times that the South is a good land? Here\r\nis a virtuous and high-born widow of a Hill Rajah on pilgrimage, she\r\nsays, to Buddha Gay.
