She it is sends us those dishes; and when thou art\r\nwell rested she would speak to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this also thy work?\xe2\x80\x9d The lama dipped deep into his snuff-gourd.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho else watched over thee since our wonderful journey began?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes danced in his head as he blew the rank smoke through his nostrils\r\nand stretched him on the dusty ground. \xe2\x80\x9cHave I failed to oversee thy\r\ncomforts, Holy One?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA blessing on thee.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama inclined his solemn head. \xe2\x80\x9cI have known\r\nmany men in my so long life, and disciples not a few. But to none among\r\nmen, if so be thou art woman-born, has my heart gone out as it has to\r\nthee\xe2\x80\x94thoughtful, wise, and courteous; but something of a small imp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I have never seen such a priest as thou.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim considered the\r\nbenevolent yellow face wrinkle by wrinkle. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is less than three days\r\nsince we took the road together, and it is as though it were a hundred\r\nyears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps in a former life it was permitted that I should have rendered\r\nthee some service. Maybe\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he smiled\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI freed thee from a trap; or,\r\nhaving caught thee on a hook in the days when I was not enlightened,\r\ncast thee back into the river.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim quietly. He had heard this sort of speculation again\r\nand again, from the mouths of many whom the English would not consider\r\nimaginative. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, as regards that woman in the bullock-cart. _I_ think\r\nshe needs a second son for her daughter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is no part of the Way,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cBut at least she is\r\nfrom the Hills. Ah, the Hills, and the snow of the Hills!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rose and stalked to the cart. Kim would have given his ears to come\r\ntoo, but the lama did not invite him; and the few words he caught were\r\nin an unknown tongue, for they spoke some common speech of the\r\nmountains. The woman seemed to ask questions which the lama turned over\r\nin his mind before answering. Now and again he heard the singsong\r\ncadence of a Chinese quotation. It was a strange picture that Kim\r\nwatched between drooped eyelids. The lama, very straight and erect, the\r\ndeep folds of his yellow clothing slashed with black in the light of\r\nthe _parao_ fires precisely as a knotted tree-trunk is slashed with the\r\nshadows of the low sun, addressed a tinsel and lacquered _ruth_ which\r\nburned like a many-coloured jewel in the same uncertain light. The\r\npatterns on the gold-worked curtains ran up and down, melting and\r\nreforming as the folds shook and quivered to the night wind; and when\r\nthe talk grew more earnest the jewelled forefinger snapped out little\r\nsparks of light between the embroideries. Behind the cart was a wall of\r\nuncertain darkness speckled with little flames and alive with\r\nhalf-caught forms and faces and shadows. The voices of early evening\r\nhad settled down to one soothing hum whose deepest note was the steady\r\nchumping of the bullocks above their chopped straw, and whose highest\r\nwas the tinkle of a Bengali dancing-girl\xe2\x80\x99s _sitar_. Most men had eaten\r\nand pulled deep at their gurgling, grunting hookahs, which in full\r\nblast sound like bull-frogs.\r\n\r\nAt last the lama returned. A hillman walked behind him with a wadded\r\ncotton-quilt and spread it carefully by the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe deserves ten thousand grandchildren,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cNone the less,\r\nbut for me, those gifts would not have come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA virtuous woman\xe2\x80\x94and a wise one.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama slackened off, joint by\r\njoint, like a slow camel. \xe2\x80\x9cThe world is full of charity to those who\r\nfollow the Way.\xe2\x80\x9d He flung a fair half of the quilt over Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what said she?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim rolled up in his share of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe asked me many questions and propounded many problems\xe2\x80\x94the most of\r\nwhich were idle tales which she had heard from devil-serving priests\r\nwho pretend to follow the Way. Some I answered, and some I said were\r\nfoolish. Many wear the Robe, but few keep the Way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. That is true.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim used the thoughtful, conciliatory tone of\r\nthose who wish to draw confidences.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut by her lights she is most right-minded. She desires greatly that\r\nwe should go with her to Buddh Gaya; her road being ours, as I\r\nunderstand, for many days\xe2\x80\x99 journey to the southward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPatience a little. To this I said that my Search came before all\r\nthings. She had heard many foolish legends, but this great truth of my\r\nRiver she had never heard. Such are the priests of the lower hills! She\r\nknew the Abbot of Lung-Cho, but she did not know of my River\xe2\x80\x94nor the\r\ntale of the Arrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI spoke therefore of the Search, and of the Way, and of matters that\r\nwere profitable; she desiring only that I should accompany her and make\r\nprayer for a second son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha! \xe2\x80\x98We women\xe2\x80\x99 do _not_ think of anything save children,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim\r\nsleepily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, since our roads run together for a while, I do not see that we in\r\nany way depart from our Search if so be we accompany her\xe2\x80\x94at least as\r\nfar as\xe2\x80\x94I have forgotten the name of the city.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xc3\xa9!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, turning and speaking in a sharp whisper to one of the\r\nOoryas a few yards away. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is your master\xe2\x80\x99s house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little behind Saharunpore, among the fruit gardens.\xe2\x80\x9d He named the\r\nvillage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was the place,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cSo far, at least, we can go with\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFlies go to carrion,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Oorya, in an abstracted voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the sick cow a crow; for the sick man a Brahmin.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim breathed the\r\nproverb impersonally to the shadow-tops of the trees overhead.\r\n\r\nThe Oorya grunted and held his peace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo then we go with her, Holy One?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there any reason against? I can still step aside and try all the\r\nrivers that the road overpasses. She desires that I should come. She\r\nvery greatly desires it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim stifled a laugh in the quilt. When once that imperious old lady had\r\nrecovered from her natural awe of a lama he thought it probable that\r\nshe would be worth listening to.\r\n\r\nHe was nearly asleep when the lama suddenly quoted a proverb: \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nhusbands of the talkative have a great reward hereafter.\xe2\x80\x9d Then Kim\r\nheard him snuff thrice, and dozed off, still laughing.\r\n\r\nThe diamond-bright dawn woke men and crows and bullocks together. Kim\r\nsat up and yawned, shook himself, and thrilled with delight. This was\r\nseeing the world in real truth; this was life as he would have\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94bustling and shouting, the buckling of belts, and beating of\r\nbullocks and creaking of wheels, lighting of fires and cooking of food,\r\nand new sights at every turn of the approving eye. The morning mist\r\nswept off in a whorl of silver, the parrots shot away to some distant\r\nriver in shrieking green hosts: all the well-wheels within ear-shot\r\nwent to work. India was awake, and Kim was in the middle of it, more\r\nawake and more excited than anyone, chewing on a twig that he would\r\npresently use as a toothbrush; for he borrowed right- and left-handedly\r\nfrom all the customs of the country he knew and loved. There was no\r\nneed to worry about food\xe2\x80\x94no need to spend a cowrie at the crowded\r\nstalls. He was the disciple of a holy man annexed by a strong-willed\r\nold lady. All things would be prepared for them, and when they were\r\nrespectfully invited so to do they would sit and eat. For the rest\xe2\x80\x94Kim\r\ngiggled here as he cleaned his teeth\xe2\x80\x94his hostess would rather heighten\r\nthe enjoyment of the road. He inspected her bullocks critically, as\r\nthey came up grunting and blowing under the yokes. If they went too\r\nfast\xe2\x80\x94it was not likely\xe2\x80\x94there would be a pleasant seat for himself along\r\nthe pole; the lama would sit beside the driver. The escort, of course,\r\nwould walk. The old lady, equally of course, would talk a great deal,\r\nand by what he had heard that conversation would not lack salt. She was\r\nalready ordering, haranguing, rebuking, and, it must be said, cursing\r\nher servants for delays.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet her her pipe. In the name of the Gods, get her her pipe and stop\r\nher ill-omened mouth,\xe2\x80\x9d cried an Oorya, tying up his shapeless bundles\r\nof bedding. \xe2\x80\x9cShe and the parrots are alike. They screech in the dawn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe lead-bullocks! Hai! Look to the lead-bullocks!\xe2\x80\x9d They were backing\r\nand wheeling as a grain-cart\xe2\x80\x99s axle caught them by the horns. \xe2\x80\x9cSon of\r\nan owl, where dost thou go?\xe2\x80\x9d This to the grinning carter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAi! Yai! Yai! That within there is the Queen of Delhi going to pray\r\nfor a son,\xe2\x80\x9d the man called back over his high load. \xe2\x80\x9cRoom for the Queen\r\nof Delhi and her Prime Minister the grey monkey climbing up his own\r\nsword!\xe2\x80\x9d Another cart loaded with bark for a down-country tannery\r\nfollowed close behind, and its driver added a few compliments as the\r\n_ruth_-bullocks backed and backed again.\r\n\r\nFrom behind the shaking curtains came one volley of invective. It did\r\nnot last long, but in kind and quality, in blistering, biting\r\nappropriateness, it was beyond anything that even Kim had heard. He\r\ncould see the carter\xe2\x80\x99s bare chest collapse with amazement, as the man\r\nsalaamed reverently to the voice, leaped from the pole, and helped the\r\nescort haul their volcano on to the main road. Here the voice told him\r\ntruthfully what sort of wife he had wedded, and what she was doing in\r\nhis absence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _shabash!_\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Kim, unable to contain himself, as the man\r\nslunk away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell done, indeed? It is a shame and a scandal that a poor woman may\r\nnot go to make prayer to her Gods except she be jostled and insulted by\r\nall the refuse of Hindustan\xe2\x80\x94that she must eat _g\xc3\xa2li_ (abuse) as men eat\r\n_ghi_. But I have yet a wag left to my tongue\xe2\x80\x94a word or two well spoken\r\nthat serves the occasion. And _still_ am I without my tobacco! Who is\r\nthe one-eyed and luckless son of shame that has not yet prepared my\r\npipe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was hastily thrust in by a hillman, and a trickle of thick smoke\r\nfrom each corner of the curtains showed that peace was restored.\r\n\r\nIf Kim had walked proudly the day before, disciple of a holy man, today\r\nhe paced with tenfold pride in the train of a semi-royal procession,\r\nwith a recognized place under the patronage of an old lady of charming\r\nmanners and infinite resource. The escort, their heads tied up\r\nnative-fashion, fell in on either side the cart, shuffling enormous\r\nclouds of dust.\r\n\r\nThe lama and Kim walked a little to one side; Kim chewing his stick of\r\nsugarcane, and making way for no one under the status of a priest. They\r\ncould hear the old lady\xe2\x80\x99s tongue clack as steadily as a rice-husker.\r\nShe bade the escort tell her what was going on on the road; and so soon\r\nas they were clear of the _parao_ she flung back the curtains and\r\npeered out, her veil a third across her face. Her men did not eye her\r\ndirectly when she addressed them, and thus the proprieties were more or\r\nless observed.\r\n\r\nA dark, sallowish District Superintendent of Police, faultlessly\r\nuniformed, an Englishman, trotted by on a tired horse, and, seeing from\r\nher retinue what manner of person she was, chaffed her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO mother,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cdo they do this in the _zenanas?_ Suppose an\r\nEnglishman came by and saw that thou hast no nose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d she shrilled back. \xe2\x80\x9cThine own mother has no nose? Why say so,\r\nthen, on the open road?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a fair counter. The Englishman threw up his hand with the\r\ngesture of a man hit at sword-play. She laughed and nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this a face to tempt virtue aside?\xe2\x80\x9d She withdrew all her veil and\r\nstared at him.\r\n\r\nIt was by no means lovely, but as the man gathered up his reins he\r\ncalled it a Moon of Paradise, a Disturber of Integrity, and a few other\r\nfantastic epithets which doubled her up with mirth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a _nut-cut_ (rogue),\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cAll police-constables are\r\n_nut-cuts;_ but the police-wallahs are the worst. Hai, my son, thou\r\nhast never learned all that since thou camest from _Belait_ (Europe).\r\nWho suckled thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA _pahareen_\xe2\x80\x94a hillwoman of Dalhousie, my mother. Keep thy beauty\r\nunder a shade\xe2\x80\x94O Dispenser of Delights,\xe2\x80\x9d and he was gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese be the sort\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she took a fine judicial tone, and stuffed her\r\nmouth with _pan_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cThese be the sort to oversee justice. They know the\r\nland and the customs of the land. The others, all new from Europe,\r\nsuckled by white women and learning our tongues from books, are worse\r\nthan the pestilence. They do harm to Kings.\xe2\x80\x9d Then she told a long, long\r\ntale to the world at large, of an ignorant young policeman who had\r\ndisturbed some small Hill Rajah, a ninth cousin of her own, in the\r\nmatter of a trivial land-case, winding up with a quotation from a work\r\nby no means devotional.\r\n\r\nThen her mood changed, and she bade one of the escort ask whether the\r\nlama would walk alongside and discuss matters of religion. So Kim\r\ndropped back into the dust and returned to his sugar-cane. For an hour\r\nor more the lama\xe2\x80\x99s tam-o\xe2\x80\x99shanter showed like a moon through the haze;\r\nand, from all he heard, Kim gathered that the old woman wept. One of\r\nthe Ooryas half apologized for his rudeness overnight, saying that he\r\nhad never known his mistress of so bland a temper, and he ascribed it\r\nto the presence of the strange priest. Personally, he believed in\r\nBrahmins, though, like all natives, he was acutely aware of their\r\ncunning and their greed. Still, when Brahmins but irritated with\r\nbegging demands the mother of his master\xe2\x80\x99s wife, and when she sent them\r\naway so angry that they cursed the whole retinue (which was the real\r\nreason of the second off-side bullock going lame, and of the pole\r\nbreaking the night before), he was prepared to accept any priest of any\r\nother denomination in or out of India. To this Kim assented with wise\r\nnods, and bade the Oorya observe that the lama took no money, and that\r\nthe cost of his and Kim\xe2\x80\x99s food would be repaid a hundred times in the\r\ngood luck that would attend the caravan henceforward. He also told\r\nstories of Lahore city, and sang a song or two which made the escort\r\nlaugh. As a town-mouse well acquainted with the latest songs by the\r\nmost fashionable composers\xe2\x80\x94they are women for the most part\xe2\x80\x94Kim had a\r\ndistinct advantage over men from a little fruit-village behind\r\nSaharunpore, but he let that advantage be inferred.\r\n\r\nAt noon they turned aside to eat, and the meal was good, plentiful, and\r\nwell-served on plates of clean leaves, in decency, out of drift of the\r\ndust. They gave the scraps to certain beggars, that all requirements\r\nmight be fulfilled, and sat down to a long, luxurious smoke. The old\r\nlady had retreated behind her curtains, but mixed most freely in the\r\ntalk, her servants arguing with and contradicting her as servants do\r\nthroughout the East. She compared the cool and the pines of the Kangra\r\nand Kulu hills with the dust and the mangoes of the South; she told a\r\ntale of some old local Gods at the edge of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s territory; she\r\nroundly abused the tobacco which she was then smoking, reviled all\r\nBrahmins, and speculated without reserve on the coming of many\r\ngrandsons.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V\r\n\r\n\r\nHere come I to my own again\r\nFed, forgiven, and known again\r\nClaimed by bone of my bone again,\r\n    And sib to flesh of my flesh!\r\nThe fatted calf is dressed for me,\r\nBut the husks have greater zest for me ...\r\nI think my pigs will be best for me,\r\n    So I\xe2\x80\x99m off to the styes afresh.\r\n\r\n_The Prodigal Son_.\r\n\r\nOnce more the lazy, string-tied, shuffling procession got under way,\r\nand she slept till they reached the next halting-stage. It was a very\r\nshort march, and time lacked an hour to sundown, so Kim cast about for\r\nmeans of amusement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why not sit and rest?\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the escort. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly the devils\r\nand the English walk to and fro without reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever make friends with the Devil, a Monkey, or a Boy. No man knows\r\nwhat they will do next,\xe2\x80\x9d said his fellow.\r\n\r\nKim turned a scornful back\xe2\x80\x94he did not want to hear the old story how\r\nthe Devil played with the boys and repented of it and walked idly\r\nacross country.\r\n\r\nThe lama strode after him. All that day, whenever they passed a stream,\r\nhe had turned aside to look at it, but in no case had he received any\r\nwarning that he had found his River. Insensibly, too, the comfort of\r\nspeaking to someone in a reasonable tongue, and of being properly\r\nconsidered and respected as her spiritual adviser by a well-born woman,\r\nhad weaned his thoughts a little from the Search. And further, he was\r\nprepared to spend serene years in his quest; having nothing of the\r\nwhite man\xe2\x80\x99s impatience, but a great faith.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere goest thou?\xe2\x80\x9d he called after Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNowhither\xe2\x80\x94it was a small march, and all this\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim waved his hands\r\nabroad\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cis new to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is beyond question a wise and a discerning woman. But it is hard\r\nto meditate when\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll women are thus.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim spoke as might have Solomon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore the lamassery was a broad platform,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama muttered, looping\r\nup the well-worn rosary, \xe2\x80\x9cof stone. On that I have left the marks of my\r\nfeet\xe2\x80\x94pacing to and fro with these.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe clicked the beads, and began the \xe2\x80\x9c_Om mane pudme hum_\xe2\x80\x9d of his\r\ndevotion; grateful for the cool, the quiet, and the absence of dust.\r\n\r\nOne thing after another drew Kim\xe2\x80\x99s idle eye across the plain. There was\r\nno purpose in his wanderings, except that the build of the huts near by\r\nseemed new, and he wished to investigate.\r\n\r\nThey came out on a broad tract of grazing-ground, brown and purple in\r\nthe afternoon light, with a heavy clump of mangoes in the centre. It\r\nstruck Kim as curious that no shrine stood in so eligible a spot: the\r\nboy was observing as any priest for these things. Far across the plain\r\nwalked side by side four men, made small by the distance. He looked\r\nintently under his curved palms and caught the sheen of brass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSoldiers. White soldiers!\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is always soldiers when thou and I go out alone together. But I\r\nhave never seen the white soldiers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey do no harm except when they are drunk. Keep behind this tree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey stepped behind the thick trunks in the cool dark of the\r\nmango-tope. Two little figures halted; the other two came forward\r\nuncertainly. They were the advance-party of a regiment on the march,\r\nsent out, as usual, to mark the camp. They bore five-foot sticks with\r\nfluttering flags, and called to each other as they spread over the flat\r\nearth.\r\n\r\nAt last they entered the mango-grove, walking heavily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s here or hereabouts\xe2\x80\x94officers\xe2\x80\x99 tents under the trees, I take it,\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 the rest of us can stay outside. Have they marked out for the\r\nbaggage-wagons behind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey cried again to their comrades in the distance, and the rough\r\nanswer came back faint and mellowed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShove the flag in here, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do they prepare?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, wonderstruck. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a great\r\nand terrible world. What is the device on the flag?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA soldier thrust a stave within a few feet of them, grunted\r\ndiscontentedly, pulled it up again, conferred with his companion, who\r\nlooked up and down the shaded cave of greenery, and returned it.\r\n\r\nKim stared with all his eyes, his breath coming short and sharp between\r\nhis teeth. The soldiers stamped off into the sunshine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Holy One!\xe2\x80\x9d he gasped. \xe2\x80\x9cMy horoscope! The drawing in the dust by the\r\npriest at Umballa! Remember what he said. First come two\xe2\x80\x94_ferashes_\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nmake all things ready\xe2\x80\x94in a dark place, as it is always at the beginning\r\nof a vision.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is not vision,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the world\xe2\x80\x99s Illusion,\r\nand no more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd after them comes the Bull\xe2\x80\x94the Red Bull on the green field. Look!\r\nIt is he!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pointed to the flag that was snap snapping in the evening breeze not\r\nten feet away. It was no more than an ordinary camp marking-flag; but\r\nthe regiment, always punctilious in matters of millinery, had charged\r\nit with the regimental device, the Red Bull, which is the crest of the\r\nMavericks\xe2\x80\x94the great Red Bull on a background of Irish green.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see, and now I remember.\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly it is thy Bull.\r\nCertainly, also, the two men came to make all ready.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are soldiers\xe2\x80\x94white soldiers. What said the priest? \xe2\x80\x98The sign over\r\nagainst the Bull is the sign of War and armed men.\xe2\x80\x99 Holy One, this\r\nthing touches my Search.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. It is true.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama stared fixedly at the device that flamed\r\nlike a ruby in the dusk. \xe2\x80\x9cThe priest at Umballa said that thine was the\r\nsign of War.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is to do now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait. Let us wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven now the darkness clears,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. It was only natural that the\r\ndescending sun should at last strike through the tree-trunks, across\r\nthe grove, filling it with mealy gold light for a few minutes; but to\r\nKim it was the crown of the Umballa Brahmin\xe2\x80\x99s prophecy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHark!\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cOne beats a drum\xe2\x80\x94far off!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt first the sound, carrying diluted through the still air, resembled\r\nthe beating of an artery in the head. Soon a sharpness was added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! The music,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim explained. He knew the sound of a regimental band,\r\nbut it amazed the lama.\r\n\r\nAt the far end of the plain a heavy, dusty column crawled in sight.\r\nThen the wind brought the tune:\r\n\r\nWe crave your condescension\r\nTo tell you what we know\r\nOf marching in the Mulligan Guards\r\nTo Sligo Port below!\r\n\r\nHere broke in the shrill-tongued fifes:\r\n\r\nWe shouldered arms,\r\nWe marched\xe2\x80\x94we marched away.\r\nFrom Ph\xc5\x93nix Park\r\nWe marched to Dublin Bay.\r\nThe drums and the fifes,\r\nOh, sweetly they did play,\r\nAs we marched\xe2\x80\x94marched\xe2\x80\x94marched\xe2\x80\x94with the\r\nMulligan Guards!\r\n\r\nIt was the band of the Mavericks playing the regiment to camp; for the\r\nmen were route-marching with their baggage. The rippling column swung\r\ninto the level\xe2\x80\x94carts behind it divided left and right, ran about like\r\nan ant-hill, and ...\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is sorcery!\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama.\r\n\r\nThe plain dotted itself with tents that seemed to rise, all spread,\r\nfrom the carts. Another rush of men invaded the grove, pitched a huge\r\ntent in silence, ran up yet eight or nine more by the side of it,\r\nunearthed cooking-pots, pans, and bundles, which were taken possession\r\nof by a crowd of native servants; and behold the mango-tope turned into\r\nan orderly town as they watched!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, sinking back afraid, as the fires twinkled\r\nand white officers with jingling swords stalked into the Mess-tent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStand back in the shadow. No one can see beyond the light of a fire,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Kim, his eyes still on the flag. He had never before watched the\r\nroutine of a seasoned regiment pitching camp in thirty minutes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook! look! look!\xe2\x80\x9d clucked the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cYonder comes a priest.\xe2\x80\x9d It was\r\nBennett, the Church of England Chaplain of the regiment, limping in\r\ndusty black. One of his flock had made some rude remarks about the\r\nChaplain\xe2\x80\x99s mettle; and to abash him Bennett had marched step by step\r\nwith the men that day. The black dress, gold cross on the watch-chain,\r\nthe hairless face, and the soft, black wideawake hat would have marked\r\nhim as a holy man anywhere in all India. He dropped into a camp-chair\r\nby the door of the Mess-tent and slid off his boots. Three or four\r\nofficers gathered round him, laughing and joking over his exploit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe talk of white men is wholly lacking in dignity,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama,\r\nwho judged only by tone. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I considered the countenance of that\r\npriest and I think he is learned. Is it likely that he will understand\r\nour talk? I would talk to him of my Search.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever speak to a white man till he is fed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, quoting a\r\nwell-known proverb. \xe2\x80\x9cThey will eat now, and\xe2\x80\x94and I do not think they are\r\ngood to beg from. Let us go back to the resting-place. After we have\r\neaten we will come again. It certainly was a Red Bull\xe2\x80\x94_my_ Red Bull.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were both noticeably absent-minded when the old lady\xe2\x80\x99s retinue set\r\ntheir meal before them; so none broke their reserve, for it is not\r\nlucky to annoy guests.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, picking his teeth, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will return to that place; but\r\nthou, O Holy One, must wait a little way off, because thy feet are\r\nheavier than mine and I am anxious to see more of that Red Bull.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how canst thou understand the talk? Walk slowly. The road is\r\ndark,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama replied uneasily.\r\n\r\nKim put the question aside. \xe2\x80\x9cI marked a place near to the trees,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere thou canst sit till I call. Nay,\xe2\x80\x9d as the lama made some sort\r\nof protest, \xe2\x80\x9cremember this is _my_ Search\xe2\x80\x94the Search for my Red Bull.\r\nThe sign in the Stars was not for thee. I know a little of the customs\r\nof white soldiers, and I always desire to see some new things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dost thou not know of this world?\xe2\x80\x9d The lama squatted obediently\r\nin a little hollow of the ground not a hundred yards from the hump of\r\nthe mango-trees dark against the star-powdered sky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay till I call.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim flitted into the dusk. He knew that in all\r\nprobability there would be sentries round the camp, and smiled to\r\nhimself as he heard the thick boots of one. A boy who can dodge over\r\nthe roofs of Lahore city on a moonlight night, using every little patch\r\nand corner of darkness to discomfit his pursuer, is not likely to be\r\nchecked by a line of well-trained soldiers. He paid them the compliment\r\nof crawling between a couple, and, running and halting, crouching and\r\ndropping flat, worked his way toward the lighted Mess-tent where, close\r\npressed behind the mango-tree, he waited till some chance word should\r\ngive him a returnable lead.\r\n\r\nThe one thing now in his mind was further information as to the Red\r\nBull. For aught he knew, and Kim\xe2\x80\x99s limitations were as curious and\r\nsudden as his expansions, the men, the nine hundred thorough devils of\r\nhis father\xe2\x80\x99s prophecy, might pray to the beast after dark, as Hindus\r\npray to the Holy Cow. That at least would be entirely right and\r\nlogical, and the padre with the gold cross would be therefore the man\r\nto consult in the matter. On the other hand, remembering sober-faced\r\npadres whom he had avoided in Lahore city, the priest might be an\r\ninquisitive nuisance who would bid him learn. But had it not been\r\nproven at Umballa that his sign in the high heavens portended War and\r\narmed men? Was he not the Friend of the Stars as well as of all the\r\nWorld, crammed to the teeth with dreadful secrets? Lastly\xe2\x80\x94and firstly\r\nas the undercurrent of all his quick thoughts\xe2\x80\x94this adventure, though he\r\ndid not know the English word, was a stupendous lark\xe2\x80\x94a delightful\r\ncontinuation of his old flights across the housetops, as well as the\r\nfulfilment of sublime prophecy. He lay belly-flat and wriggled towards\r\nthe Mess-tent door, a hand on the amulet round his neck.\r\n\r\nIt was as he suspected. The Sahibs prayed to their God; for in the\r\ncentre of the Mess-table\xe2\x80\x94its sole ornament when they were on the line\r\nof march\xe2\x80\x94stood a golden bull fashioned from old-time loot of the Summer\r\nPalace at Pekin\xe2\x80\x94a red-gold bull with lowered head, ramping upon a field\r\nof Irish green. To him the Sahibs held out their glasses and cried\r\naloud confusedly.\r\n\r\nNow the Reverend Arthur Bennett always left Mess after that toast, and\r\nbeing rather tired by his march his movements were more abrupt than\r\nusual. Kim, with slightly raised head, was still staring at his totem\r\non the table, when the Chaplain stepped on his right shoulder-blade.\r\nKim flinched under the leather, and, rolling sideways, brought down the\r\nChaplain, who, ever a man of action, caught him by the throat and\r\nnearly choked the life out of him. Kim then kicked him desperately in\r\nthe stomach. Mr Bennett gasped and doubled up, but without relaxing his\r\ngrip, rolled over again, and silently hauled Kim to his own tent. The\r\nMavericks were incurable practical jokers; and it occurred to the\r\nEnglishman that silence was best till he had made complete inquiry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s a boy!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as he drew his prize under the light of the\r\ntent-pole lantern, then shaking him severely cried: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat were you\r\ndoing? You\xe2\x80\x99re a thief. _Choor? Mallum?_\xe2\x80\x9d His Hindustanee was very\r\nlimited, and the ruffled and disgusted Kim intended to keep to the\r\ncharacter laid down for him. As he recovered his breath he was\r\ninventing a beautifully plausible tale of his relations to some\r\nscullion, and at the same time keeping a keen eye on and a little under\r\nthe Chaplain\xe2\x80\x99s left arm-pit. The chance came; he ducked for the\r\ndoorway, but a long arm shot out and clutched at his neck, snapping the\r\namulet-string and closing on the amulet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive it me. O, give it me. Is it lost? Give me the papers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe words were in English\xe2\x80\x94the tinny, saw-cut English of the\r\nnative-bred, and the Chaplain jumped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA scapular,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, opening his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, some sort of heathen\r\ncharm. Why\xe2\x80\x94why, do you speak English? Little boys who steal are beaten.\r\nYou know that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not\xe2\x80\x94I did not steal.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim danced in agony like a terrier at a\r\nlifted stick. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, give it me. It is my charm. Do not thieve it from\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Chaplain took no heed, but, going to the tent door, called aloud. A\r\nfattish, clean-shaven man appeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want your advice, Father Victor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bennett. \xe2\x80\x9cI found this boy in\r\nthe dark outside the Mess-tent. Ordinarily, I should have chastised him\r\nand let him go, because I believe him to be a thief. But it seems he\r\ntalks English, and he attaches some sort of value to a charm round his\r\nneck. I thought perhaps you might help me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBetween himself and the Roman Catholic Chaplain of the Irish contingent\r\nlay, as Bennett believed, an unbridgeable gulf, but it was noticeable\r\nthat whenever the Church of England dealt with a human problem she was\r\nvery likely to call in the Church of Rome. Bennett\xe2\x80\x99s official\r\nabhorrence of the Scarlet Woman and all her ways was only equalled by\r\nhis private respect for Father Victor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA thief talking English, is it? Let\xe2\x80\x99s look at his charm. No, it\xe2\x80\x99s not\r\na scapular, Bennett.\xe2\x80\x9d He held out his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut have we any right to open it? A sound whipping\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did _not_ thieve,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have hit me kicks all over my\r\nbody. Now give me my charm and I will go away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot quite so fast. We\xe2\x80\x99ll look first,\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor, leisurely\r\nrolling out poor Kimball O\xe2\x80\x99Hara\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9c_ne varietur_\xe2\x80\x9d parchment, his\r\nclearance-certificate, and Kim\xe2\x80\x99s baptismal certificate. On this last\r\nO\xe2\x80\x99Hara\xe2\x80\x94with some confused idea that he was doing wonders for his\r\nson\xe2\x80\x94had scrawled scores of times: \xe2\x80\x9c_Look after the boy. Please look\r\nafter the boy_\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94signing his name and regimental number in full.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPowers of Darkness below!\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor, passing all over to Mr\r\nBennett. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what these things are?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are mine, and I want to go away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not quite understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr Bennett. \xe2\x80\x9cHe probably brought them\r\non purpose. It may be a begging trick of some kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw a beggar less anxious to stay with his company, then.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s the makings of a gay mystery here. Ye believe in Providence,\r\nBennett?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I believe in miracles, so it comes to the same thing. Powers of\r\nDarkness! Kimball O\xe2\x80\x99Hara! And his son! But then he\xe2\x80\x99s a native, and I\r\nsaw Kimball married myself to Annie Shott. How long have you had these\r\nthings, boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEver since I was a little baby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFather Victor stepped forward quickly and opened the front of Kim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nupper garment. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Bennett, he\xe2\x80\x99s not very black. What\xe2\x80\x99s your\r\nname?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr Kimball?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps. Will you let me go away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat else?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey call me Kim Rishti ke. That is Kim of the Rishti.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98Rishti\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Eye_-rishti\xe2\x80\x94that was the Regiment\xe2\x80\x94my father\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIrish\xe2\x80\x94oh, I see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYess. That was how my father told me. My father, he has lived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas lived where?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas lived. Of _course_ he is dead\xe2\x80\x94gone-out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! That\xe2\x80\x99s your abrupt way of putting it, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBennett interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is possible I have done the boy an injustice.\r\nHe is certainly white, though evidently neglected. I am sure I must\r\nhave bruised him. I do not think spirits\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet him a glass of sherry, then, and let him squat on the cot. Now,\r\nKim,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Father Victor, \xe2\x80\x9cno one is going to hurt you. Drink that\r\ndown and tell us about yourself. The truth, if you\xe2\x80\x99ve no objection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim coughed a little as he put down the empty glass, and considered.\r\nThis seemed a time for caution and fancy. Small boys who prowl about\r\ncamps are generally turned out after a whipping. But he had received no\r\nstripes; the amulet was evidently working in his favour, and it looked\r\nas though the Umballa horoscope and the few words that he could\r\nremember of his father\xe2\x80\x99s maunderings fitted in most miraculously. Else\r\nwhy did the fat padre seem so impressed, and why the glass of hot\r\nyellow drink from the lean one?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father, he is dead in Lahore city since I was very little. The\r\nwoman, she kept _kabarri_ shop near where the hire-carriages are.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\r\nbegan with a plunge, not quite sure how far the truth would serve him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_No!_\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94with a gesture of disgust. \xe2\x80\x9cShe went out when I was born. My\r\nfather, he got these papers from the Jadoo-Gher what do you call that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(Bennett nodded) \xe2\x80\x9cbecause he was in good-standing. What do you call\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d (again Bennett nodded). \xe2\x80\x9cMy father told me that. He said, too,\r\nand also the Brahmin who made the drawing in the dust at Umballa two\r\ndays ago, _he_ said, that I shall find a Red Bull on a green field and\r\nthat the Bull shall help me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA phenomenal little liar,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Bennett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPowers of Darkness below, what a country!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Father Victor. \xe2\x80\x9cGo\r\non, Kim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did _not_ thieve. Besides, I am just now disciple of a very holy\r\nman. He is sitting outside. We saw two men come with flags, making the\r\nplace ready. That is _always_ so in a dream, or on account of\r\na\xe2\x80\x94a\xe2\x80\x94prophecy. So I knew it was come true. I saw the Red Bull on the\r\ngreen field, and my father he said: \xe2\x80\x98Nine hundred _pukka_ devils and\r\nthe Colonel riding on a horse will look after you when you find the Red\r\nBull!\xe2\x80\x99 I did not know what to do when I saw the Bull, but I went away\r\nand I came again when it was dark. I wanted to see the Bull again, and\r\nI saw the Bull again with the\xe2\x80\x94the Sahibs praying to it. I think the\r\nBull shall help me. The holy man said so too. He is sitting outside.\r\nWill you hurt him, if I call him a shout now? He is very holy. He can\r\nwitness to all the things I say, and he knows I am not a thief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Sahibs praying to a bull!\xe2\x80\x99 What in the world do you make of that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Bennett. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Disciple of a holy man!\xe2\x80\x99 Is the boy mad?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s O\xe2\x80\x99Hara\xe2\x80\x99s boy, sure enough. O\xe2\x80\x99Hara\xe2\x80\x99s boy leagued with all the\r\nPowers of Darkness. It\xe2\x80\x99s very much what his father would have done if\r\nhe was drunk. We\xe2\x80\x99d better invite the holy man. He may know something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe does not know anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cI will show you him if you\r\ncome. He is my master. Then afterwards we can go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPowers of Darkness!\xe2\x80\x9d was all that Father Victor could say, as Bennett\r\nmarched off, with a firm hand on Kim\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\nThey found the lama where he had dropped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Search is at an end for me,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Kim in the vernacular. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave found the Bull, but God knows what comes next. They will not hurt\r\nyou. Come to the fat priest\xe2\x80\x99s tent with this thin man and see the end.\r\nIt is all new, and they cannot talk Hindi. They are only uncurried\r\ndonkeys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it is not well to make a jest of their ignorance,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama\r\nreturned. \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad if thou art rejoiced, _chela_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDignified and unsuspicious, he strode into the little tent, saluted the\r\nChurches as a Churchman, and sat down by the open charcoal brazier. The\r\nyellow lining of the tent reflected in the lamplight made his face\r\nred-gold.\r\n\r\nBennett looked at him with the triple-ringed uninterest of the creed\r\nthat lumps nine-tenths of the world under the title of \xe2\x80\x9cheathen\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what was the end of the Search? What gift has the Red Bull\r\nbrought?\xe2\x80\x9d The lama addressed himself to Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says, \xe2\x80\x98What are you going to do?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Bennett was staring uneasily at\r\nFather Victor, and Kim, for his own ends, took upon himself the office\r\nof interpreter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not see what concern this _faquir_ has with the boy, who is\r\nprobably his dupe or his confederate,\xe2\x80\x9d Bennett began. \xe2\x80\x9cWe cannot allow\r\nan English boy\xe2\x80\x94Assuming that he is the son of a Mason, the sooner he\r\ngoes to the Masonic Orphanage the better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! That\xe2\x80\x99s your opinion as Secretary to the Regimental Lodge,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nFather Victor; \xe2\x80\x9cbut we might as well tell the old man what we are going\r\nto do. He doesn\xe2\x80\x99t look like a villain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy experience is that one can never fathom the Oriental mind. Now,\r\nKimball, I wish you to tell this man what I say word for word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim gathered the import of the next few sentences and began thus:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoly One, the thin fool who looks like a camel says that I am the son\r\nof a Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is true. He\r\nthinks that once a Sahib is always a Sahib, and between the two of them\r\nthey purpose to keep me in this Regiment or to send me to a _madrissah_\r\n(a school). It has happened before. I have always avoided it. The fat\r\nfool is of one mind and the camel-like one of another. But that is no\r\nodds. I may spend one night here and perhaps the next. It has happened\r\nbefore. Then I will run away and return to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut tell them that thou art my _chela_. Tell them how thou didst come\r\nto me when I was faint and bewildered. They laugh, and they talk of the police.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you saying?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr Bennett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah. He only says that if you do not let me go it will stop him in his\r\nbusiness\xe2\x80\x94his ur-gent private af-fairs.\xe2\x80\x9d This last was a reminiscence of\r\nsome talk with a Eurasian clerk in the Canal Department, but it only\r\ndrew a smile, which nettled him. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd if you _did_ know what his\r\nbusiness was you would not be in such a beastly hurry to interfere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor, not without feeling, as he\r\nwatched the lama\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a River in this country which he wishes to find so verree\r\nmuch. It was put out by an Arrow which\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Kim tapped his foot\r\nimpatiently as he translated in his own mind from the vernacular to his\r\nclumsy English. \xe2\x80\x9cOah, it was made by our Lord God Buddha, you know, and\r\nif you wash there you are washed away from all your sins and made as\r\nwhite as cotton-wool.\xe2\x80\x9d (Kim had heard mission-talk in his time.) \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nhis disciple, and we _must_ find that River. It is so verree valuable\r\nto us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay that again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bennett. Kim obeyed, with amplifications.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is gross blasphemy!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Church of England.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTck! Tck!\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor sympathetically. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d give a good deal\r\nto be able to talk the vernacular. A river that washes away sin! And\r\nhow long have you two been looking for it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, many days. Now we wish to go away and look for it again. It is not\r\nhere, you see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cBut he can\xe2\x80\x99t go on in that old\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s company. It would be different, Kim, if you were not a soldier\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nson. Tell him that the Regiment will take care of you and make you as\r\ngood a man as your\xe2\x80\x94as good a man as can be. Tell him that if he\r\nbelieves in miracles he must believe that\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no need to play on his credulity,\xe2\x80\x9d Bennett interrupted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m doing no such thing. He must believe that the boy\xe2\x80\x99s coming here\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nhis own Regiment\xe2\x80\x94in search of his Red Bull is in the nature of a\r\nmiracle. Consider the chances against it, Bennett. This one boy in all\r\nIndia, and our Regiment of all others on the line o\xe2\x80\x99 march for him to\r\nmeet with! It\xe2\x80\x99s predestined on the face of it. Yes, tell him it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_Kismet. Kismet, mallum?_ (Do you understand?)\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned towards the lama, to whom he might as well have talked of\r\nMesopotamia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey say,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the old man\xe2\x80\x99s eye lighted at Kim\xe2\x80\x99s speech \xe2\x80\x9cthey say that\r\nthe meaning of my horoscope is now accomplished, and that being led\r\nback\xe2\x80\x94though as thou knowest I went out of curiosity\xe2\x80\x94to these people and\r\ntheir Red Bull I must needs go to a _madrissah_ and be turned into a\r\nSahib. Now I make pretence of agreement, for at the worst it will be\r\nbut a few meals eaten away from thee. Then I will slip away and follow\r\ndown the road to Saharunpore. Therefore, Holy One, keep with that Kulu\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x94on no account stray far from her cart till I come again. Past\r\nquestion, my sign is of War and of armed men. See how they have given\r\nme wine to drink and set me upon a bed of honour! My father must have\r\nbeen some great person. So if they raise me to honour among them, good.\r\nIf not, good again. However it goes, I will run back to thee when I am\r\ntired. But stay with the Rajputni, or I shall miss thy feet ... Oah\r\nyess,\xe2\x80\x9d said the boy, \xe2\x80\x9cI have told him everything you tell me to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I cannot see any need why he should wait,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bennett, feeling\r\nin his trouser-pocket. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can investigate the details later\xe2\x80\x94and I will\r\ngive him a ru\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive him time. Maybe he\xe2\x80\x99s fond of the lad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor, half\r\narresting the clergyman\xe2\x80\x99s motion.\r\n\r\nThe lama dragged forth his rosary and pulled his huge hat-brim over his\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can he want now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim put up one hand. \xe2\x80\x9cHe says: \xe2\x80\x98Be quiet.\xe2\x80\x99 He wants to speak\r\nto me by himself. You see, you do not know one little word of what he\r\nsays, and I think if you talk he will perhaps give you very bad curses.\r\nWhen he takes those beads like that, you see, he always wants to be\r\nquiet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two Englishmen sat overwhelmed, but there was a look in Bennett\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neye that promised ill for Kim when he should be relaxed to the\r\nreligious arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA Sahib and the son of a Sahib\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d The lama\xe2\x80\x99s voice was harsh with pain.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut no white man knows the land and the customs of the land as thou\r\nknowest. How comes it this is true?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat matter, Holy One?\xe2\x80\x94but remember it is only for a night or two.\r\nRemember, I can change swiftly. It will all be as it was when I first\r\nspoke to thee under Zam-Zammah the great gun\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs a boy in the dress of white men\xe2\x80\x94when I first went to the Wonder\r\nHouse. And a second time thou wast a Hindu. What shall the third\r\nincarnation be?\xe2\x80\x9d He chuckled drearily. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, _chela_, thou has done a\r\nwrong to an old man because my heart went out to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd mine to thee. But how could I know that the Red Bull would bring\r\nme to this business?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama covered his face afresh, and nervously rattled the rosary. Kim\r\nsquatted beside him and laid hold upon a fold of his clothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow it is understood that the boy is a Sahib?\xe2\x80\x9d he went on in a muffled\r\ntone. \xe2\x80\x9cSuch a Sahib as was he who kept the images in the Wonder House.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThe lama\xe2\x80\x99s experience of white men was limited. He seemed to be\r\nrepeating a lesson. \xe2\x80\x9cSo then it is not seemly that he should do other\r\nthan as the Sahibs do. He must go back to his own people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor a day and a night and a day,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim pleaded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, ye don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d Father Victor saw Kim edging towards the door, and\r\ninterposed a strong leg.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand the customs of white men. The Priest of the Images\r\nin the Wonder House in Lahore was more courteous than the thin one\r\nhere. This boy will be taken from me. They will make a Sahib of my\r\ndisciple? Woe to me! How shall I find my River? Have _they_ no\r\ndisciples? Ask.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says he is very sorree that he cannot find the River now any more.\r\nHe says, Why have you no disciples, and stop bothering him? He wants to\r\nbe washed of his sins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeither Bennett nor Father Victor found any answer ready.\r\n\r\nSaid Kim in English, distressed for the lama\xe2\x80\x99s agony: \xe2\x80\x9cI think if you\r\nwill let me go now we will walk away quietly and not steal. We will\r\nlook for that River like before I was caught. I wish I did not come\r\nhere to find the Red Bull and all that sort of thing. I do not want\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the very best day\xe2\x80\x99s work you ever did for yourself, young man,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Bennett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to console him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor,\r\nwatching the lama intently. \xe2\x80\x9cHe can\xe2\x80\x99t take the boy away with him, and\r\nyet he\xe2\x80\x99s a good man\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m sure he\xe2\x80\x99s a good man. Bennett, if you give him\r\nthat rupee he\xe2\x80\x99ll curse you root and branch!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey listened to each other\xe2\x80\x99s breathing\xe2\x80\x94three\xe2\x80\x94five full minutes. Then\r\nthe lama raised his head, and looked forth across them into space and\r\nemptiness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am a Follower of the Way,\xe2\x80\x9d he said bitterly. \xe2\x80\x9cThe sin is mine\r\nand the punishment is mine. I made believe to myself for now I see it\r\nwas but make-belief\xe2\x80\x94that thou wast sent to me to aid in the Search. So\r\nmy heart went out to thee for thy charity and thy courtesy and the\r\nwisdom of thy little years. But those who follow the Way must permit\r\nnot the fire of any desire or attachment, for that is all Illusion. As\r\nsays ...\xe2\x80\x9d He quoted an old, old Chinese text, backed it with another,\r\nand reinforced these with a third. \xe2\x80\x9cI stepped aside from the Way, my\r\n_chela_. It was no fault of thine. I delighted in the sight of life,\r\nthe new people upon the roads, and in thy joy at seeing these things. I\r\nwas pleased with thee who should have considered my Search and my\r\nSearch alone. Now I am sorrowful because thou art taken away and my\r\nRiver is far from me. It is the Law which I have broken!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPowers of Darkness below!\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor, who, wise in the\r\nconfessional, heard the pain in every sentence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see now that the sign of the Red Bull was a sign for me as well as\r\nfor thee. All Desire is red\xe2\x80\x94and evil. I will do penance and find my\r\nRiver alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least go back to the Kulu woman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, \xe2\x80\x9cotherwise thou wilt be\r\nlost upon the roads. She will feed thee till I run back to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama waved a hand to show that the matter was finally settled in\r\nhis mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94his tone altered as he turned to Kim,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhat will they do with\r\nthee? At least I may, acquiring merit, wipe out past ill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMake me a Sahib\xe2\x80\x94so they think. The day after tomorrow I return. Do not\r\ngrieve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf what sort? Such an one as this or that man?\xe2\x80\x9d He pointed to Father\r\nVictor. \xe2\x80\x9cSuch an one as those I saw this evening, men wearing swords\r\nand stamping heavily?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not well. These men follow desire and come to emptiness. Thou\r\nmust not be of their sort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Umballa priest said that my Star was War,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim interjected. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwill ask these fools\xe2\x80\x94but there is truly no need. I will run away this\r\nnight, for all I wanted to see the new things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim put two or three questions in English to Father Victor, translating\r\nthe replies to the lama.\r\n\r\nThen: \xe2\x80\x9cHe says, \xe2\x80\x98You take him from me and you cannot say what you will\r\nmake him.\xe2\x80\x99 He says, \xe2\x80\x98Tell me before I go, for it is not a small thing\r\nto make a child.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will be sent to a school. Later on, we shall see. Kimball, I\r\nsuppose you\xe2\x80\x99d like to be a soldier?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Gorah-log_ (white-folk). No-ah! No-ah!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim shook his head violently.\r\nThere was nothing in his composition to which drill and routine\r\nappealed. \xe2\x80\x9cI will not be a soldier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will be what you\xe2\x80\x99re told to be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bennett; \xe2\x80\x9cand you should be\r\ngrateful that we\xe2\x80\x99re going to help you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim smiled compassionately. If these men lay under the delusion that he\r\nwould do anything that he did not fancy, so much the better.\r\n\r\nAnother long silence followed. Bennett fidgeted with impatience, and\r\nsuggested calling a sentry to evict the _faquir_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo they give or sell learning among the Sahibs? Ask them,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nlama, and Kim interpreted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey say that money is paid to the teacher\xe2\x80\x94but that money the Regiment\r\nwill give ... What need? It is only for a night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd\xe2\x80\x94the more money is paid the better learning is given?\xe2\x80\x9d The lama\r\ndisregarded Kim\xe2\x80\x99s plans for an early flight. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is no wrong to pay for\r\nlearning. To help the ignorant to wisdom is always a merit.\xe2\x80\x9d The rosary\r\nclicked furiously as an abacus. Then he faced his oppressors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk them for how much money do they give a wise and suitable teaching?\r\nAnd in what city is that teaching given?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor in English, when Kim had translated, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\ndepends. The Regiment would pay for you all the time you are at the\r\nMilitary Orphanage; or you might go on the Punjab Masonic Orphanage\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlist (not that he or you \xe2\x80\x99ud understand what that means); but the best\r\nschooling a boy can get in India is, of course, at St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s in\r\nPartibus at Lucknow.\xe2\x80\x9d This took some time to interpret, for Bennett\r\nwished to cut it short.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wants to know how much?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim placidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo or three hundred rupees a year.\xe2\x80\x9d Father Victor was long past any\r\nsense of amazement. Bennett, impatient, did not understand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says: \xe2\x80\x98Write that name and the money upon a paper and give it him.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAnd he says you must write your name below, because he is going to\r\nwrite a letter in some days to you. He says you are a good man. He says\r\nthe other man is a fool. He is going away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama rose suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cI follow my Search,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, and was gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll run slap into the sentries,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Father Victor, jumping up as\r\nthe lama stalked out; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I can\xe2\x80\x99t leave the boy.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim made swift\r\nmotion to follow, but checked himself. There was no sound of challenge\r\noutside. The lama had disappeared.\r\n\r\nKim settled himself composedly on the Chaplain\xe2\x80\x99s cot. At least the lama\r\nhad promised that he would stay with the Raiput woman from Kulu, and\r\nthe rest was of the smallest importance. It pleased him that the two\r\npadres were so evidently excited. They talked long in undertones,\r\nFather Victor urging some scheme on Mr Bennett, who seemed incredulous.\r\nAll this was very new and fascinating, but Kim felt sleepy. They called\r\nmen into the tent\xe2\x80\x94one of them certainly was the Colonel, as his father\r\nhad prophesied\xe2\x80\x94and they asked him an infinity of questions, chiefly\r\nabout the woman who looked after him, all of which Kim answered\r\ntruthfully. They did not seem to think the woman a good guardian.\r\n\r\nAfter all, this was the newest of his experiences. Sooner or later, if\r\nhe chose, he could escape into great, grey, formless India, beyond\r\ntents and padres and colonels. Meantime, if the Sahibs were to be\r\nimpressed, he would do his best to impress them. He too was a white\r\nman.\r\n\r\nAfter much talk that he could not comprehend, they handed him over to a\r\nsergeant, who had strict instructions not to let him escape. The\r\nRegiment would go on to Umballa, and Kim would be sent up, partly at\r\nthe expense of the Lodge and in part by subscription, to a place called\r\nSanawar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s miraculous past all whooping, Colonel,\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor, when\r\nhe had talked without a break for ten minutes. \xe2\x80\x9cHis Buddhist friend has\r\nlevanted after taking my name and address. I can\xe2\x80\x99t quite make out\r\nwhether he\xe2\x80\x99ll pay for the boy\xe2\x80\x99s education or whether he is preparing\r\nsome sort of witchcraft on his own account.\xe2\x80\x9d Then to Kim: \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll live\r\nto be grateful to your friend the Red Bull yet. We\xe2\x80\x99ll make a man of you\r\nat Sanawar\xe2\x80\x94even at the price o\xe2\x80\x99 making you a Protestant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly\xe2\x80\x94most certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bennett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you will not go to Sanawar,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we will go to Sanawar, little man. That\xe2\x80\x99s the order of the\r\nCommander-in-Chief, who\xe2\x80\x99s a trifle more important than O\xe2\x80\x99Hara\xe2\x80\x99s son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not go to Sanawar. You will go to thee War.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a shout of laughter from the full tent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you know your own Regiment a trifle better you won\xe2\x80\x99t confuse the\r\nline of march with line of battle, Kim. We hope to go to \xe2\x80\x98thee War\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsometime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah, I know all thatt.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim drew his bow again at a venture. If they\r\nwere not going to the war, at least they did not know what he knew of\r\nthe talk in the veranda at Umballa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know you are not at thee war now; but I tell _you_ that as soon as\r\nyou get to Umballa you will be sent to the war\xe2\x80\x94the new war. It is a war\r\nof eight thousand men, besides the guns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s explicit. D\xe2\x80\x99you add prophecy to your other gifts? Take him\r\nalong, sergeant. Take up a suit for him from the Drums, an\xe2\x80\x99 take care\r\nhe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t slip through your fingers. Who says the age of miracles is\r\ngone by? I think I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to bed. My poor mind\xe2\x80\x99s weakening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the far end of the camp, silent as a wild animal, an hour later sat\r\nKim, newly washed all over, in a horrible stiff suit that rasped his\r\narms and legs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA most amazin\xe2\x80\x99 young bird,\xe2\x80\x9d said the sergeant. \xe2\x80\x9cHe turns up in charge\r\nof a yellow-headed buck-Brahmin priest, with his father\xe2\x80\x99s Lodge\r\ncertificates round his neck, talkin\xe2\x80\x99 God knows what all of a red bull.\r\nThe buck-Brahmin evaporates without explanations, an\xe2\x80\x99 the bhoy sets\r\ncross-legged on the Chaplain\xe2\x80\x99s bed prophesyin\xe2\x80\x99 bloody war to the men at\r\nlarge. Injia\xe2\x80\x99s a wild land for a God-fearin\xe2\x80\x99 man. I\xe2\x80\x99ll just tie his leg\r\nto the tent-pole in case he\xe2\x80\x99ll go through the roof. What did ye say\r\nabout the war?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEight thousand men, besides guns,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cVery soon you will see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a consolin\xe2\x80\x99 little imp. Lie down between the Drums an\xe2\x80\x99 go to\r\nbye-bye. Those two boys will watch your slumbers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI\r\n\r\n\r\nNow I remember comrades\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    Old playmates on new seas\xe2\x80\x94\r\nWhenas we traded orpiment\r\n    Among the savages.\r\nTen thousand leagues to southward,\r\n    And thirty years removed\xe2\x80\x94\r\nThey knew not noble Valdez,\r\n    But me they knew and loved.\r\n\r\n_Song of Diego Valdez._\r\n\r\nVery early in the morning the white tents came down and disappeared as\r\nthe Mavericks took a side-road to Umballa. It did not skirt the\r\nresting-place, and Kim, trudging beside a baggage-cart under fire of\r\ncomments from soldiers\xe2\x80\x99 wives, was not so confident as overnight. He\r\ndiscovered that he was closely watched\xe2\x80\x94Father Victor on the one side,\r\nand Mr Bennett on the other.\r\n\r\nIn the forenoon the column checked. A camel-orderly handed the Colonel\r\na letter. He read it, and spoke to a Major. Half a mile in the rear,\r\nKim heard a hoarse and joyful clamour rolling down on him through the\r\nthick dust. Then someone beat him on the back, crying: \xe2\x80\x9cTell us how ye\r\nknew, ye little limb of Satan? Father dear, see if ye can make him\r\ntell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA pony ranged alongside, and he was hauled on to the priest\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsaddlebow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, my son, your prophecy of last night has come true. Our orders are\r\nto entrain at Umballa for the Front tomorrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is thatt?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, for \xe2\x80\x9c_front_\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c_entrain_\xe2\x80\x9d were newish\r\nwords to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are going to \xe2\x80\x98thee War,\xe2\x80\x99 as you called it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you are going to thee War. I said last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe did; but, Powers o\xe2\x80\x99 Darkness, how did ye know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim\xe2\x80\x99s eyes sparkled. He shut his lips, nodded his head, and looked\r\nunspeakable things. The Chaplain moved on through the dust, and\r\nprivates, sergeants, and subalterns called one another\xe2\x80\x99s attention to\r\nthe boy. The Colonel, at the head of the column, stared at him\r\ncuriously. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was probably some bazar rumour.\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut even\r\nthen\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He referred to the paper in his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cHang it all, the thing\r\nwas only decided within the last forty-eight hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre there many more like you in India?\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor, \xe2\x80\x9cor are\r\nyou by way o\xe2\x80\x99 being a _lusus natur\xc3\xa6?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I have told you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the boy, \xe2\x80\x9cwill you let me go back to my old\r\nman? If he has not stayed with that woman from Kulu, I am afraid he\r\nwill die.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy what I saw of him he\xe2\x80\x99s as well able to take care of himself as you.\r\nNo. Ye\xe2\x80\x99ve brought us luck, an\xe2\x80\x99 we\xe2\x80\x99re goin\xe2\x80\x99 to make a man of you. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntake ye back to your baggage-cart and ye\xe2\x80\x99ll come to me this evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor the rest of the day Kim found himself an object of distinguished\r\nconsideration among a few hundred white men. The story of his\r\nappearance in camp, the discovery of his parentage, and his prophecy,\r\nhad lost nothing in the telling. A big, shapeless white woman on a pile\r\nof bedding asked him mysteriously whether he thought her husband would\r\ncome back from the war. Kim reflected gravely, and said that he would,\r\nand the woman gave him food. In many respects, this big procession that\r\nplayed music at intervals\xe2\x80\x94this crowd that talked and laughed so\r\neasily\xe2\x80\x94resembled a festival in Lahore city. So far, there was no sign\r\nof hard work, and he resolved to lend the spectacle his patronage. At\r\nevening there came out to meet them bands of music, and played the\r\nMavericks into camp near Umballa railway station. That was an\r\ninteresting night. Men of other regiments came to visit the Mavericks.\r\nThe Mavericks went visiting on their own account. Their pickets hurried\r\nforth to bring them back, met pickets of strange regiments on the same\r\nduty; and, after a while, the bugles blew madly for more pickets with\r\nofficers to control the tumult. The Mavericks had a reputation for\r\nliveliness to live up to. But they fell in on the platform next morning\r\nin perfect shape and condition; and Kim, left behind with the sick,\r\nwomen, and boys, found himself shouting farewells excitedly as the\r\ntrains drew away. Life as a Sahib was amusing so far; but he touched it\r\nwith a cautious hand. Then they marched him back in charge of a\r\ndrummer-boy to empty, lime-washed barracks, whose floors were covered\r\nwith rubbish and string and paper, and whose ceilings gave back his\r\nlonely footfall. Native-fashion, he curled himself up on a stripped cot\r\nand went to sleep. An angry man stumped down the veranda, woke him up,\r\nand said he was a schoolmaster. This was enough for Kim, and he retired\r\ninto his shell. He could just puzzle out the various English Police\r\nnotices in Lahore city, because they affected his comfort; and among\r\nthe many guests of the woman who looked after him had been a queer\r\nGerman who painted scenery for the Parsee travelling theatre. He told\r\nKim that he had been \xe2\x80\x9con the barricades in \xe2\x80\x9cForty-eight,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\ntherefore\xe2\x80\x94at least that was how it struck Kim\xe2\x80\x94he would teach the boy to\r\nwrite in return for food. Kim had been kicked as far as single letters,\r\nbut did not think well of them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know anything. Go away!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, scenting evil. Hereupon\r\nthe man caught him by the ear, dragged him to a room in a far-off wing\r\nwhere a dozen drummer-boys were sitting on forms, and told him to be\r\nstill if he could do nothing else. This he managed very successfully.\r\nThe man explained something or other with white lines on a black board\r\nfor at least half an hour, and Kim continued his interrupted nap. He\r\nmuch disapproved of the present aspect of affairs, for this was the\r\nvery school and discipline he had spent two-thirds of his young life in\r\navoiding. Suddenly a beautiful idea occurred to him, and he wondered\r\nthat he had not thought of it before.\r\n\r\nThe man dismissed them, and first to spring through the veranda into\r\nthe open sunshine was Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Ere, you! \xe2\x80\x99Alt! Stop!\xe2\x80\x9d said a high voice at his heels. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got to\r\nlook after you. My orders are not to let you out of my sight. Where are\r\nyou goin\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was the drummer-boy who had been hanging round him all the\r\nforenoon\xe2\x80\x94a fat and freckled person of about fourteen, and Kim loathed\r\nhim from the soles of his boots to his cap-ribbons.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the bazar\xe2\x80\x94to get sweets\xe2\x80\x94for you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, after thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, the bazar\xe2\x80\x99s out o\xe2\x80\x99 bounds. If we go there we\xe2\x80\x99ll get a\r\ndressing-down. You come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow near can we go?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim did not know what bounds meant, but he wished\r\nto be polite\xe2\x80\x94for the present.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Ow near? \xe2\x80\x99Ow far, you mean! We can go as far as that tree down the\r\nroad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I will go there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t goin\xe2\x80\x99. It\xe2\x80\x99s too \xe2\x80\x99ot. I can watch you from \xe2\x80\x99ere.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s no good your runninv away. If you did, they\xe2\x80\x99d spot you by your\r\nclothes. That\xe2\x80\x99s regimental stuff you\xe2\x80\x99re wearin\xe2\x80\x99. There ain\xe2\x80\x99t a picket\r\nin Umballa wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ead you back quicker than you started out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis did not impress Kim as much as the knowledge that his raiment\r\nwould tire him out if he tried to run. He slouched to the tree at the\r\ncorner of a bare road leading towards the bazar, and eyed the natives\r\npassing. Most of them were barrack-servants of the lowest caste. Kim\r\nhailed a sweeper, who promptly retorted with a piece of unnecessary\r\ninsolence, in the natural belief that the European boy could not follow\r\nit. The low, quick answer undeceived him. Kim put his fettered soul\r\ninto it, thankful for the late chance to abuse somebody in the tongue\r\nhe knew best. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, go to the nearest letter-writer in the bazar\r\nand tell him to come here. I would write a letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94but what manner of white man\xe2\x80\x99s son art thou to need a bazar\r\nletter-writer? Is there not a schoolmaster in the barracks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy; and Hell is full of the same sort. Do my order, you\xe2\x80\x94you Od! Thy\r\nmother was married under a basket! Servant of Lal Beg\xe2\x80\x9d (Kim knew the\r\nGod of the sweepers), \xe2\x80\x9crun on my business or we will talk again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sweeper shuffled off in haste. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is a white boy by the\r\nbarracks waiting under a tree who is not a white boy,\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered to\r\nthe first bazar letter-writer he came across. \xe2\x80\x9cHe needs thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill he pay?\xe2\x80\x9d said the spruce scribe, gathering up his desk and pens\r\nand sealing-wax all in order.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know. He is not like other boys. Go and see. It is well\r\nworth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim danced with impatience when the slim young Kayeth hove in sight. As\r\nsoon as his voice could carry he cursed him volubly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst I will take my pay,\xe2\x80\x9d the letter-writer said. \xe2\x80\x9cBad words have\r\nmade the price higher. But who art thou, dressed in that fashion, to\r\nspeak in this fashion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha! That is in the letter which thou shalt write. Never was such a\r\ntale. But I am in no haste. Another writer will serve me. Umballa city\r\nis as full of them as is Lahore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFour annas,\xe2\x80\x9d said the writer, sitting down and spreading his cloth in\r\nthe shade of a deserted barrack-wing.\r\n\r\nMechanically Kim squatted beside him\xe2\x80\x94squatted as only the natives\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x94in spite of the abominable clinging trousers.\r\n\r\nThe writer regarded him sideways.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the price to ask of Sahibs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cNow fix me a true\r\none.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn anna and a half. How do I know, having written the letter, that\r\nthou wilt not run away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI must not go beyond this tree, and there is also the stamp to be\r\nconsidered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI get no commission on the price of the stamp. Once more, what manner\r\nof white boy art thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat shall be said in the letter, which is to Mahbub Ali, the\r\nhorse-dealer in the Kashmir Serai, at Lahore. He is my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWonder on wonder!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the letter-writer, dipping a reed in the\r\ninkstand. \xe2\x80\x9cTo be written in Hindi?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly. To Mahbub Ali then. Begin! _I have come down with the old\r\nman as far as Umballa in the train. At Umballa I carried the news of\r\nthe bay mare\xe2\x80\x99s pedigree._\xe2\x80\x9d After what he had seen in the garden, he was\r\nnot going to write of white stallions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSlower a little. What has a bay mare to do ... Is it Mahbub Ali, the\r\ngreat dealer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho else? I have been in his service. Take more ink. Again. _As the\r\norder was, so I did it. We then went on foot towards Benares, but on\r\nthe third day we found a certain regiment._ Is that down?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, _pulton_,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the writer, all ears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I went into their camp and was caught, and by means of the charm\r\nabout my neck, which thou knowest, it was established that I was the\r\nson of some man in the regiment: according to the prophecy of the Red\r\nBull, which thou knowest was common talk of our bazar._\xe2\x80\x9d Kim waited for\r\nthis shaft to sink into the letter-writer\xe2\x80\x99s heart, cleared his throat,\r\nand continued: \xe2\x80\x9c_A priest clothed me and gave me a new name ... One\r\npriest, however, was a fool. The clothes are very heavy, but I am a\r\nSahib and my heart is heavy too. They send me to a school and beat me.\r\nI do not like the air and water here. Come then and help me, Mahbub\r\nAli, or send me some money, for I have not sufficient to pay the writer\r\nwho writes this._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Who writes this.\xe2\x80\x99 It is my own fault that I was tricked. Thou art as\r\nclever as Husain Bux that forged the Treasury stamps at Nucklao. But\r\nwhat a tale! What a tale! Is it true by any chance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does not profit to tell lies to Mahbub Ali. It is better to help\r\nhis friends by lending them a stamp. When the money comes I will\r\nrepay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe writer grunted doubtfully, but took a stamp out of his desk, sealed\r\nthe letter, handed it over to Kim, and departed. Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s was a\r\nname of power in Umballa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the way to win a good account with the Gods,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim shouted\r\nafter him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPay me twice over when the money comes,\xe2\x80\x9d the man cried over his\r\nshoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was you bukkin\xe2\x80\x99 to that nigger about?\xe2\x80\x9d said the drummer-boy when\r\nKim returned to the veranda. \xe2\x80\x9cI was watch-in\xe2\x80\x99 you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was only talkin\xe2\x80\x99 to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou talk the same as a nigger, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo-ah! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you \xe2\x80\x99ate it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah yess!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d run away if I knew where to go to, but, as the men say, in this\r\nbloomin\xe2\x80\x99 Injia you\xe2\x80\x99re only a prisoner at large. I\xe2\x80\x99m fair sick of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been in Be\xe2\x80\x94England?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99y, I only come out last troopin\xe2\x80\x99 season with my mother. Tell me something about England. My father he came from\r\nthere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThough he would not say so, Kim of course disbelieved every word the\r\ndrummer-boy spoke about the Liverpool suburb which was his England. It\r\npassed the heavy time till dinner\xe2\x80\x94a most unappetizing meal served to\r\nthe boys and a few invalids in a corner of a barrack-room. But that he\r\nhad written to Mahbub Ali, Kim would have been almost depressed. The\r\nindifference of native crowds he was used to; but this strong\r\nloneliness among white men preyed on him. He was grateful when, in the\r\ncourse of the afternoon, a big soldier took him over to Father Victor,\r\nwho lived in another wing across another dusty parade-ground. The\r\npriest was reading an English letter written in purple ink. He looked\r\nat Kim more curiously than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 how do you like it, my son, as far as you\xe2\x80\x99ve gone? Not much, eh?\r\nIt must be hard\xe2\x80\x94very hard on a wild animal. Listen now. I\xe2\x80\x99ve an amazin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nepistle from your friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is he? D\xe2\x80\x99you know anything about his money affairs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nKim\xe2\x80\x99s face showed that he did not.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I tell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99m askin\xe2\x80\x99. It\xe2\x80\x99s written from Jagadhir Road ...\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_Sitting on wayside in grave meditation, trusting to be favoured with\r\nyour Honour\xe2\x80\x99s applause of present step, which recommend your Honour to\r\nexecute for Almighty God\xe2\x80\x99s sake.
