Otherwise no earthly use._\xe2\x80\x99 Faith, the old man\xe2\x80\x99s hit the\r\nbull\xe2\x80\x99s-eye that time! \xe2\x80\x98_If your Honour condescending giving my boy best\r\neducations Xavier_\xe2\x80\x99 (I suppose that\xe2\x80\x99s St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s in Partibus) \xe2\x80\x98_in\r\nterms of our conversation dated in your tent 15th instant_\xe2\x80\x99 (a\r\nbusiness-like touch there!) \xe2\x80\x98_then Almighty God blessing your Honour\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsucceedings to third an\xe2\x80\x99 fourth generation and_\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94now listen!\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98_confide\r\nin your Honour\xe2\x80\x99s humble servant for adequate remuneration per hoondi\r\nper annum three hundred rupees a year to one expensive education St\r\nXavier, Lucknow, and allow small time to forward same per hoondi sent\r\nto any part of India as your Honour shall address yourself. This\r\nservant of your Honour has presently no place to lay crown of his head,\r\nbut going to Benares by train on account of persecution of old woman\r\ntalking so much and unanxious residing Saharunpore in any domestic\r\ncapacity._\xe2\x80\x99 Now what in the world does that mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has asked him to be her _puro_\xe2\x80\x94her clergyman\xe2\x80\x94at Saharunpore, _I_\r\nthink. He would not do that on account of his River. She _did_ talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s clear to you, is it? It beats me altogether. \xe2\x80\x98_So going to\r\nBenares, where will find address and forward rupees for boy who is\r\napple of eye, and for Almighty God\xe2\x80\x99s sake execute this education, and\r\nyour petitioner as in duty bound shall ever awfully pray. Written by\r\nSobrao Satai, Failed Entrance Allahabad University, for Venerable\r\nTeshoo Lama the priest of Such-zen looking for a River, address care of\r\nTirthankars\xe2\x80\x99 Temple, Benares. P. M.\xe2\x80\x94Please note boy is apple of eye,\r\nand rupees shall be sent per hoondi three hundred per annum. For God\r\nAlmighty\xe2\x80\x99s sake._\xe2\x80\x99 Now, is that ravin\xe2\x80\x99 lunacy or a business\r\nproposition? I ask you, because I\xe2\x80\x99m fairly at my wits\xe2\x80\x99 end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says he will give me three hundred rupees a year? So he will give\r\nme them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s the way you look at it, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Of_ course. If he says so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe priest whistled; then he addressed Kim as an equal. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbelieve it; but we\xe2\x80\x99ll see. You were goin\xe2\x80\x99 off today to the Military\r\nOrphanage at Sanawar, where the Regiment would keep you till you were\r\nold enough to enlist. Ye\xe2\x80\x99d be brought up to the Church of England.\r\nBennett arranged for that. On the other hand, if ye go to St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nye\xe2\x80\x99ll get a better education an\xe2\x80\x94an can have the religion. D\xe2\x80\x99ye see my\r\ndilemma? Kim saw nothing save a vision of the lama going south in a\r\ntrain with none to beg for him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike most people, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to temporize. If your friend sends the\r\nmoney from Benares\xe2\x80\x94Powers of Darkness below, where\xe2\x80\x99s a street-beggar to\r\nraise three hundred rupees?\xe2\x80\x94ye\xe2\x80\x99ll go down to Lucknow and I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay your\r\nfare, because I can\xe2\x80\x99t touch the subscription-money if I intend, as I\r\ndo, to make ye a Catholic. If he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t, ye\xe2\x80\x99ll go to the Military\r\nOrphanage at the Regiment\xe2\x80\x99s expense. I\xe2\x80\x99ll allow him three days\xe2\x80\x99 grace,\r\nthough I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it at all. Even then, if he fails in his\r\npayments later on ... but it\xe2\x80\x99s beyond me. We can only walk one step at\r\na time in this world, praise God! An\xe2\x80\x99 they sent Bennett to the Front\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 left me behind. Bennett can\xe2\x80\x99t expect everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah yess,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim vaguely.\r\n\r\nThe priest leaned forward. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d give a month\xe2\x80\x99s pay to find what\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99\r\non inside that little round head of yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, and scratched it. He was wondering\r\nwhether Mahbub Ali would send him as much as a whole rupee. Then he\r\ncould pay the letter-writer and write letters to the lama at Benares.\r\nPerhaps Mahbub Ali would visit him next time he came south with horses.\r\nSurely he must know that Kim\xe2\x80\x99s delivery of the letter to the officer at\r\nUmballa had caused the great war which the men and boys had discussed\r\nso loudly over the barrack dinner-tables. But if Mahbub Ali did not\r\nknow this, it would be very unsafe to tell him so. Mahbub Ali was hard\r\nupon boys who knew, or thought they knew, too much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, till I get further news\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Father Victor\xe2\x80\x99s voice interrupted the\r\nreverie. \xe2\x80\x9cYe can run along now and play with the other boys. They\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nteach ye something\xe2\x80\x94but I don\xe2\x80\x99t think ye\xe2\x80\x99ll like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe day dragged to its weary end. When he wished to sleep he was\r\ninstructed how to fold up his clothes and set out his boots; the other\r\nboys deriding. Bugles waked him in the dawn; the schoolmaster caught\r\nhim after breakfast, thrust a page of meaningless characters under his\r\nnose, gave them senseless names and whacked him without reason. Kim\r\nmeditated poisoning him with opium borrowed from a barrack-sweeper, but\r\nreflected that, as they all ate at one table in public (this was\r\npeculiarly revolting to Kim, who preferred to turn his back on the\r\nworld at meals), the stroke might be dangerous. Then he attempted\r\nrunning off to the village where the priest had tried to drug the\r\nlama\xe2\x80\x94the village where the old soldier lived. But far-seeing sentries\r\nat every exit headed back the little scarlet figure. Trousers and\r\njacket crippled body and mind alike so he abandoned the project and\r\nfell back, Oriental-fashion, on time and chance. Three days of torment\r\npassed in the big, echoing white rooms. He walked out of afternoons\r\nunder escort of the drummer-boy, and all he heard from his companions\r\nwere the few useless words which seemed to make two-thirds of the white\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s abuse. Kim knew and despised them all long ago. The boy resented\r\nhis silence and lack of interest by beating him, as was only natural.\r\nHe did not care for any of the bazars which were in bounds. He styled\r\nall natives \xe2\x80\x9cniggers\xe2\x80\x9d; yet servants and sweepers called him abominable\r\nnames to his face, and, misled by their deferential attitude, he never\r\nunderstood. This somewhat consoled Kim for the beatings.\r\n\r\nOn the morning of the fourth day a judgement overtook that drummer.\r\nThey had gone out together towards Umballa racecourse. He returned\r\nalone, weeping, with news that young O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, to whom he had been doing\r\nnothing in particular, had hailed a scarlet-bearded nigger on\r\nhorseback; that the nigger had then and there laid into him with a\r\npeculiarly adhesive quirt, picked up young O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, and borne him off at\r\nfull gallop. These tidings came to Father Victor, and he drew down his\r\nlong upper lip. He was already sufficiently startled by a letter from\r\nthe Temple of the Tirthankars at Benares, enclosing a native banker\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnote of hand for three hundred rupees, and an amazing prayer to\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlmighty God\xe2\x80\x9d. The lama would have been more annoyed than the priest\r\nhad he known how the bazar letter-writer had translated his phrase \xe2\x80\x9cto\r\nacquire merit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPowers of Darkness below!\xe2\x80\x9d Father Victor fumbled with the note. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99\r\nnow he\xe2\x80\x99s off with another of his peep-o\xe2\x80\x99-day friends. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhether it will be a greater relief to me to get him back or to have\r\nhim lost. He\xe2\x80\x99s beyond my comprehension. How the Divil\xe2\x80\x94yes, he\xe2\x80\x99s the man\r\nI mean\xe2\x80\x94can a street-beggar raise money to educate white boys?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThree miles off, on Umballa racecourse, Mahbub Ali, reining a grey\r\nKabuli stallion with Kim in front of him, was saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Little Friend of all the World, there is _my_ honour and\r\nreputation to be considered. All the officer-Sahibs in all the\r\nregiments, and all Umballa, know Mahbub Ali. Men saw me pick thee up\r\nand chastise that boy. We are seen now from far across this plain. How\r\ncan I take thee away, or account for thy disappearing if I set thee\r\ndown and let thee run off into the crops? They would put me in jail. Be\r\npatient. Once a Sahib, always a Sahib. When thou art a man\xe2\x80\x94who\r\nknows?\xe2\x80\x94thou wilt be grateful to Mahbub Ali.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake me beyond their sentries where I can change this red. Give me\r\nmoney and I will go to Benares and be with my lama again. I do not want\r\nto be a Sahib, and remember I did deliver that message.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe stallion bounded wildly. Mahbub Ali had incautiously driven home\r\nthe sharp-edged stirrup. (He was not the new sort of fluent\r\nhorse-dealer who wears English boots and spurs.) Kim drew his own\r\nconclusions from that betrayal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was a small matter. It lay on the straight road to Benares. I and\r\nthe Sahib have by this time forgotten it. I send so many letters and\r\nmessages to men who ask questions about horses, I cannot well remember\r\none from the other. Was it some matter of a bay mare that Peters Sahib\r\nwished the pedigree of?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim saw the trap at once. If he had said \xe2\x80\x9cbay mare\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub would have\r\nknown by his very readiness to fall in with the amendment that the boy\r\nsuspected something. Kim replied therefore:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBay mare. No. _I_ do not forget my messages thus. It was a white\r\nstallion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, so it was. A white Arab stallion. But thou didst write \xe2\x80\x98bay mare\xe2\x80\x99\r\nto me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho cares to tell truth to a letter-writer?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim answered, feeling\r\nMahbub\xe2\x80\x99s palm on his heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHi! Mahbub, you old villain, pull up!\xe2\x80\x9d cried a voice, and an\r\nEnglishman raced alongside on a little polo-pony. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been chasing\r\nyou half over the country. That Kabuli of yours can go. For sale, I\r\nsuppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have some young stuff coming on made by Heaven for the delicate and\r\ndifficult polo-game. He has no equal. He\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlays polo and waits at table. Yes. We know all that. What the deuce\r\nhave you got there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA. boy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was being beaten by another boy. His\r\nfather was once a white soldier in the big war. The boy was a child in\r\nLahore city. He played with my horses when he was a babe. Now I think\r\nthey will make him a soldier. He has been newly caught by his father\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nRegiment that went up to the war last week. But I do not think he wants\r\nto be a soldier. I take him for a ride. Tell me where thy barracks are\r\nand I will set thee there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me go. I can find the barracks alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if thou runnest away who will say it is not my fault?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll run back to his dinner. Where has he to run to?\xe2\x80\x9d the Englishman\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was born in the land. He has friends. He goes where he chooses. He\r\nis a _chabuk sawai_ (a sharp chap). It needs only to change his\r\nclothing, and in a twinkling he would be a low-caste Hindu boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce he would!\xe2\x80\x9d The Englishman looked critically at the boy as\r\nMahbub headed towards the barracks. Kim ground his teeth. Mahbub was\r\nmocking him, as faithless Afghans will; for he went on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will send him to a school and put heavy boots on his feet and\r\nswaddle him in these clothes. Then he will forget all he knows. Now,\r\nwhich of the barracks is thine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim pointed\xe2\x80\x94he could not speak\xe2\x80\x94to Father Victor\xe2\x80\x99s wing, all staring\r\nwhite near by.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps he will make a good soldier,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub reflectively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will make a good orderly at least. I sent him to deliver a message\r\nonce from Lahore. A message concerning the pedigree of a white\r\nstallion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere was deadly insult on deadlier injury\xe2\x80\x94and the Sahib to whom he had\r\nso craftily given that war-waking letter heard it all. Kim beheld\r\nMahbub Ali frying in flame for his treachery, but for himself he saw\r\none long grey vista of barracks, schools, and barracks again. He gazed\r\nimploringly at the clear-cut face in which there was no glimmer of\r\nrecognition; but even at this extremity it never occurred to him to\r\nthrow himself on the white man\xe2\x80\x99s mercy or to denounce the Afghan. And\r\nMahbub stared deliberately at the Englishman, who stared as\r\ndeliberately at Kim, quivering and tongue-tied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy horse is well trained,\xe2\x80\x9d said the dealer. \xe2\x80\x9cOthers would have kicked,\r\nSahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Englishman at last, rubbing his pony\xe2\x80\x99s damp withers with\r\nhis whip-butt. \xe2\x80\x9cWho makes the boy a soldier?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says the Regiment that found him, and especially the Padre-sahib of\r\nthat regiment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is the Padre!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim choked as bare-headed Father Victor sailed\r\ndown upon them from the veranda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPowers O\xe2\x80\x99 Darkness below, O\xe2\x80\x99Hara! Meant to have come over and called before this.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m Creighton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf the Ethnological Survey?\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Victor. The Englishman\r\nnodded. \xe2\x80\x9cFaith, I\xe2\x80\x99m glad to meet ye then; an\xe2\x80\x99 I owe you some thanks for\r\nbringing back the boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo thanks to me, Padre. Besides, the boy wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going away. You don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow old Mahbub Ali.\xe2\x80\x9d The horse-dealer sat impassive in the sunlight.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will when you have been in the station a month. He sells us all\r\nour crocks. That boy is rather a curiosity. Can you tell me anything\r\nabout him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan I tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d puffed Father Victor. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be the one man that\r\ncould help me in my quandaries. Tell you! Powers o\xe2\x80\x99 Darkness, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nbursting to tell someone who knows something o\xe2\x80\x99 the native!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA groom came round the corner. Colonel Creighton raised his voice,\r\nspeaking in Urdu. \xe2\x80\x9cVery good, Mahbub Ali, but what is the use of\r\ntelling me all those stories about the pony? Not one pice more than\r\nthree hundred and fifty rupees will I give.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Sahib is a little hot and angry after riding,\xe2\x80\x9d the horse-dealer\r\nreturned, with the leer of a privileged jester. \xe2\x80\x9cPresently, he will see\r\nmy horse\xe2\x80\x99s points more clearly. What will\r\nthey give thee for blood-money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA cheerful young demon!\xe2\x80\x9d The Colonel bit his cigar, and turned\r\npolitely to Father Victor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are the letters that the fat priest is waving before the Colonel?\r\nStand behind the stallion as though looking at my bridle!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub\r\nAli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA letter from my lama which he wrote from Jagadhir Road, saying that\r\nhe will pay three hundred rupees by the year for my schooling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho! There is a big school there for the sons of Sahibs\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nhalf-Sahibs. I have seen it when I sell horses there. So the lama also\r\nloved the Friend of all the World?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy; and _he_ did not tell lies, or return me to captivity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSmall wonder the Padre does not know how to unravel the thread. I have had some few dealings in\r\n_hoondies_. The Colonel Sahib is looking at it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat good is all this to me?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim wearily. \xe2\x80\x9cThou wilt go away,\r\nand they will return me to those empty rooms where there is no good\r\nplace to sleep and where the boys beat me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think that. Have patience, child. All Pathans are not\r\nfaithless\xe2\x80\x94except in horseflesh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFive\xe2\x80\x94ten\xe2\x80\x94fifteen minutes passed, Father Victor talking energetically or\r\nasking questions which the Colonel answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you everything that I know about the boy from beginnin\r\nto end; and it\xe2\x80\x99s a blessed relief to me. Thou wilt go to school at Lucknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I meet my Holy One there?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim whimpered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least Lucknow is nearer to Benares than Umballa. It may be thou\r\nwilt go under my protection. Mahbub Ali knows this, and he will be\r\nangry if thou returnest to the Road now. Remember\xe2\x80\x94much has been told me\r\nwhich I do not forget.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will wait,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, \xe2\x80\x9cbut the boys will beat me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the bugles blew for dinner.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\n\r\n\r\nUnto whose use the pregnant suns are poised\r\n    With idiot moons and stars retracing stars?\r\nCreep thou betweene\xe2\x80\x94thy coming\xe2\x80\x99s all unnoised.\r\n    Heaven hath her high, as Earth her baser, wars.\r\nHeir to these tumults, this affright, that fraye\r\n(By Adam\xe2\x80\x99s, fathers\xe2\x80\x99, own, sin bound alway);\r\nPeer up, draw out thy horoscope and say\r\n    Which planet mends thy threadbare fate or mars!\r\n\r\nSir John Christie.\r\n\r\nIn the afternoon the red-faced schoolmaster told Kim that he had been\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cstruck off the strength\xe2\x80\x9d, which conveyed no meaning to him till he was\r\nordered to go away and play. Then he ran to the bazar, and found the\r\nyoung letter-writer to whom he owed a stamp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I pay,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim royally, \xe2\x80\x9cand now I need another letter to be\r\nwritten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMahbub Ali is in Umballa,\xe2\x80\x9d said the writer jauntily. He was, by virtue\r\nof his office, a bureau of general misinformation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is not to Mahbub, but to a priest. Take thy pen and write\r\nquickly. _To Teshoo Lama, the Holy One from Bhotiyal seeking for a\r\nRiver, who is now in the Temple of the Tirthankars at Benares._ Take\r\nmore ink! _In three days I am to go down to Nucklao to the school at\r\nNucklao. The name of the school is Xavier. I do not know where that\r\nschool is, but it is at Nucklao._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I know Nucklao,\xe2\x80\x9d the writer interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cI know the school.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him where it is, and I give half an anna.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe reed pen scratched busily. \xe2\x80\x9cHe cannot mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d The man lifted his\r\nhead. \xe2\x80\x9cWho watches us across the street?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim looked up hurriedly and saw Colonel Creighton in tennis-flannels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that is some Sahib who knows the fat priest in the barracks. He is\r\nbeckoning me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dost thou?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Colonel, when Kim trotted up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I am not running away. I send a letter to my Holy One at Benares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had not thought of that. Hast thou said that I take thee to\r\nLucknow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, I have not. Read the letter, if there be a doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why hast thou left out my name in writing to that Holy One?\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nColonel smiled a queer smile. Kim took his courage in both hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was said once to me that it is inexpedient to write the names of\r\nstrangers concerned in any matter, because by the naming of names many\r\ngood plans are brought to confusion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast been well taught,\xe2\x80\x9d the Colonel replied, and Kim flushed. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave left my cheroot-case in the Padre\xe2\x80\x99s veranda. Bring it to my house\r\nthis even.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the house?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. His quick wit told him that he was\r\nbeing tested in some fashion or another, and he stood on guard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk anyone in the big bazar.\xe2\x80\x9d The Colonel walked on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has forgotten his cheroot-case,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, returning. \xe2\x80\x9cI must bring\r\nit to him this evening. That is all my letter except, thrice over,\r\n_Come to me! Come to me! Come to me!_ Now I will pay for a stamp and\r\nput it in the post. He rose to go, and as an afterthought asked: \xe2\x80\x9cWho\r\nis that angry-faced Sahib who lost the cheroot-case?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he is only Creighton Sahib\xe2\x80\x94a very foolish Sahib, who is a Colonel\r\nSahib without a regiment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is his business?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod knows. He is always buying horses which he cannot ride, and asking\r\nriddles about the works of God\xe2\x80\x94such as plants and stones and the\r\ncustoms of people. The dealers call him the father of fools, because he\r\nis so easily cheated about a horse. Mahbub Ali says he is madder than\r\nmost other Sahibs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, and departed. His training had given him some small\r\nknowledge of character, and he argued that fools are not given\r\ninformation which leads to calling out eight thousand men besides guns.\r\nThe Commander-in-Chief of all India does not talk, as Kim had heard him\r\ntalk, to fools. Nor would Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s tone have changed, as it did\r\nevery time he mentioned the Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s name, if the Colonel had been a\r\nfool. Consequently\xe2\x80\x94and this set Kim to skipping\xe2\x80\x94there was a mystery\r\nsomewhere, and Mahbub Ali probably spied for the Colonel much as Kim\r\nhad spied for Mahbub. And, like the horse-dealer, the Colonel evidently\r\nrespected people who did not show themselves to be too clever.\r\n\r\nHe rejoiced that he had not betrayed his knowledge of the Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse; and when, on his return to barracks, he discovered that no\r\ncheroot-case had been left behind, he beamed with delight. Here was a\r\nman after his own heart\xe2\x80\x94a tortuous and indirect person playing a hidden\r\ngame. Well, if he could be a fool, so could Kim.\r\n\r\nHe showed nothing of his mind when Father Victor, for three long\r\nmornings, discoursed to him of an entirely new set of Gods and\r\nGodlings\xe2\x80\x94notably of a Goddess called Mary, who, he gathered, was one\r\nwith Bibi Miriam of Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s theology. He betrayed no emotion when,\r\nafter the lecture, Father Victor dragged him from shop to shop buying\r\narticles of outfit, nor when envious drummer-boys kicked him because he\r\nwas going to a superior school did he complain, but awaited the play of\r\ncircumstances with an interested soul. Father Victor, good man, took\r\nhim to the station, put him into an empty second-class next to Colonel\r\nCreighton\xe2\x80\x99s first, and bade him farewell with genuine feeling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll make a man o\xe2\x80\x99 you, O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, at St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94a white man, an\xe2\x80\x99, I\r\nhope, a good man. They know all about your comin\xe2\x80\x99, an\xe2\x80\x99 the Colonel will\r\nsee that ye\xe2\x80\x99re not lost or mislaid anywhere on the road. I\xe2\x80\x99ve given you\r\na notion of religious matters,\xe2\x80\x94at least I hope so,\xe2\x80\x94and you\xe2\x80\x99ll remember,\r\nwhen they ask you your religion, that you\xe2\x80\x99re a Cath\xe2\x80\x99lic. Better say\r\nRoman Cath\xe2\x80\x99lic, tho\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99m not fond of the word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim lit a rank cigarette\xe2\x80\x94he had been careful to buy a stock in the\r\nbazar\xe2\x80\x94and lay down to think. This solitary passage was very different\r\nfrom that joyful down-journey in the third-class with the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cSahibs\r\nget little pleasure of travel,\xe2\x80\x9d he reflected. \xe2\x80\x9c_Hai mai!_ I go from one\r\nplace to another as it might be a kickball. It is my _Kismet_. No man\r\ncan escape his _Kismet_. But I am to pray to Bibi Miriam, and I am a\r\nSahib.\xe2\x80\x9d He looked at his boots ruefully. \xe2\x80\x9cNo; I am Kim. This is the\r\ngreat world, and I am only Kim. Who is Kim?\xe2\x80\x9d He considered his own\r\nidentity, a thing he had never done before, till his head swam. He was\r\none insignificant person in all this roaring whirl of India, going\r\nsouthward to he knew not what fate.\r\n\r\nPresently the Colonel sent for him, and talked for a long time. So far\r\nas Kim could gather, he was to be diligent and enter the Survey of\r\nIndia as a chain-man. If he were very good, and passed the proper\r\nexaminations, he would be earning thirty rupees a month at seventeen\r\nyears old, and Colonel Creighton would see that he found suitable\r\nemployment.\r\n\r\nKim pretended at first to understand perhaps one word in three of this\r\ntalk. Then the Colonel, seeing his mistake, turned to fluent and\r\npicturesque Urdu and Kim was contented. No man could be a fool who knew\r\nthe language so intimately, who moved so gently and silently, and whose\r\neyes were so different from the dull fat eyes of other Sahibs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and thou must learn how to make pictures of roads and mountains\r\nand rivers, to carry these pictures in thine eye till a suitable time\r\ncomes to set them upon paper. Perhaps some day, when thou art a\r\nchain-man, I may say to thee when we are working together: \xe2\x80\x98Go across\r\nthose hills and see what lies beyond.\xe2\x80\x99 Then one will say: \xe2\x80\x98There are\r\nbad people living in those hills who will slay the chain-man if he be\r\nseen to look like a Sahib.\xe2\x80\x99 What then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim thought. Would it be safe to return the Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s lead?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would tell what that other man had said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if I answered: \xe2\x80\x98I will give thee a hundred rupees for knowledge of\r\nwhat is behind those hills\xe2\x80\x94for a picture of a river and a little news\r\nof what the people say in the villages there\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I tell? I am only a boy. Wait till I am a man.\xe2\x80\x9d Then, seeing\r\nthe Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s brow clouded, he went on: \xe2\x80\x9cBut I think I should in a few\r\ndays earn the hundred rupees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy what road?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim shook his head resolutely. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I said how I would earn them,\r\nanother man might hear and forestall me. It is not good to sell\r\nknowledge for nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell now.\xe2\x80\x9d The Colonel held up a rupee. Kim\xe2\x80\x99s hand half reached\r\ntowards it, and dropped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Sahib; nay. I know the price that will be paid for the answer,\r\nbut I do not know why the question is asked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake it for a gift, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Creighton, tossing it over. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is\r\na good spirit in thee. Do not let it be blunted at St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s. There\r\nare many boys there who despise the black men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTheir mothers were bazar-women,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. He knew well there is no\r\nhatred like that of the half-caste for his brother-in-law.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue; but thou art a Sahib and the son of a Sahib. Therefore, do not\r\nat any time be led to contemn the black men. I have known boys newly\r\nentered into the service of the Government who feigned not to\r\nunderstand the talk or the customs of black men. Their pay was cut for\r\nignorance. There is no sin so great as ignorance. Remember this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeveral times in the course of the long twenty-four hours\xe2\x80\x99 run south\r\ndid the Colonel send for Kim, always developing this latter text.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe be all on one lead-rope, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim at last, \xe2\x80\x9cthe Colonel,\r\nMahbub Ali, and I\xe2\x80\x94when I become a chain-man. He will use me as Mahbub\r\nAli employed me, I think. That is good, if it allows me to return to\r\nthe Road again. This clothing grows no easier by wear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen they came to the crowded Lucknow station there was no sign of the\r\nlama. He swallowed his disappointment, while the Colonel bundled him\r\ninto a _ticca-gharri_ with his neat belongings and despatched him alone\r\nto St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not say farewell, because we shall meet again,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cAgain,\r\nand many times, if thou art one of good spirit. But thou art not yet\r\ntried.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot when I brought thee\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim actually dared to use the _tum_ of\r\nequals\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ca white stallion\xe2\x80\x99s pedigree that night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch is gained by forgetting, little brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Colonel, with\r\na look that pierced through Kim\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder-blades as he scuttled into\r\nthe carriage.\r\n\r\nIt took him nearly five minutes to recover. Then he sniffed the new air\r\nappreciatively. \xe2\x80\x9cA rich city,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cRicher than Lahore. How good\r\nthe bazars must be! Coachman, drive me a little through the bazars\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy order is to take thee to the school.\xe2\x80\x9d The driver used the \xe2\x80\x9cthou\xe2\x80\x9d,\r\nwhich is rudeness when applied to a white man. In the clearest and most\r\nfluent vernacular Kim pointed out his error, climbed on to the\r\nbox-seat, and, perfect understanding established, drove for a couple of\r\nhours up and down, estimating, comparing, and enjoying. There is no\r\ncity\xe2\x80\x94except Bombay, the queen of all\xe2\x80\x94more beautiful in her garish style\r\nthan Lucknow, whether you see her from the bridge over the river, or\r\nfrom the top of the Imambara looking down on the gilt umbrellas of the\r\nChutter Munzil, and the trees in which the town is bedded. Kings have\r\nadorned her with fantastic buildings, endowed her with charities,\r\ncrammed her with pensioners, and drenched her with blood. She is the\r\ncentre of all idleness, intrigue, and luxury, and shares with Delhi the\r\nclaim to talk the only pure Urdu.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fair city\xe2\x80\x94a beautiful city.\xe2\x80\x9d The driver, as a Lucknow man, was\r\npleased with the compliment, and told Kim many astounding things where\r\nan English guide would have talked of the Mutiny.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we will go to the school,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim at last. The great old school\r\nof St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s in Partibus, block on block of low white buildings,\r\nstands in vast grounds over against the Gumti River, at some distance\r\nfrom the city.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat like of folk are they within?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYoung Sahibs\xe2\x80\x94all devils. But to speak truth, and I drive many of them\r\nto and fro from the railway station, I have never seen one that had in\r\nhim the making of a more perfect devil than thou\xe2\x80\x94this young Sahib whom\r\nI am now driving.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNaturally, for he was never trained to consider them in any way\r\nimproper, Kim had passed the time of day with one or two frivolous\r\nladies at upper windows in a certain street, and naturally, in the\r\nexchange of compliments, had acquitted himself well. He was about to\r\nacknowledge the driver\xe2\x80\x99s last insolence, when his eye\xe2\x80\x94it was growing\r\ndusk\xe2\x80\x94caught a figure sitting by one of the white plaster gate-pillars\r\nin the long sweep of wall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cStay here. I do not go to the school at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what is to pay me for this coming and re-coming?\xe2\x80\x9d said the driver\r\npetulantly. \xe2\x80\x9cIs the boy mad? Last time it was a dancing-girl. This time\r\nit is a priest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim was in the road headlong, patting the dusty feet beneath the dirty\r\nyellow robe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have waited here a day and a half,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama\xe2\x80\x99s level voice began.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, I had a disciple with me. He that was my friend at the Temple of\r\nthe Tirthankars gave me a guide for this journey. I came from Benares\r\nin the _te-rain_, when thy letter was given me. Yes, I am well fed. I\r\nneed nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why didst thou not stay with the Kulu woman, O Holy One? In what\r\nway didst thou get to Benares? My heart has been heavy since we\r\nparted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe woman wearied me by constant flux of talk and requiring charms for\r\nchildren. I separated myself from that company, permitting her to\r\nacquire merit by gifts. She is at least a woman of open hands, and I\r\nmade a promise to return to her house if need arose. Then, perceiving\r\nmyself alone in this great and terrible world, I bethought me of the\r\n_te-rain_ to Benares, where I knew one abode in the Tirthankars\xe2\x80\x99 Temple\r\nwho was a Seeker, even as I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Thy River,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten the River.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo soon, my _chela?_ I have never forgotten it. But when I had left\r\nthee it seemed better that I should go to the Temple and take counsel,\r\nfor, look you, India is very large, and it may be that wise men before\r\nus, some two or three, have left a record of the place of our River.\r\nThere is debate in the Temple of the Tirthankars on this matter; some\r\nsaying one thing, and some another. They are courteous folk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo be it; but what dost thou do now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI acquire merit in that I help thee, my _chela_, to wisdom. The priest\r\nof that body of men who serve the Red Bull wrote me that all should be\r\nas I desired for thee. I sent the money to suffice for one year, and\r\nthen I came, as thou seest me, to watch for thee going up into the\r\nGates of Learning. A day and a half have I waited, not because I was\r\nled by any affection towards thee\xe2\x80\x94that is no part of the Way\xe2\x80\x94but, as\r\nthey said at the Tirthankars\xe2\x80\x99 Temple, because, money having been paid\r\nfor learning, it was right that I should oversee the end of the matter.\r\nThey resolved my doubts most clearly. I had a fear that, perhaps, I\r\ncame because I wished to see thee\xe2\x80\x94misguided by the Red Mist of\r\naffection. It is not so ... Moreover, I am troubled by a dream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely, Holy One, thou hast not forgotten the Road and all that\r\nbefell on it. Surely it was a little to see me that thou didst come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe horses are cold, and it is past their feeding-time,\xe2\x80\x9d whined the\r\ndriver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo to Jehannum and abide there with thy reputationless aunt!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\r\nsnarled over his shoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cI am all alone in this land; I know not\r\nwhere I go nor what shall befall me. My heart was in that letter I sent\r\nthee. Except for Mahbub Ali, and he is a Pathan, I have no friend save\r\nthee, Holy One. Do not altogether go away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have considered that also,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama replied, in a shaking voice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is manifest that from time to time I shall acquire merit if before\r\nthat I have not found my River\xe2\x80\x94by assuring myself that thy feet are set\r\non wisdom. What they will teach thee I do not know, but the priest\r\nwrote me that no son of a Sahib in all India will be better taught than\r\nthou. So from time to time, therefore, I will come again. Maybe thou\r\nwilt be such a Sahib as he who gave me these spectacles\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the lama wiped\r\nthem elaborately\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cin the Wonder House at Lahore. That is my hope, for\r\nhe was a Fountain of Wisdom\xe2\x80\x94wiser than many abbots .... Again, maybe\r\nthou wilt forget me and our meetings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I eat thy bread,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Kim passionately, \xe2\x80\x9chow shall I ever forget\r\nthee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94no.\xe2\x80\x9d He put the boy aside. \xe2\x80\x9cI must go back to Benares. From time to\r\ntime, now that I know the customs of letter-writers in this land, I\r\nwill send thee a letter, and from time to time I will come and see\r\nthee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut whither shall I send my letters?\xe2\x80\x9d wailed Kim, clutching at the\r\nrobe, all forgetful that he was a Sahib.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the Temple of the Tirthankars at Benares. That is the place I have\r\nchosen till I find my River. Do not weep; for, look you, all Desire is\r\nIllusion and a new binding upon the Wheel. Go up to the Gates of\r\nLearning. Let me see thee go ... Dost thou love me? Then go, or my\r\nheart cracks ... I will come again. Surely I will come again.\r\n\r\nThe lama watched the _ticca-gharri_ rumble into the compound, and\r\nstrode off, snuffing between each long stride.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gates of Learning\xe2\x80\x9d shut with a clang.\r\n\r\nThe country born and bred boy has his own manners and customs, which do\r\nnot resemble those of any other land; and his teachers approach him by\r\nroads which an English master would not understand. Therefore, you\r\nwould scarcely be interested in Kim\xe2\x80\x99s experiences as a St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s boy\r\namong two or three hundred precocious youths, most of whom had never\r\nseen the sea. He suffered the usual penalties for breaking out of\r\nbounds when there was cholera in the city. This was before he had\r\nlearned to write fair English, and so was obliged to find a bazar\r\nletter-writer. He was, of course, indicted for smoking and for the use\r\nof abuse more full-flavoured than even St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s had ever heard. He\r\nlearned to wash himself with the Levitical scrupulosity of the\r\nnative-born, who in his heart considers the Englishman rather dirty. He\r\nplayed the usual tricks on the patient coolies pulling the punkahs in\r\nthe sleeping-rooms where the boys threshed through the hot nights\r\ntelling tales till the dawn; and quietly he measured himself against\r\nhis self-reliant mates.\r\n\r\nThey were sons of subordinate officials in the Railway, Telegraph, and\r\nCanal Services; of warrant-officers, sometimes retired and sometimes\r\nacting as commanders-in-chief to a feudatory Rajah\xe2\x80\x99s army; of captains\r\nof the Indian Marine Government pensioners, planters, Presidency\r\nshopkeepers, and missionaries. A few were cadets of the old Eurasian\r\nhouses that have taken strong root in Dhurrumtollah\xe2\x80\x94Pereiras, De\r\nSouzas, and D\xe2\x80\x99Silvas. Their parents could well have educated them in\r\nEngland, but they loved the school that had served their own youth, and\r\ngeneration followed sallow-hued generation at St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s. Their homes\r\nranged from Howrah of the railway people to abandoned cantonments like\r\nMonghyr and Chunar; lost tea-gardens Shillong-way; villages where their\r\nfathers were large landholders in Oudh or the Deccan; Mission-stations\r\na week from the nearest railway line; seaports a thousand miles south,\r\nfacing the brazen Indian surf; and cinchona-plantations south of all.\r\nThe mere story of their adventures, which to them were no adventures,\r\non their road to and from school would have crisped a Western boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhair. They were used to jogging off alone through a hundred miles of\r\njungle, where there was always the delightful chance of being delayed\r\nby tigers; but they would no more have bathed in the English Channel in\r\nan English August than their brothers across the world would have lain\r\nstill while a leopard snuffed at their palanquin. There were boys of\r\nfifteen who had spent a day and a half on an islet in the middle of a\r\nflooded river, taking charge, as by right, of a camp of frantic\r\npilgrims returning from a shrine. There were seniors who had\r\nrequisitioned a chance-met Rajah\xe2\x80\x99s elephant, in the name of St Francis\r\nXavier, when the Rains once blotted out the cart-track that led to\r\ntheir father\xe2\x80\x99s estate, and had all but lost the huge beast in a\r\nquicksand. There was a boy who, he said, and none doubted, had helped\r\nhis father to beat off with rifles from the veranda a rush of Akas in\r\nthe days when those head-hunters were bold against lonely plantations.\r\n\r\nAnd every tale was told in the even, passionless voice of the\r\nnative-born, mixed with quaint reflections, borrowed unconsciously from\r\nnative foster-mothers, and turns of speech that showed they had been\r\nthat instant translated from the vernacular. Kim watched, listened, and\r\napproved. This was not insipid, single-word talk of drummer-boys. It\r\ndealt with a life he knew and in part understood. The atmosphere suited\r\nhim, and he throve by inches. They gave him a white drill suit as the\r\nweather warmed, and he rejoiced in the new-found bodily comforts as he\r\nrejoiced to use his sharpened mind over the tasks they set him. His\r\nquickness would have delighted an English master; but at St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthey know the first rush of minds developed by sun and surroundings, as\r\nthey know the half-collapse that sets in at twenty-two or twenty-three.\r\n\r\nNone the less he remembered to hold himself lowly. When tales were told\r\nof hot nights, Kim did not sweep the board with his reminiscences; for\r\nSt Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s looks down on boys who \xe2\x80\x9cgo native all-together.\xe2\x80\x9d One must\r\nnever forget that one is a Sahib, and that some day, when examinations\r\nare passed, one will command natives. Kim made a note of this, for he\r\nbegan to understand where examinations led.\r\n\r\nThen came the holidays from August to October\xe2\x80\x94the long holidays imposed\r\nby the heat and the Rains. Kim was informed that he would go north to\r\nsome station in the hills behind Umballa, where Father Victor would\r\narrange for him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA barrack-school?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, who had asked many questions and thought\r\nmore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the master. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will not do you any harm to\r\nkeep you out of mischief. You can go up with young De Castro as far as\r\nDelhi.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim considered it in every possible light. He had been diligent, even\r\nas the Colonel advised. A boy\xe2\x80\x99s holiday was his own property\xe2\x80\x94of so much\r\nthe talk of his companions had advised him,\xe2\x80\x94and a barrack-school would\r\nbe torment after St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s. Moreover\xe2\x80\x94this was magic worth anything\r\nelse\xe2\x80\x94he could write. In three months he had discovered how men can\r\nspeak to each other without a third party, at the cost of half an anna\r\nand a little knowledge. No word had come from the lama, but there\r\nremained the Road. Kim yearned for the caress of soft mud squishing up\r\nbetween the toes, as his mouth watered for mutton stewed with butter\r\nand cabbages, for rice speckled with strong scented cardamoms, for the\r\nsaffron-tinted rice, garlic and onions, and the forbidden greasy\r\nsweetmeats of the bazars. They would feed him raw beef on a platter at\r\nthe barrack-school, and he must smoke by stealth. But again, he was a\r\nSahib and was at St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s, and that pig Mahbub Ali ... No, he would\r\nnot test Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s hospitality\xe2\x80\x94and yet ... He thought it out alone in\r\nthe dormitory, and came to the conclusion he had been unjust to Mahbub.\r\n\r\nThe school was empty; nearly all the masters had gone away; Colonel\r\nCreighton\xe2\x80\x99s railway pass lay in his hand, and Kim puffed himself that\r\nhe had not spent Colonel Creighton\xe2\x80\x99s or Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s money in riotous\r\nliving. He was still lord of two rupees seven annas. His new\r\nbullock-trunk, marked \xe2\x80\x9cK. O\xe2\x80\x99H.\xe2\x80\x9d, and bedding-roll lay in the empty\r\nsleeping-room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSahibs are always tied to their baggage,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, nodding at them.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will stay here\xe2\x80\x9d He went out into the warm rain, smiling sinfully,\r\nand sought a certain house whose outside he had noted down some time\r\nbefore...\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArre\xe2\x80\x9d! Dost thou know what manner of women we be in this quarter? Oh,\r\nshame!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas I born yesterday?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim squatted native-fashion on the cushions of\r\nthat upper room. \xe2\x80\x9cA little dyestuff and three yards of cloth to help\r\nout a jest. Is it much to ask?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is _she?_ Thou art full young, as Sahibs go, for this devilry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she? She is the daughter of a certain schoolmaster of a regiment\r\nin the cantonments. He has beaten me twice because I went over their\r\nwall in these clothes. Now I would go as a gardener\xe2\x80\x99s boy. Old men are\r\nvery jealous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true. Hold thy face still while I dab on the juice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot too black, _Naikan_. I would not appear to her as a _hubshi_\r\n(nigger).\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, love makes nought of these things. And how old is she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwelve years, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d said the shameless Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cSpread it also on the\r\nbreast. It may be her father will tear my clothes off me, and if I am\r\npiebald\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he laughed.\r\n\r\nThe girl worked busily, dabbing a twist of cloth into a little saucer\r\nof brown dye that holds longer than any walnut-juice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow send out and get me a cloth for the turban. Woe is me, my head is\r\nall unshaved! And he will surely knock off my turban.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not a barber, but I will make shift. Thou wast born to be a\r\nbreaker of hearts! All this disguise for one evening? Remember, the\r\nstuff does not wash away.\xe2\x80\x9d She shook with laughter till her bracelets\r\nand anklets jingled. \xe2\x80\x9cBut who is to pay me for this? Huneefa herself\r\ncould not have given thee better stuff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrust in the Gods, my sister,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim gravely, screwing his face\r\nround as the stain dried. \xe2\x80\x9cBesides, hast thou ever helped to paint a\r\nSahib thus before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever indeed. But a jest is not money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is worth much more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChild, thou art beyond all dispute the most shameless son of Shaitan\r\nthat I have ever known to take up a poor girl\xe2\x80\x99s time with this play,\r\nand then to say: \xe2\x80\x98Is not the jest enough?\xe2\x80\x99 Thou wilt go very far in\r\nthis world.\xe2\x80\x9d She gave the dancing-girls\xe2\x80\x99 salutation in mockery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll one. Make haste and rough-cut my head.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim shifted from foot to\r\nfoot, his eyes ablaze with mirth as he thought of the fat days before\r\nhim. He gave the girl four annas, and ran down the stairs in the\r\nlikeness of a low-caste Hindu boy\xe2\x80\x94perfect in every detail. A cookshop\r\nwas his next point of call, where he feasted in extravagance and greasy\r\nluxury.\r\n\r\nOn Lucknow station platform he watched young De Castro, all covered\r\nwith prickly-heat, get into a second-class compartment. Kim patronized\r\na third, and was the life and soul of it. He explained to the company\r\nthat he was assistant to a juggler who had left him behind sick with\r\nfever, and that he would pick up his master at Umballa. As the\r\noccupants of the carriage changed, he varied this tale, or adorned it\r\nwith all the shoots of a budding fancy, the more rampant for being held\r\noff native speech so long. In all India that night was no human being\r\nso joyful as Kim. At Umballa he got out and headed eastward, plashing\r\nover the sodden fields to the village where the old soldier lived.\r\n\r\nAbout this time Colonel Creighton at Simla was advised from Lucknow by\r\nwire that young O\xe2\x80\x99Hara had disappeared. Mahbub Ali was in town selling\r\nhorses, and to him the Colonel confided the affair one morning\r\ncantering round Annandale racecourse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that is nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said the horse-dealer. \xe2\x80\x9cMen are like horses. At\r\ncertain times they need salt, and if that salt is not in the mangers\r\nthey will lick it up from the earth. He has gone back to the Road again\r\nfor a while. The _madrissah_ wearied him. I knew it would. Another\r\ntime, I will take him upon the Road myself. Do not be troubled,\r\nCreighton Sahib. It is as though a polo-pony, breaking loose, ran out\r\nto learn the game alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he is not dead, think you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFever might kill him. I do not fear for the boy otherwise. A monkey\r\ndoes not fall among trees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNext morning, on the same course, Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s stallion ranged alongside\r\nthe Colonel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is as I had thought,\xe2\x80\x9d said the horse-dealer. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has come through\r\nUmballa at least, and there he has written a letter to me, having\r\nlearned in the bazar that I was here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRead,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Colonel, with a sigh of relief. It was absurd that a\r\nman of his position should take an interest in a little country-bred\r\nvagabond; but the Colonel remembered the conversation in the train, and\r\noften in the past few months had caught himself thinking of the queer,\r\nsilent, self-possessed boy. His evasion, of course, was the height of\r\ninsolence, but it argued some resource and nerve.\r\n\r\nMahbub\xe2\x80\x99s eyes twinkled as he reined out into the centre of the cramped\r\nlittle plain, where none could come near unseen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_The Friend of the Stars, who is the Friend of all the World_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA name we give him in Lahore city. \xe2\x80\x98_The Friend of all the World takes\r\nleave to go to his own places. He will come back upon the appointed\r\nday. Let the box and the bedding-roll be sent for; and if there has\r\nbeen a fault, let the Hand of Friendship turn aside the Whip of\r\nCalamity._\xe2\x80\x99 There is yet a little more, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter, read.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_Certain things are not known to those who eat with forks. It is\r\nbetter to eat with both hands for a while. Speak soft words to those\r\nwho do not understand this that the return may be propitious._\xe2\x80\x99 Now the\r\nmanner in which that was cast is, of course, the work of the\r\nletter-writer, but see how wisely the boy has devised the matter of it\r\nso that no hint is given except to those who know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this the Hand of Friendship to avert the Whip of Calamity?\xe2\x80\x9d laughed\r\nthe Colonel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee how wise is the boy. He would go back to the Road again, as I\r\nsaid. Not knowing yet thy trade\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not at all sure of that,\xe2\x80\x9d the Colonel muttered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe turns to me to make a peace between you. Is he not wise? He says he\r\nwill return. He is but perfecting his knowledge. Think, Sahib! He has\r\nbeen three months at the school. And he is not mouthed to that bit. For\r\nmy part, I rejoice. The pony learns the game.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, but another time he must not go alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? He went alone before he came under the Colonel Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nprotection. When he comes to the Great Game he must go alone\xe2\x80\x94alone, and\r\nat peril of his head. _Then_, if he spits, or sneezes, or sits down\r\nother than as the people do whom he watches, he may be slain. Why\r\nhinder him now? Remember how the Persians say: The jackal that lives in\r\nthe wilds of Mazanderan can only be caught by the hounds of\r\nMazanderan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. It is true, Mahbub Ali. And if he comes to no harm, I do not\r\ndesire anything better. But it is great insolence on his part.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe does not tell me, even, whither he goes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is no\r\nfool. When his time is accomplished he will come to me. It is time the\r\nhealer of pearls took him in hand. He ripens too quickly\xe2\x80\x94as Sahibs\r\nreckon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis prophecy was fulfilled to the letter a month later. Mahbub had\r\ngone down to Umballa to bring up a fresh consignment of horses, and Kim\r\nmet him on the Kalka road at dusk riding alone, begged an alms of him,\r\nwas sworn at, and replied in English. There was nobody within earshot\r\nto hear Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s gasp of amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho! And where hast thou been?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUp and down\xe2\x80\x94down and up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome under a tree, out of the wet, and tell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI stayed for a while with an old man near Umballa; anon with a\r\nhousehold of my acquaintance in Umballa. With one of these I went as\r\nfar as Delhi to the southward. That is a wondrous city. Then I drove a\r\nbullock for a _teli_ (an oilman) coming north; but I heard of a great\r\nfeast forward in Patiala, and thither went I in the company of a\r\nfirework-maker. It was a great feast\xe2\x80\x9d (Kim rubbed his stomach). \xe2\x80\x9cI saw\r\nRajahs, and elephants with gold and silver trappings; and they lit all\r\nthe fireworks at once, whereby eleven men were killed, my\r\nfire-work-maker among them, and I was blown across a tent but took no\r\nharm. Then I came back to the _r\xc3\xaal_ with a Sikh horseman, to whom I was\r\ngroom for my bread; and so here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Shabash!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub Ali.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what does the Colonel Sahib say? I do not wish to be beaten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Hand of Friendship has averted the Whip of Calamity; but another\r\ntime, when thou takest the Road it will be with me. This is too early.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLate enough for me. I have learned to read and to write English a\r\nlittle at the _madrissah_. I shall soon be altogether a Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear him!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Mahbub, looking at the little drenched figure\r\ndancing in the wet. \xe2\x80\x9cSalaam\xe2\x80\x94Sahib,\xe2\x80\x9d and he saluted ironically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, art tired of the Road, or wilt thou come on to Umballa with me\r\nand work back with the horses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI come with thee, Mahbub Ali.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\n\r\nSomething I owe to the soil that grew\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    More to the life that fed\xe2\x80\x94\r\nBut most to Allah Who gave me two\r\n    Separate sides to my head.\r\n\r\nI would go without shirts or shoes,\r\n    Friends, tobacco or bread\r\nSooner than for an instant lose\r\n    Either side of my head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen in God\xe2\x80\x99s name take blue for red,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub, alluding to the\r\nHindu colour of Kim\xe2\x80\x99s disreputable turban.\r\n\r\nKim countered with the old proverb, \xe2\x80\x9cI will change my faith and my\r\nbedding, but _thou_ must pay for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe dealer laughed till he nearly fell from his horse. At a shop on the\r\noutskirts of the city the change was made, and Kim stood up, externally\r\nat least, a Mohammedan.\r\n\r\nMahbub hired a room over against the railway station, sent for a cooked\r\nmeal of the finest with the almond-curd sweet-meats (_balushai_ we call\r\nit) and fine-chopped Lucknow tobacco.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is better than some other meat that I ate with the Sikh,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nKim, grinning as he squatted, \xe2\x80\x9cand assuredly they give no such victuals\r\nat my _madrissah_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a desire to hear of that same _madrissah_.\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub stuffed\r\nhimself with great boluses of spiced mutton fried in fat with cabbage\r\nand golden-brown onions. \xe2\x80\x9cBut tell me first, altogether and truthfully,\r\nthe manner of thy escape. For, O Friend of all the World,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he loosed\r\nhis cracking belt\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think it is often that a Sahib and the son\r\nof a Sahib runs away from there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow should they? They do not know the land. It was nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim,\r\nand began his tale. When he came to the disguisement and the interview\r\nwith the girl in the bazar, Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s gravity went from him. He\r\nlaughed aloud and beat his hand on his thigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Shabash! Shabash!_ Oh, well done, little one! What will the healer of\r\nturquoises say to this? Now, slowly, let us hear what befell\r\nafterwards\xe2\x80\x94step by step, omitting nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStep by step then, Kim told his adventures between coughs as the\r\nfull-flavoured tobacco caught his lungs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said,\xe2\x80\x9d growled Mahbub Ali to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI _said_ it was the pony\r\nbreaking out to play polo. The fruit is ripe already\xe2\x80\x94except that he\r\nmust learn his distances and his pacings, and his rods and his\r\ncompasses. Listen now. I have turned aside the Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s whip from thy\r\nskin, and that is no small service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim pulled serenely. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is true.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is not to be thought that this running out and in is any way\r\ngood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was my holiday, Hajji. I was a slave for many weeks. Why should I\r\nnot run away when the school was shut? Look, too, how I, living upon my\r\nfriends or working for my bread, as I did with the Sikh, have saved the\r\nColonel Sahib a great expense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMahbub\xe2\x80\x99s lips twitched under his well-pruned Mohammedan moustache.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are a few rupees\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the Pathan threw out his open hand\r\ncarelessly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cto the Colonel Sahib? He spends them for a purpose, not in\r\nany way for love of thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim slowly, \xe2\x80\x9cI knew a very long time ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho told?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Colonel Sahib himself. Not in those many words, but plainly enough\r\nfor one who is not altogether a mud-head. Yea, he told me in the\r\n_te-rain_ when we went down to Lucknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe it so. Then I will tell thee more, Friend of all the World, though\r\nin the telling I lend thee my head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was forfeit to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, with deep relish, \xe2\x80\x9cin Umballa, when\r\nthou didst pick me up on the horse after the drummer-boy beat me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak a little plainer. All the world may tell lies save thou and I.\r\nFor equally is thy life forfeit to me if I chose to raise my finger\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this is known to me also,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, readjusting the live\r\ncharcoal-ball on the weed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a very sure tie between us. Indeed,\r\nthy hold is surer even than mine; for who would miss a boy beaten to\r\ndeath, or, it may be, thrown into a well by the roadside? Most people\r\nhere and in Simla and across the passes behind the Hills would, on the\r\nother hand, say: \xe2\x80\x98What has come to Mahbub Ali?\xe2\x80\x99 if he were found dead\r\namong his horses. Surely, too, the Colonel Sahib would make inquiries.\r\nBut again,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim\xe2\x80\x99s face puckered with cunning,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9che would not make\r\noverlong inquiry, lest people should ask: \xe2\x80\x98What has this Colonel Sahib\r\nto do with that horse-dealer?\xe2\x80\x99 But I\xe2\x80\x94if I lived\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs thou wouldst surely die\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may be; but I say, _if_ I lived, I, and I alone, would know that\r\none had come by night, as a common thief perhaps, to Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbulkhead in the serai, and there had slain him, either before or after\r\nthat thief had made a full search into his saddlebags and between the\r\nsoles of his slippers. Is that news to tell to the Colonel, or would he\r\nsay to me\xe2\x80\x94(I have not forgotten when he sent me back for a cigar-case\r\nthat he had _not_ left behind him)\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98What is Mahbub Ali to me?\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUp went a gout of heavy smoke. There was a long pause: then Mahbub Ali\r\nspoke in admiration: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd with these things on thy mind, dost thou lie\r\ndown and rise again among all the Sahibs\xe2\x80\x99 little sons at the\r\n_madrissah_ and meekly take instruction from thy teachers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is an order,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim blandly. \xe2\x80\x9cWho am I to dispute an order?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA most finished Son of Eblis,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub Ali. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what is this tale\r\nof the thief and the search?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat which I saw,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, \xe2\x80\x9cthe night that my lama and I lay next\r\nthy place in the Kashmir Seral. The door was left unlocked, which I\r\nthink is not thy custom, Mahbub. He came in as one assured that thou\r\nwouldst not soon return. My eye was against a knot-hole in the plank.\r\nHe searched as it were for something\xe2\x80\x94not a rug, not stirrups, nor a\r\nbridle, nor brass pots\xe2\x80\x94something little and most carefully hid. Else\r\nwhy did he prick with an iron between the soles of thy slippers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa!\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub Ali smiled gently. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd seeing these things, what tale\r\ndidst thou fashion to thyself, Well of the Truth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone. I put my hand upon my amulet, which lies always next to my skin,\r\nand, remembering the pedigree of a white stallion that I had bitten out\r\nof a piece of Mussalmani bread, I went away to Umballa perceiving that\r\na heavy trust was laid upon me. At that hour, had I chosen, thy head\r\nwas forfeit. It needed only to say to that man, \xe2\x80\x98I have here a paper\r\nconcerning a horse which I cannot read.\xe2\x80\x99 And then?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim peered at\r\nMahbub under his eyebrows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen thou wouldst have drunk water twice\xe2\x80\x94perhaps thrice, afterwards. I\r\ndo not think more than thrice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true. I thought of that a little, but most I thought that I\r\nloved thee, Mahbub. Therefore I went to Umballa, as thou knowest, but\r\n(and this thou dost not know) I lay hid in the garden-grass to see what\r\nColonel Creighton Sahib might do upon reading the white stallion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npedigree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did he?\xe2\x80\x9d for Kim had bitten off the conversation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDost _thou_ give news for love, or dost thou sell it?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sell and\xe2\x80\x94I buy.\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub took a four-anna piece out of his belt and\r\nheld it up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEight!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, mechanically following the huckster instinct of the\r\nEast.\r\n\r\nMahbub laughed, and put away the coin. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is too easy to deal in that\r\nmarket, Friend of all the World. Tell me for love. Our lives lie in\r\neach other\xe2\x80\x99s hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good. I saw the Jang-i-Lat Sahib [the Commander-in-Chief] come to\r\na big dinner. I saw him in Creighton Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s office. I saw the two read\r\nthe white stallion\xe2\x80\x99s pedigree. I heard the very orders given for the\r\nopening of a great war.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHah!\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub nodded with deepest eyes afire. \xe2\x80\x9cThe game is well played.\r\nThat war is done now, and the evil, we hope, nipped before the\r\nflower\xe2\x80\x94thanks to me\xe2\x80\x94and thee. What didst thou later?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI made the news as it were a hook to catch me victual and honour among\r\nthe villagers in a village whose priest drugged my lama. But I bore\r\naway the old man\xe2\x80\x99s purse, and the Brahmin found nothing. So next\r\nmorning he was angry. Ho! Ho! And I also used the news when I fell into\r\nthe hands of that white Regiment with their Bull!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was foolishness.\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub scowled. \xe2\x80\x9cNews is not meant to be thrown\r\nabout like dung-cakes, but used sparingly\xe2\x80\x94like _bhang_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I think now, and moreover, it did me no sort of good. But that was\r\nvery long ago,\xe2\x80\x9d he made as to brush it all away with a thin brown\r\nhand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand since then, and especially in the nights under the punkah at\r\nthe _madrissah_, I have thought very greatly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it permitted to ask whither the Heaven-born\xe2\x80\x99s thought might have\r\nled?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub, with an elaborate sarcasm, smoothing his scarlet\r\nbeard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is permitted,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, and threw back the very tone. \xe2\x80\x9cThey say at\r\nNucklao that no Sahib must tell a black man that he has made a fault.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMahbub\xe2\x80\x99s hand shot into his bosom, for to call a Pathan a \xe2\x80\x9cblack man\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(_kala admi_) is a blood-insult. Then he remembered and laughed.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak, Sahib. Thy black man hears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, \xe2\x80\x9cI am _not_ a Sahib, and I say I made a fault to curse\r\nthee, Mahbub Ali, on that day at Umballa when I thought I was betrayed\r\nby a Pathan. I was senseless; for I was but newly caught, and I wished\r\nto kill that low-caste drummer-boy. I say now, Hajji, that it was well\r\ndone; and I see my road all clear before me to a good service. I will\r\nstay in the _madrissah_ till I am ripe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell said. Especially are distances and numbers and the manner of\r\nusing compasses to be learned in that game. One waits in the Hills\r\nabove to show thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will learn their teaching upon a condition\xe2\x80\x94that my time is given to\r\nme without question when the _madrissah_ is shut. Ask that for me of\r\nthe Colonel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why not ask the Colonel in the Sahibs\xe2\x80\x99 tongue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Colonel is the servant of the Government. He is sent hither and\r\nyon at a word, and must consider his own advancement. (See how much I\r\nhave already learned at Nucklao!) Moreover, the Colonel I know since\r\nthree months only. I have known one Mahbub Ali for six years. So! To\r\nthe _madrissah_ I will go. At the _madrissah_ I will learn. In the\r\n_madrissah_ I will be a Sahib. But when the _madrissah_ is shut, then\r\nmust I be free and go among my people. Otherwise I die!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who are thy people, Friend of all the World?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis great and beautiful land,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, waving his paw round the\r\nlittle clay-walled room where the oil-lamp in its niche burned heavily\r\nthrough the tobacco-smoke. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, further, I would see my lama again.\r\nAnd, further, I need money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the need of everyone,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub ruefully. \xe2\x80\x9cI will give thee\r\neight annas, for much money is not picked out of horses\xe2\x80\x99 hooves, and it\r\nmust suffice for many days. As to all the rest, I am well pleased, and\r\nno further talk is needed. Make haste to learn, and in three years, or\r\nit may be less, thou wilt be an aid\xe2\x80\x94even to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave I been such a hindrance till now?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, with a boy\xe2\x80\x99s giggle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not give answers,\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub grunted. \xe2\x80\x9cThou art my new horse-boy. Go\r\nand bed among my men. They are near the north end of the station, with\r\nthe horses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will beat me to the south end of the station if I come without\r\nauthority.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMahbub felt in his belt, wetted his thumb on a cake of Chinese ink, and\r\ndabbed the impression on a piece of soft native paper. From Balkh to\r\nBombay men know that rough-ridged print with the old scar running\r\ndiagonally across it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is enough to show my headman. I come in the morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy which road?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the road from the city. There is but one, and then we return to\r\nCreighton Sahib. I have saved thee a beating.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah! What is a beating when the very head is loose on the\r\nshoulders?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim slid out quietly into the night, walked half round the house,\r\nkeeping close to the walls, and headed away from the station for a mile\r\nor so. Then, fetching a wide compass, he worked back at leisure, for he\r\nneeded time to invent a story if any of Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s retainers asked\r\nquestions.\r\n\r\nThey were camped on a piece of waste ground beside the railway, and,\r\nbeing natives, had not, of course, unloaded the two trucks in which\r\nMahbub\xe2\x80\x99s animals stood among a consignment of country-breds bought by\r\nthe Bombay tram-company. The headman, a broken-down,\r\nconsumptive-looking Mohammedan, promptly challenged Kim, but was\r\npacified at sight of Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s sign-manual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Hajji has of his favour given me service,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim testily. \xe2\x80\x9cIf\r\nthis be doubted, wait till he comes in the morning. Meantime, a place\r\nby the fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFollowed the usual aimless babble that every low-caste native must\r\nraise on every occasion. It died down, and Kim lay out behind the\r\nlittle knot of Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s followers, almost under the wheels of a\r\nhorse-truck, a borrowed blanket for covering. Now a bed among brickbats\r\nand ballast-refuse on a damp night, between overcrowded horses and\r\nunwashed Baltis, would not appeal to many white boys; but Kim was\r\nutterly happy. Change of scene, service, and surroundings were the\r\nbreath of his little nostrils, and thinking of the neat white cots of\r\nSt Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s all arow under the punkah gave him joy as keen as the\r\nrepetition of the multiplication-table in English.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very old,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought sleepily. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery month I become a year more\r\nold. I was very young, and a fool to boot, when I took Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s message\r\nto Umballa. Even when I was with that white Regiment I was very young\r\nand small and had no wisdom. But now I learn every day, and in three\r\nyears the Colonel will take me out of the _madrissah_ and let me go\r\nupon the Road with Mahbub hunting for horses\xe2\x80\x99 pedigrees, or maybe I\r\nshall go by myself; or maybe I shall find the lama and go with him.\r\nYes; that is best. To walk again as a _chela_ with my lama when he\r\ncomes back to Benares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe thoughts came more slowly and disconnectedly. He was plunging into\r\na beautiful dreamland when his ears caught a whisper, thin and sharp,\r\nabove the monotonous babble round the fire. It came from behind the\r\niron-skinned horse-truck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is not here, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere should he be but roystering in the city. Who looks for a rat in\r\na frog-pond? Come away. He is not our man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must not go back beyond the Passes a second time. It is the order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHire some woman to drug him. It is a few rupees only, and there is no\r\nevidence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept the woman. It must be more certain; and remember the price upon\r\nhis head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, but the police have a long arm, and we are far from the Border. If\r\nit were in Peshawur, now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94in Peshawur,\xe2\x80\x9d the second voice sneered. \xe2\x80\x9cPeshawur, full of his\r\nblood-kin\xe2\x80\x94full of bolt-holes and women behind whose clothes he will\r\nhide. Yes, Peshawur or Jehannum would suit us equally well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what is the plan?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO fool, have I not told it a hundred times? Wait till he comes to lie\r\ndown, and then one sure shot. The trucks are between us and pursuit. We\r\nhave but to run back over the lines and go our way. They will not see\r\nwhence the shot came. Wait here at least till the dawn. What manner of\r\n_faquir_ art thou, to shiver at a little watching?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Kim, behind close-shut eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cOnce again it is Mahbub.\r\nIndeed a white stallion\xe2\x80\x99s pedigree is not a good thing to peddle to\r\nSahibs! Or maybe Mahbub has been selling other news. Now what is to do,\r\nKim? I know not where Mahbub houses, and if he comes here before the\r\ndawn they will shoot him. That would be no profit for thee, Kim. And\r\nthis is not a matter for the police. That would be no profit for\r\nMahbub; and\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he giggled almost aloud\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI do not remember any lesson at\r\nNucklao which will help me. Allah! Here is Kim and yonder are they.\r\nFirst, then, Kim must wake and go away, so that they shall not suspect.\r\nA bad dream wakes a man\xe2\x80\x94thus\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe threw the blanket off his face, and raised himself suddenly with the\r\nterrible, bubbling, meaningless yell of the Asiatic roused by\r\nnightmare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUrr-urr-urr-urr! Ya-la-la-la-la! _Narain!_ The _churel!_ The\r\n_churel!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA _churel_ is the peculiarly malignant ghost of a woman who has died in\r\nchild-bed. She haunts lonely roads, her feet are turned backwards on\r\nthe ankles, and she leads men to torment.\r\n\r\nLouder rose Kim\xe2\x80\x99s quavering howl, till at last he leaped to his feet\r\nand staggered off sleepily, while the camp cursed him for waking them.\r\nSome twenty yards farther up the line he lay down again, taking care\r\nthat the whisperers should hear his grunts and groans as he recomposed\r\nhimself. After a few minutes he rolled towards the road and stole away\r\ninto the thick darkness.\r\n\r\nHe paddled along swiftly till he came to a culvert, and dropped behind\r\nit, his chin on a level with the coping-stone. Here he could command\r\nall the night-traffic, himself unseen.\r\n\r\nTwo or three carts passed, jingling out to the suburbs; a coughing\r\npoliceman and a hurrying foot-passenger or two who sang to keep off\r\nevil spirits. Then rapped the shod feet of a horse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! This is more like Mahbub,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Kim, as the beast shied at the\r\nlittle head above the culvert.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x9d, Mahbub Ali,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, \xe2\x80\x9chave a care!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe horse was reined back almost on its haunches, and forced towards\r\nthe culvert.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub, \xe2\x80\x9cwill I take a shod horse for night-work.\r\nThey pick up all the bones and nails in the city.\xe2\x80\x9d He stooped to lift\r\nits forefoot, and that brought his head within a foot of Kim\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDown\xe2\x80\x94keep down,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered. \xe2\x80\x9cThe night is full of eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo men wait thy coming behind the horse-trucks. They will shoot thee\r\nat thy lying down, because there is a price on thy head. I heard,\r\nsleeping near the horses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidst thou see them? ... Hold still, Sire of Devils!\xe2\x80\x9d This furiously\r\nto the horse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas one dressed belike as a _faquir?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne said to the other, \xe2\x80\x98What manner of _faquir_ art thou, to shiver at\r\na little watching?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. Go back to the camp and lie down. I do not die tonight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMahbub wheeled his horse and vanished. Kim tore back down the ditch\r\ntill he reached a point opposite his second resting-place, slipped\r\nacross the road like a weasel, and re-coiled himself in the blanket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least Mahbub knows,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought contentedly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd certainly he\r\nspoke as one expecting it. I do not think those two men will profit by\r\ntonight\xe2\x80\x99s watch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn hour passed, and, with the best will in the world to keep awake all\r\nnight, he slept deeply. Now and again a night train roared along the\r\nmetals within twenty feet of him; but he had all the Oriental\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nindifference to mere noise, and it did not even weave a dream through\r\nhis slumber.\r\n\r\nMahbub was anything but asleep. It annoyed him vehemently that people\r\noutside his tribe and unaffected by his casual amours should pursue him\r\nfor the life. His first and natural impulse was to cross the line lower\r\ndown, work up again, and, catching his well-wishers from behind,\r\nsummarily slay them. Here, he reflected with sorrow, another branch of\r\nthe Government, totally unconnected with Colonel Creighton, might\r\ndemand explanations which would be hard to supply; and he knew that\r\nsouth of the Border a perfectly ridiculous fuss is made about a corpse\r\nor so. He had not been troubled in this way since he sent Kim to\r\nUmballa with the message, and hoped that suspicion had been finally\r\ndiverted.\r\n\r\nThen a most brilliant notion struck him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe English do eternally tell the truth,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9ctherefore we of\r\nthis country are eternally made foolish.
