By Allah, I will tell the\r\ntruth to an Englishman! Of what use is the Government police if a poor\r\nKabuli be robbed of his horses in their very trucks. This is as bad as\r\nPeshawur! I should lay a complaint at the station. Better still, some\r\nyoung Sahib on the Railway! They are zealous, and if they catch thieves\r\nit is remembered to their honour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe tied up his horse outside the station, and strode on to the\r\nplatform.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo, Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x9d said a young Assistant District Traffic\r\nSuperintendent who was waiting to go down the line\xe2\x80\x94a tall, tow-haired,\r\nhorsey youth in dingy white linen. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing here? Selling\r\nweeds\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I am not troubled for my horses. I come to look for Lutuf Ullah. I\r\nhave a truck-load up the line. Could anyone take them out without the\r\nRailway\xe2\x80\x99s knowledge?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShouldn\xe2\x80\x99t think so, Mahbub. You can claim against us if they do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen two men crouching under the wheels of one of the trucks\r\nnearly all night. _Faquirs_ do not steal horses, so I gave them no more\r\nthought. I would find Lutuf Ullah, my partner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce you did? And you didn\xe2\x80\x99t bother your head about it? \xe2\x80\x99Pon my\r\nword, it\xe2\x80\x99s just almost as well that I met you. What were they like,\r\neh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were only _faquirs_. They will no more than take a little grain,\r\nperhaps, from one of the trucks. There are many up the line. The State\r\nwill never miss the dole. I came here seeking for my partner, Lutuf\r\nUllah.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind your partner. Where are your horse-trucks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little to this side of the farthest place where they make lamps for\r\nthe trains.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe signal-box! Yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd upon the rail nearest to the road upon the right-hand side\xe2\x80\x94looking\r\nup the line thus. But as regards Lutuf Ullah\xe2\x80\x94a tall man with a broken\r\nnose, and a Persian greyhound Aie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy had hurried off to wake up a young and enthusiastic policeman;\r\nfor, as he said, the Railway had suffered much from depredations in the\r\ngoods-yard. Mahbub Ali chuckled in his dyed beard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will walk in their boots, making a noise, and then they will\r\nwonder why there are no _faquirs_. They are very clever boys\xe2\x80\x94Barton\r\nSahib and Young Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe waited idly for a few minutes, expecting to see them hurry up the\r\nline girt for action. A light engine slid through the station, and he\r\ncaught a glimpse of young Barton in the cab.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did that child an injustice. He is not altogether a fool,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMahbub Ali. \xe2\x80\x9cTo take a fire-carriage for a thief is a new game!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Mahbub Ali came to his camp in the dawn, no one thought it worth\r\nwhile to tell him any news of the night. No one, at least, but one\r\nsmall horseboy, newly advanced to the great man\xe2\x80\x99s service, whom Mahbub\r\ncalled to his tiny tent to assist in some packing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all known to me,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Kim, bending above saddlebags. \xe2\x80\x99Two\r\nSahibs came up on a _te-train_. I was running to and fro in the dark on\r\nthis side of the trucks as the _te-train_ moved up and down slowly.\r\nThey fell upon two men sitting under this truck\xe2\x80\x94Hajji, what shall I do\r\nwith this lump of tobacco? Wrap it in paper and put it under the\r\nsalt-bag? Yes\xe2\x80\x94and struck them down. But one man struck at a Sahib with\r\na _faquir\xe2\x80\x99s_ buck\xe2\x80\x99s horn\xe2\x80\x9d (Kim meant the conjoined black-buck horns,\r\nwhich are a _faquir\xe2\x80\x99s_ sole temporal weapon)\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe blood came. So the\r\nother Sahib, first smiting his own man senseless, smote the stabber\r\nwith a short gun which had rolled from the first man\xe2\x80\x99s hand. They all\r\nraged as though mad together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMahbub smiled with heavenly resignation. \xe2\x80\x9cNo! That is not so much\r\n_dewanee_ (madness, or a case for the civil court\xe2\x80\x94the word can be\r\npunned upon both ways) as _nizamut_ (a criminal case). A gun, sayest\r\nthou? Ten good years in jail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen they both lay still, but I think they were nearly dead when they\r\nwere put on the _te-train_. Their heads moved thus. And there is much\r\nblood on the line. Come and see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen blood before. Jail is the sure place\xe2\x80\x94and assuredly they\r\nwill give false names, and assuredly no man will find them for a long\r\ntime. They were unfriends of mine. Thy fate and mine seem on one\r\nstring. What a tale for the healer of pearls! Now swiftly with the\r\nsaddle-bags and the cooking-platter. We will take out the horses and\r\naway to Simla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSwiftly\xe2\x80\x94as Orientals understand speed\xe2\x80\x94with long explanations, with\r\nabuse and windy talk, carelessly, amid a hundred checks for little\r\nthings forgotten, the untidy camp broke up and led the half-dozen stiff\r\nand fretful horses along the Kalka road in the fresh of the rain-swept\r\ndawn. Kim, regarded as Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s favourite by all who wished to\r\nstand well with the Pathan, was not called upon to work. They strolled\r\non by the easiest of stages, halting every few hours at a wayside\r\nshelter. Very many Sahibs travel along the Kalka road; and, as Mahbub\r\nAli says, every young Sahib must needs esteem himself a judge of a\r\nhorse, and, though he be over head in debt to the money-lender, must\r\nmake as if to buy. That was the reason that Sahib after Sahib, rolling\r\nalong in a stage-carriage, would stop and open talk. Some would even\r\ndescend from their vehicles and feel the horses\xe2\x80\x99 legs; asking inane\r\nquestions, or, through sheer ignorance of the vernacular, grossly\r\ninsulting the imperturbable trader.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen first I dealt with Sahibs, and that was when Colonel Soady Sahib\r\nwas Governor of Fort Abazai and flooded the Commissioner\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncamping-ground for spite,\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub confided to Kim as the boy filled his\r\npipe under a tree, \xe2\x80\x9cI did not know how greatly they were fools, and\r\nthis made me wroth. As thus\xe2\x80\x94,\xe2\x80\x9d and he told Kim a tale of an expression,\r\nmisused in all innocence, that doubled Kim up with mirth. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I see,\r\nhowever,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he exhaled smoke slowly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthat it is with them as with all\r\nmen\xe2\x80\x94in certain matters they are wise, and in others most foolish. Very\r\nfoolish it is to use the wrong word to a stranger; for though the heart\r\nmay be clean of offence, how is the stranger to know that? He is more\r\nlike to search truth with a dagger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. True talk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim solemnly. \xe2\x80\x9cFools speak of a cat when a\r\nwoman is brought to bed, for instance. I have heard them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTherefore, in one situate as thou art, it particularly behoves thee to\r\nremember this with both kinds of faces. Among Sahibs, never forgetting\r\nthou art a Sahib; among the folk of Hind, always remembering thou art\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe paused, with a puzzled smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I? Mussalman, Hindu, Jain, or Buddhist? That is a hard knot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art beyond question an unbeliever, and therefore thou wilt be\r\ndamned. So says my Law\xe2\x80\x94or I think it does. But thou art also my Little\r\nFriend of all the World, and I love thee. So says my heart. This matter\r\nof creeds is like horseflesh. The wise man knows horses are good\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nthere is a profit to be made from all; and for myself\xe2\x80\x94but that I am a\r\ngood Sunni and hate the men of Tirah\xe2\x80\x94I could believe the same of all\r\nthe Faiths. Now manifestly a Kathiawar mare taken from the sands of her\r\nbirthplace and removed to the west of Bengal founders\xe2\x80\x94nor is even a\r\nBalkh stallion (and there are no better horses than those of Balkh,\r\nwere they not so heavy in the shoulder) of any account in the great\r\nNorthern deserts beside the snow-camels I have seen. Therefore I say in\r\nmy heart the Faiths are like the horses. Each has merit in its own\r\ncountry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my lama said altogether a different thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he is an old dreamer of dreams from Bhotiyal. My heart is a little\r\nangry, Friend of all the World, that thou shouldst see such worth in a\r\nman so little known.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true, Hajji; but that worth do I see, and to him my heart is\r\ndrawn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd his to thine, I hear. Hearts are like horses. They come and they\r\ngo against bit or spur. Shout Gul Sher Khan yonder to drive in that bay\r\nstallion\xe2\x80\x99s pickets more firmly. We do not want a horse-fight at every\r\nresting-stage, and the dun and the black will be locked in a little ...\r\nNow hear me. Is it necessary to the comfort of thy heart to see that\r\nlama?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is one part of my bond,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I do not see him, and if he\r\nis taken from me, I will go out of that _madrissah_ in Nucklao and,\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94once gone, who is to find me again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true. Never was colt held on a lighter heel-rope than thou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMahbub nodded his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not be afraid.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim spoke as though he could have vanished on the\r\nmoment. \xe2\x80\x9cMy lama has said that he will come to see me at the\r\n_madrissah_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA beggar and his bowl in the presence of those young Sa\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot all!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim cut in with a snort. \xe2\x80\x9cTheir eyes are blued and their\r\nnails are blackened with low-caste blood, many of them. Sons of\r\n_mehteeranees_\xe2\x80\x94brothers-in-law to the _bhungi_ (sweeper).\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe need not follow the rest of the pedigree; but Kim made his little\r\npoint clearly and without heat, chewing a piece of sugar-cane the\r\nwhile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFriend of all the World,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub, pushing over the pipe for the\r\nboy to clean, \xe2\x80\x9cI have met many men, women, and boys, and not a few\r\nSahibs. I have never in all my days met such an imp as thou art.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why? When I always tell thee the truth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps the very reason, for this is a world of danger to honest men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMahbub Ali hauled himself off the ground, girt in his belt, and went\r\nover to the horses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr sell it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was that in the tone that made Mahbub halt and turn. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat new\r\ndevilry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEight annas, and I will tell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, grinning. \xe2\x80\x9cIt touches thy\r\npeace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Shaitan!\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub gave the money.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRememberest thou the little business of the thieves in the dark, down\r\nyonder at Umballa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeeing they sought my life, I have not altogether forgotten. Why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRememberest thou the Kashmir Serai?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will twist thy ears in a moment\xe2\x80\x94Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo need\xe2\x80\x94Pathan. Only, the second _faquir_, whom the Sahibs beat\r\nsenseless, was the man who came to search thy bulkhead at Lahore. I saw\r\nhis face as they helped him on the engine. The very same man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didst thou not tell before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he will go to jail, and be safe for some years. There is no need\r\nto tell more than is necessary at any one time. Besides, I did not then\r\nneed money for sweetmeats.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah kerim!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub Ah. \xe2\x80\x9cWilt thou some day sell my head for a\r\nfew sweetmeats if the fit takes thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim will remember till he dies that long, lazy journey from Umballa,\r\nthrough Kalka and the Pinjore Gardens near by, up to Simla. A sudden\r\nspate in the Gugger River swept down one horse (the most valuable, be\r\nsure), and nearly drowned Kim among the dancing boulders. Farther up\r\nthe road the horses were stampeded by a Government elephant, and being\r\nin high condition of grass food, it cost a day and a half to get them\r\ntogether again. Then they met Sikandar Khan coming down with a few\r\nunsaleable screws\xe2\x80\x94remnants of his string\xe2\x80\x94and Mahbub, who has more of\r\nhorse-coping in his little fingernail than Sikandar Khan in all his\r\ntents, must needs buy two of the worst, and that meant eight hours\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlaborious diplomacy and untold tobacco. But it was all pure delight\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nwandering road, climbing, dipping, and sweeping about the growing\r\nspurs; the flush of the morning laid along the distant snows; the\r\nbranched cacti, tier upon tier on the stony hillsides; the voices of a\r\nthousand water-channels; the chatter of the monkeys; the solemn\r\ndeodars, climbing one after another with down-drooped branches; the\r\nvista of the Plains rolled out far beneath them; the incessant twanging\r\nof the tonga-horns and the wild rush of the led horses when a tonga\r\nswung round a curve; the halts for prayers (Mahbub was very religious\r\nin dry-washings and bellowings when time did not press); the evening\r\nconferences by the halting-places, when camels and bullocks chewed\r\nsolemnly together and the stolid drivers told the news of the Road\xe2\x80\x94all\r\nthese things lifted Kim\xe2\x80\x99s heart to song within him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, when the singing and dancing is done,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub Ali, \xe2\x80\x9ccomes\r\nthe Colonel Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s, and that is not so sweet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fair land\xe2\x80\x94a most beautiful land is this of Hind\xe2\x80\x94and the land of the\r\nFive Rivers is fairer than all,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim half chanted. \xe2\x80\x9cInto it I will go\r\nagain if Mahbub Ali or the Colonel lift hand or foot against me. Once\r\ngone, who shall find me? Look, Hajji, is yonder the city of Simla?\r\nAllah, what a city!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father\xe2\x80\x99s brother, and he was an old man when Mackerson Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s well\r\nwas new at Peshawur, could recall when there were but two houses in\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe led the horses below the main road into the lower Simla bazar\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ncrowded rabbit-warren that climbs up from the valley to the Town Hall\r\nat an angle of forty-five. A man who knows his way there can defy all\r\nthe police of India\xe2\x80\x99s summer capital, so cunningly does veranda\r\ncommunicate with veranda, alley-way with alley-way, and bolt-hole with\r\nbolt-hole. Here live those who minister to the wants of the glad\r\ncity\xe2\x80\x94jhampanis who pull the pretty ladies\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9crickshaws by night and\r\ngamble till the dawn; grocers, oil-sellers, curio-vendors,\r\nfirewood-dealers, priests, pickpockets, and native employees of the\r\nGovernment. Here are discussed by courtesans the things which are\r\nsupposed to be profoundest secrets of the India Council; and here\r\ngather all the sub-sub-agents of half the Native States. Here, too,\r\nMahbub Ali rented a room, much more securely locked than his bulkhead\r\nat Lahore, in the house of a Mohammedan cattle-dealer. It was a place\r\nof miracles, too, for there went in at twilight a Mohammedan horseboy,\r\nand there came out an hour later a Eurasian lad\xe2\x80\x94the Lucknow girl\xe2\x80\x99s dye\r\nwas of the best\xe2\x80\x94in badly-fitting shop-clothes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have spoken with Creighton Sahib,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Mahbub Ali, \xe2\x80\x9cand a second\r\ntime has the Hand of Friendship averted the Whip of Calamity. He says\r\nthat thou hast altogether wasted sixty days upon the Road, and it is\r\ntoo late, therefore, to send thee to any Hill-school.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have said that my holidays are my own. I do not go to school twice\r\nover. That is one part of my bond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Colonel Sahib is not yet aware of that contract. Thou art to lodge\r\nin Lurgan Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s house till it is time to go again to Nucklao.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had sooner lodge with thee, Mahbub.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou dost not know the honour. Lurgan Sahib himself asked for thee.\r\nThou wilt go up the hill and along the road atop, and there thou must\r\nforget for a while that thou hast ever seen or spoken to me, Mahbub\r\nAli, who sells horses to Creighton Sahib, whom thou dost not know.\r\nRemember this order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim nodded. \xe2\x80\x9cGood,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cand who is Lurgan Sahib? Nay\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he caught\r\nMahbub\xe2\x80\x99s sword-keen glance\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cindeed I have never heard his name. Is he\r\nby chance\xe2\x80\x94he lowered his voice\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cone of us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat talk is this of _us_, Sahib?\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub Ali returned, in the tone he\r\nused towards Europeans. \xe2\x80\x9cI am a Pathan; thou art a Sahib and the son of\r\na Sahib. Lurgan Sahib has a shop among the European shops. All Simla\r\nknows it. Ask there ... and, Friend of all the World, he is one to be\r\nobeyed to the last wink of his eyelashes. Men say he does magic, but\r\nthat should not touch thee. Go up the hill and ask. Here begins the\r\nGreat Game.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\n\r\n\r\nS\xe2\x80\x99 doaks was son of Yelth the wise\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    Chief of the Raven clan.\r\nItswoot the Bear had him in care\r\n    To make him a medicine-man.\r\n\r\nHe was quick and quicker to learn\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    Bold and bolder to dare:\r\nHe danced the dread Kloo-Kwallie Dance\r\n    To tickle Itswoot the Bear!\r\n\r\n_Oregon Legend._\r\n\r\nKim flung himself whole-heartedly upon the next turn of the wheel. He\r\nwould be a Sahib again for a while. In that idea, so soon as he had\r\nreached the broad road under Simla Town Hall, he cast about for one to\r\nimpress. A Hindu child, some ten years old, squatted under a lamp-post.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is Mr Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s house?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand English,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer, and Kim shifted his\r\nspeech accordingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will show.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTogether they set off through the mysterious dusk, full of the noises\r\nof a city below the hillside, and the breath of a cool wind in\r\ndeodar-crowned Jakko, shouldering the stars. The house-lights,\r\nscattered on every level, made, as it were, a double firmament. Some\r\nwere fixed, others belonged to the \xe2\x80\x9crickshaws of the careless,\r\nopen-spoken English folk, going out to dinner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim\xe2\x80\x99s guide, and halted in a veranda flush with the\r\nmain road. No door stayed them, but a curtain of beaded reeds that\r\nsplit up the lamplight beyond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is come,\xe2\x80\x9d said the boy, in a voice little louder than a sigh, and\r\nvanished. Kim felt sure that the boy had been posted to guide him from\r\nthe first, but, putting a bold face on it, parted the curtain. A\r\nblack-bearded man, with a green shade over his eyes, sat at a table,\r\nand, one by one, with short, white hands, picked up globules of light\r\nfrom a tray before him, threaded them on a glancing silken string, and\r\nhummed to himself the while. Kim was conscious that beyond the circle\r\nof light the room was full of things that smelt like all the temples of\r\nall the East. A whiff of musk, a puff of sandal-wood, and a breath of\r\nsickly jessamine-oil caught his opened nostrils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim at last, speaking in the vernacular: the smells\r\nmade him forget that he was to be a Sahib.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeventy-nine, eighty, eighty-one,\xe2\x80\x9d the man counted to himself,\r\nstringing pearl after pearl so quickly that Kim could scarcely follow\r\nhis fingers. He slid off the green shade and looked fixedly at Kim for\r\na full half-minute. The pupils of the eye dilated and closed to\r\npin-pricks, as if at will. There was a _faquir_ by the Taksali Gate who\r\nhad just this gift and made money by it, especially when cursing silly\r\nwomen. Kim stared with interest. His disreputable friend could further\r\ntwitch his ears, almost like a goat, and Kim was disappointed that this\r\nnew man could not imitate him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not be afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan Sahib suddenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I fear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou wilt sleep here tonight, and stay with me till it is time to go\r\nagain to Nucklao. It is an order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is an order,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cBut where shall I sleep?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, in this room.\xe2\x80\x9d Lurgan Sahib waved his hand towards the darkness\r\nbehind him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo be it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim composedly. \xe2\x80\x9cNow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe nodded and held the lamp above his head. As the light swept them,\r\nthere leaped out from the walls a collection of Tibetan devil-dance\r\nmasks, hanging above the fiend-embroidered draperies of those ghastly\r\nfunctions\xe2\x80\x94horned masks, scowling masks, and masks of idiotic terror. In\r\na corner, a Japanese warrior, mailed and plumed, menaced him with a\r\nhalberd, and a score of lances and _khandas_ and _kuttars_ gave back\r\nthe unsteady gleam. But what interested Kim more than all these\r\nthings\xe2\x80\x94he had seen devil-dance masks at the Lahore Museum\xe2\x80\x94was a glimpse\r\nof the soft-eyed Hindu child who had left him in the doorway, sitting\r\ncross-legged under the table of pearls with a little smile on his\r\nscarlet lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that Lurgan Sahib wishes to make me afraid. And I am sure that\r\nthat devil\xe2\x80\x99s brat below the table wishes to see me afraid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis place,\xe2\x80\x9d he said aloud, \xe2\x80\x9cis like a Wonder House. Where is my bed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLurgan Sahib pointed to a native quilt in a corner by the loathsome\r\nmasks, picked up the lamp, and left the room black.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas that Lurgan Sahib?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim asked as he cuddled down. No answer. He\r\ncould hear the Hindu boy breathing, however, and, guided by the sound,\r\ncrawled across the floor, and cuffed into the darkness, crying: \xe2\x80\x9cGive\r\nanswer, devil! Is this the way to lie to a Sahib?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFrom the darkness he fancied he could hear the echo of a chuckle. It\r\ncould not be his soft-fleshed companion, because he was weeping. So Kim\r\nlifted up his voice and called aloud:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLurgan Sahib! O Lurgan Sahib! Is it an order that thy servant does not\r\nspeak to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is an order.\xe2\x80\x9d The voice came from behind him and he started.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good. But remember,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, as he resought the quilt, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwill beat thee in the morning. I do not love Hindus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was no cheerful night; the room being overfull of voices and\r\nmusic. Kim was waked twice by someone calling his name. The second time\r\nhe set out in search, and ended by bruising his nose against a box that\r\ncertainly spoke with a human tongue, but in no sort of human accent. It\r\nseemed to end in a tin trumpet and to be joined by wires to a smaller\r\nbox on the floor\xe2\x80\x94so far, at least, as he could judge by touch. And the\r\nvoice, very hard and whirring, came out of the trumpet. Kim rubbed his\r\nnose and grew furious, thinking, as usual, in Hindi.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis with a beggar from the bazar might be good, but\xe2\x80\x94I am a Sahib and\r\nthe son of a Sahib and, which is twice as much more beside, a student\r\nof Nucklao. Yess\xe2\x80\x9d (here he turned to English), \xe2\x80\x9ca boy of St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\nDamn Mr Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s eyes!\xe2\x80\x94It is some sort of machinery like a\r\nsewing-machine. Oh, it is a _great_ cheek of him\xe2\x80\x94we are not frightened\r\nthat way at Lucknow\xe2\x80\x94No!\xe2\x80\x9d Then in Hindi: \xe2\x80\x9cBut what does _he_ gain? He is\r\nonly a trader\xe2\x80\x94I am in his shop. But Creighton Sahib is a Colonel\xe2\x80\x94and I\r\nthink Creighton Sahib gave orders that it should be done. _How_ I will\r\nbeat that Hindu in the morning! What is this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe trumpet-box was pouring out a string of the most elaborate abuse\r\nthat even Kim had ever heard, in a high uninterested voice, that for a\r\nmoment lifted the short hairs of his neck. When the vile thing drew\r\nbreath, Kim was reassured by the soft, sewing-machine-like whirr.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ch\xc3\xbbp!_ (Be still)\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, and again he heard a chuckle that\r\ndecided him. \xe2\x80\x9c_Ch\xc3\xbbp_\xe2\x80\x94or I break your head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe box took no heed. Kim wrenched at the tin trumpet and something\r\nlifted with a click. He had evidently raised a lid. If there were a\r\ndevil inside, now was its time, for\xe2\x80\x94he sniffed\xe2\x80\x94thus did the\r\nsewing-machines of the bazar smell. He would clean that _shaitan_. He\r\nslipped off his jacket, and plunged it into the box\xe2\x80\x99s mouth. Something\r\nlong and round bent under the pressure, there was a whirr and the voice\r\nstopped\xe2\x80\x94as voices must if you ram a thrice-doubled coat on to the wax\r\ncylinder and into the works of an expensive phonograph. Kim finished\r\nhis slumbers with a serene mind.\r\n\r\nIn the morning he was aware of Lurgan Sahib looking down on him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, firmly resolved to cling to his Sahib-dom. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was\r\na box in the night that gave me bad talk. So I stopped it. Was it your\r\nbox?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man held out his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShake hands, O\xe2\x80\x99Hara,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it was my box. I keep such things\r\nbecause my friends the Rajahs like them. That one is broken, but it was\r\ncheap at the price. Yes, my friends, the Kings, are very fond of\r\ntoys\xe2\x80\x94and so am I sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim looked him over out of the corners of his eyes. He was a Sahib in\r\nthat he wore Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s clothes; the accent of his Urdu, the intonation of\r\nhis English, showed that he was anything but a Sahib. He seemed to\r\nunderstand what moved in Kim\xe2\x80\x99s mind ere the boy opened his mouth, and\r\nhe took no pains to explain himself as did Father Victor or the Lucknow\r\nmasters. Sweetest of all\xe2\x80\x94he treated Kim as an equal on the Asiatic\r\nside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry you cannot beat my boy this morning. He says he will kill\r\nyou with a knife or poison. He is jealous, so I have put him in the\r\ncorner and I shall not speak to him today. He has just tried to kill\r\nme. You must help me with the breakfast. He is almost too jealous to\r\ntrust, just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow a genuine imported Sahib from England would have made a great to-do\r\nover this tale. Lurgan Sahib stated it as simply as Mahbub Ali was used\r\nto record his little affairs in the North.\r\n\r\nThe back veranda of the shop was built out over the sheer hillside, and\r\nthey looked down into their neighbours\xe2\x80\x99 chimney-pots, as is the custom\r\nof Simla. But even more than the purely Persian meal cooked by Lurgan\r\nSahib with his own hands, the shop fascinated Kim. The Lahore Museum\r\nwas larger, but here were more wonders\xe2\x80\x94ghost-daggers and prayer-wheels\r\nfrom Tibet; turquoise and raw amber necklaces; green jade bangles;\r\ncuriously packed incense-sticks in jars crusted over with raw garnets;\r\nthe devil-masks of overnight and a wall full of peacock-blue draperies;\r\ngilt figures of Buddha, and little portable lacquer altars; Russian\r\nsamovars with turquoises on the lid; egg-shell china sets in quaint\r\noctagonal cane boxes; yellow ivory crucifixes\xe2\x80\x94from Japan of all places\r\nin the world, so Lurgan Sahib said; carpets in dusty bales, smelling\r\natrociously, pushed back behind torn and rotten screens of geometrical\r\nwork; Persian water-jugs for the hands after meals; dull copper\r\nincense-burners neither Chinese nor Persian, with friezes of fantastic\r\ndevils running round them; tarnished silver belts that knotted like raw\r\nhide; hairpins of jade, ivory, and plasma; arms of all sorts and kinds,\r\nand a thousand other oddments were cased, or piled, or merely thrown\r\ninto the room, leaving a clear space only round the rickety deal table,\r\nwhere Lurgan Sahib worked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose things are nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said his host, following Kim\xe2\x80\x99s glance. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nbuy them because they are pretty, and sometimes I sell\xe2\x80\x94if I like the\r\nbuyer\xe2\x80\x99s look. My work is on the table\xe2\x80\x94some of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt blazed in the morning light\xe2\x80\x94all red and blue and green flashes,\r\npicked out with the vicious blue-white spurt of a diamond here and\r\nthere. Kim opened his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, they are quite well, those stones. It will not hurt them to take\r\nthe sun. Besides, they are cheap. But with sick stones it is very\r\ndifferent.\xe2\x80\x9d He piled Kim\xe2\x80\x99s plate anew. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no one but _me_ can\r\ndoctor a sick pearl and re-blue turquoises. I grant you opals\xe2\x80\x94any fool\r\ncan cure an opal\xe2\x80\x94but for a sick pearl there is only me. Suppose I were\r\nto die! Then there would be no one ... Oh no! _You_ cannot do anything\r\nwith jewels. It will be quite enough if you understand a little about\r\nthe Turquoise\xe2\x80\x94some day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe moved to the end of the veranda to refill the heavy, porous clay\r\nwater-jug from the filter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want drink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim nodded. Lurgan Sahib, fifteen feet off, laid one hand on the jar.\r\nNext instant, it stood at Kim\xe2\x80\x99s elbow, full to within half an inch of\r\nthe brim\xe2\x80\x94the white cloth only showing, by a small wrinkle, where it had\r\nslid into place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWah!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim in most utter amazement. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is magic.\xe2\x80\x9d Lurgan\r\nSahib\xe2\x80\x99s smile showed that the compliment had gone home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThrow it back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will break.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, throw it back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim pitched it at random. It fell short and crashed into fifty pieces,\r\nwhile the water dripped through the rough veranda boarding.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said it would break.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll one. Look at it. Look at the largest piece.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat lay with a sparkle of water in its curve, as it were a star on the\r\nfloor. Kim looked intently. Lurgan Sahib laid one hand gently on the\r\nnape of his neck, stroked it twice or thrice, and whispered: \xe2\x80\x9cLook! It\r\nshall come to life again, piece by piece. First the big piece shall\r\njoin itself to two others on the right and the left\xe2\x80\x94on the right and\r\nthe left. Look!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo save his life, Kim could not have turned his head. The light touch\r\nheld him as in a vice, and his blood tingled pleasantly through him.\r\nThere was one large piece of the jar where there had been three, and\r\nabove them the shadowy outline of the entire vessel. He could see the\r\nveranda through it, but it was thickening and darkening with each beat\r\nof his pulse. Yet the jar\xe2\x80\x94how slowly the thoughts came!\xe2\x80\x94the jar had\r\nbeen smashed before his eyes. Another wave of prickling fire raced down\r\nhis neck, as Lurgan Sahib moved his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook! It is coming into shape,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan Sahib.\r\n\r\nSo far Kim had been thinking in Hindi, but a tremor came on him, and\r\nwith an effort like that of a swimmer before sharks, who hurls himself\r\nhalf out of the water, his mind leaped up from a darkness that was\r\nswallowing it and took refuge in\xe2\x80\x94the multiplication-table in English!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook! It is coming into shape,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Lurgan Sahib.\r\n\r\nThe jar had been smashed\xe2\x80\x94yess, smashed\xe2\x80\x94not the native word, he would\r\nnot think of that\xe2\x80\x94but smashed\xe2\x80\x94into fifty pieces, and twice three was\r\nsix, and thrice three was nine, and four times three was twelve. He\r\nclung desperately to the repetition. The shadow-outline of the jar\r\ncleared like a mist after rubbing eyes. There were the broken shards;\r\nthere was the spilt water drying in the sun, and through the cracks of\r\nthe veranda showed, all ribbed, the white house-wall below\xe2\x80\x94and thrice\r\ntwelve was thirty-six!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook! Is it coming into shape?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Lurgan Sahib.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is smashed\xe2\x80\x94smashed,\xe2\x80\x9d he gasped\xe2\x80\x94Lurgan Sahib had been muttering\r\nsoftly for the last half-minute. Kim wrenched his head aside. \xe2\x80\x9cLook!\r\n_Dekho!_ It is there as it was there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is there as it was there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan, watching Kim closely while\r\nthe boy rubbed his neck. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you are the first of many who has ever\r\nseen it so.\xe2\x80\x9d He wiped his broad forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas that more magic?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim asked suspiciously. The tingle had gone from\r\nhis veins; he felt unusually wide awake.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that was not magic. It was only to see if there was\xe2\x80\x94a flaw in a\r\njewel. Sometimes very fine jewels will fly all to pieces if a man holds\r\nthem in his hand, and knows the proper way. That is why one must be\r\ncareful before one sets them. Tell me, did you see the shape of the\r\npot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor a little time. It began to grow like a flower from the ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then what did you do? I mean, how did you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah! I knew it was broken, and so, I think, that was what I\r\nthought\xe2\x80\x94and it was broken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHm! Has anyone ever done that same sort of magic to you before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it was,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim \xe2\x80\x9cdo you think I should let it again? I should run\r\naway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now you are not afraid\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLurgan Sahib looked at him more closely than ever. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall ask Mahbub\r\nAli\xe2\x80\x94not now, but some day later,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered. \xe2\x80\x9cI am pleased with\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94yes; and I am pleased with you\xe2\x80\x94no. You are the first that ever\r\nsaved himself. I wish I knew what it was that ... But you are right.\r\nYou should not tell that\xe2\x80\x94not even to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned into the dusky gloom of the shop, and sat down at the table,\r\nrubbing his hands softly. No!)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do _you_ think he will do?\xe2\x80\x9d He turned suddenly on Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah! I do not know. Let him go, perhaps. Why did he want to poison\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he is so fond of me. Suppose you were fond of someone, and you\r\nsaw someone come, and the man you were fond of was more pleased with\r\nhim than he was with you, what would you do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim thought. Lurgan repeated the sentence slowly in the vernacular.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should not poison that man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim reflectively, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I should\r\nbeat that boy\xe2\x80\x94_if_ that boy was fond of my man. But first, I would ask\r\nthat boy if it were true.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! He thinks everyone must be fond of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I think he is a fool.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHearest thou?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan Sahib to the shaking shoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nSahib\xe2\x80\x99s son thinks thou art a little fool. Come out, and next time thy\r\nheart is troubled, do not try white arsenic quite so openly. Surely the\r\nDevil Dasim was lord of our table-cloth that day! It might have made me\r\nill, child, and then a stranger would have guarded the jewels. Come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe child, heavy-eyed with much weeping, crept out from behind the bale\r\nand flung himself passionately at Lurgan Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s feet, with an\r\nextravagance of remorse that impressed even Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will look into the ink-pools\xe2\x80\x94I will faithfully guard the jewels! Oh,\r\nmy Father and my Mother, send _him_ away!\xe2\x80\x9d He indicated Kim with a\r\nbackward jerk of his bare heel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot yet\xe2\x80\x94not yet. In a little while he will go away again. But now he\r\nis at school\xe2\x80\x94at a new _madrissah_\xe2\x80\x94and thou shalt be his teacher. Play\r\nthe Play of the Jewels against him. I will keep tally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe child dried his tears at once, and dashed to the back of the shop,\r\nwhence he returned with a copper tray.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me!\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Lurgan Sahib. \xe2\x80\x9cLet them come from thy hand, for\r\nhe may say that I knew them before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGently\xe2\x80\x94gently,\xe2\x80\x9d the man replied, and from a drawer under the table\r\ndealt a half-handful of clattering trifles into the tray.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said the child, waving an old newspaper. \xe2\x80\x9cLook on them as long\r\nas thou wilt, stranger. Count and, if need be, handle. One look is\r\nenough for _me_.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned his back proudly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what is the game?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen thou hast counted and handled and art sure that thou canst\r\nremember them all, I cover them with this paper, and thou must tell\r\nover the tally to Lurgan Sahib. _I_ will write mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah!\xe2\x80\x9d The instinct of competition waked in his breast. He bent over\r\nthe tray. There were but fifteen stones on it. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is easy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\nafter a minute. The child slipped the paper over the winking jewels and\r\nscribbled in a native account-book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are under that paper five blue stones\xe2\x80\x94one big, one smaller, and\r\nthree small,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, all in haste. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are four green stones, and\r\none with a hole in it; there is one yellow stone that I can see\r\nthrough, and one like a pipe-stem. There are two red stones, and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nmade the count fifteen, but two I have forgotten. No! Give me time. One\r\nwas of ivory, little and brownish; and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94give me time...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne\xe2\x80\x94two\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Lurgan Sahib counted him out up to ten. Kim shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear my count!\xe2\x80\x9d the child burst in, trilling with laughter. \xe2\x80\x9cFirst,\r\nare two flawed sapphires\xe2\x80\x94one of two ruttees and one of four as I should\r\njudge. The four-ruttee sapphire is chipped at the edge. There is one\r\nTurkestan turquoise, plain with black veins, and there are two\r\ninscribed\xe2\x80\x94one with a Name of God in gilt, and the other being cracked\r\nacross, for it came out of an old ring, I cannot read. We have now all\r\nfive blue stones. Four flawed emeralds there are, but one is drilled in\r\ntwo places, and one is a little carven\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTheir weights?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan Sahib impassively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree\xe2\x80\x94five\xe2\x80\x94five\xe2\x80\x94and four ruttees as I judge it. There is one piece of\r\nold greenish pipe amber, and a cut topaz from Europe. There is one ruby\r\nof Burma, of two ruttees, without a flaw, and there is a balas-ruby,\r\nflawed, of two ruttees. There is a carved ivory from China representing\r\na rat sucking an egg; and there is last\xe2\x80\x94ah ha!\xe2\x80\x94a ball of crystal as big\r\nas a bean set on a gold leaf.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe clapped his hands at the close.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is thy master,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan Sahib, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh! He knew the names of the stones,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, flushing. \xe2\x80\x9cTry again!\r\nWith common things such as he and I both know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey heaped the tray again with odds and ends gathered from the shop,\r\nand even the kitchen, and every time the child won, till Kim marvelled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBind my eyes\xe2\x80\x94let me feel once with my fingers, and even _then_ I will\r\nleave thee opened-eyed behind,\xe2\x80\x9d he challenged.\r\n\r\nKim stamped with vexation when the lad made his boast good.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it were men\xe2\x80\x94or horses,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI could do better. This playing\r\nwith tweezers and knives and scissors is too little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLearn first\xe2\x80\x94teach later,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan Sahib. \xe2\x80\x9cIs he thy master?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTruly. But how is it done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy doing it many times over till it is done perfectly\xe2\x80\x94for it is worth\r\ndoing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Hindu boy, in highest feather, actually patted Kim on the back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not despair,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI myself will teach thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I will see that thou art well taught,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan Sahib, still\r\nspeaking in the vernacular, \xe2\x80\x9cfor except my boy here\xe2\x80\x94it was foolish of\r\nhim to buy so much white arsenic when, if he had asked, I could have\r\ngiven it\xe2\x80\x94except my boy here I have not in a long time met with one\r\nbetter worth teaching. And there are ten days more ere thou canst\r\nreturn to Lucknao where they teach nothing\xe2\x80\x94at the long price. We shall,\r\nI think, be friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were a most mad ten days, but Kim enjoyed himself too much to\r\nreflect on their craziness. In the morning they played the Jewel\r\nGame\xe2\x80\x94sometimes with veritable stones, sometimes with piles of swords\r\nand daggers, sometimes with photo-graphs of natives. Through the\r\nafternoons he and the Hindu boy would mount guard in the shop, sitting\r\ndumb behind a carpet-bale or a screen and watching Mr Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s many and\r\nvery curious visitors. There were small Rajahs, escorts coughing in the\r\nveranda, who came to buy curiosities\xe2\x80\x94such as phonographs and mechanical\r\ntoys. There were ladies in search of necklaces, and men, it seemed to\r\nKim\xe2\x80\x94but his mind may have been vitiated by early training\xe2\x80\x94in search of\r\nthe ladies; natives from independent and feudatory Courts whose\r\nostensible business was the repair of broken necklaces\xe2\x80\x94rivers of light\r\npoured out upon the table\xe2\x80\x94but whose true end seemed to be to raise\r\nmoney for angry Maharanees or young Rajahs. There were Babus to whom\r\nLurgan Sahib talked with austerity and authority, but at the end of\r\neach interview he gave them money in coined silver and currency notes.\r\nThere were occasional gatherings of long-coated theatrical natives who\r\ndiscussed metaphysics in English and Bengali, to Mr Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s great\r\nedification. He was always interested in religions. At the end of the\r\nday, Kim and the Hindu boy\xe2\x80\x94whose name varied at Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s pleasure\xe2\x80\x94were\r\nexpected to give a detailed account of all that they had seen and\r\nheard\xe2\x80\x94their view of each man\xe2\x80\x99s character, as shown in his face, talk,\r\nand manner, and their notions of his real errand. After dinner, Lurgan\r\nSahib\xe2\x80\x99s fancy turned more to what might be called dressing-up, in which\r\ngame he took a most informing interest. He could paint faces to a\r\nmarvel; with a brush-dab here and a line there changing them past\r\nrecognition. The shop was full of all manner of dresses and turbans,\r\nand Kim was apparelled variously as a young Mohammedan of good family,\r\nan oilman, and once\xe2\x80\x94which was a joyous evening\xe2\x80\x94as the son of an Oudh\r\nlandholder in the fullest of full dress. Lurgan Sahib had a hawk\xe2\x80\x99s eye\r\nto detect the least flaw in the make-up; and lying on a worn teak-wood\r\ncouch, would explain by the half-hour together how such and such a\r\ncaste talked, or walked, or coughed, or spat, or sneezed, and, since\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chows\xe2\x80\x9d matter little in this world, the \xe2\x80\x9cwhy\xe2\x80\x9d of everything. The Hindu\r\nchild played this game clumsily. That little mind, keen as an icicle\r\nwhere tally of jewels was concerned, could not temper itself to enter\r\nanother\xe2\x80\x99s soul; but a demon in Kim woke up and sang with joy as he put\r\non the changing dresses, and changed speech and gesture therewith.\r\n\r\nCarried away by enthusiasm, he volunteered to show Lurgan Sahib one\r\nevening how the disciples of a certain caste of _faquir_, old Lahore\r\nacquaintances, begged doles by the roadside; and what sort of language\r\nhe would use to an Englishman, to a Punjabi farmer going to a fair, and\r\nto a woman without a veil. Lurgan Sahib laughed immensely, and begged\r\nKim to stay as he was, immobile for half an hour\xe2\x80\x94cross-legged,\r\nash-smeared, and wild-eyed, in the back room. At the end of that time\r\nentered a hulking, obese Babu whose stockinged legs shook with fat, and\r\nKim opened on him with a shower of wayside chaff. Lurgan Sahib\xe2\x80\x94this\r\nannoyed Kim\xe2\x80\x94watched the Babu and not the play.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Babu heavily, lighting a cigarette, \xe2\x80\x9cI am of\r\nopeenion that it is most extraordinary and effeecient performance.\r\nExcept that you had told me I should have opined that\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x94that you\r\nwere pulling my legs. How soon can he become approximately effeecient\r\nchain-man? Because _then_ I shall indent for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is what he must learn at Lucknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen order him to be jolly-dam\xe2\x80\x99-quick. Good-night, Lurgan.\xe2\x80\x9d The Babu\r\nswung out with the gait of a bogged cow.\r\n\r\nWhen they were telling over the day\xe2\x80\x99s list of visitors, Lurgan Sahib\r\nasked Kim who he thought the man might be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod knows!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim cheerily. The tone might almost have deceived\r\nMahbub Ali, but it failed entirely with the healer of sick pearls.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true. God, He knows; but I wish to know what you think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim glanced sideways at his companion, whose eye had a way of\r\ncompelling truth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I think he will want me when I come from the school,\r\nbut\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94confidentially, as Lurgan Sahib nodded approval\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI do not\r\nunderstand how _he_ can wear many dresses and talk many tongues.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou wilt understand many things later. He is a writer of tales for a\r\ncertain Colonel. His honour is great only in Simla, and it is\r\nnoticeable that he has no name, but only a number and a letter\xe2\x80\x94that is\r\na custom among us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is there a price upon his head too\xe2\x80\x94as upon Mah\xe2\x80\x94all the others?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot yet; but if a boy rose up who is now sitting here and went\xe2\x80\x94look,\r\nthe door is open!\xe2\x80\x94as far as a certain house with a red-painted veranda,\r\nbehind that which was the old theatre in the Lower Bazar, and whispered\r\nthrough the shutters: \xe2\x80\x98Hurree Chunder Mookerjee bore the bad news of\r\nlast month\xe2\x80\x99, that boy might take away a belt full of rupees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim promptly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive hundred\xe2\x80\x94a thousand\xe2\x80\x94as many as he might ask for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. And for how long might such a boy live after the news was told?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe smiled merrily at Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s very beard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! That is to be well thought of. Perhaps if he were very clever, he\r\nmight live out the day\xe2\x80\x94but not the night. By no means the night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what is the Babu\xe2\x80\x99s pay if so much is put upon his head?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEighty\xe2\x80\x94perhaps a hundred\xe2\x80\x94perhaps a hundred and fifty rupees; but the\r\npay is the least part of the work. From time to time, God causes men to\r\nbe born\xe2\x80\x94and thou art one of them\xe2\x80\x94who have a lust to go abroad at the\r\nrisk of their lives and discover news\xe2\x80\x94today it may be of far-off\r\nthings, tomorrow of some hidden mountain, and the next day of some\r\nnear-by men who have done a foolishness against the State. These souls\r\nare very few; and of these few, not more than ten are of the best.\r\nAmong these ten I count the Babu, and that is curious. How great,\r\ntherefore, and desirable must be a business that brazens the heart of a\r\nBengali!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. But the days go slowly for me. I am yet a boy, and it is only\r\nwithin two months I learned to write Angrezi. Even now I cannot read it\r\nwell. And there are yet years and years and long years before I can be\r\neven a chain-man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave patience, Friend of all the World\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim started at the title.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould I had a few of the years that so irk thee. I have proved thee in\r\nseveral small ways. This will not be forgotten when I make my report to\r\nthe Colonel Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d Then, changing suddenly into English with a deep\r\nlaugh:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove! O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, I think there is a great deal in you; but you must\r\nnot become proud and you must not talk. You must go back to Lucknow and\r\nbe a good little boy and mind your book, as the English say, and\r\nperhaps, next holidays if you care, you can come back to me!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nface fell. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I mean _if_ you like. I know where you want to go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFour days later a seat was booked for Kim and his small trunk at the\r\nrear of a Kalka tonga. His companion was the whale-like Babu, who, with\r\na fringed shawl wrapped round his head, and his fat openwork-stockinged\r\nleft leg tucked under him, shivered and grunted in the morning chill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow comes it that this man is one of _us?_\xe2\x80\x9d thought Kim considering\r\nthe jelly back as they jolted down the road; and the reflection threw\r\nhim into most pleasant day-dreams. Lurgan Sahib had given him five\r\nrupees\xe2\x80\x94a splendid sum\xe2\x80\x94as well as the assurance of his protection if he\r\nworked. Unlike Mahbub, Lurgan Sahib had spoken most explicitly of the\r\nreward that would follow obedience, and Kim was content. If only, like\r\nthe Babu, he could enjoy the dignity of a letter and a number\xe2\x80\x94and a\r\nprice upon his head! Some day he would be all that and more. Some day\r\nhe might be almost as great as Mahbub Ali! The housetops of his search\r\nshould be half India; he would follow Kings and Ministers, as in the\r\nold days he had followed _vakils_ and lawyers\xe2\x80\x99 touts across Lahore city\r\nfor Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s sake. Meantime, there was the present, and not at all\r\nunpleasant, fact of St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s immediately before him. There would be\r\nnew boys to condescend to, and there would be tales of holiday\r\nadventures to hear. Young Martin, son of the tea-planter at Manipur,\r\nhad boasted that he would go to war, with a rifle, against the\r\nhead-hunters.\r\n\r\nThat might be, but it was certain young Martin had not been blown half\r\nacross the forecourt of a Patiala palace by an explosion of fireworks;\r\nnor had he... Kim fell to telling himself the story of his own\r\nadventures through the last three months. He could paralyse St\r\nXavier\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94even the biggest boys who shaved\xe2\x80\x94with the recital, were that\r\npermitted. But it was, of course, out of the question. There would be a\r\nprice upon his head in good time, as Lurgan Sahib had assured him; and\r\nif he talked foolishly now, not only would that price never be set, but\r\nColonel Creighton would cast him off\xe2\x80\x94and he would be left to the wrath\r\nof Lurgan Sahib and Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x94for the short space of life that would\r\nremain to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I should lose Delhi for the sake of a fish,\xe2\x80\x9d was his proverbial\r\nphilosophy. It behoved him to forget his holidays (there would always\r\nremain the fun of inventing imaginary adventures) and, as Lurgan Sahib\r\nhad said, to work. Of all the boys hurrying back to St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s, from\r\nSukkur in the sands to Galle beneath the palms, none was so filled with\r\nvirtue as Kimball O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, jiggeting down to Umballa behind Hurree\r\nChunder Mookerjee, whose name on the books of one section of the\r\nEthnological Survey was R.17.\r\n\r\nAnd if additional spur were needed, the Babu supplied it. After a huge\r\nmeal at Kalka, he spoke uninterruptedly. Was Kim going to school? Then\r\nhe, an M A of Calcutta University, would explain the advantages of\r\neducation. There were marks to be gained by due attention to Latin and\r\nWordsworth\xe2\x80\x99s _Excursion_ (all this was Greek to Kim). French, too was\r\nvital, and the best was to be picked up in Chandernagore a few miles\r\nfrom Calcutta. Also a man might go far, as he himself had done, by\r\nstrict attention to plays called _Lear_ and _Julius C\xc3\xa6sar_, both much\r\nin demand by examiners. _Lear_ was not so full of historical allusions\r\nas _Julius C\xc3\xa6sar;_ the book cost four annas, but could be bought\r\nsecond-hand in Bow Bazar for two. Still more important than Wordsworth,\r\nor the eminent authors, Burke and Hare, was the art and science of\r\nmensuration. A boy who had passed his examination in these branches\xe2\x80\x94for\r\nwhich, by the way, there were no cram-books\xe2\x80\x94could, by merely marching\r\nover a country with a compass and a level and a straight eye, carry\r\naway a picture of that country which might be sold for large sums in\r\ncoined silver. But as it was occasionally inexpedient to carry about\r\nmeasuring-chains a boy would do well to know the precise length of his\r\nown foot-pace, so that when he was deprived of what Hurree Chunder\r\ncalled adventitious aids he might still tread his distances. To keep\r\ncount of thousands of paces, Hurree Chunder\xe2\x80\x99s experience had shown him\r\nnothing more valuable than a rosary of eighty-one or a hundred and\r\neight beads, for \xe2\x80\x9cit was divisible and sub-divisible into many\r\nmultiples and sub-multiples\xe2\x80\x9d. Through the volleying drifts of English,\r\nKim caught the general trend of the talk, and it interested him very\r\nmuch. Here was a new craft that a man could tuck away in his head and\r\nby the look of the large wide world unfolding itself before him, it\r\nseemed that the more a man knew the better for him.\r\n\r\nSaid the Babu when he had talked for an hour and a half \xe2\x80\x9cI hope some\r\nday to enjoy your offeecial acquaintance. _Ad interim_, if I may be\r\npardoned that expression, I shall give you this betel-box, which is\r\nhighly valuable article and cost me two rupees only four years ago.\xe2\x80\x9d It\r\nwas a cheap, heart-shaped brass thing with three compartments for\r\ncarrying the eternal betel-nut, lime and _pan_-leaf; but it was filled\r\nwith little tabloid-bottles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is reward of merit for your performance in character of that holy\r\nman. You see, you are so young you think you will last for ever and not\r\ntake care of your body. It is great nuisance to go sick in the middle\r\nof business. I am fond of drugs myself, and they are handy to cure poor\r\npeople too. These are good Departmental drugs\xe2\x80\x94quinine and so on. I give\r\nit you for souvenir. Now good-bye. I have urgent private business here\r\nby the roadside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe slipped out noiselessly as a cat, on the Umballa road, hailed a\r\npassing cart and jingled away, while Kim, tongue-tied, twiddled the\r\nbrass betel-box in his hands.\r\n\r\nThe record of a boy\xe2\x80\x99s education interests few save his parents, and, as\r\nyou know, Kim was an orphan. It is written in the books of St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nin Partibus that a report of Kim\xe2\x80\x99s progress was forwarded at the end of\r\neach term to Colonel Creighton and to Father Victor, from whose hands\r\nduly came the money for his schooling. It is further recorded in the\r\nsame books that he showed a great aptitude for mathematical studies as\r\nwell as map-making, and carried away a prize (_The Life of Lord\r\nLawrence_, tree-calf, two vols., nine rupees, eight annas) for\r\nproficiency therein; and the same term played in St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s eleven\r\nagainst the Alighur Mohammedan College, his age being fourteen years\r\nand ten months. He was also re-vaccinated (from which we may assume\r\nthat there had been another epidemic of smallpox at Lucknow) about the\r\nsame time. Pencil notes on the edge of an old muster-roll record that\r\nhe was punished several times for \xe2\x80\x9cconversing with improper persons\xe2\x80\x9d,\r\nand it seems that he was once sentenced to heavy pains for \xe2\x80\x9cabsenting\r\nhimself for a day in the company of a street beggar\xe2\x80\x9d. That was when he\r\ngot over the gate and pleaded with the lama through a whole day down\r\nthe banks of the Gumti to accompany him on the Road next holidays\xe2\x80\x94for\r\none month\xe2\x80\x94for a little week; and the lama set his face as a flint\r\nagainst it, averring that the time had not yet come. Kim\xe2\x80\x99s business,\r\nsaid the old man as they ate cakes together, was to get all the wisdom\r\nof the Sahibs and then he would see. The Hand of Friendship must in\r\nsome way have averted the Whip of Calamity, for six weeks later Kim\r\nseems to have passed an examination in elementary surveying \xe2\x80\x9cwith great\r\ncredit\xe2\x80\x9d, his age being fifteen years and eight months. From this date\r\nthe record is silent. His name does not appear in the year\xe2\x80\x99s batch of\r\nthose who entered for the subordinate Survey of India, but against it\r\nstand the words \xe2\x80\x9cremoved on appointment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeveral times in those three years, cast up at the Temple of the\r\nTirthankars in Benares the lama, a little thinner and a shade yellower,\r\nif that were possible, but gentle and untainted as ever. Sometimes it\r\nwas from the South that he came\xe2\x80\x94from south of Tuticorin, whence the\r\nwonderful fire-boats go to Ceylon where are priests who know Pali;\r\nsometimes it was from the wet green West and the thousand\r\ncotton-factory chimneys that ring Bombay; and once from the North,\r\nwhere he had doubled back eight hundred miles to talk for a day with\r\nthe Keeper of the Images in the Wonder House. He would stride to his\r\ncell in the cool, cut marble\xe2\x80\x94the priests of the Temple were good to the\r\nold man,\xe2\x80\x94wash off the dust of travel, make prayer, and depart for\r\nLucknow, well accustomed now to the way of the rail, in a third-class\r\ncarriage. Returning, it was noticeable, as his friend the Seeker\r\npointed out to the head-priest, that he ceased for a while to mourn the\r\nloss of his River, or to draw wondrous pictures of the Wheel of Life,\r\nbut preferred to talk of the beauty and wisdom of a certain mysterious\r\n_chela_ whom no man of the Temple had ever seen. Yes, he had followed\r\nthe traces of the Blessed Feet throughout all India. (The Curator has\r\nstill in his possession a most marvellous account of his wanderings and\r\nmeditations.) There remained nothing more in life but to find the River\r\nof the Arrow. Yet it was shown to him in dreams that it was a matter\r\nnot to be undertaken with any hope of success unless that seeker had\r\nwith him the one _chela_ appointed to bring the event to a happy issue,\r\nand versed in great wisdom\xe2\x80\x94such wisdom as white-haired Keepers of\r\nImages possess. For example (here came out the snuff-gourd, and the\r\nkindly Jain priests made haste to be silent):\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLong and long ago, when Devadatta was King of Benares\xe2\x80\x94let all listen\r\nto the _J\xc3\xa2taka!_\xe2\x80\x94an elephant was captured for a time by the king\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhunters and ere he broke free, beringed with a grievous legiron. This\r\nhe strove to remove with hate and frenzy in his heart, and hurrying up\r\nand down the forests, besought his brother-elephants to wrench it\r\nasunder. One by one, with their strong trunks, they tried and failed.\r\nAt the last they gave it as their opinion that the ring was not to be\r\nbroken by any bestial power. And in a thicket, new-born, wet with\r\nmoisture of birth, lay a day-old calf of the herd whose mother had\r\ndied. The fettered elephant, forgetting his own agony, said: \xe2\x80\x98If I do\r\nnot help this suckling it will perish under our feet.\xe2\x80\x99 So he stood\r\nabove the young thing, making his legs buttresses against the uneasily\r\nmoving herd; and he begged milk of a virtuous cow, and the calf throve,\r\nand the ringed elephant was the calf\xe2\x80\x99s guide and defence. Now the days\r\nof an elephant\xe2\x80\x94let all listen to the _J\xc3\xa2taka!_\xe2\x80\x94are thirty-five years to\r\nhis full strength, and through thirty-five Rains the ringed elephant\r\nbefriended the younger, and all the while the fetter ate into the\r\nflesh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen one day the young elephant saw the half-buried iron, and turning\r\nto the elder said: \xe2\x80\x98What is this?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98It is even my sorrow,\xe2\x80\x99 said he who\r\nhad befriended him. Then that other put out his trunk and in the\r\ntwinkling of an eyelash abolished the ring, saying: \xe2\x80\x98The appointed time\r\nhas come.\xe2\x80\x99 So the virtuous elephant who had waited temperately and done\r\nkind acts was relieved, at the appointed time, by the very calf whom he\r\nhad turned aside to cherish\xe2\x80\x94let all listen to the _J\xc3\xa2taka!_ for the\r\nElephant was Ananda, and the Calf that broke the ring was none other\r\nthan The Lord Himself...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he would shake his head benignly, and over the ever-clicking\r\nrosary point out how free that elephant-calf was from the sin of pride.\r\nHe was as humble as a _chela_ who, seeing his master sitting in the\r\ndust outside the Gates of Learning, over-leapt the gates (though they\r\nwere locked) and took his master to his heart in the presence of the\r\nproud-stomached city. Rich would be the reward of such a master and\r\nsuch a _chela_ when the time came for them to seek freedom together!\r\n\r\nSo did the lama speak, coming and going across India as softly as a\r\nbat. A sharp-tongued old woman in a house among the fruit-trees behind\r\nSaharunpore honoured him as the woman honoured the prophet, but his\r\nchamber was by no means upon the wall. In an apartment of the forecourt\r\noverlooked by cooing doves he would sit, while she laid aside her\r\nuseless veil and chattered of spirits and fiends of Kulu, of\r\ngrandchildren unborn, and of the free-tongued brat who had talked to\r\nher in the resting-place. Once, too, he strayed alone from the Grand\r\nTrunk Road below Umballa to the very village whose priest had tried to\r\ndrug him; but the kind Heaven that guards lamas sent him at twilight\r\nthrough the crops, absorbed and unsuspicious, to the Rissaldar\xe2\x80\x99s door.\r\nHere was like to have been a grave misunderstanding, for the old\r\nsoldier asked him why the Friend of the Stars had gone that way only\r\nsix days before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may not be,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has gone back to his own people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe sat in that corner telling a hundred merry tales five nights ago,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhis host insisted. \xe2\x80\x9cTrue, he vanished somewhat suddenly in the dawn\r\nafter foolish talk with my granddaughter. He grows apace, but he is the\r\nsame Friend of the Stars as brought me true word of the war. Have ye\r\nparted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94and no,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama replied. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x94we have not altogether parted, but\r\nthe time is not ripe that we should take the Road together. He acquires\r\nwisdom in another place. We must wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll one\xe2\x80\x94but if it were not the boy how did he come to speak so\r\ncontinually of thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what said he?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the lama eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSweet words\xe2\x80\x94an hundred thousand\xe2\x80\x94that thou art his father and mother\r\nand such all. Pity that he does not take the Qpeen\xe2\x80\x99s service. He is\r\nfearless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis news amazed the lama, who did not then know how religiously Kim\r\nkept to the contract made with Mahbub Ali, and perforce ratified by\r\nColonel Creighton...\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no holding the young pony from the game,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nhorse-dealer when the Colonel pointed out that vagabonding over India\r\nin holiday time was absurd. \xe2\x80\x9cIf permission be refused to go and come as\r\nhe chooses, he will make light of the refusal. Then who is to catch\r\nhim? Colonel Sahib, only once in a thousand years is a horse born so\r\nwell fitted for the game as this our colt. And we need men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\n\r\n\r\nYour tiercel\xe2\x80\x99s too long at hack, Sire. He\xe2\x80\x99s no eyass\r\nBut a passage-hawk that footed ere we caught him,\r\nDangerously free o\xe2\x80\x99 the air. Faith! were he mine\r\n(As mine\xe2\x80\x99s the glove he binds to for his tirings)\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d fly him with a make-hawk. He\xe2\x80\x99s in yarak\r\nPlumed to the very point\xe2\x80\x94so manned, so weathered...\r\nGive him the firmament God made him for,\r\nAnd what shall take the air of him?\xe2\x80\x94_Old Play._\r\n\r\nLurgan Sahib did not use as direct speech, but his advice tallied with\r\nMahbub\xe2\x80\x99s; and the upshot was good for Kim. He knew better now than to\r\nleave Lucknow city in native garb, and if Mahbub were anywhere within\r\nreach of a letter, it was to Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s camp he headed, and made his\r\nchange under the Pathan\xe2\x80\x99s wary eye. Could the little Survey paint-box\r\nthat he used for map-tinting in term-time have found a tongue to tell\r\nof holiday doings, he might have been expelled. Once Mahbub and he went\r\ntogether as far as the beautiful city of Bombay, with three truckloads\r\nof tram-horses, and Mahbub nearly melted when Kim proposed a sail in a\r\ndhow across the Indian Ocean to buy Gulf Arabs, which, he understood\r\nfrom a hanger-on of the dealer Abdul Rahman, fetched better prices than\r\nmere Kabulis.\r\n\r\nHe dipped his hand into the dish with that great trader when Mahbub and\r\na few co-religionists were invited to a big Haj dinner. They came back\r\nby way of Karachi by sea, when Kim took his first experience of\r\nsea-sickness sitting on the fore-hatch of a coasting-steamer, well\r\npersuaded he had been poisoned. The Babu\xe2\x80\x99s famous drug-box proved\r\nuseless, though Kim had restocked it at Bombay. Mahbub had business at\r\nQuetta, and there Kim, as Mahbub admitted, earned his keep, and perhaps\r\na little over, by spending four curious days as scullion in the house\r\nof a fat Commissariat sergeant, from whose office-box, in an auspicious\r\nmoment, he removed a little vellum ledger which he copied out\xe2\x80\x94it seemed\r\nto deal entirely with cattle and camel sales\xe2\x80\x94by moonlight, lying behind\r\nan outhouse, all through one hot night. Then he returned the ledger to\r\nits place, and, at Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s word, left that service unpaid, rejoining\r\nhim six miles down the road, the clean copy in his bosom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat soldier is a small fish,\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub Ali explained, \xe2\x80\x9cbut in time we\r\nshall catch the larger one. He only sells oxen at two prices\xe2\x80\x94one for\r\nhimself and one for the Government\xe2\x80\x94which I do not think is a sin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy could not I take away the little book and be done with it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he would have been frightened, and he would have told his master.\r\nThen we should miss, perhaps, a great number of new rifles which seek\r\ntheir way up from Quetta to the North. The Game is so large that one\r\nsees but a little at a time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, and held his tongue. That was in the monsoon holidays,\r\nafter he had taken the prize for mathematics. The Christmas holidays he\r\nspent\xe2\x80\x94deducting ten days for private amusements\xe2\x80\x94with Lurgan Sahib,\r\nwhere he sat for the most part in front of a roaring wood-fire\xe2\x80\x94Jakko\r\nroad was four feet deep in snow that year\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94the small Hindu had gone\r\naway to be married\xe2\x80\x94helped Lurgan to thread pearls. He made Kim learn\r\nwhole chapters of the Koran by heart, till he could deliver them with\r\nthe very roll and cadence of a mullah. Moreover, he told Kim the names\r\nand properties of many native drugs, as well as the runes proper to\r\nrecite when you administer them. And in the evenings he wrote charms on\r\nparchment\xe2\x80\x94elaborate pentagrams crowned with the names of devils\xe2\x80\x94Murra,\r\nand Awan the Companion of Kings\xe2\x80\x94all fantastically written in the\r\ncorners. More to the point, he advised Kim as to the care of his own\r\nbody, the cure of fever-fits, and simple remedies of the Road. A week\r\nbefore it was time to go down, Colonel Creighton Sahib\xe2\x80\x94this was\r\nunfair\xe2\x80\x94sent Kim a written examination paper that concerned itself\r\nsolely with rods and chains and links and angles.\r\n\r\nNext holidays he was out with Mahbub, and here, by the way, he nearly\r\ndied of thirst, plodding through the sand on a camel to the mysterious\r\ncity of Bikanir, where the wells are four hundred feet deep, and lined\r\nthroughout with camel-bone. It was not an amusing trip from Kim\xe2\x80\x99s point\r\nof view, because\xe2\x80\x94in defiance of the contract\xe2\x80\x94the Colonel ordered him to\r\nmake a map of that wild, walled city; and since Mohammedan horse-boys\r\nand pipe-tenders are not expected to drag Survey-chains round the\r\ncapital of an independent Native State, Kim was forced to pace all his\r\ndistances by means of a bead rosary. He used the compass for bearings\r\nas occasion served\xe2\x80\x94after dark chiefly, when the camels had been fed\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nby the help of his little Survey paint-box of six colour-cakes and\r\nthree brushes, he achieved something not remotely unlike the city of\r\nJeysulmir. Mahbub laughed a great deal, and advised him to make up a\r\nwritten report as well; and in the back of the big account-book that\r\nlay under the flap of Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s pet saddle Kim fell to work..\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must hold everything that thou hast seen or touched or considered.\r\nWrite as though the Jung-i-Lat Sahib himself had come by stealth with a\r\nvast army outsetting to war.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow great an army?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, half a lakh of men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFolly! Remember how few and bad were the wells in the sand. Not a\r\nthousand thirsty men could come near by here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen write that down\xe2\x80\x94also all the old breaches in the walls and whence\r\nthe firewood is cut\xe2\x80\x94and what is the temper and disposition of the King.\r\nI stay here till all my horses are sold. I will hire a room by the\r\ngateway, and thou shalt be my accountant. There is a good lock to the\r\ndoor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe report in its unmistakable St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s running script, and the\r\nbrown, yellow, and lake-daubed map, was on hand a few years ago (a\r\ncareless clerk filed it with the rough notes of E\xe2\x80\x99s second Seistan\r\nsurvey), but by now the pencil characters must be almost illegible. Kim\r\ntranslated it, sweating under the light of an oil-lamp, to Mahbub, the\r\nsecond day of their return-journey.\r\n\r\nThe Pathan rose and stooped over his dappled saddle-bags.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew it would be worthy a dress of honour, and so I made one ready,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cWere I Amir of Afghanistan (and some day we may see\r\nhim), I would fill thy mouth with gold.\xe2\x80\x9d He laid the garments formally\r\nat Kim\xe2\x80\x99s feet. There was a gold-embroidered Peshawur turban-cap, rising\r\nto a cone, and a big turban-cloth ending in a broad fringe of gold.\r\nThere was a Delhi embroidered waistcoat to slip over a milky white\r\nshirt, fastening to the right, ample and flowing; green pyjamas with\r\ntwisted silk waist-string; and that nothing might be lacking,\r\nrussia-leather slippers, smelling divinely, with arrogantly curled\r\ntips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon a Wednesday, and in the morning, to put on new clothes is\r\nauspicious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub solemnly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we must not forget the wicked\r\nfolk in the world. So!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe capped all the splendour, that was taking Kim\xe2\x80\x99s delighted breath\r\naway, with a mother-of-pearl, nickel-plated, self-extracting .450\r\nrevolver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had thought of a smaller bore, but reflected that this takes\r\nGovernment bullets. A man can always come by those\xe2\x80\x94especially across\r\nthe Border. Stand up and let me look.\xe2\x80\x9d He clapped Kim on the shoulder.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay you never be tired, Pathan! Oh, the hearts to be broken! Oh, the\r\neyes under the eyelashes, looking sideways!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim turned about, pointed his toes, stretched, and felt mechanically\r\nfor the moustache that was just beginning. Then he stooped towards\r\nMahbub\xe2\x80\x99s feet to make proper acknowledgment with fluttering,\r\nquick-patting hands; his heart too full for words. Mahbub forestalled\r\nand embraced him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy son, said he, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat need of words between us? But is not the little\r\ngun a delight? All six cartridges come out at one twist. It is borne in\r\nthe bosom next the skin, which, as it were, keeps it oiled. Never put\r\nit elsewhere, and please God, thou shalt some day kill a man with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Hai mai!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim ruefully. \xe2\x80\x9cIf a Sahib kills a man he is hanged in\r\nthe jail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue: but one pace beyond the Border, men are wiser. Put it away; but\r\nfill it first. Of what use is a gun unfed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I go back to the _madrissah_ I must return it. They do not allow\r\nlittle guns. Thou wilt keep it for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSon, I am wearied of that _madrissah_, where they take the best years\r\nof a man to teach him what he can only learn upon the Road. The folly\r\nof the Sahibs has neither top nor bottom. No matter. Maybe thy written\r\nreport shall save thee further bondage; and God He knows we need men\r\nmore and more in the Game.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey marched, jaw-bound against blowing sand, across the salt desert to\r\nJodhpur, where Mahbub and his handsome nephew Habib Ullah did much\r\ntrading; and then sorrowfully, in European clothes, which he was fast\r\noutgrowing, Kim went second-class to St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s. Three weeks later,\r\nColonel Creighton, pricing Tibetan ghost-daggers at Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s shop,\r\nfaced Mahbub Ali openly mutinous. Lurgan Sahib operated as support in\r\nreserve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe pony is made\xe2\x80\x94finished\xe2\x80\x94mouthed and paced, Sahib! From now on, day\r\nby day, he will lose his manners if he is kept at tricks. Drop the rein\r\non his back and let go,\xe2\x80\x9d said the horse-dealer. \xe2\x80\x9cWe need him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he is so young, Mahbub\xe2\x80\x94not more than sixteen\xe2\x80\x94is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was fifteen, I had shot my man and begot my man, Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou impenitent old heathen!\xe2\x80\x9d Creighton turned to Lurgan. The black\r\nbeard nodded assent to the wisdom of the Afghan\xe2\x80\x99s dyed scarlet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ should have used him long ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan. \xe2\x80\x9cThe younger the\r\nbetter. That is why I always have my really valuable jewels watched by\r\na child. You sent him to me to try. I tried him in every way: he is the\r\nonly boy I could not make to see things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the crystal\xe2\x80\x94in the ink-pool?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Mahbub.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Under my hand, as I told you. That has never happened before. It\r\nmeans that he is strong enough\xe2\x80\x94but you think it skittles, Colonel\r\nCreighton\xe2\x80\x94to make anyone do anything he wants. And that is three years\r\nago. I have taught him a good deal since, Colonel Creighton. I think\r\nyou waste him now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHmm! Maybe you\xe2\x80\x99re right. But, as you know, there is no Survey work for\r\nhim at present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him out let him go,\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cWho expects any colt to\r\ncarry heavy weight at first? Let him run with the caravans\xe2\x80\x94like our\r\nwhite camel-colts\xe2\x80\x94for luck. I would take him myself, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a little business where he would be most useful\xe2\x80\x94in the\r\nSouth,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan, with peculiar suavity, dropping his heavy blued\r\neyelids.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cE.23 has that in hand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Creighton quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe must not go down\r\nthere. Besides, he knows no Turki.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly tell him the shape and the smell of the letters we want and he\r\nwill bring them back,\xe2\x80\x9d Lurgan insisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. That is a man\xe2\x80\x99s job,\xe2\x80\x9d said Creighton.\r\n\r\nIt was a wry-necked matter of unauthorized and incendiary\r\ncorrespondence between a person who claimed to be the ultimate\r\nauthority in all matters of the Mohammedan religion throughout the\r\nworld, and a younger member of a royal house who had been brought to\r\nbook for kidnapping women within British territory.
