The Moslem\r\nArchbishop had been emphatic and over-arrogant; the young prince was\r\nmerely sulky at the curtailment of his privileges, but there was no\r\nneed he should continue a correspondence which might some day\r\ncompromise him. One letter indeed had been procured, but the finder was\r\nlater found dead by the roadside in the habit of an Arab trader, as\r\nE.23, taking up the work, duly reported.\r\n\r\nThese facts, and a few others not to be published, made both Mahbub and\r\nCreighton shake their heads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him go out with his Red Lama,\xe2\x80\x9d said the horse-dealer with visible\r\neffort. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is fond of the old man. He can learn his paces by the\r\nrosary at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have had some dealings with the old man\xe2\x80\x94by letter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colonel\r\nCreighton, smiling to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cWhither goes he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUp and down the land, as he has these three years. He seeks a River of\r\nHealing. God\xe2\x80\x99s curse upon all\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub checked himself. \xe2\x80\x9cHe beds down\r\nat the Temple of the Tirthankars or at Buddh Gaya when he is in from\r\nthe Road. Then he goes to see the boy at the _madrissah_, as we know\r\nfor the boy was punished for it twice or thrice. He is quite mad, but a\r\npeaceful man. I have met him. The Babu also has had dealings with him.\r\nWe have watched him for three years. Red Lamas are not so common in\r\nHind that one loses track.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBabus are very curious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lurgan meditatively. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what\r\nHurree Babu really wants? He wants to be made a member of the Royal\r\nSociety by taking ethnological notes. I tell you, I tell him about the\r\nlama everything which Mahbub and the boy have told me. Hurree Babu goes\r\ndown to Benares\xe2\x80\x94at his own expense, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Creighton briefly. He had paid Hurree\xe2\x80\x99s travelling\r\nexpenses, out of a most lively curiosity to learn what the lama might\r\nbe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he applies to the lama for information on lamaism, and\r\ndevil-dances, and spells and charms, several times in these few years.\r\nHoly Virgin! I could have told him all that yeears ago. I think Hurree\r\nBabu is getting too old for the Road. He likes better to collect\r\nmanners and customs information. Yes, he wants to be an FRS.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurree thinks well of the boy, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, very indeed\xe2\x80\x94we have had some pleasant evenings at my little\r\nplace\xe2\x80\x94but I think it would be waste to throw him away with Hurree on\r\nthe Ethnological side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for a first experience. How does that strike you, Mahbub? Let the\r\nboy run with the lama for six months. After that we can see. He will\r\nget experience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has it already, Sahib\xe2\x80\x94as a fish controls the water he swims in. But\r\nfor every reason it will be well to loose him from the school.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Creighton, half to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cHe can go with the\r\nlama, and if Hurree Babu cares to keep an eye on them so much the\r\nbetter. He won\xe2\x80\x99t lead the boy into any danger as Mahbub would.\r\nCurious\xe2\x80\x94his wish to be an F R S. Very human, too. He _is_ best on the\r\nEthnological side\xe2\x80\x94Hurree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo money and no preferment would have drawn Creighton from his work on\r\nthe Indian Survey, but deep in his heart also lay the ambition to write\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cF R S\xe2\x80\x9d after his name. Honours of a sort he knew could be obtained by\r\ningenuity and the help of friends, but, to the best of his belief,\r\nnothing save work\xe2\x80\x94papers representing a life of it\xe2\x80\x94took a man into the\r\nSociety which he had bombarded for years with monographs on strange\r\nAsiatic cults and unknown customs. Nine men out of ten would flee from\r\na Royal Society soiree in extremity of boredom; but Creighton was the\r\ntenth, and at times his soul yearned for the crowded rooms in easy\r\nLondon where silver-haired, bald-headed gentlemen who know nothing of\r\nthe Army move among spectroscopic experiments, the lesser plants of the\r\nfrozen tundras, electric flight-measuring machines, and apparatus for\r\nslicing into fractional millimetres the left eye of the female\r\nmosquito. By all right and reason, it was the Royal Geographical that\r\nshould have appealed to him, but men are as chancy as children in their\r\nchoice of playthings. So Creighton smiled, and thought the better of\r\nHurree Babu, moved by like desire.\r\n\r\nHe dropped the ghost-dagger and looked up at Mahbub.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow soon can we get the colt from the stable?\xe2\x80\x9d said the horse-dealer,\r\nreading his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHmm! If I withdraw him by order now\xe2\x80\x94what will he do, think you? I have\r\nnever before assisted at the teaching of such an one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will come to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub promptly. \xe2\x80\x9cLurgan Sahib and I will\r\nprepare him for the Road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo be it, then. For six months he shall run at his choice. But who\r\nwill be his sponsor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLurgan slightly inclined his head. \xe2\x80\x9cHe will not tell anything, if that\r\nis what you are afraid of, Colonel Creighton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only a boy, after all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe-es; but first, he has nothing to tell; and secondly, he knows what\r\nwould happen. Also, he is very fond of Mahbub, and of me a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill he draw pay?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded the practical horse-dealer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFood and water allowance only. Twenty rupees a month.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne advantage of the Secret Service is that it has no worrying audit.\r\nThat Service is ludicrously starved, of course, but the funds are\r\nadministered by a few men who do not call for vouchers or present\r\nitemized accounts. Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s eyes lighted with almost a Sikh\xe2\x80\x99s love of\r\nmoney. Even Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s impassive face changed. He considered the years to\r\ncome when Kim would have been entered and made to the Great Game that\r\nnever ceases day and night, throughout India. He foresaw honour and\r\ncredit in the mouths of a chosen few, coming to him from his pupil.\r\nLurgan Sahib had made E.23 what E.23 was, out of a bewildered,\r\nimpertinent, lying, little North-West Province man.\r\n\r\nBut the joy of these masters was pale and smoky beside the joy of Kim\r\nwhen St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s Head called him aside, with word that Colonel\r\nCreighton had sent for him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand, O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, that he has found you a place as an assistant\r\nchain-man in the Canal Department: that comes of taking up mathematics.\r\nIt is great luck for you, for you are only sixteen; but of course you\r\nunderstand that you do not become _pukka_ (permanent) till you have\r\npassed the autumn examination. So you must not think you are going out\r\ninto the world to enjoy yourself, or that your fortune is made. There\r\nis a great deal of hard work before you. Only, if you succeed in\r\nbecoming _pukka_, you can rise, you know, to four hundred and fifty a\r\nmonth.\xe2\x80\x9d Whereat the Principal gave him much good advice as to his\r\nconduct, and his manners, and his morals; and others, his elders, who\r\nhad not been wafted into billets, talked as only Anglo-Indian lads can,\r\nof favouritism and corruption. Indeed, young Cazalet, whose father was\r\na pensioner at Chunar, hinted very broadly that Colonel Creighton\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninterest in Kim was directly paternal; and Kim, instead of retaliating,\r\ndid not even use language. He was thinking of the immense fun to come,\r\nof Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s letter of the day before, all neatly written in English,\r\nmaking appointment for that afternoon in a house the very name of which\r\nwould have crisped the Principal\xe2\x80\x99s hair with horror...\r\n\r\nSaid Kim to Mahbub in Lucknow railway station that evening, above the\r\nluggage-scales: \xe2\x80\x9cI feared lest at the last, the roof would fall upon me\r\nand cheat me. It is indeed all finished, O my father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMahbub snapped his fingers to show the utterness of that end, and his\r\neyes blazed like red coals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen where is the pistol that I may wear it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSoftly! A half-year, to run without heel-ropes. I begged that much\r\nfrom Colonel Creighton Sahib. At twenty rupees a month. Old Red Hat\r\nknows that thou art coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will pay thee _dustoorie_ (commission) on my pay for three months,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Kim gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cYea, two rupees a month. But first we must get rid\r\nof these.\xe2\x80\x9d He plucked his thin linen trousers and dragged at his\r\ncollar. \xe2\x80\x9cI have brought with me all that I need on the Road. My trunk\r\nhas gone up to Lurgan Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho sends his salaams to thee\xe2\x80\x94Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLurgan Sahib is a very clever man. But what dost thou do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI go North again, upon the Great Game. What else? Is thy mind still\r\nset on following old Red Hat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not forget he made me that I am\xe2\x80\x94though he did not know it. Year by\r\nyear, he sent the money that taught me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would have done as much\xe2\x80\x94had it struck my thick head,\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub\r\ngrowled. \xe2\x80\x9cCome away. The lamps are lit now, and none will mark thee in\r\nthe bazar. We go to Huneefa\xe2\x80\x99s house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the way thither, Mahbub gave him much the same sort of advice as his\r\nmother gave to Lemuel, and curiously enough, Mahbub was exact to point\r\nout how Huneefa and her likes destroyed kings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I remember,\xe2\x80\x9d he quoted maliciously, \xe2\x80\x9cone who said, \xe2\x80\x98Trust a snake\r\nbefore an harlot, and an harlot before a Pathan, Mahbub Ali.\xe2\x80\x99 Now,\r\nexcepting as to Pathans, of whom I am one, all that is true. Most true\r\nis it in the Great Game, for it is by means of women that all plans\r\ncome to ruin and we lie out in the dawning with our throats cut. So it\r\nhappened to such a one.\xe2\x80\x9d He gave the reddest particulars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim paused before a filthy staircase that climbed to the\r\nwarm darkness of an upper chamber, in the ward that is behind Azim\r\nUllah\xe2\x80\x99s tobacco-shop. Those who know it call it The Birdcage\xe2\x80\x94it is so\r\nfull of whisperings and whistlings and chirrupings.\r\n\r\nThe room, with its dirty cushions and half-smoked hookahs, smelt\r\nabominably of stale tobacco. In one corner lay a huge and shapeless\r\nwoman clad in greenish gauzes, and decked, brow, nose, ear, neck,\r\nwrist, arm, waist, and ankle with heavy native jewellery. When she\r\nturned it was like the clashing of copper pots. A lean cat in the\r\nbalcony outside the window mewed hungrily. Kim checked, bewildered, at\r\nthe door-curtain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that the new stuff, Mahbub?\xe2\x80\x9d said Huneefa lazily, scarce troubling\r\nto remove the mouthpiece from her lips. \xe2\x80\x9cO Buktanoos!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94like most of her\r\nkind, she swore by the Djinns\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cO Buktanoos! He is very good to look\r\nupon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is part of the selling of the horse,\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub explained to Kim,\r\nwho laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have heard that talk since my Sixth Day,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, squatting by\r\nthe light. \xe2\x80\x9cWhither does it lead?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo protection. Tonight we change thy colour. This sleeping under roofs\r\nhas blanched thee like an almond. But Huneefa has the secret of a\r\ncolour that catches. No painting of a day or two. Also, we fortify thee\r\nagainst the chances of the Road. That is _my_ gift to thee, my son.\r\nTake out all metals on thee and lay them here. Make ready, Huneefa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim dragged forth his compass, Survey paint-box, and the new-filled\r\nmedicine-box. They had all accompanied his travels, and boylike he\r\nvalued them immensely.\r\n\r\nThe woman rose slowly and moved with her hands a little spread before\r\nher. Then Kim saw that she was blind. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d she muttered, \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nPathan speaks truth\xe2\x80\x94my colour does not go in a week or a month, and\r\nthose whom I protect are under strong guard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen one is far off and alone, it would not be well to grow blotched\r\nand leprous of a sudden,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen thou wast with me I could\r\noversee the matter. Besides, a Pathan is a fair-skin. Strip to the\r\nwaist now and look how thou art whitened.\xe2\x80\x9d Huneefa felt her way back\r\nfrom an inner room. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is no matter, she cannot see.\xe2\x80\x9d He took a pewter\r\nbowl from her ringed hand.\r\n\r\nThe dye-stuff showed blue and gummy. Kim experimented on the back of\r\nhis wrist, with a dab of cotton-wool; but Huneefa heard him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, \xe2\x80\x9cthe thing is not done thus, but with the proper\r\nceremonies. The colouring is the least part. I give thee the full\r\nprotection of the Road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Jadoo?_ (magic),\xe2\x80\x9dsaid Kim, with a half start. He did not like the\r\nwhite, sightless eyes. Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s hand on his neck bowed him to the\r\nfloor, nose within an inch of the boards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe still. No harm comes to thee, my son. I am thy sacrifice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe could not see what the woman was about, but heard the dish-clash of\r\nher jewellery for many minutes. A match lit up the darkness; he caught\r\nthe well-known purr and fizzle of grains of incense. Then the room\r\nfilled with smoke\xe2\x80\x94heavy aromatic, and stupefying. Through growing\r\ndrowse he heard the names of devils\xe2\x80\x94of Zulbazan, Son of Eblis, who\r\nlives in bazars and _paraos_, making all the sudden lewd wickedness of\r\nwayside halts; of Dulhan, invisible about mosques, the dweller among\r\nthe slippers of the faithful, who hinders folk from their prayers; and\r\nMusboot, Lord of lies and panic. Huneefa, now whispering in his ear,\r\nnow talking as from an immense distance, touched him with horrible soft\r\nfingers, but Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s grip never shifted from his neck till, relaxing\r\nwith a sigh, the boy lost his senses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah! How he fought! We should never have done it but for the drugs.\r\nThat was his white blood, I take it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub testily. \xe2\x80\x9cGo on with\r\nthe _dawut_ (invocation). Give him full Protection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_O Hearer! Thou that hearest with ears, be present. Listen, O\r\nHearer!_\xe2\x80\x9d Huneefa moaned, her dead eyes turned to the west. The dark\r\nroom filled with moanings and snortings.\r\n\r\nFrom the outer balcony, a ponderous figure raised a round bullet head\r\nand coughed nervously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not interrupt this ventriloquial necromanciss, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d it said\r\nin English. \xe2\x80\x9cI opine that it is very disturbing to you, but no\r\nenlightened observer is jolly-well upset.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c..._I will lay a plot for their ruin! O Prophet, bear with the\r\nunbelievers. Let them alone awhile!_\xe2\x80\x9d Huneefa\xe2\x80\x99s face, turned to the\r\nnorthward, worked horribly, and it was as though voices from the\r\nceiling answered her.\r\n\r\nHurree Babu returned to his note-book, balanced on the window-sill, but\r\nhis hand shook. Huneefa, in some sort of drugged ecstasy, wrenched\r\nherself to and fro as she sat cross-legged by Kim\xe2\x80\x99s still head, and\r\ncalled upon devil after devil, in the ancient order of the ritual,\r\nbinding them to avoid the boy\xe2\x80\x99s every action.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_With Him are the keys of the Secret Things! None knoweth them besides\r\nHimself He knoweth that which is in the dry land and in the sea!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAgain broke out the unearthly whistling responses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I apprehend it is not at all malignant in its operation?\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nBabu, watching the throat-muscles quiver and jerk as Huneefa spoke with\r\ntongues. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x94it is not likely that she has killed the boy? If so, I\r\ndecline to be witness at the trial .....What was the last hypothetical\r\ndevil mentioned?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBabuji,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub in the vernacular. \xe2\x80\x9cI have no regard for the\r\ndevils of Hind, but the Sons of Eblis are far otherwise, and whether\r\nthey be _jumalee_ (well-affected) or _jullalee_ (terrible) they love\r\nnot Kafirs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you think I had better go?\xe2\x80\x9d said Hurree Babu, half rising. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\r\nare, of course, dematerialized phenomena. Spencer says.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuneefa\xe2\x80\x99s crisis passed, as these things must, in a paroxysm of\r\nhowling, with a touch of froth at the lips. She lay spent and\r\nmotionless beside Kim, and the crazy voices ceased.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWah! That work is done. May the boy be better for it; and Huneefa is\r\nsurely a mistress of _dawut_. Help haul her aside, Babu. Do not be\r\nafraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow am I to fear the absolutely non-existent?\xe2\x80\x9d said Hurree Babu,\r\ntalking English to reassure himself. It is an awful thing still to\r\ndread the magic that you contemptuously investigate\xe2\x80\x94to collect\r\nfolk-lore for the Royal Society with a lively belief in all Powers of\r\nDarkness.\r\n\r\nMahbub chuckled. He had been out with Hurree on the Road ere now. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\r\nus finish the colouring,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cThe boy is well protected if\xe2\x80\x94if the\r\nLords of the Air have ears to hear. I am a _sufi_ (free-thinker), but\r\nwhen one can get blind-sides of a woman, a stallion, or a devil, why go\r\nround to invite a kick? Set him upon the way, Babu, and see that old\r\nRed Hat does not lead him beyond our reach. I must get back to my\r\nhorses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll raight,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hurree Babu. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is at present curious spectacle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAbout third cockcrow, Kim woke after a sleep of thousands of years.\r\nHuneefa, in her corner, snored heavily, but Mahbub was gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you were not frightened,\xe2\x80\x9d said an oily voice at his elbow. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsuperintended entire operation, which was most interesting from\r\nethnological point of view. It was high-class _dawut_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, recognizing Hurree Babu, who smiled ingratiatingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd also I had honour to bring down from Lurgan your present costume.\r\nI am not in the habit offeecially of carrying such gauds to\r\nsubordinates, but\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he giggled\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cyour case is noted as exceptional on the\r\nbooks. I hope Mr Lurgan will note my action.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim yawned and stretched himself. It was good to turn and twist within\r\nloose clothes once again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this?\xe2\x80\x9d He looked curiously at the heavy duffle-stuff loaded\r\nwith the scents of the far North.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho! That is inconspicuous dress of _chela_ attached to service of\r\nlamaistic lama. _Com_plete in every particular,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hurree Babu,\r\nrolling into the balcony to clean his teeth at a goglet. \xe2\x80\x9cI am of\r\nopeenion it is not your old gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s precise releegion, but rather\r\nsub-variant of same. I have contributed rejected notes To Whom It May\r\nConcern: _Asiatic Quarterly Review_ on these subjects. Now it is\r\ncurious that the old gentleman himself is totally devoid of\r\nreleegiosity. He is not a dam\xe2\x80\x99 particular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHurree Babu held up his hand to show he was engaged in the prescribed\r\nrites that accompany tooth-cleaning and such things among decently bred\r\nBengalis. Then he recited in English an Arya-Somaj prayer of a\r\ntheistical nature, and stuffed his mouth with _pan_ and betel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah yes. I have met him several times at Benares, and also at Buddh\r\nGaya, to interrogate him on releegious points and devil-worship. He is\r\npure agnostic\xe2\x80\x94same as me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuneefa stirred in her sleep, and Hurree Babu jumped nervously to the\r\ncopper incense-burner, all black and discoloured in morning-light,\r\nrubbed a finger in the accumulated lamp-black, and drew it diagonally\r\nacross his face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho has died in thy house?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Kim in the vernacular.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone. But she may have the Evil Eye\xe2\x80\x94that sorceress,\xe2\x80\x9d the Babu replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dost thou do now, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will set thee on thy way to Benares, if thou goest thither, and tell\r\nthee what must be known by Us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI go. At what hour runs the _te-rain?_\xe2\x80\x9d He rose to his feet, looked\r\nround the desolate chamber and at the yellow-wax face of Huneefa as the\r\nlow sun stole across the floor. \xe2\x80\x9cIs there money to be paid that witch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. She has charmed thee against all devils and all dangers in the\r\nname of her devils. It was Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s desire.\xe2\x80\x9d In English: \xe2\x80\x9cHe is highly\r\nobsolete, _I_ think, to indulge in such supersteetion. Why, it is all\r\nventri_lo_quy. Belly-speak\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim snapped his fingers mechanically to avert whatever evil\xe2\x80\x94Mahbub, he\r\nknew, meditated none\xe2\x80\x94might have crept in through Huneefa\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nministrations; and Hurree giggled once more. But as he crossed the room\r\nhe was careful not to step in Huneefa\xe2\x80\x99s blotched, squat shadow on the\r\nboards. Witches\xe2\x80\x94when their time is on them\xe2\x80\x94can lay hold of the heels of\r\na man\xe2\x80\x99s soul if he does that.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you must well listen,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Babu when they were in the fresh\r\nair. \xe2\x80\x9cPart of these ceremonies which we witnessed they include supply\r\nof effeecient amulet to those of our Department. If you feel in your\r\nneck you will find one small silver amulet, verree cheap. That is\r\n_ours_. Do you understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah yes, _hawa-dilli_ (a heart-lifter),\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, feeling at his\r\nneck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuneefa she makes them for two rupees twelve annas with\xe2\x80\x94oh, all sorts\r\nof exorcisms. They are quite common, except they are partially black\r\nenamel, and there is a paper inside each one full of names of local\r\nsaints and such things. _Thatt_ is Huneefa\xe2\x80\x99s look-out, you see? Huneefa\r\nmakes them onlee for us, but in case she does not, when we get them we\r\nput in, before issue, one small piece of turquoise. Mr Lurgan he gives\r\nthem. There is no other source of supply; but it was me invented all\r\nthis. It is strictly unoffeecial of course, but convenient for\r\nsubordinates. Colonel Creighton he does not know. He is European. The\r\nturquoise is wrapped in the paper ... Yes, that is road to railway\r\nstation ... Now suppose you go with the lama, or with me, I hope, some\r\nday, or with Mahbub. Suppose we get into a dam\xe2\x80\x99-tight place. I am a\r\nfearful man\xe2\x80\x94most fearful\xe2\x80\x94but I tell you I have been in dam\xe2\x80\x99-tight\r\nplaces more than hairs on my head. You say: \xe2\x80\x98I am Son of the Charm.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nVerree good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand quite. We must not be heard talking English here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all raight. I am only Babu showing off my English to you. All\r\nwe Babus talk English to show off;\xe2\x80\x9d said Hurree, flinging his\r\nshoulder-cloth jauntily. \xe2\x80\x9cAs I was about to say, \xe2\x80\x98Son of the Charm\xe2\x80\x99\r\nmeans that you may be member of the _Sat Bhai_\xe2\x80\x94the Seven Brothers,\r\nwhich is Hindi _and_ Tantric. It is popularly supposed to be extinct\r\nSociety, but I have written notes to show it is still extant. You see,\r\nit is all my invention. Verree good. _Sat Bhai_ has many members, and\r\nperhaps before they jolly-well-cut-your-throat they may give you just a\r\nchance of life. That is useful, anyhow. And moreover, these foolish\r\nnatives\xe2\x80\x94if they are not too excited\xe2\x80\x94they always stop to think before\r\nthey kill a man who says he belongs to any speecific organization. You\r\nsee? You say then when you are in tight place, \xe2\x80\x98I am Son of the Charm\xe2\x80\x99,\r\nand you get\xe2\x80\x94perhaps\xe2\x80\x94ah\xe2\x80\x94your second wind. That is only in extreme\r\ninstances, or to open negotiations with a stranger. Can you quite see?\r\nVerree good. But suppose now, I, or any one of the Department, come to\r\nyou dressed quite different. You would not know me at all unless I\r\nchoose, I bet you. Some day I will prove it. I come as Ladakhi\r\ntrader\xe2\x80\x94oh, anything\xe2\x80\x94and I say to you: \xe2\x80\x98You want to buy precious\r\nstones?\xe2\x80\x99 You say: \xe2\x80\x98Do I look like a man who buys precious stones?\xe2\x80\x99 Then\r\nI say: \xe2\x80\x98Even verree poor man can buy a turquoise or _tarkeean_.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is _kichree_\xe2\x80\x94vegetable curry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it is. You say: \xe2\x80\x98Let me see the _tarkeean_.\xe2\x80\x99 Then I say: \xe2\x80\x98It\r\nwas cooked by a woman, and perhaps it is bad for your caste.\xe2\x80\x99 Then you\r\nsay: \xe2\x80\x98There is no caste when men go to\xe2\x80\x94look for _tarkeean_.\xe2\x80\x99 You stop a\r\nlittle between those words, \xe2\x80\x98to\xe2\x80\x94look\xe2\x80\x99. That is thee whole secret. The\r\nlittle stop before the words.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim repeated the test-sentence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all right. Then I will show you my turquoise if there is time,\r\nand then you know who I am, and then we exchange views and documents\r\nand those-all things. And so it is with any other man of us. We talk\r\nsometimes about turquoises and sometimes about _tarkeean_, but always\r\nwith that little stop in the words. It is verree easy. First, \xe2\x80\x98Son of\r\nthe Charm\xe2\x80\x99, if you are in a tight place. Perhaps that may help\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94perhaps not. Then what I have told you about the _tarkeean_, if you\r\nwant to transact offeecial business with a strange man. Of course, at\r\npresent, you have no offeecial business. You are\xe2\x80\x94ah ha!\xe2\x80\x94supernumerary\r\non probation. Quite unique specimen. If you were Asiatic of birth you\r\nmight be employed right off; but this half-year of leave is to make you\r\nde-Englishized, you see? The lama he expects you, because I have\r\ndemi-offeecially informed him you have passed all your examinations,\r\nand will soon obtain Government appointment. Oh ho! You are on\r\nacting-allowance, you see: so if you are called upon to help Sons of\r\nthe Charm mind you jolly-well try. Now I shall say good-bye, my dear\r\nfellow, and I hope you\xe2\x80\x94ah\xe2\x80\x94will come out top-side all raight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHurree Babu stepped back a pace or two into the crowd at the entrance\r\nof Lucknow station and\xe2\x80\x94was gone. Kim drew a deep breath and hugged\r\nhimself all over. The nickel-plated revolver he could feel in the bosom\r\nof his sad-coloured robe, the amulet was on his neck; begging-gourd,\r\nrosary, and ghost-dagger (Mr Lurgan had forgotten nothing) were all to\r\nhand, with medicine, paint-box, and compass, and in a worn old\r\npurse-belt embroidered with porcupine-quill patterns lay a month\xe2\x80\x99s pay.\r\nKings could be no richer. He bought sweetmeats in a leaf-cup from a\r\nHindu trader, and ate them with glad rapture till a policeman ordered\r\nhim off the steps.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\n\r\n\r\nGive the man who is not made\r\n    To his trade\r\nSwords to fling and catch again,\r\nCoins to ring and snatch again,\r\nMen to harm and cure again,\r\nSnakes to charm and lure again\xe2\x80\x94\r\nHe\xe2\x80\x99ll be hurt by his own blade,\r\nBy his serpents disobeyed,\r\nBy his clumsiness bewrayed,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nBy the people mocked to scorn\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    So \xe2\x80\x99tis not with juggler born!\r\nPinch of dust or withered flower,\r\n    Chance-flung fruit or borrowed staff,\r\nServe his need and shore his power,\r\n    Bind the spell, or loose the laugh!\r\n\r\n            _But a man who, etc._, Op. 15\r\n\r\nFollowed a sudden natural reaction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow am I alone\xe2\x80\x94all alone,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought. \xe2\x80\x9cIn all India is no one so\r\nalone as I! If I die today, who shall bring the news\xe2\x80\x94and to whom? If I\r\nlive and God is good, there will be a price upon my head, for I am a\r\nSon of the Charm\xe2\x80\x94I, Kim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA very few white people, but many Asiatics, can throw themselves into a\r\nmazement as it were by repeating their own names over and over again to\r\nthemselves, letting the mind go free upon speculation as to what is\r\ncalled personal identity. When one grows older, the power, usually,\r\ndeparts, but while it lasts it may descend upon a man at any moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is Kim\xe2\x80\x94Kim\xe2\x80\x94Kim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe squatted in a corner of the clanging waiting-room, rapt from all\r\nother thoughts; hands folded in lap, and pupils contracted to\r\npin-points. In a minute\xe2\x80\x94in another half-second\xe2\x80\x94he felt he would arrive\r\nat the solution of the tremendous puzzle; but here, as always happens,\r\nhis mind dropped away from those heights with a rush of a wounded bird,\r\nand passing his hand before his eyes, he shook his head.\r\n\r\nA long-haired Hindu _bairagi_ (holy man), who had just bought a ticket,\r\nhalted before him at that moment and stared intently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI also have lost it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is one of the Gates to the\r\nWay, but for me it has been shut many years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the talk?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, abashed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou wast wondering there in thy spirit what manner of thing thy soul\r\nmight be. The seizure came of a sudden. _I_ know. Who should know but\r\nI? Whither goest thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToward Kashi [Benares].\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are no Gods there. I have proved them. I go to Prayag\r\n[Allahabad] for the fifth time\xe2\x80\x94seeking the Road to Enlightenment. Of\r\nwhat faith art thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI too am a Seeker,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, using one of the lama\xe2\x80\x99s pet words.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThough\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he forgot his Northern dress for the moment\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthough Allah\r\nalone knoweth what I seek.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old fellow slipped the _bairagi\xe2\x80\x99s_ crutch under his armpit and sat\r\ndown on a patch of ruddy leopard\xe2\x80\x99s skin as Kim rose at the call for the\r\nBenares train.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo in hope, little brother,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a long road to the feet\r\nof the One; but thither do we all travel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim did not feel so lonely after this, and ere he had sat out twenty\r\nmiles in the crowded compartment, was cheering his neighbours with a\r\nstring of most wonderful yarns about his own and his master\xe2\x80\x99s magical\r\ngifts.\r\n\r\nBenares struck him as a peculiarly filthy city, though it was pleasant\r\nto find how his cloth was respected. At least one-third of the\r\npopulation prays eternally to some group or other of the many million\r\ndeities, and so reveres every sort of holy man. Kim was guided to the\r\nTemple of the Tirthankars, about a mile outside the city, near Sarnath,\r\nby a chance-met Punjabi farmer\xe2\x80\x94a Kamboh from Jullundur-way who had\r\nappealed in vain to every God of his homestead to cure his small son,\r\nand was trying Benares as a last resort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art from the North?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, shouldering through the press of\r\nthe narrow, stinking streets much like his own pet bull at home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, I know the Punjab. My mother was a pahareen, but my father came\r\nfrom Amritzar\xe2\x80\x94by Jandiala,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, oiling his ready tongue for the\r\nneeds of the Road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJandiala\xe2\x80\x94Jullundur? Oho! Then we be neighbours in some sort, as it\r\nwere.\xe2\x80\x9d He nodded tenderly to the wailing child in his arms. \xe2\x80\x9cWhom dost\r\nthou serve?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA most holy man at the Temple of the Tirthankers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are all most holy and\xe2\x80\x94most greedy,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jat with bitterness.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have walked the pillars and trodden the temples till my feet are\r\nflayed, and the child is no whit better. And the mother being sick too\r\n... Hush, then, little one ... We changed his name when the fever came.\r\nWe put him into girl\xe2\x80\x99s clothes. There was nothing we did not do,\r\nexcept\xe2\x80\x94I said to his mother when she bundled me off to Benares\xe2\x80\x94she\r\nshould have come with me\xe2\x80\x94I _said_ Sakhi Sarwar Sultan would serve us\r\nbest. We know His generosity, but these down-country Gods are\r\nstrangers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe child turned on the cushion of the huge corded arms and looked at\r\nKim through heavy eyelids.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd was it all worthless?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim asked, with easy interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll worthless\xe2\x80\x94all worthless,\xe2\x80\x9d said the child, lips cracking with\r\nfever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gods have given him a good mind, at least\xe2\x80\x9d said the father\r\nproudly. \xe2\x80\x9cTo think he should have listened so cleverly. Yonder is thy\r\nTemple. Now I am a poor man\xe2\x80\x94many priests have dealt with me\xe2\x80\x94but my son\r\nis my son, and if a gift to thy master can cure him\xe2\x80\x94I am at my very\r\nwits\xe2\x80\x99 end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim considered for a while, tingling with pride. Three years ago he\r\nwould have made prompt profit on the situation and gone his way without\r\na thought; but now, the very respect the Jat paid him proved that he\r\nwas a man. Moreover, he had tasted fever once or twice already, and\r\nknew enough to recognize starvation when he saw it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCall him forth and I will give him a bond on my best yoke, so that the\r\nchild is cured.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim halted at the carved outer door of the temple. A white-clad Oswal\r\nbanker from Ajmir, his sins of usury new wiped out, asked him what he\r\ndid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am _chela_ to Teshoo Lama, an Holy One from Bhotiyal\xe2\x80\x94within there.\r\nHe bade me come. I wait. Tell him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not forget the child,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the importunate Jat over his shoulder,\r\nand then bellowed in Punjabi; \xe2\x80\x9cO Holy One\xe2\x80\x94O disciple of the Holy One\xe2\x80\x94O\r\nGods above all the Worlds\xe2\x80\x94behold affliction sitting at the gate!\xe2\x80\x9d That\r\ncry is so common in Benares that the passers never turned their heads.\r\n\r\nThe Oswal, at peace with mankind, carried the message into the darkness\r\nbehind him, and the easy, uncounted Eastern minutes slid by; for the\r\nlama was asleep in his cell, and no priest would wake him. When the\r\nclick of his rosary again broke the hush of the inner court where the\r\ncalm images of the Arhats stand, a novice whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cThy _chela_ is\r\nhere,\xe2\x80\x9d and the old man strode forth, forgetting the end of that prayer.\r\n\r\nHardly had the tall figure shown in the doorway than the Jat ran before\r\nhim, and, lifting up the child, cried: \xe2\x80\x9cLook upon this, Holy One; and\r\nif the Gods will, he lives\xe2\x80\x94he lives!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe fumbled in his waist-belt and drew out a small silver coin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is now?\xe2\x80\x9d The lama\xe2\x80\x99s eyes turned to Kim. It was noticeable he\r\nspoke far clearer Urdu than long ago, under ZamZammah; but father would\r\nallow no private talk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no more than a fever,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cThe child is not well fed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe sickens at everything, and his mother is not here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it be permitted, I may cure, Holy One.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! Have they made thee a healer? Wait here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, and he\r\nsat down by the Jat upon the lowest step of the temple, while Kim,\r\nlooking out of the corner of his eyes, slowly opened the little\r\nbetel-box. He had dreamed dreams at school of returning to the lama as\r\na Sahib\xe2\x80\x94of chaffing the old man before he revealed himself\xe2\x80\x94boy\xe2\x80\x99s dreams\r\nall. There was more drama in this abstracted, brow-puckered search\r\nthrough the tabloid-bottles, with a pause here and there for thought\r\nand a muttered invocation between whiles. Quinine he had in tablets,\r\nand dark brown meat-lozenges\xe2\x80\x94beef most probably, but that was not _his_\r\nbusiness. The little thing would not eat, but it sucked at a lozenge\r\ngreedily, and said it liked the salt taste.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake then these six.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim handed them to the man. \xe2\x80\x9cPraise the Gods,\r\nand boil three in milk; other three in water. After he has drunk the\r\nmilk give him this\xe2\x80\x9d (it was the half of a quinine pill), \xe2\x80\x9cand wrap him\r\nwarm. Give him the water of the other three, and the other half of this\r\nwhite pill when he wakes. Meantime, here is another brown medicine that\r\nhe may suck at on the way home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGods, what wisdom!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Kamboh, snatching.\r\n\r\nIt was as much as Kim could remember of his own treatment in a bout of\r\nautumn malaria\xe2\x80\x94if you except the patter that he added to impress the\r\nlama.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow go! Come again in the morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the price\xe2\x80\x94the price,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jat, and threw back his sturdy\r\nshoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cMy son is my son. Now that he will be whole again, how\r\nshall I go back to his mother and say I took help by the wayside and\r\ndid not even give a bowl of curds in return?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are alike, these Jats,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim softly. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Jat stood on his\r\ndunghill and the King\xe2\x80\x99s elephants went by. \xe2\x80\x98O driver,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98what\r\nwill you sell those little donkeys for?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Jat burst into a roar of laughter, stifled with apologies to the\r\nlama. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the saying of my own country the very talk of it. So are\r\nwe Jats all. I will come tomorrow with the child; and the blessing of\r\nthe Gods of the Homesteads\xe2\x80\x94who are good little Gods\xe2\x80\x94be on you both ...\r\nNow, son, we grow strong again. Do not spit it out, little Princeling!\r\nKing of my Heart, do not spit it out, and we shall be strong men,\r\nwrestlers and club-wielders, by morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe moved away, crooning and mumbling. The lama turned to Kim, and all\r\nthe loving old soul of him looked out through his narrow eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo heal the sick is to acquire merit; but first one gets knowledge.\r\nThat was wisely done, O Friend of all the World.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was made wise by thee, Holy One,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, forgetting the little\r\nplay just ended; forgetting St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s; forgetting his white blood;\r\nforgetting even the Great Game as he stooped, Mohammedan-fashion, to\r\ntouch his master\xe2\x80\x99s feet in the dust of the Jain temple. \xe2\x80\x9cMy teaching I\r\nowe to thee. I have eaten thy bread three years. My time is finished. I\r\nam loosed from the schools. I come to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHerein is my reward. Enter! Enter! And is all well?\xe2\x80\x9d They passed to\r\nthe inner court, where the afternoon sun sloped golden across. \xe2\x80\x9cStand\r\nthat I may see. So!\xe2\x80\x9d He peered critically. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is no longer a child,\r\nbut a man, ripened in wisdom, walking as a physician. I did well\xe2\x80\x94I did\r\nwell when I gave thee up to the armed men on that black night. Dost\r\nthou remember our first day under Zam-Zammah?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cDost thou remember when I leapt off the carriage the\r\nfirst day I went to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gates of Learning? Truly. And the day that we ate the cakes\r\ntogether at the back of the river by Nucklao. Aha! Many times hast thou\r\nbegged for me, but that day I begged for thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood reason,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cI was then a scholar in the Gates of\r\nLearning, and attired as a Sahib. Do not forget, Holy One,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on\r\nplayfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI am still a Sahib\xe2\x80\x94by thy favour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. And a Sahib in most high esteem. Come to my cell, _chela_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is that known to thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cFirst by means of letters from the kindly priest whom\r\nwe met in the camp of armed men; but he is now gone to his own country,\r\nand I sent the money to his brother.\xe2\x80\x9d Colonel Creighton, who had\r\nsucceeded to the trusteeship when Father Victor went to England with\r\nthe Mavericks, was hardly the Chaplain\xe2\x80\x99s brother. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I do not well\r\nunderstand Sahibs\xe2\x80\x99 letters. They must be interpreted to me. I chose a\r\nsurer way. Many times when I returned from my Search to this Temple,\r\nwhich has always been a nest to me, there came one seeking\r\nEnlightenment\xe2\x80\x94a man from Leh\xe2\x80\x94that had been, he said, a Hindu, but\r\nwearied of all those Gods.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama pointed to the Arhats.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fat man?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, a twinkle in his eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery fat; but I perceived in a little his mind was wholly given up to\r\nuseless things\xe2\x80\x94such as devils and charms and the form and fashion of\r\nour tea-drinkings in the monasteries, and by what road we initiated the\r\nnovices. A man abounding in questions; but he was a friend of thine,\r\n_chela_. He told me that thou wast on the road to much honour as a\r\nscribe. And I see thou art a physician.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that am I\xe2\x80\x94a scribe, when I am a Sahib, but it is set aside when I\r\ncome as thy disciple. I have accomplished the years appointed for a\r\nSahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs it were a novice?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, nodding his head. \xe2\x80\x9cArt thou freed\r\nfrom the schools? I would not have thee unripe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am all free. In due time I take service under the Government as a\r\nscribe\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot as a warrior. That is well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut first I come to wander with thee. Therefore I am here. Who begs\r\nfor thee, these days?\xe2\x80\x9d he went on quickly. The ice was thin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery often I beg myself; but, as thou knowest, I am seldom here,\r\nexcept when I come to look again at my disciple. From one end to\r\nanother of Hind have I travelled afoot and in the _te-rain_. A great\r\nand a wonderful land! But here, when I put in, is as though I were in\r\nmy own Bhotiyal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked round the little clean cell complacently. A low cushion gave\r\nhim a seat, on which he had disposed himself in the cross-legged\r\nattitude of the Bodhisat emerging from meditation; a black teak-wood\r\ntable, not twenty inches high, set with copper tea-cups, was before\r\nhim. In one corner stood a tiny altar, also of heavily carved teak,\r\nbearing a copper-gilt image of the seated Buddha and fronted by a lamp,\r\nan incense-holder, and a pair of copper flower-pots.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Keeper of the Images in the Wonder House acquired merit by giving\r\nme these a year since,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, following Kim\xe2\x80\x99s eye. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen one is far\r\nfrom one\xe2\x80\x99s own land such things carry remembrance; and we must\r\nreverence the Lord for that He showed the Way. See!\xe2\x80\x9d He pointed to a\r\ncuriously-built mound of coloured rice crowned with a fantastic metal\r\nornament. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was Abbot in my own place\xe2\x80\x94before I came to better\r\nknowledge I made that offering daily. It is the Sacrifice of the\r\nUniverse to the Lord. Thus do we of Bhotiyal offer all the world daily\r\nto the Excellent Law. And I do it even now, though I know that the\r\nExcellent One is beyond all pinchings and pattings.\xe2\x80\x9d He snuffed from\r\nhis gourd.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is well done, Holy One,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim murmured, sinking at ease on the\r\ncushions, very happy and rather tired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd also,\xe2\x80\x9d the old man chuckled, \xe2\x80\x9cI write pictures of the Wheel of\r\nLife. Three days to a picture. I was busied on it\xe2\x80\x94or it may be I shut\r\nmy eyes a little\xe2\x80\x94when they brought word of thee. It is good to have\r\nthee here: I will show thee my art\xe2\x80\x94not for pride\xe2\x80\x99s sake, but because\r\nthou must learn. The Sahibs have not _all_ this world\xe2\x80\x99s wisdom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drew from under the table a sheet of strangely scented yellow\r\nChinese paper, the brushes, and slab of Indian ink. In cleanest,\r\nseverest outline he had traced the Great Wheel with its six spokes,\r\nwhose centre is the conjoined Hog, Snake, and Dove (Ignorance, Anger,\r\nand Lust), and whose compartments are all the Heavens and Hells, and\r\nall the chances of human life. Men say that the Bodhisat Himself first\r\ndrew it with grains of rice upon dust, to teach His disciples the cause\r\nof things. Many ages have crystallized it into a most wonderful\r\nconvention crowded with hundreds of little figures whose every line\r\ncarries a meaning. Few can translate the picture-parable; there are not\r\ntwenty in all the world who can draw it surely without a copy: of those\r\nwho can both draw and expound are but three.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a little learned to draw,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cBut this is a marvel\r\nbeyond marvels.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have written it for many years,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cTime was when I\r\ncould write it all between one lamp-lighting and the next. I will teach\r\nthee the art\xe2\x80\x94after due preparation; and I will show thee the meaning of\r\nthe Wheel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe take the Road, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Road and our Search. I was but waiting for thee. It was made plain\r\nto me in a hundred dreams\xe2\x80\x94notably one that came upon the night of the\r\nday that the Gates of Learning first shut that without thee I should\r\nnever find my River. Again and again, as thou knowest, I put this from\r\nme, fearing an illusion. Therefore I would not take thee with me that\r\nday at Lucknow, when we ate the cakes. I would not take thee till the\r\ntime was ripe and auspicious. From the Hills to the Sea, from the Sea\r\nto the Hills have I gone, but it was vain. Then I remembered the\r\n_J\xc3\xa2taka_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe told Kim the story of the elephant with the leg-iron, as he had told\r\nit so often to the Jam priests.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFurther testimony is not needed,\xe2\x80\x9d he ended serenely. \xe2\x80\x9cThou wast sent\r\nfor an aid. That aid removed, my Search came to naught. Therefore we\r\nwill go out again together, and our Search sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhither go we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat matters, Friend of all the World? The Search, I say, is sure. If\r\nneed be, the River will break from the ground before us. I acquired\r\nmerit when I sent thee to the Gates of Learning, and gave thee the\r\njewel that is Wisdom. Thou didst return, I saw even now, a follower of\r\nSakyamuni, the Physician, whose altars are many in Bhotiyal. It is\r\nsufficient. We are together, and all things are as they were\xe2\x80\x94Friend of\r\nall the World\xe2\x80\x94Friend of the Stars\xe2\x80\x94my _chela!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen they talked of matters secular; but it was noticeable that the\r\nlama never demanded any details of life at St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s, nor showed the\r\nfaintest curiosity as to the manners and customs of Sahibs. His mind\r\nmoved all in the past, and he revived every step of their wonderful\r\nfirst journey together, rubbing his hands and chuckling, till it\r\npleased him to curl himself up into the sudden sleep of old age.\r\n\r\nKim watched the last dusty sunshine fade out of the court, and played\r\nwith his ghost-dagger and rosary. The clamour of Benares, oldest of all\r\nearth\xe2\x80\x99s cities awake before the Gods, day and night, beat round the\r\nwalls as the sea\xe2\x80\x99s roar round a breakwater. Now and again, a Jain\r\npriest crossed the court, with some small offering to the images, and\r\nswept the path about him lest by chance he should take the life of a\r\nliving thing. A lamp twinkled, and there followed the sound of a\r\nprayer. Kim watched the stars as they rose one after another in the\r\nstill, sticky dark, till he fell asleep at the foot of the altar. That\r\nnight he dreamed in Hindustani, with never an English word...\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoly One, there is the child to whom we gave the medicine,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nabout three o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning, when the lama, also waking from\r\ndreams, would have fared forth on pilgrimage. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Jat will be here at\r\nthe light.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am well answered. In my haste I would have done a wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d He sat\r\ndown on the cushions and returned to his rosary. \xe2\x80\x9cSurely old folk are\r\nas children,\xe2\x80\x9d he said pathetically. \xe2\x80\x9cThey desire a matter\xe2\x80\x94behold, it\r\nmust be done at once, or they fret and weep! Many times when I was upon\r\nthe Road I have been ready to stamp with my feet at the hindrance of an\r\nox-cart in the way, or a mere cloud of dust. It was not so when I was a\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94a long time ago. None the less it is wrongful\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou art indeed old, Holy One.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe thing was done. A Cause was put out into the world, and, old or\r\nyoung, sick or sound, knowing or unknowing, who can rein in the effect\r\nof that Cause? Does the Wheel hang still if a child spin it\xe2\x80\x94or a\r\ndrunkard? _Chela_, this is a great and a terrible world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it good,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim yawned. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is there to eat? I have not eaten\r\nsince yesterday even.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten thy need. Yonder is good Bhotiyal tea and cold rice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe cannot walk far on such stuff.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim felt all the European\xe2\x80\x99s lust\r\nfor flesh-meat, which is not accessible in a Jain temple. Yet, instead\r\nof going out at once with the begging-bowl, he stayed his stomach on\r\nslabs of cold rice till the full dawn. It brought the farmer, voluble,\r\nstuttering with gratitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the night the fever broke and the sweat came,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cFeel\r\nhere\xe2\x80\x94his skin is fresh and new! He esteemed the salt lozenges, and took\r\nmilk with greed.\xe2\x80\x9d He drew the cloth from the child\xe2\x80\x99s face, and it\r\nsmiled sleepily at Kim. A little knot of Jain priests, silent but\r\nall-observant, gathered by the temple door. They knew, and Kim knew\r\nthat they knew, how the old lama had met his disciple. Being courteous\r\nfolk, they had not obtruded themselves overnight by presence, word, or\r\ngesture. Wherefore Kim repaid them as the sun rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank the Gods of the Jains, brother,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, not knowing how those\r\nGods were named. \xe2\x80\x9cThe fever is indeed broken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook! See!\xe2\x80\x9d The lama beamed in the background upon his hosts of three\r\nyears. \xe2\x80\x9cWas there ever such a _chela?_ He follows our Lord the Healer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow the Jains officially recognize all the Gods of the Hindu creed, as\r\nwell as the Lingam and the Snake. They wear the Brahminical thread;\r\nthey adhere to every claim of Hindu caste-law. But, because they knew\r\nand loved the lama, because he was an old man, because he sought the\r\nWay, because he was their guest, and because he collogued long of\r\nnights with the head-priest\xe2\x80\x94as free-thinking a metaphysician as ever\r\nsplit one hair into seventy\xe2\x80\x94they murmured assent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemember,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim bent over the child\xe2\x80\x94. \xe2\x80\x9cthis trouble may come again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if thou hast the proper spell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut in a little while we go away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama to all the Jains. \xe2\x80\x9cWe go now together upon the\r\nSearch whereof I have often spoken. I waited till my _chela_ was ripe.\r\nBehold him! We go North. Never again shall I look upon this place of my\r\nrest, O people of good will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I am not a beggar.\xe2\x80\x9d The cultivator rose to his feet, clutching the\r\nchild.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe still. Do not trouble the Holy One,\xe2\x80\x9d a priest cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cMeet us again under the big railway bridge, and\r\nfor the sake of all the Gods of our Punjab, bring food\xe2\x80\x94curry, pulse,\r\ncakes fried in fat, and sweetmeats. Specially sweetmeats. Be swift!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pallor of hunger suited Kim very well as he stood, tall and slim,\r\nin his sand-coloured, sweeping robes, one hand on his rosary and the\r\nother in the attitude of benediction, faithfully copied from the lama.\r\nAn English observer might have said that he looked rather like the\r\nyoung saint of a stained-glass window, whereas he was but a growing lad\r\nfaint with emptiness.\r\n\r\nLong and formal were the farewells, thrice ended and thrice renewed.\r\nThe Seeker\xe2\x80\x94he who had invited the lama to that haven from far-away\r\nTibet, a silver-faced, hairless ascetic\xe2\x80\x94took no part in it, but\r\nmeditated, as always, alone among the images. The others were very\r\nhuman; pressing small comforts upon the old man\xe2\x80\x94a betel-box, a fine new\r\niron pencase, a food-bag, and such-like\xe2\x80\x94warning him against the dangers\r\nof the world without, and prophesying a happy end to the Search.\r\nMeantime Kim, lonelier than ever, squatted on the steps, and swore to\r\nhimself in the language of St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is my own fault,\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded. \xe2\x80\x9cWith Mahbub, I ate Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbread, or Lurgan Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s. At St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s, three meals a day. Here I\r\nmust jolly-well look out for myself. Besides, I am not in good\r\ntraining. _How_ I could eat a plate of beef now! ... Is it finished,\r\nHoly One?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama, both hands raised, intoned a final blessing in ornate\r\nChinese. \xe2\x80\x9cI must lean on thy shoulder,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, as the temple gates\r\nclosed. \xe2\x80\x9cWe grow stiff, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe weight of a six-foot man is not light to steady through miles of\r\ncrowded streets, and Kim, loaded down with bundles and packages for the\r\nway, was glad to reach the shadow of the railway bridge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere we eat,\xe2\x80\x9d he said resolutely, as the Kamboh, blue-robed and\r\nsmiling, hove in sight, a basket in one hand and the child in the\r\nother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFall to, Holy Ones!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried from fifty yards. (They were by the\r\nshoal under the first bridge-span, out of sight of hungry priests.)\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRice and good curry, cakes all warm and well scented with _hing_\r\n(asaf\xc5\x93tida), curds and sugar. King of my fields,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94this to the small\r\nson\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9clet us show these holy men that we Jats of Jullundur can pay a\r\nservice ... I had heard the Jains would eat nothing that they had not\r\ncooked, but truly\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he looked away politely over the broad river\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhere\r\nthere is no eye there is no caste.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd we,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, turning his back and heaping a leafplatter for the\r\nlama, \xe2\x80\x9care beyond all castes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey gorged themselves on the good food in silence. Nor till he had\r\nlicked the last of the sticky sweetstuff from his little finger did Kim\r\nnote that the Kamboh too was girt for travel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf our roads lie together,\xe2\x80\x9d he said roughly, \xe2\x80\x9cI go with thee. One does\r\nnot often find a worker of miracles, and the child is still weak. But I\r\nam not altogether a reed.\xe2\x80\x9d He picked up his _lathi_\xe2\x80\x94a five-foot\r\nmale-bamboo ringed with bands of polished iron\xe2\x80\x94and flourished it in the\r\nair. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Jats are called quarrel-some, but that is not true. Except\r\nwhen we are crossed, we are like our own buffaloes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo be it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cA good stick is a good reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama gazed placidly up-stream, where in long, smudged perspective\r\nthe ceaseless columns of smoke go up from the burning-ghats by the\r\nriver. Now and again, despite all municipal regulations, the fragment\r\nof a half-burned body bobbed by on the full current.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut for thee,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Kamboh to Kim, drawing the child into his\r\nhairy breast, \xe2\x80\x9cI might today have gone thither\xe2\x80\x94with this one. The\r\npriests tell us that Benares is holy\xe2\x80\x94which none doubt\xe2\x80\x94and desirable to\r\ndie in. But I do not know their Gods, and they ask for money; and when\r\none has done one worship a shaved-head vows it is of none effect except\r\none do another. Wash here! Wash there! Pour, drink, lave, and scatter\r\nflowers\xe2\x80\x94but always pay the priests. No, the Punjab for me, and the soil\r\nof the Jullundur-_doab_ for the best soil in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have said many times\xe2\x80\x94in the Temple, I think\xe2\x80\x94that if need be, the\r\nRiver will open at our feet. We will therefore go North,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nlama, rising. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember a pleasant place, set about with fruit-trees,\r\nwhere one can walk in meditation\xe2\x80\x94and the air is cooler there. It comes\r\nfrom the Hills and the snow of the Hills.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the name?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow should I know? Didst thou not\xe2\x80\x94no, that was after the Army rose out\r\nof the earth and took thee away. I abode there in meditation in a room\r\nagainst the dovecot\xe2\x80\x94except when she talked eternally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho! the woman from Kulu. That is by Saharunpore.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow does the spirit move thy master? Does he go afoot, for the sake of\r\npast sins?\xe2\x80\x9d the Jat demanded cautiously. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a far cry to Delhi.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cI will beg a _tikkut_ for the _te-rain_.\xe2\x80\x9d One does not\r\nown to the possession of money in India.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, in the name of the Gods, let us take the fire-carriage. My son\r\nis best in his mother\xe2\x80\x99s arms. The Government has brought on us many\r\ntaxes, but it gives us one good thing\xe2\x80\x94the _te-rain_ that joins friends\r\nand unites the anxious. A wonderful matter is the _te-rain_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey all piled into it a couple of hours later, and slept through the\r\nheat of the day. The Kamboh plied Kim with ten thousand questions as to\r\nthe lama\xe2\x80\x99s walk and work in life, and received some curious answers.\r\nKim was content to be where he was, to look out upon the flat\r\nNorth-Western landscape, and to talk to the changing mob of\r\nfellow-passengers. Even today, tickets and ticket-clipping are dark\r\noppression to Indian rustics. They do not understand why, when they\r\nhave paid for a magic piece of paper, strangers should punch great\r\npieces out of the charm. So, long and furious are the debates between\r\ntravellers and Eurasian ticket-collectors. Kim assisted at two or three\r\nwith grave advice, meant to darken counsel and to show off his wisdom\r\nbefore the lama and the admiring Kamboh. But at Somna Road the Fates\r\nsent him a matter to think upon. There tumbled into the compartment, as\r\nthe train was moving off, a mean, lean little person\xe2\x80\x94a Mahratta, so far\r\nas Kim could judge by the cock of the tight turban. His face was cut,\r\nhis muslin upper-garment was badly torn, and one leg was bandaged. He\r\ntold them that a country-cart had upset and nearly slain him: he was\r\ngoing to Delhi, where his son lived. Kim watched him closely. If, as he\r\nasserted, he had been rolled over and over on the earth, there should\r\nhave been signs of gravel-rash on the skin. But all his injuries seemed\r\nclean cuts, and a mere fall from a cart could not cast a man into such\r\nextremity of terror. As, with shaking fingers, he knotted up the torn\r\ncloth about his neck he laid bare an amulet of the kind called a\r\nkeeper-up of the heart. Now, amulets are common enough, but they are\r\nnot generally strung on square-plaited copper wire, and still fewer\r\namulets bear black enamel on silver. There were none except the Kamboh\r\nand the lama in the compartment, which, luckily, was of an old type\r\nwith solid ends. Kim made as to scratch in his bosom, and thereby\r\nlifted his own amulet. The Mahratta\xe2\x80\x99s face changed altogether at the\r\nsight, and he disposed the amulet fairly on his breast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on to the Kamboh, \xe2\x80\x9cI was in haste, and the cart, driven\r\nby a bastard, bound its wheel in a water-cut, and besides the harm done\r\nto me there was lost a full dish of _tarkeean_. I was not a Son of the\r\nCharm [a lucky man] that day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was a great loss,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Kamboh, withdrawing interest. His\r\nexperience of Benares had made him suspicious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho cooked it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA woman.\xe2\x80\x9d The Mahratta raised his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut all women can cook _tarkeean_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Kamboh. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a good\r\ncurry, as I know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, it is a good curry,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mahratta.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd cheap,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what about caste?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, there is no caste where men go to\xe2\x80\x94look for _tarkeean_,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nMahratta replied, in the prescribed cadence. \xe2\x80\x9cOf whose service art\r\nthou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf the service of this Holy One.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim pointed to the happy, drowsy\r\nlama, who woke with a jerk at the well-loved word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, he was sent from Heaven to aid me. He is called the Friend of all\r\nthe World. He is also called the Friend of the Stars. He walks as a\r\nphysician\xe2\x80\x94his time being ripe. Great is his wisdom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd a Son of the Charm,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim under his breath, as the Kamboh made\r\nhaste to prepare a pipe lest the Mahratta should beg.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who is _that?_\xe2\x80\x9d the Mahratta asked, glancing sideways nervously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne whose child I\xe2\x80\x94we have cured, who lies under great debt to us. Sit\r\nby the window, man from Jullundur. Here is a sick one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHumph! _I_ have no desire to mix with chance-met wastrels. _My_ ears\r\nare not long. _I_ am not a woman wishing to overhear secrets.\xe2\x80\x9d The Jat\r\nslid himself heavily into a far corner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArt thou anything of a healer? I am ten leagues deep in calamity,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncried the Mahratta, picking up the cue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis man is cut and bruised all over. I go about to cure him,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\r\nretorted. \xe2\x80\x9cNone interfered between thy babe and me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am rebuked,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Kamboh meekly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am thy debtor for the life\r\nof my son. Thou art a miracle-worker\xe2\x80\x94I know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShow me the cuts.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim bent over the Mahratta\xe2\x80\x99s neck, his heart nearly\r\nchoking him; for this was the Great Game with a vengeance. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, tell\r\nthy tale swiftly, brother, while I say a charm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI come from the South, where my work lay. One of us they slew by the\r\nroadside. Hast thou heard?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim shook his head. He, of course, knew\r\nnothing of E\xe2\x80\x99s predecessor, slain down South in the habit of an Arab\r\ntrader. \xe2\x80\x9cHaving found a certain letter which I was sent to seek, I came\r\naway. I escaped from the city and ran to Mhow. So sure was I that none\r\nknew, I did not change my face. At Mhow a woman brought charge against\r\nme of theft of jewellery in that city which I had left. Then I saw the\r\ncry was out against me. I ran from Mhow by night, bribing the police,\r\nwho had been bribed to hand me over without question to my enemies in\r\nthe South. Then I lay in old Chitor city a week, a penitent in a\r\ntemple, but I could not get rid of the letter which was my charge. I\r\nburied it under the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Stone, at Chitor, in the place known to us\r\nall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim did not know, but not for worlds would he have broken the thread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt Chitor, look you, I was all in Kings\xe2\x80\x99 country; for Kotah to the\r\neast is beyond the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s law, and east again lie Jaipur and Gwalior.\r\nNeither love spies, and there is no justice. I was hunted like a wet\r\njackal; but I broke through at Bandakui, where I heard there was a\r\ncharge against me of murder in the city I had left\xe2\x80\x94of the murder of a\r\nboy. They have both the corpse and the witnesses waiting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut cannot the Government protect?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe of the Game are beyond protection. If we die, we die. Our names are\r\nblotted from the book. That is all. At Bandakui, where lives one of Us,\r\nI thought to slip the scent by changing my face, and so made me a\r\nMahratta. Then I came to Agra, and would have turned back to Chitor to\r\nrecover the letter. So sure I was I had slipped them. Therefore I did\r\nnot send a tar [telegram] to any one saying where the letter lay. I\r\nwished the credit of it all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim nodded. He understood that feeling well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut at Agra, walking in the streets, a man cried a debt against me,\r\nand approaching with many witnesses, would hale me to the courts then\r\nand there. Oh, they are clever in the South! He recognized me as his\r\nagent for cotton. May he burn in Hell for it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd wast thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO fool! I was the man they sought for the matter of the letter! I ran\r\ninto the Fleshers\xe2\x80\x99 Ward and came out by the House of the Jew, who\r\nfeared a riot and pushed me forth. I came afoot to Somna Road\xe2\x80\x94I had\r\nonly money for my _tikkut_ to Delhi\xe2\x80\x94and there, while I lay in a ditch\r\nwith a fever, one sprang out of the bushes and beat me and cut me and\r\nsearched me from head to foot. Within earshot of the _te-rain_ it was!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did he not slay thee out of hand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are not so foolish. If I am taken in Delhi at the instance of\r\nlawyers, upon a proven charge of murder, my body is handed over to the\r\nState that desires it. I go back guarded, and then\xe2\x80\x94I die slowly for an\r\nexample to the rest of Us. The South is not my country. I run in\r\ncircles\xe2\x80\x94like a goat with one eye. I have not eaten for two days. I am\r\nmarked\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he touched the filthy bandage on his leg\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cso that they will\r\nknow me at Delhi.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art safe in the _te-rain_, at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLive a year at the Great Game and tell me that again! The wires will\r\nbe out against me at Delhi, describing every tear and rag upon me.\r\nTwenty\xe2\x80\x94a hundred, if need be\xe2\x80\x94will have seen me slay that boy. And thou\r\nart useless!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim knew enough of native methods of attack not to doubt that the case\r\nwould be deadly complete\xe2\x80\x94even to the corpse. The Mahratta twitched his\r\nfingers with pain from time to time. The Kamboh in his corner glared\r\nsullenly; the lama was busy over his beads; and Kim, fumbling\r\ndoctor-fashion at the man\xe2\x80\x99s neck, thought out his plan between\r\ninvocations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou a charm to change my shape? Else I am dead. Five\xe2\x80\x94ten minutes\r\nalone, if I had not been so pressed, and I might\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he cured yet, miracle-worker?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Kamboh jealously. \xe2\x80\x9cThou\r\nhast chanted long enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay. There is no cure for his hurts, as I see, except he sit for three\r\ndays in the habit of a _bairagi_.\xe2\x80\x9d This is a common penance, often\r\nimposed on a fat trader by his spiritual teacher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne priest always goes about to make another priest,\xe2\x80\x9d was the retort.\r\nLike most grossly superstitious folk, the Kamboh could not keep his\r\ntongue from deriding his Church.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill thy son be a priest, then? It is time he took more of my\r\nquinine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe Jats are all buffaloes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Kamboh, softening anew.\r\n\r\nKim rubbed a finger-tip of bitterness on the child\xe2\x80\x99s trusting little\r\nlips. \xe2\x80\x9cI have asked for nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said sternly to the father,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cexcept food. Dost thou grudge me that? I go to heal another man. Have\r\nI thy leave\xe2\x80\x94Prince?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUp flew the man\xe2\x80\x99s huge paws in supplication. \xe2\x80\x9cNay\xe2\x80\x94nay. Do not mock me\r\nthus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt pleases me to cure this sick one. Thou shalt acquire merit by\r\naiding. What colour ash is there in thy pipe-bowl? White. That is\r\nauspicious. Was there raw turmeric among thy foodstuffs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOpen thy bundle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was the usual collection of small oddments: bits of cloth, quack\r\nmedicines, cheap fairings, a clothful of _atta_\xe2\x80\x94greyish, rough-ground\r\nnative flour\xe2\x80\x94twists of down-country tobacco, tawdry pipe-stems, and a\r\npacket of curry-stuff, all wrapped in a quilt. Kim turned it over with\r\nthe air of a wise warlock, muttering a Mohammedan invocation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is wisdom I learned from the Sahibs,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered to the lama;\r\nand here, when one thinks of his training at Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s, he spoke no more\r\nthan the truth. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is a great evil in this man\xe2\x80\x99s fortune, as shown\r\nby the Stars, which\xe2\x80\x94which troubles him. Shall I take it away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFriend of the Stars, thou hast done well in all things. Let it be at\r\nthy pleasure. Is it another healing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick! Be quick!\xe2\x80\x9d gasped the Mahratta. \xe2\x80\x9cThe train may stop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA healing against the shadow of death,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, mixing the Kamboh\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nflour with the mingled charcoal and tobacco ash in the red-earth bowl\r\nof the pipe. E, without a word, slipped off his turban and shook down\r\nhis long black hair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my food\xe2\x80\x94priest,\xe2\x80\x9d the jat growled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA buffalo in the temple! Hast thou dared to look even thus far?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nKim. \xe2\x80\x9cI must do mysteries before fools; but have a care for thine eyes.\r\nIs there a film before them already? I save the babe, and for return\r\nthou\xe2\x80\x94oh, shameless!\xe2\x80\x9d The man flinched at the direct gaze, for Kim was\r\nwholly in earnest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I curse thee, or shall I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He picked up the outer cloth of the\r\nbundle and threw it over the bowed head. \xe2\x80\x9cDare so much as to think a\r\nwish to see, and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94even I cannot save thee. Sit! Be dumb!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am blind\xe2\x80\x94dumb. Forbear to curse! Co\xe2\x80\x94come, child; we will play a game\r\nof hiding. Do not, for my sake, look from under the cloth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see hope,\xe2\x80\x9d said E23. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is thy scheme?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis comes next,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, plucking the thin body-shirt. E23\r\nhesitated, with all a North-West man\xe2\x80\x99s dislike of baring his body.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is caste to a cut throat?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, rending it to the waist. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nmust make thee a yellow Saddhu all over. Strip\xe2\x80\x94strip swiftly, and shake\r\nthy hair over thine eyes while I scatter the ash. Now, a caste-mark on\r\nthy forehead.\xe2\x80\x9d He drew from his bosom the little Survey paint-box and a\r\ncake of crimson lake.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArt thou only a beginner?\xe2\x80\x9d said E23, labouring literally for the dear\r\nlife, as he slid out of his body-wrappings and stood clear in the\r\nloin-cloth while Kim splashed in a noble caste-mark on the ash-smeared\r\nbrow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut two days entered to the Game, brother,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim replied. \xe2\x80\x9cSmear more\r\nash on the bosom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou met\xe2\x80\x94a physician of sick pearls?\xe2\x80\x9d He switched out his long,\r\ntight-rolled turban-cloth and, with swiftest hands, rolled it over and\r\nunder about his loins into the intricate devices of a Saddhu\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncincture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHah! Dost thou know his touch, then? He was my teacher for a while. We\r\nmust bar thy legs. Ash cures wounds. Smear it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was his pride once, but thou art almost better. The Gods are kind to\r\nus! Give me _that_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a tin box of opium pills among the rubbish of the Jat\xe2\x80\x99s bundle.\r\nE23 gulped down a half handful. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are good against hunger, fear,\r\nand chill. And they make the eyes red too,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I shall\r\nhave heart to play the Game. We lack only a Saddhu\xe2\x80\x99s tongs. What of the\r\nold clothes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim rolled them small, and stuffed them into the slack folds of his\r\ntunic. With a yellow-ochre paint cake he smeared the legs and the\r\nbreast, great streaks against the background of flour, ash, and\r\nturmeric.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blood on them is enough to hang thee, brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe; but no need to throw them out of the window ... It is\r\nfinished.\xe2\x80\x9d His voice thrilled with a boy\xe2\x80\x99s pure delight in the Game.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTurn and look, O Jat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gods protect us,\xe2\x80\x9d said the hooded Kamboh, emerging like a buffalo\r\nfrom the reeds. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94whither went the Mahratta? What hast thou done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim had been trained by Lurgan Sahib; E23, by virtue of his business,\r\nwas no bad actor. In place of the tremulous, shrinking trader there\r\nlolled against the corner an all but naked, ash-smeared, ochre-barred,\r\ndusty-haired Saddhu, his swollen eyes\xe2\x80\x94opium takes quick effect on an\r\nempty stomach\xe2\x80\x94luminous with insolence and bestial lust, his legs\r\ncrossed under him, Kim\xe2\x80\x99s brown rosary round his neck, and a scant yard\r\nof worn, flowered chintz on his shoulders. The child buried his face in\r\nhis amazed father\xe2\x80\x99s arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook up, Princeling! We travel with warlocks, but they will not hurt\r\nthee. Oh, do not cry ... What is the sense of curing a child one day\r\nand killing him with fright the next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe child will be fortunate all his life. He has seen a great healing.\r\nWhen I was a child I made clay men and horses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have made them too. S\xc3\xadr Ban\xc3\xa1s, he comes in the night and makes them\r\nall alive at the back of our kitchen-midden,\xe2\x80\x9d piped the child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so thou art not frightened at anything. Eh, Prince?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was frightened because my father was frightened. I felt his arms\r\nshake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, chicken-man!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, and even the abashed Jat laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cI have\r\ndone a healing on this poor trader. He must forsake his gains and his\r\naccount-books, and sit by the wayside three nights to overcome the\r\nmalignity of his enemies. The Stars are against him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe fewer money-lenders the better, say I; but, Saddhu or no Saddhu,\r\nhe should pay for my stuff on his shoulders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo? But that is thy child on thy shoulder\xe2\x80\x94given over to the\r\nburning-ghat not two days ago. There remains one thing more. I did this\r\ncharm in thy presence because need was great. I changed his shape and\r\nhis soul. None the less, if, by any chance, O man from Jullundur, thou\r\nrememberest what thou hast seen, either among the elders sitting under\r\nthe village tree, or in thine own house, or in company of thy priest\r\nwhen he blesses thy cattle, a murrain will come among the buffaloes,\r\nand a fire in thy thatch, and rats in the corn-bins, and the curse of\r\nour Gods upon thy fields that they may be barren before thy feet and\r\nafter thy ploughshare.\xe2\x80\x9d This was part of an old curse picked up from a\r\n_faquir_ by the Taksali Gate in the days of Kim\xe2\x80\x99s innocence.
