It lost\r\nnothing by repetition.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCease, Holy One! In mercy, cease!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Jat. \xe2\x80\x9cDo not curse the\r\nhousehold. I saw nothing! I heard nothing! I am thy cow!\xe2\x80\x9d and he made\r\nto grab at Kim\xe2\x80\x99s bare foot beating rhythmically on the carriage floor.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut since thou hast been permitted to aid me in the matter of a pinch\r\nof flour and a little opium and such trifles as I have honoured by\r\nusing in my art, so will the Gods return a blessing,\xe2\x80\x9d and he gave it at\r\nlength, to the man\xe2\x80\x99s immense relief. It was one that he had learned\r\nfrom Lurgan Sahib.\r\n\r\nThe lama stared through his spectacles as he had not stared at the\r\nbusiness of disguisement. \xe2\x80\x9cFriend of the Stars,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cthou\r\nhast acquired great wisdom. Beware that it do not give birth to pride.\r\nNo man having the Law before his eyes speaks hastily of any matter\r\nwhich he has seen or encountered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94no\xe2\x80\x94no, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the farmer, fearful lest the master should be\r\nminded to improve on the pupil. E23, with relaxed mouth, gave himself\r\nup to the opium that is meat, tobacco, and medicine to the spent\r\nAsiatic.\r\n\r\nSo, in a silence of awe and great miscomprehension, they slid into\r\nDelhi about lamp-lighting time.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII\r\n\r\n\r\nWho hath desired the Sea\xe2\x80\x94the sight of salt-water unbounded?\r\nThe heave and the halt and the hurl and the crash of the comber\r\nwind-hounded?\r\nThe sleek-barrelled swell before storm\xe2\x80\x94grey, foamless, enormous, and\r\ngrowing?\r\nStark calm on the lap of the Line\xe2\x80\x94or the crazy-eyed hurricane blowing?\r\nHis Sea in no showing the same\xe2\x80\x94his Sea and the same \xe2\x80\x99neath all showing\xe2\x80\x94\r\n            His Sea that his being fulfils?\r\nSo and no otherwise\xe2\x80\x94so and no otherwise Hill-men desire their Hills!\r\n\r\nThe Sea and the Hills.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have found my heart again,\xe2\x80\x9d said E23, under cover of the platform\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntumult. \xe2\x80\x9cHunger and fear make men dazed, or I might have thought of\r\nthis escape before. I was right. They come to hunt for me. Thou hast\r\nsaved my head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA group of yellow-trousered Punjab policemen, headed by a hot and\r\nperspiring young Englishman, parted the crowd about the carriages.\r\nBehind them, inconspicuous as a cat, ambled a small fat person who\r\nlooked like a lawyer\xe2\x80\x99s tout.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee the young Sahib reading from a paper. My description is in his\r\nhand,\xe2\x80\x9d said E23. \xe2\x80\x9cThev go carriage by carriage, like fisher-folk\r\nnetting a pool.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the procession reached their compartment, E23 was counting his\r\nbeads with a steady jerk of the wrist; while Kim jeered at him for\r\nbeing so drugged as to have lost the ringed fire-tongs which are the\r\nSaddhu\xe2\x80\x99s distinguishing mark. The lama, deep in meditation, stared\r\nstraight before him; and the farmer, glancing furtively, gathered up\r\nhis belongings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing here but a parcel of holy-bolies,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Englishman aloud,\r\nand passed on amid a ripple of uneasiness; for native police mean\r\nextortion to the native all India over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe trouble now,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered E23, \xe2\x80\x9clies in sending a wire as to the\r\nplace where I hid that letter I was sent to find. I cannot go to the\r\n_tar_-office in this guise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it not enough I have saved thy neck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if the work be left unfinished. Did never the healer of sick\r\npearls tell thee so? Comes another Sahib! Ah!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was a tallish, sallowish District Superintendent of Police\xe2\x80\x94belt,\r\nhelmet, polished spurs and all\xe2\x80\x94strutting and twirling his dark\r\nmoustache.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat fools are these Police Sahibs!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim genially.\r\n\r\nE23 glanced up under his eyelids. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is well said,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered in a\r\nchanged voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI go to drink water. Keep my place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe blundered out almost into the Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s arms, and was bad-worded\r\nin clumsy Urdu.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Tum mut?_ You drunk? You mustn\xe2\x80\x99t bang about as though Delhi station\r\nbelonged to you, my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nE23, not moving a muscle of his countenance, answered with a stream of\r\nthe filthiest abuse, at which Kim naturally rejoiced. It reminded him\r\nof the drummer-boys and the barrack-sweepers at Umballa in the terrible\r\ntime of his first schooling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy good fool,\xe2\x80\x9d the Englishman drawled. \xe2\x80\x9c_Nickle-jao!_ Go back to your\r\ncarriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStep by step, withdrawing deferentially and dropping his voice, the\r\nyellow Saddhu clomb back to the carriage, cursing the D.S.P. to\r\nremotest posterity, by\xe2\x80\x94here Kim almost jumped\xe2\x80\x94by the curse of the\r\nQueen\xe2\x80\x99s Stone, by the writing under the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Stone, and by an\r\nassortment of Gods with wholly, new names.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you\xe2\x80\x99re saying,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the Englishman flushed angrily\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cbut\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s some piece of blasted impertinence. Come out of that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nE23, affecting to misunderstand, gravely produced his ticket, which the\r\nEnglishman wrenched angrily from his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _zoolum!_ What oppression!\xe2\x80\x9d growled the Jat from his corner. \xe2\x80\x9cAll\r\nfor the sake of a jest too.\xe2\x80\x9d He had been grinning at the freedom of the\r\nSaddhu\xe2\x80\x99s tongue. \xe2\x80\x9cThy charms do not work well today, Holy One!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Saddhu followed the policeman, fawning and supplicating. The ruck\r\nof passengers, busy, with their babies and their bundles, had not\r\nnoticed the affair. Kim slipped out behind him; for it flashed through\r\nhis head that he had heard this angry, stupid Sahib discoursing loud\r\npersonalities to an old lady near Umballa three years ago.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is well\xe2\x80\x9d, the Saddhu whispered, jammed in the calling, shouting,\r\nbewildered press\xe2\x80\x94a Persian greyhound between his feet and a cageful of\r\nyelling hawks under charge of a Rajput falconer in the small of his\r\nback. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has gone now to send word of the letter which I hid. They\r\ntold me he was in Peshawur. I might have known that he is like the\r\ncrocodile\xe2\x80\x94always at the other ford. He has saved me from present\r\ncalamity, but I owe my life to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he also one of Us?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim ducked under a Mewar camel-driver\xe2\x80\x99s greasy\r\narmpit and cannoned off a covey of jabbering Sikh matrons.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot less than the greatest. We are both fortunate! I will make report\r\nto him of what thou hast done. I am safe under his protection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bored through the edge of the crowd besieging the carriages, and\r\nsquatted by the bench near the telegraph-office.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReturn, or they take thy place! Have no fear for the work, brother\xe2\x80\x94or\r\nmy life. Thou hast given me breathing-space, and Strickland Sahib has\r\npulled me to land. We may work together at the Game yet. Farewell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim hurried to his carriage: elated, bewildered, but a little nettled\r\nin that he had no key to the secrets about him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am only a beginner at the Game, that is sure. _I_ could not have\r\nleaped into safety as did the Saddhu. He knew it was darkest under the\r\nlamp. _I_ could not have thought to tell news under pretence of cursing\r\n... and how clever was the Sahib! No matter, I saved the life of one\r\n... Where is the Kamboh gone, Holy One?\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, as he took his\r\nseat in the now crowded compartment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fear gripped him,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama replied, with a touch of tender malice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe saw thee change the Mahratta to a Saddhu in the twinkling of an\r\neye, as a protection against evil. That shook him. Then he saw the\r\nSaddhu fall sheer into the hands of the _polis_\xe2\x80\x94all the effect of thy\r\nart. Then he gathered up his son and fled; for he said that thou didst\r\nchange a quiet trader into an impudent bandier of words with the\r\nSahibs, and he feared a like fate. Where is the Saddhu?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith the _polis_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim ... \xe2\x80\x9cYet I saved the Kamboh\xe2\x80\x99s child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama snuffed blandly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, _chela_, see how thou art overtaken! Thou didst cure the Kamboh\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nchild solely to acquire merit. But thou didst put a spell on the\r\nMahratta with prideful workings\xe2\x80\x94I watched thee\xe2\x80\x94and with sidelong\r\nglances to bewilder an old old man and a foolish farmer: whence\r\ncalamity and suspicion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim controlled himself with an effort beyond his years. Not more than\r\nany other youngster did he like to eat dirt or to be misjudged, but he\r\nsaw himself in a cleft stick. The train rolled out of Delhi into the\r\nnight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere I have offended thee I have done\r\nwrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is more, _chela_. Thou hast loosed an Act upon the world, and as a\r\nstone thrown into a pool so spread the consequences thou canst not tell\r\nhow far.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis ignorance was well both for Kim\xe2\x80\x99s vanity and for the lama\xe2\x80\x99s peace\r\nof mind, when we think that there was then being handed in at Simla a\r\ncode-wire reporting the arrival of E23 at Delhi, and, more important,\r\nthe whereabouts of a letter he had been commissioned to\xe2\x80\x94abstract.\r\nIncidentally, an over-zealous policeman had arrested, on charge of\r\nmurder done in a far southern State, a horribly indignant Ajmir\r\ncotton-broker, who was explaining himself to a Mr Strickland on Delhi\r\nplatform, while E23 was paddling through byways into the locked heart\r\nof Delhi city. In two hours several telegrams had reached the angry\r\nminister of a southern State reporting that all trace of a somewhat\r\nbruised Mahratta had been lost; and by the time the leisurely train\r\nhalted at Saharunpore the last ripple of the stone Kim had helped to\r\nheave was lapping against the steps of a mosque in far-away Roum\xe2\x80\x94where\r\nit disturbed a pious man at prayers.\r\n\r\nThe lama made his in ample form near the dewy bougainvillea-trellis\r\nnear the platform, cheered by the clear sunshine and the presence of\r\nhis disciple. \xe2\x80\x9cWe will put these things behind us,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, indicating\r\nthe brazen engine and the gleaming track. \xe2\x80\x9cThe jolting of the\r\n_te-rain_\xe2\x80\x94though a wonderful thing\xe2\x80\x94has turned my bones to water. We\r\nwill use clean air henceforward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go to the Kulu woman\xe2\x80\x99s house\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, and stepped forth\r\ncheerily under the bundles. Early morning Saharunpore-way is clean and\r\nwell scented. He thought of the other mornings at St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s, and it\r\ntopped his already thrice-heaped contentment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is this new haste born from? Wise men do not run about like\r\nchickens in the sun. We have come hundreds upon hundreds of _kos_\r\nalready, and, till now, I have scarcely been alone with thee an\r\ninstant. How canst thou receive instruction all jostled of crowds? How\r\ncan I, whelmed by a flux of talk, meditate upon the Way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer tongue grows no shorter with the years, then?\xe2\x80\x9d the disciple\r\nsmiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor her desire for charms. I remember once when I spoke of the Wheel\r\nof Life\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the lama fumbled in his bosom for his latest copy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cshe was\r\nonly curious about the devils that besiege children. She shall acquire\r\nmerit by entertaining us\xe2\x80\x94in a little while\xe2\x80\x94at an after-occasion\xe2\x80\x94softly,\r\nsoftly. Now we will wander loose-foot, waiting upon the Chain of\r\nThings. The Search is sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo they travelled very easily across and among the broad bloomful\r\nfruit-gardens\xe2\x80\x94by way of Aminabad, Sahaigunge, Akrola of the Ford, and\r\nlittle Phulesa\xe2\x80\x94the line of the Siwaliks always to the north, and behind\r\nthem again the snows. After long, sweet sleep under the dry stars came\r\nthe lordly, leisurely passage through a waking village\xe2\x80\x94begging-bowl\r\nheld forth in silence, but eyes roving in defiance of the Law from\r\nsky\xe2\x80\x99s edge to sky\xe2\x80\x99s edge. Then would Kim return soft-footed through the\r\nsoft dust to his master under the shadow of a mango-tree or the thinner\r\nshade of a white Doon _siris_, to eat and drink at ease. At mid-day,\r\nafter talk and a little wayfaring, they slept; meeting the world\r\nrefreshed when the air was cooler. Night found them adventuring into\r\nnew territory\xe2\x80\x94some chosen village spied three hours before across the\r\nfat land, and much discussed upon the road.\r\n\r\nThere they told their tale\xe2\x80\x94a new one each evening so far as Kim was\r\nconcerned\xe2\x80\x94and there were they made welcome, either by priest or\r\nheadman, after the custom of the kindly East.\r\n\r\nWhen the shadows shortened and the lama leaned more heavily upon Kim,\r\nthere was always the Wheel of Life to draw forth, to hold flat under\r\nwiped stones, and with a long straw to expound cycle by cycle. Here sat\r\nthe Gods on high\xe2\x80\x94and they were dreams of dreams. Here was our Heaven\r\nand the world of the demi-Gods\xe2\x80\x94horsemen fighting among the hills. Here\r\nwere the agonies done upon the beasts, souls ascending or descending\r\nthe ladder and therefore not to be interfered with. Here were the\r\nHells, hot and cold, and the abodes of tormented ghosts. Let the\r\n_chela_ study the troubles that come from over-eating\xe2\x80\x94bloated stomach\r\nand burning bowels. Obediently, then, with bowed head and brown finger\r\nalert to follow the pointer, did the _chela_ study; but when they came\r\nto the Human World, busy and profitless, that is just above the Hells,\r\nhis mind was distracted; for by the roadside trundled the very Wheel\r\nitself, eating, drinking, trading, marrying, and quarrelling\xe2\x80\x94all warmly\r\nalive. Often the lama made the living pictures the matter of his text,\r\nbidding Kim\xe2\x80\x94too ready\xe2\x80\x94note how the flesh takes a thousand shapes,\r\ndesirable or detestable as men reckon, but in truth of no account\r\neither way; and how the stupid spirit, bond-slave to the Hog, the Dove,\r\nand the Serpent\xe2\x80\x94lusting after betel-nut, a new yoke of oxen, women, or\r\nthe favour of kings\xe2\x80\x94is bound to follow the body through all the Heavens\r\nand all the Hells, and strictly round again. Sometimes a woman or a\r\npoor man, watching the ritual\xe2\x80\x94it was nothing less\xe2\x80\x94when the great yellow\r\nchart was unfolded, would throw a few flowers or a handful of cowries\r\nupon its edge. It sufficed these humble ones that they had met a Holy\r\nOne who might be moved to remember them in his prayers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCure them if they are sick,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, when Kim\xe2\x80\x99s sporting\r\ninstincts woke. \xe2\x80\x9cCure them if they have fever, but by no means work\r\ncharms. Remember what befell the Mahratta.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen all Doing is evil?\xe2\x80\x9d Kim replied, lying out under a big tree at\r\nthe fork of the Doon road, watching the little ants run over his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo abstain from action is well\xe2\x80\x94except to acquire merit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the Gates of Learning we were taught that to abstain from action\r\nwas unbefitting a Sahib. And I am a Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFriend of all the World,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the lama looked directly at Kim\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI am an old\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94pleased with shows as are children. To those who follow the Way\r\nthere is neither black nor white, Hind nor Bhotiyal. We be all souls\r\nseeking escape. No matter what thy wisdom learned among Sahibs, when we\r\ncome to my River thou wilt be freed from all illusion\xe2\x80\x94at my side. Hai!\r\nMy bones ache for that River, as they ached in the _te-rain;_ but my\r\nspirit sits above my bones, waiting. The Search is sure!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am answered. Is it permitted to ask a question?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama inclined his stately head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ate thy bread for three years\xe2\x80\x94as thou knowest. Holy One, whence\r\ncame\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is much wealth, as men count it, in Bhotiyal,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama returned\r\nwith composure. \xe2\x80\x9cIn my own place I have the illusion of honour. I ask\r\nfor that I need. I am not concerned with the account. That is for my\r\nmonastery. Ai! The black high seats in the monastery, and novices all\r\nin order!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he told stories, tracing with a finger in the dust, of the immense\r\nand sumptuous ritual of avalanche-guarded cathedrals; of processions\r\nand devil-dances; of the changing of monks and nuns into swine; of holy\r\ncities fifteen thousand feet in the air; of intrigue between monastery\r\nand monastery; of voices among the hills, and of that mysterious mirage\r\nthat dances on dry snow. He spoke even of Lhassa and of the Dalai Lama,\r\nwhom he had seen and adored.\r\n\r\nEach long, perfect day rose behind Kim for a barrier to cut him off\r\nfrom his race and his mother-tongue. He slipped back to thinking and\r\ndreaming in the vernacular, and mechanically followed the lama\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nceremonial observances at eating, drinking, and the like. The old man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmind turned more and more to his monastery as his eyes turned to the\r\nsteadfast snows. His River troubled him nothing. Now and again, indeed,\r\nhe would gaze long and long at a tuft or a twig, expecting, he said,\r\nthe earth to cleave and deliver its blessing; but he was content to be\r\nwith his disciple, at ease in the temperate wind that comes down from\r\nthe Doon. This was not Ceylon, nor Buddh Gaya, nor Bombay, nor some\r\ngrass-tangled ruins that he seemed to have stumbled upon two years ago.\r\nHe spoke of those places as a scholar removed from vanity, as a Seeker\r\nwalking in humility, as an old man, wise and temperate, illumining\r\nknowledge with brilliant insight. Bit by bit, disconnectedly, each tale\r\ncalled up by some wayside thing, he spoke of all his wanderings up and\r\ndown Hind; till Kim, who had loved him without reason, now loved him\r\nfor fifty good reasons. So they enjoyed themselves in high felicity,\r\nabstaining, as the Rule demands, from evil words, covetous desires; not\r\nover-eating, not lying on high beds, nor wearing rich clothes. Their\r\nstomachs told them the time, and the people brought them their food, as\r\nthe saying is. They were lords of the villages of Aminabad, Sahaigunge,\r\nAkrola of the Ford, and little Phulesa, where Kim gave the soulless\r\nwoman a blessing.\r\n\r\nBut news travels fast in India, and too soon shuffled across the\r\ncrop-land, bearing a basket of fruits with a box of Kabul grapes and\r\ngilt oranges, a white-whiskered servitor\xe2\x80\x94a lean, dry Oorya\xe2\x80\x94begging them\r\nto bring the honour of their presence to his mistress, distressed in\r\nher mind that the lama had neglected her so long.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow do I remember\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the lama spoke as though it were a wholly new\r\nproposition. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is virtuous, but an inordinate talker.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim was sitting on the edge of a cow\xe2\x80\x99s manger, telling stories to a\r\nvillage smith\xe2\x80\x99s children.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will only ask for another son for her daughter. I have not\r\nforgotten her,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cLet her acquire merit. Send word that we will\r\ncome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey covered eleven miles through the fields in two days, and were\r\noverwhelmed with attentions at the end; for the old lady held a fine\r\ntradition of hospitality, to which she forced her son-in-law, who was\r\nunder the thumb of his women-folk and bought peace by borrowing of the\r\nmoney-lender. Age had not weakened her tongue or her memory, and from a\r\ndiscreetly barred upper window, in the hearing of not less than a dozen\r\nservants, she paid Kim compliments that would have flung European\r\naudiences into unclean dismay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou art still the shameless beggar-brat of the _parao_,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nshrilled. \xe2\x80\x9cI have not forgotten thee. Wash ye and eat. The father of my\r\ndaughter\xe2\x80\x99s son is gone away awhile. So we poor women are dumb and\r\nuseless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor proof, she harangued the entire household unsparingly till food and\r\ndrink were brought; and in the evening\xe2\x80\x94the smoke-scented evening,\r\ncopper-dun and turquoise across the fields\xe2\x80\x94it pleased her to order her\r\npalanquin to be set down in the untidy forecourt by smoky torchlight;\r\nand there, behind not too closely drawn curtains, she gossiped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad the Holy One come alone, I should have received him otherwise; but\r\nwith this rogue, who can be too careful?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaharanee,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, choosing as always the amplest title, \xe2\x80\x9cis it my\r\nfault that none other than a Sahib\xe2\x80\x94a _polis_-sahib\xe2\x80\x94called the Maharanee\r\nwhose face he\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cChutt! That was on the pilgrimage. When we travel\xe2\x80\x94thou\r\nknowest the proverb.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCalled the Maharanee a Breaker of Hearts and a Dispenser of Delights?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo remember that! It was true. So he did. That was in the time of the\r\nbloom of my beauty.\xe2\x80\x9d She chuckled like a contented parrot above the\r\nsugar lump. \xe2\x80\x9cNow tell me of thy goings and comings\xe2\x80\x94as much as may be\r\nwithout shame. How many maids, and whose wives, hang upon thine\r\neyelashes? Ye hail from Benares? I would have gone there again this\r\nyear, but my daughter\xe2\x80\x94we have only two sons. Phaii! Such is the effect\r\nof these low plains. Now in Kulu men are elephants. But I would ask thy\r\nHoly One\xe2\x80\x94stand aside, rogue\xe2\x80\x94a charm against most lamentable windy\r\ncolics that in mango-time overtake my daughter\xe2\x80\x99s eldest. Two years back\r\nhe gave me a powerful spell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Holy One!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, bubbling with mirth at the lama\xe2\x80\x99s rueful\r\nface.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true. I gave her one against wind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTeeth\xe2\x80\x94teeth\xe2\x80\x94teeth,\xe2\x80\x9d snapped the old woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Cure them if they are sick,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Kim quoted relishingly, \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cbut by no\r\nmeans work charms. Remember what befell the Mahratta.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was two Rains ago; she wearied me with her continual\r\nimportunity.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama groaned as the Unjust Judge had groaned before\r\nhim. \xe2\x80\x9cThus it comes\xe2\x80\x94take note, my _chela_\xe2\x80\x94that even those who would\r\nfollow the Way are thrust aside by idle women. Three days through, when\r\nthe child was sick, she talked to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArre! and to whom else should I talk? The boy\xe2\x80\x99s mother knew nothing,\r\nand the father\xe2\x80\x94in the nights of the cold weather it was\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98Pray to the\r\nGods,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, forsooth, and turning over, snored!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI gave her the charm. What is an old man to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98To abstain from action is well\xe2\x80\x94except to acquire merit.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh _chela_, if thou desertest me, I am all alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe found his milk-teeth easily at any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nall priests are alike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim coughed severely. Being young, he did not approve of her flippancy.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo importune the wise out of season is to invite calamity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a talking _mynah_\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the thrust came back with the\r\nwell-remembered snap of the jewelled fore-finger\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cover the stables\r\nwhich has picked up the very tone of the family priest. Maybe I forget\r\nhonour to my guests, but if ye had seen _him_ double his fists into his\r\nbelly, which was like a half-grown gourd, and cry: \xe2\x80\x98Here is the pain!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nye would forgive. I am half minded to take the _hakim\xe2\x80\x99s_ medicine. He\r\nsells it cheap, and certainly it makes him fat as Shiv\xe2\x80\x99s own bull. _He_\r\ndoes not deny remedies, but I doubted for the child because of the\r\nin-auspicious colour of the bottles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama, under cover of the monologue, had faded out into the darkness\r\ntowards the room prepared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast angered him, belike,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot he. He is wearied, and I forgot, being a grandmother. (None but a\r\ngrandmother should ever oversee a child. Mothers are only fit for\r\nbearing.) Tomorrow, when he sees how my daughter\xe2\x80\x99s son is grown, he\r\nwill write the charm. Then, too, he can judge of the new _hakim\xe2\x80\x99s_\r\ndrugs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is the _hakim_, Maharanee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA wanderer, as thou art, but a most sober Bengali from Dacca\xe2\x80\x94a master\r\nof medicine. He relieved me of an oppression after meat by means of a\r\nsmall pill that wrought like a devil unchained. He travels about now,\r\nvending preparations of great value. He has even papers, printed in\r\nAngrezi, telling what things he has done for weak-backed men and slack\r\nwomen. He has been here four days; but hearing ye were coming (_hakims_\r\nand priests are snake and tiger the world over) he has, as I take it,\r\ngone to cover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhile she drew breath after this volley, the ancient servant, sitting\r\nunrebuked on the edge of the torchlight, muttered: \xe2\x80\x9cThis house is a\r\ncattle-pound, as it were, for all charlatans and\xe2\x80\x94priests. Let the boy\r\nstop eating mangoes ... but who can argue with a grandmother?\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nraised his voice respectfully: \xe2\x80\x9cSahiba, the _hakim_ sleeps after his\r\nmeat. He is in the quarters behind the dovecote.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim bristled like an expectant terrier. To outface and down-talk a\r\nCalcutta-taught Bengali, a voluble Dacca drug-vendor, would be a good\r\ngame. It was not seemly that the lama, and incidentally himself, should\r\nbe thrown aside for such an one. He knew those curious bastard English\r\nadvertisements at the backs of native newspapers. St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s boys\r\nsometimes brought them in by stealth to snigger over among their mates;\r\nfor the language of the grateful patient recounting his symptoms is\r\nmost simple and revealing. The Oorya, not unanxious to play off one\r\nparasite against the other, slunk away towards the dovecote.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, with measured scorn. \xe2\x80\x9cTheir stock-in-trade is a little\r\ncoloured water and a very great shamelessness. Their prey are\r\nbroken-down kings and overfed Bengalis. Their profit is in children\xe2\x80\x94who\r\nare not born.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old lady chuckled. \xe2\x80\x9cDo not be envious. Charms are better, eh? _I_\r\nnever gainsaid it. See that thy Holy One writes me a good amulet by the\r\nmorning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone but the ignorant deny\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94a thick, heavy voice boomed through the\r\ndarkness, as a figure came to rest squatting\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cNone but the ignorant\r\ndeny the value of charms. None but the ignorant deny the value of\r\nmedicines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA rat found a piece of turmeric. Said he: \xe2\x80\x98I will open a grocer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshop,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Kim retorted.\r\n\r\nBattle was fairly joined now, and they heard the old lady stiffen to\r\nattention.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe priest\xe2\x80\x99s son knows the names of his nurse and three Gods. Says he:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Hear me, or I will curse you by the three million Great Ones.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nDecidedly this invisible had an arrow or two in his quiver. He went on:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am but a teacher of the alphabet. I have learned all the wisdom of\r\nthe Sahibs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Sahibs never grow old. They dance and they play like children when\r\nthey are grandfathers. A strong-backed breed,\xe2\x80\x9d piped the voice inside\r\nthe palanquin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have, too, our drugs which loosen humours of the head in hot and\r\nangry men. _Sin\xc3\xa1_ well compounded when the moon stands in the proper\r\nHouse; yellow earths I have\xe2\x80\x94_arplan_ from China that makes a man renew\r\nhis youth and astonish his household; saffron from Kashmir, and the\r\nbest salep of Kabul. Many people have died before\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I surely believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey knew the value of my drugs. I do not give _my_ sick the mere ink\r\nin which a charm is written, but hot and rending drugs which descend\r\nand wrestle with the evil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery mightily they do so,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed the old lady.\r\n\r\nThe voice launched into an immense tale of misfortune and bankruptcy,\r\nstudded with plentiful petitions to the Government. \xe2\x80\x9cBut for my fate,\r\nwhich overrules all, I had been now in Government employ. I bear a\r\ndegree from the great school at Calcutta\xe2\x80\x94whither, maybe, the son of\r\nthis House shall go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe shall indeed. If our neighbour\xe2\x80\x99s brat can in a few years be made an\r\nF A\xe2\x80\x9d (First Arts\xe2\x80\x94she used the English word, of which she had heard so\r\noften), \xe2\x80\x9chow much more shall children clever as some that I know bear\r\naway prizes at rich Calcutta.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever,\xe2\x80\x9d said the voice, \xe2\x80\x9chave I seen such a child! Born in an\r\nauspicious hour, and\xe2\x80\x94but for that colic which, alas! turning into black\r\ncholers, may carry him off like a pigeon\xe2\x80\x94destined to many years, he is\r\nenviable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Hai mai!_\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady. \xe2\x80\x9cTo praise children is inauspicious, or\r\nI could listen to this talk. But the back of the house is unguarded,\r\nand even in this soft air men think themselves to be men, and women we\r\nknow ... The child\xe2\x80\x99s father is away too, and I must be _chowkedar_\r\n(watchman) in my old age. Up! Up! Take up the palanquin. Let the\r\n_hakim_ and the young priest settle between them whether charms or\r\nmedicine most avail. Ho! worthless people, fetch tobacco for the\r\nguests, and\xe2\x80\x94round the homestead go I!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe palanquin reeled off, followed by straggling torches and a horde of\r\ndogs. Twenty villages knew the Sahiba\xe2\x80\x94her failings, her tongue, and her\r\nlarge charity. Twenty villages cheated her after immemorial custom, but\r\nno man would have stolen or robbed within her jurisdiction for any gift\r\nunder heaven. None the less, she made great parade of her formal\r\ninspections, the riot of which could be heard half-way to Mussoorie.\r\n\r\nKim relaxed, as one augur must when he meets another. The _hakim_,\r\nstill squatting, slid over his hookah with a friendly foot, and Kim\r\npulled at the good weed. The hangers-on expected grave professional\r\ndebate, and perhaps a little free doctoring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo discuss medicine before the ignorant is of one piece with teaching\r\nthe peacock to sing,\xe2\x80\x9d said the _hakim_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue courtesy,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim echoed, \xe2\x80\x9cis very often inattention.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese, be it understood, were company-manners, designed to impress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHi! I have an ulcer on my leg,\xe2\x80\x9d cried a scullion. \xe2\x80\x9cLook at it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet hence! Remove!\xe2\x80\x9d said the _hakim_. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it the habit of the place to\r\npester honoured guests? Ye crowd in like buffaloes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the Sahiba knew\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Kim began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAi! Ai! Come away. They are meat for our mistress. When her young\r\nShaitan\xe2\x80\x99s colics are cured perhaps we poor people may be suffered to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe mistress fed thy wife when thou wast in jail for breaking the\r\nmoney-lender\xe2\x80\x99s head. Who speaks against her?\xe2\x80\x9d The old servitor curled\r\nhis white moustaches savagely in the young moonlight. \xe2\x80\x9c_I_ am\r\nresponsible for the honour of this house. Go!\xe2\x80\x9d and he drove the\r\nunderlings before him.\r\n\r\nSaid the _hakim_, hardly more than shaping the words with his lips:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you do, Mister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara? I am jolly glad to see you again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim\xe2\x80\x99s hand clenched about the pipe-stem. Anywhere on the open road,\r\nperhaps, he would not have been astonished; but here, in this quiet\r\nbackwater of life, he was not prepared for Hurree Babu. It annoyed him,\r\ntoo, that he had been hoodwinked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh ha! I told you at Lucknow\xe2\x80\x94_resurgam_\xe2\x80\x94I shall rise again and you\r\nshall not know me. How much did you bet\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe chewed leisurely upon a few cardamom seeds, but he breathed\r\nuneasily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why come here, Babuji?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! _Thatt_ is the question, as Shakespeare hath it. I come to\r\ncongratulate you on your extraordinary effeecient performance at Delhi.\r\nOah! I tell you we are all proud of you. It was verree neat and handy.\r\nOur mutual friend, he is old friend of mine. He has been in some\r\ndam\xe2\x80\x99-tight places. Now he will be in some more. He told me; I tell Mr\r\nLurgan; and he is pleased you graduate so nicely. All the Department is\r\npleased.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor the first time in his life, Kim thrilled to the clean pride (it can\r\nbe a deadly pitfall, none the less) of Departmental praise\xe2\x80\x94ensnaring\r\npraise from an equal of work appreciated by fellow-workers. Earth has\r\nnothing on the same plane to compare with it. But, cried the Oriental\r\nin him, Babus do not travel far to retail compliments.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell thy tale, Babu,\xe2\x80\x9d he said authoritatively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah, it is nothing. Onlee I was at Simla when the wire came in about\r\nwhat our mutual friend said he had hidden, and old Creighton\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nlooked to see how Kim would take this piece of audacity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Colonel Sahib,\xe2\x80\x9d the boy from St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s corrected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Of_ course. He found me at a loose string, and I had to go down to\r\nChitor to find that beastly letter. I do not like the South\xe2\x80\x94too much\r\nrailway travel; but I drew good travelling allowance. Ha! Ha! I meet\r\nour mutual at Delhi on the way back. He lies quiett just now, and says\r\nSaddhu-disguise suits him to the ground. Well, there I hear what you\r\nhave done so well, so quickly, upon the instantaneous spur of the\r\nmoment. I tell our mutual you take the bally bun, by Jove! It was\r\nsplendid. I come to tell you so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmm!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe frogs were busy in the ditches, and the moon slid to her setting.\r\nSome happy servant had gone out to commune with the night and to beat\r\nupon a drum. Kim\xe2\x80\x99s next sentence was in the vernacular.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow didst thou follow us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah. Thatt was nothing. I know from our mutual friend you go to\r\nSaharunpore. So I come on. Red Lamas are not inconspicuous persons. I\r\nbuy myself my drug-box, and I am very good doctor really. I go to\r\nAkrola of the Ford, and hear all about you, and I talk here and talk\r\nthere. All the common people know what you do. I knew when the\r\nhospitable old lady sent the _dooli_. They have great recollections of\r\nthe old lama\xe2\x80\x99s visits here. I know old ladies cannot keep their hands\r\nfrom medicines. So I am a doctor, and\xe2\x80\x94you hear my talk? _I_ think it is\r\nverree good. My word, Mister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, they know about you and the lama\r\nfor fifty miles\xe2\x80\x94the common people. So I come. Do you mind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBabuji,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, looking up at the broad, grinning face, \xe2\x80\x9cI am a\r\nSahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Mister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I hope to play the Great Game.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are subordinate to me departmentally at present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why talk like an ape in a tree? Men do not come after one from\r\nSimla and change their dress, for the sake of a few sweet words. I am\r\nnot a child. Talk Hindi and let us get to the yolk of the egg. Thou art\r\nhere\xe2\x80\x94speaking not one word of truth in ten. Why art thou here? Give a\r\nstraight answer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so verree disconcerting of the Europeans, Mister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara. _You_\r\nshould know a heap better at your time of life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I want to know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it is the Game, I may\r\nhelp. How can I do anything if you _bukh_ (babble) all round the shop?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHurree Babu reached for the pipe, and sucked it till it gurgled again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I will speak vernacular. You sit tight, Mister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara ... It\r\nconcerns the pedigree of a white stallion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill? That was finished long ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen everyone is dead the Great Game is finished. Not before. Listen\r\nto me till the end. There were Five Kings who prepared a sudden war\r\nthree years ago, when thou wast given the stallion\xe2\x80\x99s pedigree by Mahbub\r\nAli. Upon them, because of that news, and ere they were ready, fell our\r\nArmy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy\xe2\x80\x94eight thousand men with guns. I remember that night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the war was not pushed. That is the Government custom. The troops\r\nwere recalled because the Government believed the Five Kings were\r\ncowed; and it is not cheap to feed men among the high Passes. Hil\xc3\xa1s and\r\nBun\xc3\xa1r\xe2\x80\x94Rajahs with guns\xe2\x80\x94undertook for a price to guard the Passes\r\nagainst all coming from the North. They protested both fear and\r\nfriendship.\xe2\x80\x9d He broke off with a giggle into English: \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, I\r\ntell you this unoffeecially to elucidate political situation, Mister\r\nO\xe2\x80\x99Hara. Offeecially, I am debarred from criticizing any action of\r\nsuperiors. Now I go on.\xe2\x80\x94This pleased the Government, anxious to avoid\r\nexpense, and a bond was made for so many rupees a month that Hil\xc3\xa1s and\r\nBun\xc3\xa1r should guard the Passes as soon as the State\xe2\x80\x99s troops were\r\nwithdrawn. At that time\xe2\x80\x94it was after we two met\xe2\x80\x94I, who had been selling\r\ntea in Leh, became a clerk of accounts in the Army. When the troops\r\nwere withdrawn, I was left behind to pay the coolies who made new roads\r\nin the Hills. This road-making was part of the bond between Bun\xc3\xa1r,\r\nHil\xc3\xa1s, and the Government.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo? And then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you, it was jolly-beastly cold up there too, after summer,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Hurree Babu confidentially. \xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid these Bun\xc3\xa1r men would\r\ncut my throat every night for thee pay-chest. My native sepoy-guard,\r\nthey laughed at me! By Jove! I was such a fearful man. Nevar mind\r\nthatt. I go on colloquially ... I send word many times that these two\r\nKings were sold to the North; and Mahbub Ali, who was yet farther\r\nNorth, amply confirmed it. Nothing was done. Only my feet were frozen,\r\nand a toe dropped off. I sent word that the roads for which I was\r\npaying money to the diggers were being made for the feet of strangers\r\nand enemies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the Russians. The thing was an open jest among the coolies. Then I\r\nwas called down to tell what I knew by speech of tongue. Mahbub came\r\nSouth too. See the end! Over the Passes this year after\r\nsnow-melting\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he shivered afresh\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ccome two strangers under cover of\r\nshooting wild goats. They bear guns, but they bear also chains and\r\nlevels and compasses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho! The thing gets clearer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are well received by Hil\xc3\xa1s and Bun\xc3\xa1r. They make great promises;\r\nthey speak as the mouthpiece of a Kaisar with gifts. Up the valleys,\r\ndown the valleys go they, saying, \xe2\x80\x98Here is a place to build a\r\nbreastwork; here can ye pitch a fort. Here can ye hold the road against\r\nan army\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94the very roads for which I paid out the rupees monthly. The\r\nGovernment knows, but does nothing. The three other Kings, who were\r\n_not_ paid for guarding the Passes, tell them by runner of the bad\r\nfaith of Bun\xc3\xa1r and Hil\xc3\xa1s. When all the evil is done, look you\xe2\x80\x94when\r\nthese two strangers with the levels and the compasses make the Five\r\nKings to believe that a great army will sweep the Passes tomorrow or\r\nthe next day\xe2\x80\x94Hill-people are all fools\xe2\x80\x94comes the order to me, Hurree\r\nBabu, \xe2\x80\x98Go North and see what those strangers do.\xe2\x80\x99 I say to Creighton\r\nSahib, \xe2\x80\x98This is not a lawsuit, that we go about to collect evidence.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHurree returned to his English with a jerk: \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98By Jove,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98why\r\nthe dooce do you not issue demi-offeecial orders to some brave man to\r\npoison them, for an example? It is, if you permit the observation, most\r\nreprehensible laxity on your part.\xe2\x80\x99 And Colonel Creighton, he laughed\r\nat me! It is all your beastly English pride. You think no one dare\r\nconspire! That is all tommy-rott.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim smoked slowly, revolving the business, so far as he understood it,\r\nin his quick mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen thou goest forth to follow the strangers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. To meet them. They are coming in to Simla to send down their horns\r\nand heads to be dressed at Calcutta. They are exclusively sporting\r\ngentlemen, and they are allowed special faceelities by the Government.\r\nOf course, we always do that. It is our British pride.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what is to fear from them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove, they are not black people. I can do all sorts of things with\r\nblack people, of course. They are Russians, and highly unscrupulous\r\npeople. I\xe2\x80\x94I do not want to consort with them without a witness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill they kill thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah, thatt is nothing. I am good enough Herbert Spencerian, I trust,\r\nto meet little thing like death, which is all in my fate, you know.\r\nBut\xe2\x80\x94but they may beat me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHurree Babu snapped his fingers with irritation. \xe2\x80\x9cOf _course_ I shall\r\naffeeliate myself to their camp in supernumerary capacity as perhaps\r\ninterpreter, or person mentally impotent and hungree, or some such\r\nthing. And then I must pick up what I can, I suppose. That is as easy\r\nfor me as playing Mister Doctor to the old lady. Onlee\xe2\x80\x94onlee\xe2\x80\x94you see,\r\nMister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, I am unfortunately Asiatic, which is serious detriment\r\nin some respects. And _all_so I am Bengali\xe2\x80\x94a fearful man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod made the Hare and the Bengali. What shame?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, quoting the\r\nproverb.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was process of Evolution, _I_ think, from Primal Necessity, but the\r\nfact remains in all the _cui bono_. I am, oh, awfully fearful!\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nremember once they wanted to cut off my head on the road to Lhassa.\r\n(No, I have never reached to Lhassa.) I sat down and cried, Mister\r\nO\xe2\x80\x99Hara, anticipating Chinese tortures. I do not suppose these two\r\ngentlemen will torture me, but I like to provide for possible\r\ncontingency with European assistance in emergency.\xe2\x80\x9d He coughed and spat\r\nout the cardamoms. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is purely unoffeecial indent, to which you can\r\nsay \xe2\x80\x98No, Babu\xe2\x80\x99. If you have no pressing engagement with your old\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94perhaps you might divert him; perhaps I can seduce his fancies\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nshould like you to keep in Departmental touch with me till I find those\r\nsporting coves. I have great opeenion of you since I met my friend at\r\nDelhi. And also I will embody your name in my offeecial report when\r\nmatter is finally adjudicated. It will be a great feather in your cap.\r\nThat is why I come really.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHumph! The end of the tale, I think, is true; but what of the\r\nfore-part?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout the Five Kings? Oah! there is ever so much truth in it. A lots\r\nmore than you would suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hurree earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou come\xe2\x80\x94eh? I go\r\nfrom here straight into the Doon. It is verree verdant and painted\r\nmeads. I shall go to Mussoorie to good old Munsoorie Pahar, as the\r\ngentlemen and ladies say. Then by Rampur into Chini. That is the only\r\nway they can come. I do not like waiting in the cold, but we must wait\r\nfor them. I want to walk with them to Simla. You see, one Russian is a\r\nFrenchman, and I know my French pretty well. I have friends in\r\nChandernagore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_He_ would certainly rejoice to see the Hills again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim\r\nmeditatively. \xe2\x80\x9cAll his speech these ten days past has been of little\r\nelse. If we go together\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah! We can be quite strangers on the road, if your lama prefers. I\r\nshall just be four or five miles ahead. There is no hurry for\r\nHurree\xe2\x80\x94that is an Europe pun, ha! ha!\xe2\x80\x94and you come after. There is\r\nplenty of time; they will plot and survey and map, of course. I shall\r\ngo tomorrow, and you the next day, if you choose. Eh? You go think on\r\nit till morning. By Jove, it is near morning now.\xe2\x80\x9d He yawned\r\nponderously, and with never a civil word lumbered off to his\r\nsleeping-place. But Kim slept little, and his thoughts ran in\r\nHindustani:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell is the Game called great! I was four days a scullion at Quetta,\r\nwaiting on the wife of the man whose book I stole. And that was part of\r\nthe Great Game! From the South\xe2\x80\x94God knows how far\xe2\x80\x94came up the Mahratta,\r\nplaying the Great Game in fear of his life. Now I shall go far and far\r\ninto the North playing the Great Game. Truly, it runs like a shuttle\r\nthroughout all Hind. And my share and my joy\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he smiled to the\r\ndarkness\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI owe to the lama here. Also to Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x94also to Creighton\r\nSahib, but chiefly to the Holy One. He is right\xe2\x80\x94a great and a wonderful\r\nworld\xe2\x80\x94and I am Kim\xe2\x80\x94Kim\xe2\x80\x94Kim\xe2\x80\x94alone\xe2\x80\x94one person\xe2\x80\x94in the middle of it all.\r\nBut I will see these strangers with their levels and chains...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was the upshot of last night\xe2\x80\x99s babble?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, after his\r\norisons.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere came a strolling seller of drugs\xe2\x80\x94a hanger-on of the Sahiba\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\nHim I abolished by arguments and prayers, proving that our charms are\r\nworthier than his coloured waters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas, my charms! Is the virtuous woman still bent upon a new one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery strictly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it must be written, or she will deafen me with her clamour.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nfumbled at his pencase.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the Plains,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, \xe2\x80\x9care always too many people. In the Hills,\r\nas I understand, there are fewer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! the Hills, and the snows upon the Hills.\xe2\x80\x9d The lami tore off a tiny\r\nsquare of paper fit to go in an amulet. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what dost thou know of the\r\nHills?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are very close.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim thrust open the door and looked at the long,\r\npeaceful line of the Himalayas flushed in morning-gold. \xe2\x80\x9cExcept in the\r\ndress of a Sahib, I have never set foot among them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama snuffed the wind wistfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we go North,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Kim put the question to the waking sunrise\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwould not\r\nmuch mid-day heat be avoided by walking among the lower hills at least?\r\n... Is the charm made, Holy One?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have written the names of seven silly devils\xe2\x80\x94not one of whom is\r\nworth a grain of dust in the eye. Thus do foolish women drag us from\r\nthe Way!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHurree Babu came out from behind the dovecote washing his teeth with\r\nostentatious ritual. Full-fleshed, heavy-haunched, bull-necked, and\r\ndeep-voiced, he did not look like \xe2\x80\x9ca fearful man\xe2\x80\x9d. Kim signed almost\r\nimperceptibly that matters were in good train, and when the morning\r\ntoilet was over, Hurree Babu, in flowery speech, came to do honour to\r\nthe lama. They ate, of course, apart, and afterwards the old lady, more\r\nor less veiled behind a window, returned to the vital business of\r\ngreen-mango colics in the young. The lama\xe2\x80\x99s knowledge of medicine was,\r\nof course, sympathetic only. He believed that the dung of a black\r\nhorse, mixed with sulphur, and carried in a snake-skin, was a sound\r\nremedy for cholera; but the symbolism interested him far more than the\r\nscience. Hurree Babu deferred to these views with enchanting\r\npoliteness, so that the lama called him a courteous physician. Hurree\r\nBabu replied that he was no more than an inexpert dabbler in the\r\nmysteries; but at least\xe2\x80\x94he thanked the Gods therefore\xe2\x80\x94he knew when he\r\nsat in the presence of a master. He himself had been taught by the\r\nSahibs, who do not consider expense, in the lordly halls of Calcutta;\r\nbut, as he was ever first to acknowledge, there lay a wisdom behind\r\nearthly wisdom\xe2\x80\x94the high and lonely lore of meditation. Kim looked on\r\nwith envy. The Hurree Babu of his knowledge\xe2\x80\x94oily, effusive, and\r\nnervous\xe2\x80\x94was gone; gone, too, was the brazen drug-vendor of overnight.\r\nThere remained\xe2\x80\x94polished, polite, attentive\xe2\x80\x94a sober, learned son of\r\nexperience and adversity, gathering wisdom from the lama\xe2\x80\x99s lips. The\r\nold lady confided to Kim that these rare levels were beyond her. She\r\nliked charms with plenty of ink that one could wash off in water,\r\nswallow, and be done with. Else what was the use of the Gods? She liked\r\nmen and women, and she spoke of them\xe2\x80\x94of kinglets she had known in the\r\npast; of her own youth and beauty; of the depredations of leopards and\r\nthe eccentricities of love Asiatic; of the incidence of taxation,\r\nrack-renting, funeral ceremonies, her son-in-law (this by allusion,\r\neasy to be followed), the care of the young, and the age\xe2\x80\x99s lack of\r\ndecency. And Kim, as interested in the life of this world as she soon\r\nto leave it, squatted with his feet under the hem of his robe, drinking\r\nall in, while the lama demolished one after another every theory of\r\nbody-curing put forward by Hurree Babu.\r\n\r\nAt noon the Babu strapped up his brass-bound drug-box, took his\r\npatent-leather shoes of ceremony in one hand, a gay blue-and-white\r\numbrella in the other, and set off northwards to the Doon, where, he\r\nsaid, he was in demand among the lesser kings of those parts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will go in the cool of the evening, _chela_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\ndoctor, learned in physic and courtesy, affirms that the people among\r\nthese lower hills are devout, generous, and much in need of a teacher.\r\nIn a very short time\xe2\x80\x94so says the _hakim_\xe2\x80\x94we come to cool air and the\r\nsmell of pines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe go to the Hills? And by Kulu road? Oh, thrice happy!\xe2\x80\x9d shrilled the\r\nold lady. \xe2\x80\x9cBut that I am a little pressed with the care of the\r\nhomestead I would take palanquin ... but that would be shameless, and\r\nmy reputation would be cracked. Ho! Ho! I know the road\xe2\x80\x94every march of\r\nthe road I know. Ye will find charity throughout\xe2\x80\x94it is not denied to\r\nthe well-looking. I will give orders for provision. A servant to set\r\nyou forth upon your journey? No ... Then I will at least cook ye good\r\nfood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a woman is the Sahiba!\xe2\x80\x9d said the white-bearded Oorya, when a\r\ntumult rose by the kitchen quarters. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has never forgotten a friend:\r\nshe has never forgotten an enemy in all her years. And her\r\ncookery\xe2\x80\x94wah!\xe2\x80\x9d He rubbed his slim stomach.\r\n\r\nThere were cakes, there were sweetmeats, there was cold fowl stewed to\r\nrags with rice and prunes\xe2\x80\x94enough to burden Kim like a mule.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am old and useless,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cNone now love me\xe2\x80\x94and none\r\nrespect\xe2\x80\x94but there are few to compare with me when I call on the Gods\r\nand squat to my cooking-pots. Come again, O people of good will. Holy\r\nOne and disciple, come again. The room is always prepared; the welcome\r\nis always ready ... See the women do not follow thy _chela_ too openly.\r\nI know the women of Kulu. Take heed, _chela_, lest he run away when he\r\nsmells his Hills again ... Hai! Do not tilt the rice-bag upside down\r\n... Bless the household, Holy One, and forgive thy servant her\r\nstupidities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe wiped her red old eyes on a corner of her veil, and clucked\r\nthroatily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWomen talk,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama at last, \xe2\x80\x9cbut that is a woman\xe2\x80\x99s infirmity.\r\nI gave her a charm. She is upon the Wheel and wholly given over to the\r\nshows of this life, but none the less, _chela_, she is virtuous,\r\nkindly, hospitable\xe2\x80\x94of a whole and zealous heart. Who shall say she does\r\nnot acquire merit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot I, Holy One,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, reslinging the bountiful provision on his\r\nshoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cIn my mind\xe2\x80\x94behind my eyes\xe2\x80\x94I have tried to picture such an\r\none altogether freed from the Wheel\xe2\x80\x94desiring nothing, causing nothing\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nnun, as it were.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, O imp?\xe2\x80\x9d The lama almost laughed aloud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot make the picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor I. But there are many, many millions of lives before her. She will\r\nget wisdom a little, it may be, in each one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will she forget how to make stews with saffron upon that road?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy mind is set on things unworthy. But she has skill. I am refreshed\r\nall over. When we reach the lower hills I shall be yet stronger. The\r\n_hakim_ spoke truly to me this morn when he said a breath from the\r\nsnows blows away twenty years from the life of a man. We will go up\r\ninto the Hills\xe2\x80\x94the high hills\xe2\x80\x94up to the sound of snow-waters and the\r\nsound of the trees\xe2\x80\x94for a little while. The _hakim_ said that at any\r\ntime we may return to the Plains, for we do no more than skirt the\r\npleasant places. The _hakim_ is full of learning; but he is in no way\r\nproud. I spoke to him\xe2\x80\x94when thou wast talking to the Sahiba\xe2\x80\x94of a certain\r\ndizziness that lays hold upon the back of my neck in the night, and he\r\nsaid it rose from excessive heat\xe2\x80\x94to be cured by cool air. Upon\r\nconsideration, I marvelled that I had not thought of such a simple\r\nremedy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidst thou tell him of thy Search?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, a little jealously. He\r\npreferred to sway the lama by his own speech\xe2\x80\x94not through the wiles of\r\nHurree Babu.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly. I told him of my dream, and of the manner by which I had\r\nacquired merit by causing thee to be taught wisdom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou didst not say I was a Sahib?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat need? I have told thee many times we be but two souls seeking\r\nescape. He said\xe2\x80\x94and he is just herein\xe2\x80\x94that the River of Healing will\r\nbreak forth even as I dreamed\xe2\x80\x94at my feet, if need be. Having found the\r\nWay, seest thou, that shall free me from the Wheel, need I trouble to\r\nfind a way about the mere fields of earth\xe2\x80\x94which are illusion? That were\r\nsenseless. I have my dreams, night upon night repeated; I have\r\n_J\xc3\xa2taka;_ and I have thee, Friend of all the World. It was written in\r\nthy horoscope that a Red Bull on a green field\xe2\x80\x94I have not\r\nforgotten\xe2\x80\x94should bring thee to honour. Who but I saw that prophecy\r\naccomplished? Indeed, I was the instrument. Thou shalt find me my\r\nRiver, being in return the instrument. The Search is sure!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe set his ivory-yellow face, serene and untroubled, towards the\r\nbeckoning Hills; his shadow shouldering far before him in the dust.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII\r\n\r\n\r\nWho hath desired the Sea\xe2\x80\x94the immense and contemptuous surges?\r\nThe shudder, the stumble, the swerve ere the star-stabbing bowsprit\r\nmerges\xe2\x80\x94\r\nThe orderly clouds of the Trades and the ridged roaring sapphire\r\nthereunder\xe2\x80\x94\r\nUnheralded cliff-lurking flaws and the head-sails\xe2\x80\x99 low-volleying\r\nthunder?\r\nHis Sea in no wonder the same\xe2\x80\x94his Sea and the same in each wonder\xe2\x80\x94\r\nHis Sea that his being fulfils?\r\nSo and no otherwise\xe2\x80\x94so and no otherwise hill-men desire their hills!\r\n\r\nThe Sea and the Hills.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho goes to the hills goes to his mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had crossed the Siwaliks and the half-tropical Doon, left\r\nMussoorie behind them, and headed north along the narrow hill-roads.\r\nDay after day they struck deeper into the huddled mountains, and day\r\nafter day Kim watched the lama return to a man\xe2\x80\x99s strength. Among the\r\nterraces of the Doon he had leaned on the boy\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, ready to\r\nprofit by wayside halts. Under the great ramp to Mussoorie he drew\r\nhimself together as an old hunter faces a well-remembered bank, and\r\nwhere he should have sunk exhausted swung his long draperies about him,\r\ndrew a deep double-lungful of the diamond air, and walked as only a\r\nhillman can. Kim, plains-bred and plains-fed, sweated and panted\r\nastonished. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is _my_ country,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cBeside Such-zen,\r\nthis is flatter than a rice-field\xe2\x80\x9d; and with steady, driving strokes\r\nfrom the loins he strode upwards. But it was on the steep downhill\r\nmarches, three thousand feet in three hours, that he went utterly away\r\nfrom Kim, whose back ached with holding back, and whose big toe was\r\nnigh cut off by his grass sandal-string. Through the speckled shadow of\r\nthe great deodar-forests; through oak feathered and plumed with ferns;\r\nbirch, ilex, rhododendron, and pine, out on to the bare hillsides\xe2\x80\x99\r\nslippery sunburnt grass, and back into the woodlands\xe2\x80\x99 coolth again,\r\ntill oak gave way to bamboo and palm of the valley, the lama swung\r\nuntiring.\r\n\r\nGlancing back in the twilight at the huge ridges behind him and the\r\nfaint, thin line of the road whereby they had come, he would lay out,\r\nwith a hillman\xe2\x80\x99s generous breadth of vision, fresh marches for the\r\nmorrow; or, halting in the neck of some uplifted pass that gave on\r\nSpiti and Kulu, would stretch out his hands yearningly towards the high\r\nsnows of the horizon. In the dawns they flared windy-red above stark\r\nblue, as Kedarnath and Badrinath\xe2\x80\x94kings of that wilderness\xe2\x80\x94took the\r\nfirst sunlight. All day long they lay like molten silver under the sun,\r\nand at evening put on their jewels again. At first they breathed\r\ntemperately upon the travellers, winds good to meet when one crawled\r\nover some gigantic hog\xe2\x80\x99s-back; but in a few days, at a height of nine\r\nor ten thousand feet, those breezes bit; and Kim kindly allowed a\r\nvillage of hillmen to acquire merit by giving him a rough blanket-coat.\r\nThe lama was mildly surprised that anyone should object to the\r\nknife-edged breezes which had cut the years off his shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese are but the lower hills, _chela_. There is no cold till we come\r\nto the true Hills.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAir and water are good, and the people are devout enough, but the food\r\nis very bad,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim growled; \xe2\x80\x9cand we walk as though we were mad\xe2\x80\x94or\r\nEnglish. It freezes at night, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little, maybe; but only enough to make old bones rejoice in the sun.\r\nWe must not always delight in soft beds and rich food.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe might at least keep to the road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim had all a plainsman\xe2\x80\x99s affection for the well-trodden track, not six\r\nfeet wide, that snaked among the mountains; but the lama, being\r\nTibetan, could not refrain from short cuts over spurs and the rims of\r\ngravel-strewn slopes. As he explained to his limping disciple, a man\r\nbred among mountains can prophesy the course of a mountain-road, and\r\nthough low-lying clouds might be a hindrance to a short-cutting\r\nstranger, they made no earthly difference to a thoughtful man. Thus,\r\nafter long hours of what would be reckoned very fair mountaineering in\r\ncivilized countries, they would pant over a saddle-back, sidle past a\r\nfew landslips, and drop through forest at an angle of forty-five onto\r\nthe road again. Along their track lay the villages of the hillfolk\xe2\x80\x94mud\r\nand earth huts, timbers now and then rudely carved with an axe\xe2\x80\x94clinging\r\nlike swallows\xe2\x80\x99 nests against the steeps, huddled on tiny flats half-way\r\ndown a three-thousand-foot glissade; jammed into a corner between\r\ncliffs that funnelled and focused every wandering blast; or, for the\r\nsake of summer pasture, cowering down on a neck that in winter would be\r\nten feet deep in snow. And the people\xe2\x80\x94the sallow, greasy, duffle-clad\r\npeople, with short bare legs and faces almost Esquimaux\xe2\x80\x94would flock out\r\nand adore. The Plains\xe2\x80\x94kindly and gentle\xe2\x80\x94had treated the lama as a holy\r\nman among holy men. But the Hills worshipped him as one in the\r\nconfidence of all their devils. Theirs was an almost obliterated\r\nBuddhism, overlaid with a nature-worship fantastic as their own\r\nlandscapes, elaborate as the terracing of their tiny fields; but they\r\nrecognized the big hat, the clicking rosary, and the rare Chinese texts\r\nfor great authority; and they respected the man beneath the hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe saw thee come down over the black Breasts of Eua,\xe2\x80\x9d said a Betah who\r\ngave them cheese, sour milk, and stone-hard bread one evening. \xe2\x80\x9cWe do\r\nnot use that often\xe2\x80\x94except when calving cows stray in summer. There is a\r\nsudden wind among those stones that casts men down on the stillest day.\r\nBut what should such folk care for the Devil of Eua!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen did Kim, aching in every fibre, dizzy with looking down, footsore\r\nwith cramping desperate toes into inadequate crannies, take joy in the\r\nday\xe2\x80\x99s march\xe2\x80\x94such joy as a boy of St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s who had won the\r\nquarter-mile on the flat might take in the praises of his friends. The\r\nhills sweated the _ghi_ and sugar suet off his bones; the dry air,\r\ntaken sobbingly at the head of cruel passes, firmed and built out his\r\nupper ribs; and the tilted levels put new hard muscles into calf and\r\nthigh.\r\n\r\nThey meditated often on the Wheel of Life\xe2\x80\x94the more so since, as the\r\nlama said, they were freed from its visible temptations. Except the\r\ngrey eagle and an occasional far-seen bear grubbing and rooting on the\r\nhillside; a vision of a furious painted leopard met at dawn in a still\r\nvalley devouring a goat; and now and again a bright-coloured bird, they\r\nwere alone with the winds and the grass singing under the wind. The\r\nwomen of the smoky huts over whose roofs the two walked as they\r\ndescended the mountains, were unlovely and unclean, wives of many\r\nhusbands, and afflicted with goitre. The men were woodcutters when they\r\nwere not farmers\xe2\x80\x94meek, and of an incredible simplicity. But that\r\nsuitable discourse might not fail, Fate sent them, overtaking and\r\novertaken upon the road, the courteous Dacca physician, who paid for\r\nhis food in ointments good for goitre and counsels that restore peace\r\nbetween men and women. He seemed to know these hills as well as he knew\r\nthe hill dialects, and gave the lama the lie of the land towards Ladakh\r\nand Tibet. He said they could return to the Plains at any moment.\r\nMeantime, for such as loved mountains, yonder road might amuse. This\r\nwas not all revealed in a breath, but at evening encounters on the\r\nstone threshing-floors, when, patients disposed of, the doctor would\r\nsmoke and the lama snuff, while Kim watched the wee cows grazing on the\r\nhousetops, or threw his soul after his eyes across the deep blue gulfs\r\nbetween range and range. And there were talks apart in the dark woods,\r\nwhen the doctor would seek herbs, and Kim, as budding physician, must\r\naccompany him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Mister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, I do not know what the deuce-an\xe2\x80\x99 all I shall\r\ndo when I find our sporting friends; but if you will kindly keep within\r\nsight of my umbrella, which is fine fixed point for cadastral survey, I\r\nshall feel much better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim looked out across the jungle of peaks. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is not my country,\r\n_hakim_. Easier, I think, to find one louse in a bear-skin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah, thatt is my strong points. There is no hurry for Hurree. They\r\nwere at Leh not so long ago. They said they had come down from the\r\nKarakorum with their heads and horns and all. I am onlee afraid they\r\nwill have sent back all their letters and compromising things from Leh\r\ninto Russian territoree. Of course they will walk away as far to the\r\nEast as possible\xe2\x80\x94just to show that they were never among the Western\r\nStates. You do not know the Hills?\xe2\x80\x9d He scratched with a twig on the\r\nearth. \xe2\x80\x9cLook! They should have come in by Srinagar or Abbottabad.\r\n_Thatt_ is their short road\xe2\x80\x94down the river by Bunji and Astor. But they\r\nhave made mischief in the West. So\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he drew a furrow from left to\r\nright\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthey march and they march away East to Leh (ah! it is cold\r\nthere), and down the Indus to Hanl\xc3\xa9 (I know that road), and then down,\r\nyou see, to Bushahr and Chini valley. That is ascertained by process of\r\nelimination, and also by asking questions from people that I cure so\r\nwell. Our friends have been a long time playing about and producing\r\nimpressions. So they are well known from far off. You will see me catch\r\nthem somewhere in Chini valley. Please keep your eye on the umbrella.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt nodded like a wind-blown harebell down the valleys and round the\r\nmountain sides, and in due time the lama and Kim, who steered by\r\ncompass, would overhaul it, vending ointments and powders at eventide.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe came by such and such a way!\xe2\x80\x9d The lama would throw a careless\r\nfinger backward at the ridges, and the umbrella would expend itself in\r\ncompliments.\r\n\r\nThey crossed a snowy pass in cold moonlight, when the lama, mildly\r\nchaffing Kim, went through up to his knees, like a Bactrian camel\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nsnow-bred, shag-haired sort that came into the Kashmir Serai. They\r\ndipped across beds of light snow and snow-powdered shale, where they\r\ntook refuge from a gale in a camp of Tibetans hurrying down tiny sheep,\r\neach laden with a bag of borax. They came out upon grassy shoulders\r\nstill snow-speckled, and through forest, to grass anew. For all their\r\nmarchings, Kedarnath and Badrinath were not impressed; and it was only\r\nafter days of travel that Kim, uplifted upon some insignificant\r\nten-thousand-foot hummock, could see that a shoulder-knot or horn of\r\nthe two great lords had\xe2\x80\x94ever so slightly\xe2\x80\x94changed outline.\r\n\r\nAt last they entered a world within a world\xe2\x80\x94a valley of leagues where\r\nthe high hills were fashioned of a mere rubble and refuse from off the\r\nknees of the mountains. Here one day\xe2\x80\x99s march carried them no farther,\r\nit seemed, than a dreamer\xe2\x80\x99s clogged pace bears him in a nightmare. They\r\nskirted a shoulder painfully for hours, and, behold, it was but an\r\noutlying boss in an outlying buttress of the main pile! A rounded\r\nmeadow revealed itself, when they had reached it, for a vast tableland\r\nrunning far into the valley. Three days later, it was a dim fold in the\r\nearth to southward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely the Gods live here!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, beaten down by the silence and\r\nthe appalling sweep and dispersal of the cloud-shadows after rain.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is no place for men!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLong and long ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, as to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cit was asked of the\r\nLord whether the world were everlasting. On this the Excellent One\r\nreturned no answer ... When I was in Ceylon, a wise Seeker confirmed\r\nthat from the gospel which is written in Pali. Certainly, since we know\r\nthe way to Freedom, the question were unprofitable, but\xe2\x80\x94look, and know\r\nillusion, _chela!_ These\xe2\x80\x94are the true Hills! They are like my hills by\r\nSuchzen. Never were such hills!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAbove them, still enormously above them, earth towered away towards the\r\nsnow-line, where from east to west across hundreds of miles, ruled as\r\nwith a ruler, the last of the bold birches stopped. Above that, in\r\nscarps and blocks upheaved, the rocks strove to fight their heads above\r\nthe white smother. Above these again, changeless since the world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeginning, but changing to every mood of sun and cloud, lay out the\r\neternal snow. They could see blots and blurs on its face where storm\r\nand wandering wullie-wa got up to dance. Below them, as they stood, the\r\nforest slid away in a sheet of blue-green for mile upon mile; below the\r\nforest was a village in its sprinkle of terraced fields and steep\r\ngrazing-grounds. Below the village they knew, though a thunderstorm\r\nworried and growled there for the moment, a pitch of twelve or fifteen\r\nhundred feet gave to the moist valley where the streams gather that are\r\nthe mothers of young Sutluj.\r\n\r\nAs usual, the lama had led Kim by cow-track and by-road, far from the\r\nmain route along which Hurree Babu, that \xe2\x80\x9cfearful man\xe2\x80\x9d, had bucketed\r\nthree days before through a storm to which nine Englishmen out of ten\r\nwould have given full right of way. Hurree was no game-shot\xe2\x80\x94the snick\r\nof a trigger made him change colour\xe2\x80\x94but, as he himself would have said,\r\nhe was \xe2\x80\x9cfairly effeecient stalker\xe2\x80\x9d, and he had raked the huge valley\r\nwith a pair of cheap binoculars to some purpose. Moreover, the white of\r\nworn canvas tents against green carries far. Hurree Babu had seen all\r\nhe wanted to see when he sat on the threshing-floor of Ziglaur, twenty\r\nmiles away as the eagle flies, and forty by road\xe2\x80\x94that is to say, two\r\nsmall dots which one day were just below the snow-line, and the next\r\nhad moved downward perhaps six inches on the hillside. Once cleaned out\r\nand set to the work, his fat bare legs could cover a surprising amount\r\nof ground, and this was the reason why, while Kim and the lama lay in a\r\nleaky hut at Ziglaur till the storm should be over-past, an oily, wet,\r\nbut always smiling Bengali, talking the best of English with the vilest\r\nof phrases, was ingratiating himself with two sodden and rather\r\nrheumatic foreigners. He had arrived, revolving many wild schemes, on\r\nthe heels of a thunderstorm which had split a pine over against their\r\ncamp, and so convinced a dozen or two forcibly impressed\r\nbaggage-coolies the day was inauspicious for farther travel that with\r\none accord they had thrown down their loads and jibbed. They were\r\nsubjects of a Hill Rajah who farmed out their services, as is the\r\ncustom, for his private gain; and, to add to their personal distresses,\r\nthe strange Sahibs had already threatened them with rifles. The most of\r\nthem knew rifles and Sahibs of old: they were trackers and _shikarris_\r\nof the Northern valleys, keen after bear and wild goat; but they had\r\nnever been thus treated in their lives. So the forest took them to her\r\nbosom, and, for all oaths and clamour, refused to restore. There was no\r\nneed to feign madness or\xe2\x80\x94the Babu had thought of another means of\r\nsecuring a welcome. He wrung out his wet clothes, slipped on his\r\npatent-leather shoes, opened the blue-and-white umbrella, and with\r\nmincing gait and a heart beating against his tonsils appeared as \xe2\x80\x9cagent\r\nfor His Royal Highness, the Rajah of Rampur, gentlemen. What can I do\r\nfor you, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe gentlemen were delighted. One was visibly French, the other\r\nRussian, but they spoke English not much inferior to the Babu\xe2\x80\x99s. They\r\nbegged his kind offices. Their native servants had gone sick at Leh.\r\nThey had hurried on because they were anxious to bring the spoils of\r\nthe chase to Simla ere the skins grew moth-eaten. They bore a general\r\nletter of introduction (the Babu salaamed to it orientally) to all\r\nGovernment officials. No, they had not met any other shooting-parties\r\n_en route_. They did for themselves. They had plenty of supplies. They\r\nonly wished to push on as soon as might be. At this he waylaid a\r\ncowering hillman among the trees, and after three minutes\xe2\x80\x99 talk and a\r\nlittle silver (one cannot be economical upon State service, though\r\nHurree\xe2\x80\x99s heart bled at the waste) the eleven coolies and the three\r\nhangers-on reappeared. At least the Babu would be a witness to their\r\noppression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy royal master, he will be much annoyed, but these people are onlee\r\ncommon people and grossly ignorant. If your honours will kindly\r\noverlook unfortunate affair, I shall be much pleased. In a little while\r\nrain will stop and we can then proceed. You have been shooting, eh?\r\nThat is fine performance!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe skipped nimbly from one _kilta_ to the next, making pretence to\r\nadjust each conical basket. The Englishman is not, as a rule, familiar\r\nwith the Asiatic, but he would not strike across the wrist a kindly\r\nBabu who had accidentally upset a _kilta_ with a red oilskin top. On\r\nthe other hand, he would not press drink upon a Babu were he never so\r\nfriendly, nor would he invite him to meat. The strangers did all these\r\nthings, and asked many questions\xe2\x80\x94about women mostly\xe2\x80\x94to which Hurree\r\nreturned gay and unstudied answers. They gave him a glass of whitish\r\nfluid like to gin, and then more; and in a little time his gravity\r\ndeparted from him. He became thickly treasonous, and spoke in terms of\r\nsweeping indecency of a Government which had forced upon him a white\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s education and neglected to supply him with a white man\xe2\x80\x99s salary.\r\nHe babbled tales of oppression and wrong till the tears ran down his\r\ncheeks for the miseries of his land. Then he staggered off, singing\r\nlove-songs of Lower Bengal, and collapsed upon a wet tree-trunk. Never\r\nwas so unfortunate a product of English rule in India more unhappily\r\nthrust upon aliens.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are all just of that pattern,\xe2\x80\x9d said one sportsman to the other in\r\nFrench. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen we get into India proper thou wilt see. I should like to\r\nvisit his Rajah. One might speak the good word there. It is possible\r\nthat he has heard of us and wishes to signify his good-will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have not time. We must get into Simla as soon as may be,\xe2\x80\x9d his\r\ncompanion replied. \xe2\x80\x9cFor my own part, I wish our reports had been sent\r\nback from Hil\xc3\xa1s, or even Leh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe English post is better and safer. Remember we are given all\r\nfacilities\xe2\x80\x94and Name of God!\xe2\x80\x94they give them to us too! Is it\r\nunbelievable stupidity?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is pride\xe2\x80\x94pride that deserves and will receive punishment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! To fight a fellow-Continental in our game is something. There is\r\na risk attached, but these people\xe2\x80\x94bah! It is too easy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPride\xe2\x80\x94all pride, my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow what the deuce is good of Chandernagore being so close to Calcutta\r\nand all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hurree, snoring open-mouthed on the sodden moss, \xe2\x80\x9cif I\r\ncannot understand their French? They talk so par_tic_ularly fast! It\r\nwould have been much better to cut their beastly throats.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen he presented himself again he was racked with a headache\xe2\x80\x94penitent,\r\nand volubly afraid that in his drunkenness he might have been\r\nindiscreet. He loved the British Government\xe2\x80\x94it was the source of all\r\nprosperity and honour, and his master at Rampur held the very same\r\nopinion.
