Upon this the men began to deride him and to quote past words,\r\ntill step by step, with deprecating smirks, oily grins, and leers of\r\ninfinite cunning, the poor Babu was beaten out of his defences and\r\nforced to speak\xe2\x80\x94truth. When Lurgan was told the tale later, he mourned\r\naloud that he could not have been in the place of the stubborn,\r\ninattentive coolies, who with grass mats over their heads and the\r\nraindrops puddling in their footprints, waited on the weather. All the\r\nSahibs of their acquaintance\xe2\x80\x94rough-clad men joyously returning year\r\nafter year to their chosen gullies\xe2\x80\x94had servants and cooks and\r\norderlies, very often hillmen. These Sahibs travelled without any\r\nretinue. Therefore they were poor Sahibs, and ignorant; for no Sahib in\r\nhis senses would follow a Bengali\xe2\x80\x99s advice. But the Bengali, appearing\r\nfrom somewhere, had given them money, and could make shift with their\r\ndialect. Used to comprehensive ill-treatment from their own colour,\r\nthey suspected a trap somewhere, and stood by to run if occasion\r\noffered.\r\n\r\nThen through the new-washed air, steaming with delicious earth-smells,\r\nthe Babu led the way down the slopes\xe2\x80\x94walking ahead of the coolies in\r\npride; walking behind the foreigners in humility. His thoughts were\r\nmany and various. The least of them would have interested his\r\ncompanions beyond words. But he was an agreeable guide, ever keen to\r\npoint out the beauties of his royal master\xe2\x80\x99s domain. He peopled the\r\nhills with anything thev had a mind to slay\xe2\x80\x94thar, ibex, or markhor, and\r\nbear by Elisha\xe2\x80\x99s allowance. He discoursed of botany and ethnology with\r\nunimpeachable inaccuracy, and his store of local legends\xe2\x80\x94he had been a\r\ntrusted agent of the State for fifteen years, remember\xe2\x80\x94was\r\ninexhaustible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDecidedly this fellow is an original,\xe2\x80\x9d said the taller of the two\r\nforeigners. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is like the nightmare of a Viennese courier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe represents _in petto_ India in transition\xe2\x80\x94the monstrous hybridism\r\nof East and West,\xe2\x80\x9d the Russian replied. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is _we_ who can deal with\r\nOrientals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has lost his own country and has not acquired any other. But he has\r\na most complete hatred of his conquerors. Listen. He confided to me\r\nlast night,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other.\r\n\r\nUnder the striped umbrella Hurree Babu was straining ear and brain to\r\nfollow the quick-poured French, and keeping both eyes on a _kilta_ full\r\nof maps and documents\xe2\x80\x94an extra-large one with a double red oil-skin\r\ncover. He did not wish to steal anything. He only desired to know what\r\nto steal, and, incidentally, how to get away when he had stolen it. He\r\nthanked all the Gods of Hindustan, and Herbert Spencer, that there\r\nremained some valuables to steal.\r\n\r\nOn the second day the road rose steeply to a grass spur above the\r\nforest; and it was here, about sunset, that they came across an aged\r\nlama\xe2\x80\x94but they called him a bonze\xe2\x80\x94sitting cross-legged above a\r\nmysterious chart held down by stones, which he was explaining to a\r\nyoung man, evidently a neophyte, of singular, though unwashen, beauty.\r\nThe striped umbrella had been sighted half a march away, and Kim had\r\nsuggested a halt till it came up to them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hurree Babu, resourceful as Puss-in-Boots. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is eminent\r\nlocal holy man. Probably subject of my royal master.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is he doing? It is very curious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is expounding holy picture\xe2\x80\x94_all_ hand-worked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two men stood bareheaded in the wash of the afternoon sunlight low\r\nacross the gold-coloured grass. The sullen coolies, glad of the check,\r\nhalted and slid down their loads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Frenchman. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is like a picture for the birth of a\r\nreligion\xe2\x80\x94the first teacher and the first disciple. Is he a Buddhist?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf some debased kind,\xe2\x80\x9d the other answered. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are no true\r\nBuddhists among the Hills. But look at the folds of the drapery. Look\r\nat his eyes\xe2\x80\x94how insolent! Why does this make one feel that we are so\r\nyoung a people?\xe2\x80\x9d The speaker struck passionately at a tall weed. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nhave nowhere left our mark yet. Nowhere! _That_, do you understand, is\r\nwhat disquiets me.\xe2\x80\x9d He scowled at the placid face, and the monumental\r\ncalm of the pose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave patience. We shall make your mark together\xe2\x80\x94we and you young\r\npeople. Meantime, draw his picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Babu advanced loftily; his back out of all keeping with his\r\ndeferential speech, or his wink towards Kim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoly One, these be Sahibs. My medicines cured one of a flux, and I go\r\ninto Simla to oversee his recovery. They wish to see thy picture\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo heal the sick is always good. This is the Wheel of Life,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nlama, \xe2\x80\x9cthe same I showed thee in the hut at Ziglaur when the rain\r\nfell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to hear thee expound it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama\xe2\x80\x99s eyes lighted at the prospect of new listeners. \xe2\x80\x9cTo expound\r\nthe Most Excellent Way is good. Have they any knowledge of Hindi, such\r\nas had the Keeper of Images?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little, maybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHereat, simply as a child engrossed with a new game, the lama threw\r\nback his head and began the full-throated invocation of the Doctor of\r\nDivinity ere he opens the full doctrine. The strangers leaned on their\r\nalpenstocks and listened. Kim, squatting humbly, watched the red\r\nsunlight on their faces, and the blend and parting of their long\r\nshadows. They wore un-English leggings and curious girt-in belts that\r\nreminded him hazily of the pictures in a book in St Xavier\xe2\x80\x99s library\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_The Adventures of a Young Naturalist in Mexico_\xe2\x80\x99 was its name. Yes,\r\nthey looked very like the wonderful M. Sumichrast of that tale, and\r\nvery unlike the \xe2\x80\x9chighly unscrupulous folk\xe2\x80\x9d of Hurree Babu\xe2\x80\x99s imagining.\r\nThe coolies, earth-coloured and mute, crouched reverently some twenty\r\nor thirty yards away, and the Babu, the slack of his thin gear snapping\r\nlike a marking-flag in the chill breeze, stood by with an air of happy\r\nproprietorship.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese are the men,\xe2\x80\x9d Hurree whispered, as the ritual went on and the\r\ntwo whites followed the grass-blade sweeping from Hell to Heaven and\r\nback again. \xe2\x80\x9cAll their books are in the large _kilta_ with the reddish\r\ntop\xe2\x80\x94books and reports and maps\xe2\x80\x94and I have seen a King\xe2\x80\x99s letter that\r\neither Hil\xc3\xa1s or Bun\xc3\xa1r has written. They guard it most carefully. They\r\nhave sent nothing back from Hil\xc3\xa1s or Leh. That is sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is with them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly the _beegar_-coolies. They have no servants. They are so close\r\nthey cook their own food.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what am I to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait and see. Only if any chance comes to me thou wilt know where to\r\nseek for the papers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis were better in Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s hands than a Bengali\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim\r\nscornfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are more ways of getting to a sweetheart than butting down a\r\nwall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee here the Hell appointed for avarice and greed. Flanked upon the\r\none side by Desire and on the other by Weariness.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama warmed to\r\nhis work, and one of the strangers sketched him in the quick-fading\r\nlight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is enough,\xe2\x80\x9d the man said at last brusquely. \xe2\x80\x9cI cannot understand\r\nhim, but I want that picture. He is a better artist than I. Ask him if\r\nhe will sell it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says \xe2\x80\x98No, sar,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d the Babu replied. The lama, of course, would no\r\nmore have parted with his chart to a casual wayfarer than an archbishop\r\nwould pawn the holy vessels of his cathedral. All Tibet is full of\r\ncheap reproductions of the Wheel; but the lama was an artist, as well\r\nas a wealthy Abbot in his own place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps in three days, or four, or ten, if I perceive that the Sahib\r\nis a Seeker and of good understanding, I may myself draw him another.\r\nBut _this_ was used for the initiation of a novice. Tell him so,\r\n_hakim_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wishes it now\xe2\x80\x94for money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama shook his head slowly and began to fold up the Wheel. The\r\nRussian, on his side, saw no more than an unclean old man haggling over\r\na dirty piece of paper. He drew out a handful of rupees, and snatched\r\nhalf-jestingly at the chart, which tore in the lama\xe2\x80\x99s grip. A low\r\nmurmur of horror went up from the coolies\xe2\x80\x94some of whom were Spiti men\r\nand, by their lights, good Buddhists. The lama rose at the insult; his\r\nhand went to the heavy iron pencase that is the priest\xe2\x80\x99s weapon, and\r\nthe Babu danced in agony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you see\xe2\x80\x94you see why I wanted witnesses. They are highly\r\nunscrupulous people. Oh, sar! sar! You must _not_ hit holyman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Chela!_ He has defiled the Written Word!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was too late. Before Kim could ward him off, the Russian struck the\r\nold man full on the face. Next instant he was rolling over and over\r\ndownhill with Kim at his throat. The blow had waked every unknown Irish\r\ndevil in the boy\xe2\x80\x99s blood, and the sudden fall of his enemy did the\r\nrest. The lama dropped to his knees, half-stunned; the coolies under\r\ntheir loads fled up the hill as fast as plainsmen run aross the level.\r\nThey had seen sacrilege unspeakable, and it behoved them to get away\r\nbefore the Gods and devils of the hills took vengeance. The Frenchman\r\nran towards the lama, fumbling at his revolver with some notion of\r\nmaking him a hostage for his companion. A shower of cutting\r\nstones\xe2\x80\x94hillmen are very straight shots\xe2\x80\x94drove him away, and a coolie\r\nfrom Ao-chung snatched the lama into the stampede. All came about as\r\nswiftly as the sudden mountain-darkness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have taken the baggage and all the guns,\xe2\x80\x9d yelled the Frenchman,\r\nfiring blindly into the twilight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, sar! All right! Don\xe2\x80\x99t shoot. I go to rescue,\xe2\x80\x9d and Hurree,\r\npounding down the slope, cast himself bodily upon the delighted and\r\nastonished Kim, who was banging his breathless foe\xe2\x80\x99s head against a\r\nboulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo back to the coolies,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered the Babu in his ear. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have the\r\nbaggage. The papers are in the _kilta_ with the red top, but look\r\nthrough all. Take their papers, and specially the _murasla_ (King\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nletter). Go! The other man comes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim tore uphill. A revolver-bullet rang on a rock by his side, and he\r\ncowered partridge-wise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you shoot,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Hurree, \xe2\x80\x9cthey will descend and annihilate us. I\r\nhave rescued the gentleman, sar. This is par_tic_ularly dangerous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim was thinking hard in English. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is dam\xe2\x80\x99-tight place,\r\nbut _I_ think it is self-defence.\xe2\x80\x9d He felt in his bosom for Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngift, and uncertainly\xe2\x80\x94save for a few practice shots in the Bikanir\r\ndesert, he had never used the little gun\xe2\x80\x94pulled the trigger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did I say, sar!\xe2\x80\x9d The Babu seemed to be in tears. \xe2\x80\x9cCome down here\r\nand assist to resuscitate. We are all up a tree, I tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe shots ceased. There was a sound of stumbling feet, and Kim hurried\r\nupward through the gloom, swearing like a cat\xe2\x80\x94or a country-bred.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid they wound thee, _chela?_\xe2\x80\x9d called the lama above him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. And thou?\xe2\x80\x9d He dived into a clump of stunted firs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnhurt. Come away. We go with these folk to Shamlegh-under-the-Snow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut not before we have done justice,\xe2\x80\x9d a voice cried. \xe2\x80\x9cI have got the\r\nSahibs\xe2\x80\x99 guns\xe2\x80\x94all four. Let us go down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe struck the Holy One\xe2\x80\x94we saw it! Our cattle will be barren\xe2\x80\x94our wives\r\nwill cease to bear! The snows will slide upon us as we go home... Atop\r\nof all other oppression too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe little fir-clump filled with clamouring coolies\xe2\x80\x94panic-stricken, and\r\nin their terror capable of anything. The man from Ao-chung clicked the\r\nbreech-bolt of his gun impatiently, and made as to go downhill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a little, Holy One; they cannot go far. Wait till I return,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is this person who has suffered wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, his hand\r\nover his brow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor that very reason,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf this person overlooks it, your hands are clean. Moreover, ye\r\nacquire merit by obedience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait, and we will all go to Shamlegh together,\xe2\x80\x9d the man insisted.\r\n\r\nFor a moment, for just so long as it needs to stuff a cartridge into a\r\nbreech-loader, the lama hesitated. Then he rose to his feet, and laid a\r\nfinger on the man\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou heard? _I_ say there shall be no killing\xe2\x80\x94I who was Abbot of\r\nSuch-zen. Is it any lust of thine to be re-born as a rat, or a snake\r\nunder the eaves\xe2\x80\x94a worm in the belly of the most mean beast? Is it thy\r\nwish to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man from Ao-chung fell to his knees, for the voice boomed like a\r\nTibetan devil-gong.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAi! ai!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Spiti men. \xe2\x80\x9cDo not curse us\xe2\x80\x94do not curse him. It\r\nwas but his zeal, Holy One! ... Put down the rifle, fool!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnger on anger! Evil on evil! There will be no killing. Let the\r\npriest-beaters go in bondage to their own acts. Just and sure is the\r\nWheel, swerving not a hair! They will be born many times\xe2\x80\x94in torment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHis head drooped, and he leaned heavily on Kim\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have come near to great evil, _chela_,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered in that dead\r\nhush under the pines. \xe2\x80\x9cI was tempted to loose the bullet; and truly, in\r\nTibet there would have been a heavy and a slow death for them ... He\r\nstruck me across the face ... upon the flesh ...\xe2\x80\x9d He slid to the\r\nground, breathing heavily, and Kim could hear the over-driven heart\r\nbump and check.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave they hurt him to the death?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Ao-chung man, while the\r\nothers stood mute.\r\n\r\nKim knelt over the body in deadly fear. \xe2\x80\x9cNay,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried passionately,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthis is only a weakness.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he remembered that he was a white man,\r\nwith a white man\xe2\x80\x99s camp-fittings at his service. \xe2\x80\x9cOpen the _kiltas!_\r\nThe Sahibs may have a medicine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho! Then I know it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Ao-chung man with a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cNot for\r\nfive years was I Yankling Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s _shikarri_ without knowing that\r\nmedicine. I too have tasted it. Behold!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drew from his breast a bottle of cheap whisky\xe2\x80\x94such as is sold to\r\nexplorers at Leh\xe2\x80\x94and cleverly forced a little between the lama\xe2\x80\x99s teeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I did when Yankling Sahib twisted his foot beyond Astor. Aha! I\r\nhave already looked into their baskets\xe2\x80\x94but we will make fair division\r\nat Shamlegh. Give him a little more. It is good medicine. Feel! His\r\nheart goes better now. Lay his head down and rub a little on the chest.\r\nIf he had waited quietly while I accounted for the Sahibs this would\r\nnever have come. But perhaps the Sahibs may chase us here. Then it\r\nwould not be wrong to shoot them with their own guns, heh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne is paid, I think, already,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim between his teeth. \xe2\x80\x9cI kicked\r\nhim in the groin as we went downhill. Would I had killed him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is well to be brave when one does not live in Rampur,\xe2\x80\x9d said one\r\nwhose hut lay within a few miles of the Rajah\xe2\x80\x99s rickety palace. \xe2\x80\x9cIf we\r\nget a bad name among the Sahibs, none will employ us as _shikarris_ any\r\nmore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but these are not Angrezi Sahibs\xe2\x80\x94not merry-minded men like Fostum\r\nSahib or Yankling Sahib. They are foreigners\xe2\x80\x94they cannot speak Angrezi\r\nas do Sahibs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the lama coughed and sat up, groping for the rosary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere shall be no killing,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cJust is the Wheel! Evil on\r\nevil\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Holy One. We are all here.\xe2\x80\x9d The Ao-chung man timidly patted his\r\nfeet. \xe2\x80\x9cExcept by thy order, no one shall be slain. Rest awhile. We will\r\nmake a little camp here, and later, as the moon rises, we go to\r\nShamlegh-under-the-Snow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter a blow,\xe2\x80\x9d said a Spiti man sententiously, \xe2\x80\x9cit is best to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is, as it were, a dizziness at the back of my neck, and a\r\npinching in it. Let me lay my head on thy lap, _chela_. I am an old\r\nman, but not free from passion ... We must think of the Cause of\r\nThings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive him a blanket. We dare not light a fire lest the Sahibs see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter get away to Shamlegh. None will follow us to Shamlegh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was the nervous Rampur man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been Fostum Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s _shikarri_, and I am Yankling Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_shikarri_. I should have been with Yankling Sahib now but for this\r\ncursed _beegar_ (the _corv\xc3\xa9e_). Let two men watch below with the guns\r\nlest the Sahibs do more foolishness. _I_ shall not leave this Holy\r\nOne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey sat down a little apart from the lama, and, after listening\r\nawhile, passed round a water-pipe whose receiver was an old Day and\r\nMartin blacking-bottle. The glow of the red charcoal as it went from\r\nhand to hand lit up the narrow, blinking eyes, the high Chinese\r\ncheek-bones, and the bull-throats that melted away into the dark duffle\r\nfolds round the shoulders. They looked like kobolds from some magic\r\nmine\xe2\x80\x94gnomes of the hills in conclave. And while they talked, the voices\r\nof the snow-waters round them diminished one by one as the night-frost\r\nchoked and clogged the runnels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow he stood up against us!\xe2\x80\x9d said a Spiti man admiring. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember an\r\nold ibex, out Ladakh-way, that Dupont Sahib missed on a shoulder-shot,\r\nseven seasons back, standing up just like him. Dupont Sahib was a good\r\n_shikarri_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot as good as Yankling Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d The Ao-chung man took a pull at the\r\nwhisky-bottle and passed it over. \xe2\x80\x9cNow hear me\xe2\x80\x94unless any other man\r\nthinks he knows more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe challenge was not taken up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe go to Shamlegh when the moon rises. There we will fairly divide the\r\nbaggage between us. I am content with this new little rifle and all its\r\ncartridges.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre the bears only bad on thy holding? said a mate, sucking at the\r\npipe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but musk-pods are worth six rupees apiece now, and thy women can\r\nhave the canvas of the tents and some of the cooking-gear. We will do\r\nall that at Shamlegh before dawn. Then we all go our ways, remembering\r\nthat we have never seen or taken service with these Sahibs, who may,\r\nindeed, say that we have stolen their baggage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is well for thee, but what will our Rajah say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is to tell him? Those Sahibs, who cannot speak our talk, or the\r\nBabu, who for his own ends gave us money? Will _he_ lead an army\r\nagainst us? What evidence will remain? That we do not need we shall\r\nthrow on Shamlegh-midden, where no man has yet set foot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is at Shamlegh this summer?\xe2\x80\x9d The place was only a grazing centre\r\nof three or four huts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Woman of Shamlegh. _She_ has no love for Sahibs, as we know. The\r\nothers can be pleased with little presents; and here is enough for us\r\nall.\xe2\x80\x9d He patted the fat sides of the nearest basket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have said they are not true Sahibs. All their skins and heads were\r\nbought in the bazar at Leh. _I_ know the marks. I showed them to ye\r\nlast march.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. They were all bought skins and heads. Some had even the moth in\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was a shrewd argument, and the Ao-chung man knew his fellows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the worst comes to the worst, I shall tell Yankling Sahib, who is a\r\nman of a merry mind, and he will laugh. We are not doing any wrong to\r\nany Sahibs whom we know. _They_ are priest-beaters. They frightened us.\r\nWe fled! Who knows where we dropped the baggage? Do ye think Yankling\r\nSahib will permit down-country police to wander all over the hills,\r\ndisturbing his game? It is a far cry from Simla to Chini, and farther\r\nfrom Shamlegh to Shamlegh-midden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo be it, but I carry the big _kilta_. The basket with the red top\r\nthat the Sahibs pack themselves every morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThus it is proved,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Shamlegh man adroitly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat they are\r\nSahibs of no account. Who ever heard of Fostum Sahib, or Yankling\r\nSahib, or even the little Peel Sahib that sits up of nights to shoot\r\nserow\xe2\x80\x94I say, who, ever heard of these Sahibs coming into the hills\r\nwithout a down-country cook, and a bearer, and\xe2\x80\x94and all manner of\r\nwell-paid, high-handed and oppressive folk in their tail? How can they\r\nmake trouble? What of the _kilta?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, but that it is full of the Written Word\xe2\x80\x94books and papers in\r\nwhich they wrote, and strange instruments, as of worship.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShamlegh-midden will take them all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue! But how if we insult the Sahibs\xe2\x80\x99 Gods thereby! I do not like to\r\nhandle the Written Word in that fashion. And their brass idols are\r\nbeyond my comprehension. It is no plunder for simple hill-folk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe old man still sleeps. Hst! We will ask his _chela_.\xe2\x80\x9d The Ao-chung\r\nman refreshed himself, and swelled with pride of leadership.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have here,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, \xe2\x80\x9ca _kilta_ whose nature we do not know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim cautiously. The lama drew breath in natural, easy\r\nsleep, and Kim had been thinking of Hurree\xe2\x80\x99s last words. As a player of\r\nthe Great Game, he was disposed just then to reverence the Babu. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\na _kilta_ with a red top full of very wonderful things, not to be\r\nhandled by fools.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said it; I said it,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the bearer of that burden. \xe2\x80\x9cThinkest thou\r\nit will betray us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if it be given to me. I can draw out its magic. Otherwise it will\r\ndo great harm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA priest always takes his share.\xe2\x80\x9d Whisky was demoralizing the Ao-chung\r\nman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no matter to me.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim answered, with the craft of his\r\nmother-country. \xe2\x80\x9cShare it among you, and see what comes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot I. I was only jesting. Give the order. There is more than enough\r\nfor us all. We go our way from Shamlegh in the dawn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey arranged and re-arranged their artless little plans for another\r\nhour, while Kim shivered with cold and pride. The humour of the\r\nsituation tickled the Irish and the Oriental in his soul. Here were the\r\nemissaries of the dread Power of the North, very possibly as great in\r\ntheir own land as Mahbub or Colonel Creighton, suddenly smitten\r\nhelpless. One of them, he privately knew, would be lame for a time.\r\nThey had made promises to Kings. Tonight they lay out somewhere below\r\nhim, chartless, foodless, tentless, gunless\xe2\x80\x94except for Hurree Babu,\r\nguideless. And this collapse of their Great Game (Kim wondered to whom\r\nthey would report it), this panicky bolt into the night, had come about\r\nthrough no craft of Hurree\xe2\x80\x99s or contrivance of Kim\xe2\x80\x99s, but simply,\r\nbeautifully, and inevitably as the capture of Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s _faquir_-friends\r\nby the zealous young policeman at Umballa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are there\xe2\x80\x94with nothing; and, by Jove, it is cold! I am here with\r\nall their things. Oh, they will be angry! I am sorry for Hurree Babu.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim might have saved his pity, for though at that moment the Bengali\r\nsuffered acutely in the flesh, his soul was puffed and lofty. A mile\r\ndown the hill, on the edge of the pine-forest, two half-frozen men\xe2\x80\x94one\r\npowerfully sick at intervals\xe2\x80\x94were varying mutual recriminations with\r\nthe most poignant abuse of the Babu, who seemed distraught with terror.\r\nThey demanded a plan of action. He explained that they were very lucky\r\nto be alive; that their coolies, if not then stalking them, had passed\r\nbeyond recall; that the Rajah, his master, was ninety miles away, and,\r\nso far from lending them money and a retinue for the Simla journey,\r\nwould surely cast them into prison if he heard that they had hit a\r\npriest. He enlarged on this sin and its consequences till they bade him\r\nchange the subject. Their one hope, said he, was unostentatious flight\r\nfrom village to village till they reached civilization; and, for the\r\nhundredth time dissolved in tears, he demanded of the high stars why\r\nthe Sahibs \xe2\x80\x9chad beaten holy man\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\nTen steps would have taken Hurree into the creaking gloom utterly\r\nbeyond their reach\xe2\x80\x94to the shelter and food of the nearest village,\r\nwhere glib-tongued doctors were scarce. But he preferred to endure\r\ncold, belly-pinch, bad words, and occasional blows in the company of\r\nhis honoured employers. Crouched against a tree-trunk, he sniffed\r\ndolefully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd have you thought,\xe2\x80\x9d said the uninjured man hotly, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat sort of\r\nspectacle we shall present wandering through these hills among these\r\naborigines?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHurree Babu had thought of little else for some hours, but the remark\r\nwas not to his address.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe cannot wander! I can hardly walk,\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Kim\xe2\x80\x99s victim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps the holy man will be merciful in loving-kindness, sar,\r\notherwise\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI promise myself a peculiar pleasure in emptying my revolver into that\r\nyoung bonze when next we meet,\xe2\x80\x9d was the unchristian answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRevolvers! Vengeance! Bonzes!\xe2\x80\x9d Hurree crouched lower. The war was\r\nbreaking out afresh. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you no consideration for our loss? The\r\nbaggage! The baggage!\xe2\x80\x9d He could hear the speaker literally dancing on\r\nthe grass. \xe2\x80\x9cEverything we bore! Everything we have secured! Our gains!\r\nEight months\xe2\x80\x99 work! Do you know what that means? \xe2\x80\x98Decidedly it is we\r\nwho can deal with Orientals!\xe2\x80\x99 Oh, you have done well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey fell to it in several tongues, and Hurree smiled. Kim was with the\r\n_kiltas_, and in the _kiltas_ lay eight months of good diplomacy. There\r\nwas no means of communicating with the boy, but he could be trusted.\r\nFor the rest, Hurree could so stage-manage the journey through the\r\nhills that Hil\xc3\xa1s, Bun\xc3\xa1r, and four hundred miles of hill-roads should\r\ntell the tale for a generation. Men who cannot control their own\r\ncoolies are little respected in the Hills, and the hillman has a very\r\nkeen sense of humour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I had done it myself,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Hurree, \xe2\x80\x9cit would not have been\r\nbetter; and, by Jove, now I think of it, of course I arranged it\r\nmyself. How quick I have been! Just when I ran downhill I thought it!\r\nThee outrage was accidental, but onlee me could have worked it\xe2\x80\x94ah\xe2\x80\x94for\r\nall it was dam\xe2\x80\x99-well worth. Consider the moral effect upon these\r\nignorant peoples! No treaties\xe2\x80\x94no papers\xe2\x80\x94no written documents at all\xe2\x80\x94and\r\n_me_ to interpret for them. How I shall laugh with the Colonel! I wish\r\nI had their papers also: but you cannot occupy two places in space\r\nsimultaneously. Thatt is axiomatic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV\r\n\r\n\r\nMy brother kneels (so saith Kabir)\r\n    To stone and brass in heathen wise,\r\nBut in my brother\xe2\x80\x99s voice I hear\r\n    My own unanswered agonies.\r\nHis God is as his Fates assign\xe2\x80\x94\r\nHis prayer is all the world\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94and mine.\r\n\r\nKABIR.\r\n\r\nAt moonrise the cautious coolies got under way. The lama, refreshed by\r\nhis sleep and the spirit, needed no more than Kim\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder to bear\r\nhim along\xe2\x80\x94a silent, swift-striding man. They held the shale-sprinkled\r\ngrass for an hour, swept round the shoulder of an immortal cliff, and\r\nclimbed into a new country entirely blocked off from all sight of Chini\r\nvalley. A huge pasture-ground ran up fan-shaped to the living snow. At\r\nits base was perhaps half an acre of flat land, on which stood a few\r\nsoil and timber huts. Behind them\xe2\x80\x94for, hill-fashion, they were perched\r\non the edge of all things\xe2\x80\x94the ground fell sheer two thousand feet to\r\nShamlegh-midden, where never yet man has set foot.\r\n\r\nThe men made no motion to divide the plunder till they had seen the\r\nlama bedded down in the best room of the place, with Kim shampooing his\r\nfeet, Mohammedan-fashion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will send food,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Ao-chung man, \xe2\x80\x9cand the red-topped\r\n_kilta_. By dawn there will be none to give evidence, one way or the\r\nother. If anything is not needed in the _kilta_\xe2\x80\x94see here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pointed through the window\xe2\x80\x94opening into space that was filled with\r\nmoonlight reflected from the snow\xe2\x80\x94and threw out an empty whisky-bottle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo need to listen for the fall. This is the world\xe2\x80\x99s end,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and\r\nwent out. The lama looked forth, a hand on either sill, with eyes that\r\nshone like yellow opals. From the enormous pit before him white peaks\r\nlifted themselves yearning to the moonlight. The rest was as the\r\ndarkness of interstellar space.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly, \xe2\x80\x9care indeed my Hills. Thus should a man abide,\r\nperched above the world, separated from delights, considering vast\r\nmatters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; if he has a _chela_ to prepare tea for him, and to fold a blanket\r\nfor his head, and to chase out calving cows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA smoky lamp burned in a niche, but the full moonlight beat it down;\r\nand by the mixed light, stooping above the food-bag and cups, Kim moved\r\nlike a tall ghost.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAi! But now I have let the blood cool, my head still beats and drums,\r\nand there is a cord round the back of my neck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo wonder. It was a strong blow. May he who dealt it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut for my own passions there would have been no evil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat evil? Thou hast saved the Sahibs from the death they deserved a\r\nhundred times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe lesson is not well learnt, _chela_.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama came to rest on a\r\nfolded blanket, as Kim went forward with his evening routine. \xe2\x80\x9cThe blow\r\nwas but a shadow upon a shadow. Evil in itself\xe2\x80\x94my legs weary apace\r\nthese latter days!\xe2\x80\x94it met evil in me: anger, rage, and a lust to return\r\nevil. These wrought in my blood, woke tumult in my stomach, and dazzled\r\nmy ears.\xe2\x80\x9d Here he drank scalding black-tea ceremonially, taking the hot\r\ncup from Kim\xe2\x80\x99s hand. \xe2\x80\x9cHad I been passionless, the evil blow would have\r\ndone only bodily evil\xe2\x80\x94a scar, or a bruise\xe2\x80\x94which is illusion. But my\r\nmind was _not_ abstracted, for rushed in straightway a lust to let the\r\nSpiti men kill. In fighting that lust, my soul was torn and wrenched\r\nbeyond a thousand blows. Not till I had repeated the Blessings\xe2\x80\x9d (he\r\nmeant the Buddhist Beatitudes) \xe2\x80\x9cdid I achieve calm. But the evil\r\nplanted in me by that moment\xe2\x80\x99s carelessness works out to its end. Just\r\nis the Wheel, swerving not a hair! Learn the lesson, _chela_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is too high for me,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim muttered. \xe2\x80\x9cI am still all shaken. I am\r\nglad I hurt the man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI felt that, sleeping upon thy knees, in the wood below. It disquieted\r\nme in my dreams\xe2\x80\x94the evil in thy soul working through to mine. Yet on\r\nthe other hand\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he loosed his rosary\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI have acquired merit by saving\r\ntwo lives\xe2\x80\x94the lives of those that wronged me. Now I must see into the\r\nCause of Things. The boat of my soul staggers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSleep, and be strong. That is wisest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI meditate. There is a need greater than thou knowest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTill the dawn, hour after hour, as the moonlight paled on the high\r\npeaks, and that which had been belted blackness on the sides of the far\r\nhills showed as tender green forest, the lama stared fixedly at the\r\nwall. From time to time he groaned. Outside the barred door, where\r\ndiscomfited kine came to ask for their old stable, Shamlegh and the\r\ncoolies gave itself up to plunder and riotous living. The Ao-chung man\r\nwas their leader, and once they had opened the Sahibs\xe2\x80\x99 tinned foods and\r\nfound that they were very good they dared not turn back. Shamlegh\r\nkitchen-midden took the dunnage.\r\n\r\nWhen Kim, after a night of bad dreams, stole forth to brush his teeth\r\nin the morning chill, a fair-coloured woman with turquoise-studded\r\nheadgear drew him aside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe others have gone. They left thee this _kilta_ as the promise was.\r\nI do not love Sahibs, but thou wilt make us a charm in return for it.\r\nWe do not wish little Shamlegh to get a bad name on account of\r\nthe\xe2\x80\x94accident. I am the Woman of Shamlegh.\xe2\x80\x9d She looked him over with\r\nbold, bright eyes, unlike the usual furtive glance of hillwomen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly. But it must be done in secret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe raised the heavy _kilta_ like a toy and slung it into her own hut.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut and bar the door! Let none come near till it is finished,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nKim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut afterwards\xe2\x80\x94we may talk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim tilted the _kilta_ on the floor\xe2\x80\x94a cascade of Survey-instruments,\r\nbooks, diaries, letters, maps, and queerly scented native\r\ncorrespondence. At the very bottom was an embroidered bag covering a\r\nsealed, gilded, and illuminated document such as one King sends to\r\nanother. Kim caught his breath with delight, and reviewed the situation\r\nfrom a Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s point of view.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe books I do not want. Besides, they are logarithms\xe2\x80\x94Survey, I\r\nsuppose.\xe2\x80\x9d He laid them aside. \xe2\x80\x9cThe letters I do not understand, but\r\nColonel Creighton will. They must all be kept. The maps\xe2\x80\x94they draw\r\nbetter maps than me\xe2\x80\x94of course. All the native letters\xe2\x80\x94oho!\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nparticularly the _murasla_.\xe2\x80\x9d He sniffed the embroidered bag. \xe2\x80\x9cThat must\r\nbe from Hil\xc3\xa1s or Bun\xc3\xa1r, and Hurree Babu spoke truth. By Jove! It is a\r\nfine haul. I wish Hurree could know ... The rest must go out of the\r\nwindow.\xe2\x80\x9d He fingered a superb prismatic compass and the shiny top of a\r\ntheodolite. But after all, a Sahib cannot very well steal, and the\r\nthings might be inconvenient evidence later. He sorted out every scrap\r\nof manuscript, every map, and the native letters. They made one softish\r\nslab. The three locked ferril-backed books, with five worn\r\npocket-books, he put aside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe letters and the _murasla_ I must carry inside my coat and under my\r\nbelt, and the hand-written books I must put into the food-bag. It will\r\nbe very heavy. No. I do not think there is anything more. If there is,\r\nthe coolies have thrown it down the _khud_, so _thatt_ is all right.\r\nNow you go too.\xe2\x80\x9d He repacked the _kilta_ with all he meant to lose, and\r\nhove it up on to the windowsill. A thousand feet below lay a long,\r\nlazy, round-shouldered bank of mist, as yet untouched by the morning\r\nsun. A thousand feet below that was a hundred-year-old pine-forest. He\r\ncould see the green tops looking like a bed of moss when a wind-eddy\r\nthinned the cloud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! I don\xe2\x80\x99t think any one will go after _you!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe wheeling basket vomited its contents as it dropped. The theodolite\r\nhit a jutting cliff-ledge and exploded like a shell; the books,\r\ninkstands, paint-boxes, compasses, and rulers showed for a few seconds\r\nlike a swarm of bees. Then they vanished; and, though Kim, hanging half\r\nout of the window, strained his young ears, never a sound came up from\r\nthe gulf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive hundred\xe2\x80\x94a thousand rupees could not buy them,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought\r\nsorrowfully. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was verree wasteful, but I have all their other\r\nstuff\xe2\x80\x94everything they did\xe2\x80\x94I hope. Now how the deuce am I to tell Hurree\r\nBabu, and _whatt_ the deuce am I to do? And my old man is sick. I must\r\ntie up the letters in oilskin. That is something to do first\xe2\x80\x94else they\r\nwill get all sweated ... And I am all alone!\xe2\x80\x9d He bound them into a neat\r\npacket, swedging down the stiff, sticky oilskin at the comers, for his\r\nroving life had made him as methodical as an old hunter in matters of\r\nthe road. Then with double care he packed away the books at the bottom\r\nof the food-bag.\r\n\r\nThe woman rapped at the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou hast made no charm,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no need.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim had completely overlooked the necessity for a\r\nlittle patter-talk. The woman laughed at his confusion irreverently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone\xe2\x80\x94for thee. Thou canst cast a spell by the mere winking of an eye.\r\nBut think of us poor people when thou art gone. They were all too drunk\r\nlast night to hear a woman. Thou art not drunk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a priest.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim had recovered himself, and, the woman being aught\r\nbut unlovely, thought best to stand on his office.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI warned them that the Sahibs will be angry and will make an\r\ninquisition and a report to the Rajah. There is also the Babu with\r\nthem. Clerks have long tongues.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that all thy trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d The plan rose fully formed in Kim\xe2\x80\x99s mind,\r\nand he smiled ravishingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot all,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth the woman, putting out a hard brown hand all covered\r\nwith turquoises set in silver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can finish that in a breath,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Babu is the\r\nvery _hakim_ (thou hast heard of him?) who was wandering among the\r\nhills by Ziglaur. I know him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will tell for the sake of a reward. Sahibs cannot distinguish one\r\nhillman from another, but Babus have eyes for men\xe2\x80\x94and women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCarry a word to him from me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is nothing I would not do for thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe accepted the compliment calmly, as men must in lands where women\r\nmake the love, tore a leaf from a note-book, and with a patent\r\nindelible pencil wrote in gross Shikast\xe2\x80\x94the script that bad little boys\r\nuse when they write dirt on walls: \xe2\x80\x9c_I have everything that they have\r\nwritten: their pictures of the country, and many letters. Especially\r\nthe murasla. Tell me what to do. I am at Shamlegh-under-the-Snow. The\r\nold man is sick._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake this to him. It will altogether shut his mouth. He cannot have\r\ngone far.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed no. They are still in the forest across the spur. Our children\r\nwent to watch them when the light came, and have cried the news as they\r\nmoved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim looked his astonishment; but from the edge of the sheep-pasture\r\nfloated a shrill, kite-like trill. A child tending cattle had picked it\r\nup from a brother or sister on the far side of the slope that commanded\r\nChini valley.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy husbands are also out there gathering wood.\xe2\x80\x9d She drew a handful of\r\nwalnuts from her bosom, split one neatly, and began to eat. Kim\r\naffected blank ignorance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDost thou not know the meaning of the walnut\xe2\x80\x94priest?\xe2\x80\x9d she said coyly,\r\nand handed him the half-shells.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell thought of.\xe2\x80\x9d He slipped the piece of paper between them quickly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou a little wax to close them on this letter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman sighed aloud, and Kim relented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no payment till service has been rendered. Carry this to the\r\nBabu, and say it was sent by the Son of the Charm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAi! Truly! Truly! By a magician\xe2\x80\x94who is like a Sahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, a Son of the Charm: and ask if there be any answer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if he offer a rudeness? I\xe2\x80\x94I am afraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is, I have no doubt, very tired and very hungry. The\r\nHills make cold bedfellows. Hai, my\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94it was on the tip of his tongue to\r\nsay Mother, but he turned it to Sister\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthou art a wise and witty\r\nwoman. By this time all the villages know what has befallen the\r\nSahibs\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. News was at Ziglaur by midnight, and by tomorrow should be at\r\nKotgarh. The villages are both afraid and angry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo need. Tell the villages to feed the Sahibs and pass them on, in\r\npeace. We must get them quietly away from our valleys. To steal is one\r\nthing\xe2\x80\x94to kill another. The Babu will understand, and there will be no\r\nafter-complaints. Be swift. I must tend my master when he wakes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo be it. After service\xe2\x80\x94thou hast said?\xe2\x80\x94comes the reward. I am the\r\nWoman of Shamlegh, and I hold from the Rajah. I am no common bearer of\r\nbabes. Shamlegh is thine: hoof and horn and hide, milk and butter. Take\r\nor leave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned resolutely uphill, her silver necklaces clicking on her\r\nbroad breast, to meet the morning sun fifteen hundred feet above them.\r\nThis time Kim thought in the vernacular as he waxed down the oilskin\r\nedges of the packets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can a man follow the Way or the Great Game when he is so\xe2\x80\x94always\r\npestered by women? There was that girl at Akrola of the Ford; and there\r\nwas the scullion\xe2\x80\x99s wife behind the dovecot\xe2\x80\x94not counting the others\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nnow comes this one! When I was a child it was well enough, but now I am\r\na man and they will not regard me as a man. Walnuts, indeed! Ho! ho! It\r\nis almonds in the Plains!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went out to levy on the village\xe2\x80\x94not with a begging-bowl, which might\r\ndo for down-country, but in the manner of a prince. Shamlegh\xe2\x80\x99s summer\r\npopulation is only three families\xe2\x80\x94four women and eight or nine men.\r\nThey were all full of tinned meats and mixed drinks, from ammoniated\r\nquinine to white vodka, for they had taken their full share in the\r\novernight loot. The neat Continental tents had been cut up and shared\r\nlong ago, and there were patent aluminium saucepans abroad.\r\n\r\nBut they considered the lama\xe2\x80\x99s presence a perfect safeguard against all\r\nconsequences, and impenitently brought Kim of their best\xe2\x80\x94even to a\r\ndrink of _chang_\xe2\x80\x94the barley-beer that comes from Ladakh-way. Then they\r\nthawed out in the sun, and sat with their legs hanging over infinite\r\nabysses, chattering, laughing, and smoking. They judged India and its\r\nGovernment solely from their experience of wandering Sahibs who had\r\nemployed them or their friends as _shikarris_. Kim heard tales of shots\r\nmissed upon ibex, serow, or markhor, by Sahibs twenty years in their\r\ngraves\xe2\x80\x94every detail lighted from behind like twigs on tree-tops seen\r\nagainst lightning. They told him of their little diseases, and, more\r\nimportant, the diseases of their tiny, sure-footed cattle; of trips as\r\nfar as Kotgarh, where the strange missionaries live, and beyond even to\r\nmarvellous Simla, where the streets are paved with silver, and anyone,\r\nlook you, can get service with the Sahibs, who ride about in\r\ntwo-wheeled carts and spend money with a spade. Presently, grave and\r\naloof, walking very heavily, the lama joined himself to the chatter\r\nunder the eaves, and they gave him great room. The thin air refreshed\r\nhim, and he sat on the edge of precipices with the best of them, and,\r\nwhen talk languished, flung pebbles into the void. Thirty miles away,\r\nas the eagle flies, lay the next range, seamed and channelled and\r\npitted with little patches of brush\xe2\x80\x94forests, each a day\xe2\x80\x99s dark march.\r\nBehind the village, Shamlegh hill itself cut off all view to southward.\r\nIt was like sitting in a swallow\xe2\x80\x99s nest under the eaves of the roof of\r\nthe world.\r\n\r\nFrom time to time the lama stretched out his hand, and with a little\r\nlow-voiced prompting would point out the road to Spiti and north across\r\nthe Parungla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeyond, where the hills lie thickest, lies De-ch\xe2\x80\x99en\xe2\x80\x9d (he meant\r\nHan-l\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99), \xe2\x80\x9cthe great Monastery. s\xe2\x80\x99Tag-stan-ras-ch\xe2\x80\x99en built it, and of\r\nhim there runs this tale.\xe2\x80\x9d Whereupon he told it: a fantastic piled\r\nnarrative of bewitchment and miracles that set Shamlegh a-gasping.\r\nTurning west a little, he steered for the green hills of Kulu, and\r\nsought Kailung under the glaciers. \xe2\x80\x9cFor thither came I in the old, old\r\ndays. From Leh I came, over the Baralachi.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes; we know it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the far-faring people of Shamlegh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I slept two nights with the priests of Kailung. These are the\r\nHills of my delight! Shadows blessed above all other shadows! There my\r\neyes opened on this world; there my eyes were opened to this world;\r\nthere I found Enlightenment; and there I girt my loins for my Search.\r\nOut of the Hills I came\xe2\x80\x94the high Hills and the strong winds. Oh, just\r\nis the Wheel!\xe2\x80\x9d He blessed them in detail\xe2\x80\x94the great glaciers, the naked\r\nrocks, the piled moraines and tumbled shale; dry upland, hidden\r\nsalt-lake, age-old timber and fruitful water-shot valley one after the\r\nother, as a dying man blesses his folk; and Kim marvelled at his\r\npassion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94yes. There is no place like our Hills,\xe2\x80\x9d said the people of\r\nShamlegh. And they fell to wondering how a man could live in the hot\r\nterrible Plains where the cattle run as big as elephants, unfit to\r\nplough on a hillside; where village touches village, they had heard,\r\nfor a hundred miles; where folk went about stealing in gangs, and what\r\nthe robbers spared the Police carried utterly away.\r\n\r\nSo the still forenoon wore through, and at the end of it Kim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmessenger dropped from the steep pasture as unbreathed as when she had\r\nset out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sent a word to the _hakim_,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim explained, while she made\r\nreverence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe joined himself to the idolaters? Nay, I remember he did a healing\r\nupon one of them. He has acquired merit, though the healed employed his\r\nstrength for evil. Just is the Wheel! What of the _hakim?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feared that thou hadst been bruised and\xe2\x80\x94and I knew he was wise.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim\r\ntook the waxed walnut-shell and read in English on the back of his\r\nnote: _Your favour received. Cannot get away from present company at\r\npresent, but shall take them into Simla. After which, hope to rejoin\r\nyou. Inexpedient to follow angry gentlemen. Return by same road you\r\ncame, and will overtake. Highly gratified about correspondence due to\r\nmy forethought._ \xe2\x80\x9cHe says, Holy One, that he will escape from the\r\nidolaters, and will return to us. Shall we wait awhile at Shamlegh,\r\nthen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lama looked long and lovingly upon the hills and shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may not be, _chela_. From my bones outward I do desire it, but it\r\nis forbidden. I have seen the Cause of Things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? When the Hills give thee back thy strength day by day? Remember\r\nwe were weak and fainting down below there in the Doon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI became strong to do evil and to forget. A brawler and a swashbuckler\r\nupon the hillsides was I.\xe2\x80\x9d Kim bit back a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cJust and perfect is\r\nthe Wheel, swerving not a hair. When I was a man\xe2\x80\x94a long time ago\xe2\x80\x94I did\r\npilgrimage to Guru Ch\xe2\x80\x99wan among the poplars\xe2\x80\x9d (he pointed Bhotanwards),\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhere they keep the Sacred Horse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuiet, be quiet!\xe2\x80\x9d said Shamlegh, all arow. \xe2\x80\x9cHe speaks of\r\nJam-lin-nin-k\xe2\x80\x99or, the Horse That Can Go Round The World In a Day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI speak to my _chela_ only,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, in gentle reproof, and\r\nthey scattered like frost on south eaves of a morning. \xe2\x80\x9cI did not seek\r\ntruth in those days, but the talk of doctrine. All illusion! I drank\r\nthe beer and ate the bread of Guru Ch\xe2\x80\x99wan. Next day one said: \xe2\x80\x98We go\r\nout to fight Sangor Gutok down the valley to discover\xe2\x80\x99 (mark again how\r\nLust is tied to Anger!) \xe2\x80\x98which Abbot shall bear rule in the valley and\r\ntake the profit of the prayers they print at Sangor Gutok.\xe2\x80\x99 I went, and\r\nwe fought a day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how, Holy One?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith our long pencases as I could have shown ... I say, we fought\r\nunder the poplars, both Abbots and all the monks, and one laid open my\r\nforehead to the bone. See!\xe2\x80\x9d He tilted back his cap and showed a\r\npuckered silvery scar. \xe2\x80\x9cJust and perfect is the Wheel! Yesterday the\r\nscar itched, and after fifty years I recalled how it was dealt and the\r\nface of him who dealt it; dwelling a little in illusion. Followed that\r\nwhich thou didst see\xe2\x80\x94strife and stupidity. Just is the Wheel! The\r\nidolater\xe2\x80\x99s blow fell upon the scar. Then I was shaken in my soul: my\r\nsoul was darkened, and the boat of my soul rocked upon the waters of\r\nillusion. Not till I came to Shamlegh could I meditate upon the Cause\r\nof Things, or trace the running grass-roots of Evil. I strove all the\r\nlong night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Holy One, thou art innocent of all evil. May I be thy sacrifice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim was genuinely distressed at the old man\xe2\x80\x99s sorrow, and Mahbub Ali\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nphrase slipped out unawares.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the dawn,\xe2\x80\x9d the lama went on more gravely, ready rosary clicking\r\nbetween the slow sentences, \xe2\x80\x9ccame enlightenment. It is here ... I am an\r\nold man ... hill-bred, hill-fed, never to sit down among my Hills.\r\nThree years I travelled through Hind, but\xe2\x80\x94can earth be stronger than\r\nMother Earth? My stupid body yearned to the Hills and the snows of the\r\nHills, from below there. I said, and it is true, my Search is sure. So,\r\nat the Kulu woman\xe2\x80\x99s house I turned hillward, over-persuaded by myself.\r\nThere is no blame to the _hakim_. He\xe2\x80\x94following Desire\xe2\x80\x94foretold that the\r\nHills would make me strong. They strengthened me to do evil, to forget\r\nmy Search. I delighted in life and the lust of life. I desired strong\r\nslopes to climb. I cast about to find them. I measured the strength of\r\nmy body, which is evil, against the high Hills, I made a mock of thee\r\nwhen thy breath came short under Jamnotri. I jested when thou wouldst\r\nnot face the snow of the pass.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what harm? I _was_ afraid. It was just. I am not a hillman; and I\r\nloved thee for thy new strength.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than once I remember\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he rested his cheek dolefully on his\r\nhand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI sought thy praise and the _hakim\xe2\x80\x99s_ for the mere strength of my\r\nlegs. Thus evil followed evil till the cup was full. Just is the Wheel!\r\nAll Hind for three years did me all honour. From the Fountain of Wisdom\r\nin the Wonder House to\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he smiled\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ca little child playing by a big\r\ngun\xe2\x80\x94the world prepared my road. And why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause we loved thee. It is only the fever of the blow. I myself am\r\nstill sick and shaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! It was because I was upon the Way\xe2\x80\x94tuned as are _si-nen_ (cymbals)\r\nto the purpose of the Law. I departed from that ordinance. The tune was\r\nbroken: followed the punishment. In my own Hills, on the edge of my own\r\ncountry, in the very place of my evil desire, comes the buffet\xe2\x80\x94here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(He touched his brow.) \xe2\x80\x9cAs a novice is beaten when he misplaces the\r\ncups, so am I beaten, who was Abbot of Such-zen. No word, look you, but\r\na blow, _chela_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the Sahibs did not know thee, Holy One?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were well matched. Ignorance and Lust met Ignorance and Lust upon\r\nthe road, and they begat Anger. The blow was a sign to me, who am no\r\nbetter than a strayed yak, that my place is not here. Who can read the\r\nCause of an act is halfway to Freedom! \xe2\x80\x98Back to the path,\xe2\x80\x99 says the\r\nBlow. \xe2\x80\x98The Hills are not for thee. Thou canst not choose Freedom and go\r\nin bondage to the delight of life.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould we had never met that cursed Russian!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur Lord Himself cannot make the Wheel swing backward. And for my\r\nmerit that I had acquired I gain yet another sign.\xe2\x80\x9d He put his hand in\r\nhis bosom, and drew forth the Wheel of Life. \xe2\x80\x9cLook! I considered this\r\nafter I had meditated. There remains untorn by the idolater no more\r\nthan the breadth of my fingernail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much, then, is the span of my life in this body. I have served the\r\nWheel all my days. Now the Wheel serves me. But for the merit I have\r\nacquired in guiding thee upon the Way, there would have been added to\r\nme yet another life ere I had found my River. Is it plain, _chela?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim stared at the brutally disfigured chart. From left to right\r\ndiagonally the rent ran\xe2\x80\x94from the Eleventh House where Desire gives\r\nbirth to the Child (as it is drawn by Tibetans)\xe2\x80\x94across the human and\r\nanimal worlds, to the Fifth House\xe2\x80\x94the empty House of the Senses. The\r\nlogic was unanswerable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore our Lord won Enlightenment\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the lama folded all away with\r\nreverence\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cHe was tempted. I too have been tempted, but it is finished.\r\nThe Arrow fell in the Plains\xe2\x80\x94not in the Hills. Therefore, what make we\r\nhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we at least wait for the _hakim?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know how long I shall live in this body. What can a _hakim_ do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou art all sick and shaken. Thou canst not walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I be sick if I see Freedom?\xe2\x80\x9d He rose unsteadily to his feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I must get food from the village. Oh, the weary Road!\xe2\x80\x9d Kim felt\r\nthat he too needed rest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is lawful. Let us eat and go. The Arrow fell in the Plains ...\r\nbut I yielded to Desire. Make ready, _chela_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim turned to the woman with the turquoise headgear who had been idly\r\npitching pebbles over the cliff. She smiled very kindly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI found him like a strayed buffalo in a cornfield\xe2\x80\x94the Babu; snorting\r\nand sneezing with cold. He was so hungry that he forgot his dignity and\r\ngave me sweet words. The Sahibs have nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d She flung out an empty\r\npalm. \xe2\x80\x9cOne is very sick about the stomach. Thy work?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim nodded, with a bright eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI spoke to the Bengali first\xe2\x80\x94and to the people of a near-by village\r\nafter. The Sahibs will be given food as they need it\xe2\x80\x94nor will the\r\npeople ask money. The plunder is already distributed. The Babu makes\r\nlying speeches to the Sahibs. Why does he not leave them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut of the greatness of his heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas never a Bengali yet had one bigger than a dried walnut. But it is\r\nno matter ... Now as to walnuts. After service comes reward. I have\r\nsaid the village is thine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my loss,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim began. \xe2\x80\x9cEven now I had planned desirable things in\r\nmy heart which\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94there is no need to go through the compliments proper\r\nto these occasions. He sighed deeply ... \xe2\x80\x9cBut my master, led by a\r\nvision\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh! What can old eyes see except a full begging-bowl?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94turns from this village to the Plains again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBid him stay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cI know my Holy One, and his rage if he be\r\ncrossed,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied impressively. \xe2\x80\x9cHis curses shake the Hills.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPity they did not save him from a broken head! I heard that thou wast\r\nthe tiger-hearted one who smote the Sahib. Let him dream a little\r\nlonger. Stay!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHillwoman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, with austerity that could not harden the\r\noutlines of his young oval face, \xe2\x80\x9cthese matters are too high for thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gods be good to us! Since when have men and women been other than\r\nmen and women?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA priest is a priest. He says he will go upon this hour. I am his\r\n_chela_, and I go with him. We need food for the Road. He is an\r\nhonoured guest in all the villages, but\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he broke into a pure boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngrin\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe food here is good. Give me some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat if I do not give it thee? I am the woman of this village.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I curse thee\xe2\x80\x94a little\xe2\x80\x94not greatly, but enough to remember.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\ncould not help smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast cursed me already by the down-dropped eyelash and the\r\nuplifted chin. Curses? What should I care for mere words?\xe2\x80\x9d She clenched\r\nher hands upon her bosom ... \xe2\x80\x9cBut I would not have thee to go in anger,\r\nthinking hardly of me\xe2\x80\x94a gatherer of cow-dung and grass at Shamlegh, but\r\nstill a woman of substance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, \xe2\x80\x9cbut that I am grieved to go, for I am\r\nvery weary; and that we need food. Here is the bag.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman snatched it angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cI was foolish,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cWho is thy\r\nwoman in the Plains? Fair or black? I was fair once. Laughest thou?\r\nOnce, long ago, if thou canst believe, a Sahib looked on me with\r\nfavour. Once, long ago, I wore European clothes at the Mission-house\r\nyonder.\xe2\x80\x9d She pointed towards Kotgarh. \xe2\x80\x9cOnce, long ago. I was\r\n_Ker-lis-ti-an_ and spoke English\xe2\x80\x94as the Sahibs speak it. Yes. My Sahib\r\nsaid he would return and wed me\xe2\x80\x94yes, wed me. He went away\xe2\x80\x94I had nursed\r\nhim when he was sick\xe2\x80\x94but he never returned. Then I saw that the Gods of\r\nthe Kerlistians lied, and I went back to my own people ... I have never\r\nset eyes on a Sahib since. (Do not laugh at me. The fit is past, little\r\npriestling.) Thy face and thy walk and thy fashion of speech put me in\r\nmind of my Sahib, though thou art only a wandering mendicant to whom I\r\ngive a dole. Curse me? Thou canst neither curse nor bless!\xe2\x80\x9d She set her\r\nhands on her hips and laughed bitterly. \xe2\x80\x9cThy Gods are lies; thy works\r\nare lies; thy words are lies. There are no Gods under all the Heavens.\r\nI know it ... But for awhile I thought it was my Sahib come back, and\r\nhe was my God. Yes, once I made music on a _pianno_ in the\r\nMission-house at Kotgarh. Now I give alms to priests who are\r\n_heatthen_.\xe2\x80\x9d She wound up with the English word, and tied the mouth of\r\nthe brimming bag.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wait for thee, _chela_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, leaning against the\r\ndoor-post.\r\n\r\nThe woman swept the tall figure with her eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cHe walk! He cannot\r\ncover half a mile. Whither would old bones go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this Kim, already perplexed by the lama\xe2\x80\x99s collapse and foreseeing\r\nthe weight of the bag, fairly lost his temper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it to thee, woman of ill-omen, where he goes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing\xe2\x80\x94but something to thee, priest with a Sahib\xe2\x80\x99s face. Wilt thou\r\ncarry him on thy shoulders?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI go to the Plains. None must hinder my return. I have wrestled with\r\nmy soul till I am strengthless. The stupid body is spent, and we are\r\nfar from the Plains.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBehold!\xe2\x80\x9d she said simply, and drew aside to let Kim see his own utter\r\nhelplessness. \xe2\x80\x9cCurse me. Maybe it will give him strength. Make a charm!\r\nCall on thy great God. Thou art a priest.\xe2\x80\x9d She turned away.\r\n\r\nThe lama had squatted limply, still holding by the door-post. One\r\ncannot strike down an old man that he recovers again like a boy in the\r\nnight. Weakness bowed him to the earth, but his eyes that hung on Kim\r\nwere alive and imploring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the thin air that weakens thee. In a\r\nlittle while we go! It is the mountain-sickness. I too am a little sick\r\nat stomach,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and he knelt and comforted with such poor words as came\r\nfirst to his lips. Then the woman returned, more erect than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy Gods useless, heh? Try mine. I am the Woman of Shamlegh.\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nhailed hoarsely, and there came out of a cow-pen her two husbands and\r\nthree others with a _dooli_, the rude native litter of the Hills, that\r\nthey use for carrying the sick and for visits of state. \xe2\x80\x9cThese\r\ncattle\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she did not condescend to look at them\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9care thine for so long\r\nas thou shalt need.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we will not go Simla-way. We will not go near the Sahibs,\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nthe first husband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will not run away as the others did, nor will they steal baggage.\r\nTwo I know for weaklings. Stand to the rear-pole, Sonoo and Taree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThey obeyed swiftly. \xe2\x80\x9cLower now, and lift in that holy man. I will see\r\nto the village and your virtuous wives till ye return.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen will that be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk the priests. Do not pester me. Lay the food-bag at the foot, it\r\nbalances better so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Holy One, thy Hills are kinder than our Plains!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Kim,\r\nrelieved, as the lama tottered to the litter. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a very king\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbed\xe2\x80\x94a place of honour and ease. And we owe it to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA woman of ill-omen. I need thy blessings as much as I do thy curses.\r\nIt is _my_ order and none of thine. Lift and away! Here! Hast thou\r\nmoney for the road?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe beckoned Kim to her hut, and stooped above a battered English\r\ncash-box under her cot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not need anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, angered where he should have been\r\ngrateful. \xe2\x80\x9cI am already rudely loaded with favours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked up with a curious smile and laid a hand on his shoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cAt\r\nleast, thank me. I am foul-faced and a hillwoman, but, as thy talk\r\ngoes, I have acquired merit. Shall I show thee how the Sahibs render\r\nthanks?\xe2\x80\x9d and her hard eyes softened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am but a wandering priest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, his eyes lighting in answer.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou needest neither my blessings nor my curses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay. But for one little moment\xe2\x80\x94thou canst overtake the _dooli_ in ten\r\nstrides\xe2\x80\x94_if_ thou wast a Sahib, shall I show thee what thou wouldst\r\ndo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow if I guess, though?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim, and putting his arm round her\r\nwaist, he kissed her on the cheek, adding in English: \xe2\x80\x9cThank you verree\r\nmuch, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKissing is practically unknown among Asiatics, which may have been the\r\nreason that she leaned back with wide-open eyes and a face of panic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNext time,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim went on, \xe2\x80\x9cyou must not be so sure of your heatthen\r\npriests. Now I say good-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d He held out his hand English-fashion. She\r\ntook it mechanically. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she was remembering her English words one by\r\none\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cyou will come back again? Good-bye, and\xe2\x80\x94thee God bless you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later, as the creaking litter jolted up the hill path that\r\nleads south-easterly from Shamlegh, Kim saw a tiny figure at the hut\r\ndoor waving a white rag.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has acquired merit beyond all others,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cFor to set\r\na man upon the way to Freedom is half as great as though she had\r\nherself found it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmm,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim thoughtfully, considering the past. \xe2\x80\x9cIt may be that I\r\nhave acquired merit also ... At least she did not treat me like a\r\nchild.\xe2\x80\x9d He hitched the front of his robe, where lay the slab of\r\ndocuments and maps, re-stowed the precious food-bag at the lama\xe2\x80\x99s feet,\r\nlaid his hand on the litter\xe2\x80\x99s edge, and buckled down to the slow pace\r\nof the grunting husbands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese also acquire merit,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama after three miles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than that, they shall be paid in silver,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Kim. The Woman of\r\nShamlegh had given it to him; and it was only fair, he argued, that her\r\nmen should earn it back again.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV\r\n\r\n\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d not give room for an Emperor\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    I\xe2\x80\x99d hold my road for a King.\r\nTo the Triple Crown I\xe2\x80\x99d not bow down\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    But this is a different thing!\r\n_I_\xe2\x80\x99ll not fight with the Powers of Air\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    Sentries pass him through!\r\nDrawbridge let fall\xe2\x80\x94He\xe2\x80\x99s the Lord of us all\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    The Dreamer whose dream came true!\r\n\r\n_The Siege of the Fairies._\r\n\r\nTwo hundred miles north of Chini, on the blue shale of Ladakh, lies\r\nYankling Sahib, the merry-minded man, spy-glassing wrathfully across\r\nthe ridges for some sign of his pet tracker\xe2\x80\x94a man from Ao-chung. But\r\nthat renegade, with a new Mannlicher rifle and two hundred cartridges,\r\nis elsewhere, shooting musk-deer for the market, and Yankling Sahib\r\nwill learn next season how very ill he has been.\r\n\r\nUp the valleys of Bushahr\xe2\x80\x94the far-beholding eagles of the Himalayas\r\nswerve at his new blue-and-white gored umbrella\xe2\x80\x94hurries a Bengali, once\r\nfat and well-looking, now lean and weather-worn. He has received the\r\nthanks of two foreigners of distinction, piloted not unskilfully to\r\nMashobra tunnel, which leads to the great and gay capital of India. It\r\nwas not his fault that, blanketed by wet mists, he conveyed them past\r\nthe telegraph-station and European colony of Kotgarh. It was not his\r\nfault, but that of the Gods, of whom he discoursed so engagingly, that\r\nhe led them into the borders of Nahan, where the Rajah of that state\r\nmistook them for deserting British soldiery. Hurree Babu explained the\r\ngreatness and glory, in their own country, of his companions, till the\r\ndrowsy kinglet smiled. He explained it to everyone who asked\xe2\x80\x94many\r\ntimes\xe2\x80\x94aloud\xe2\x80\x94variously. He begged food, arranged accommodation, proved a\r\nskilful leech for an injury of the groin\xe2\x80\x94such a blow as one may receive\r\nrolling down a rock-covered hillside in the dark\xe2\x80\x94and in all things\r\nindispensable. The reason of his friendliness did him credit. With\r\nmillions of fellow-serfs, he had learned to look upon Russia as the\r\ngreat deliverer from the North. He was a fearful man. He had been\r\nafraid that he could not save his illustrious employers from the anger\r\nof an excited peasantry. He himself would just as lief hit a holy man\r\nas not, but ... He was deeply grateful and sincerely rejoiced that he\r\nhad done his \xe2\x80\x9clittle possible\xe2\x80\x9d towards bringing their venture\r\nto\xe2\x80\x94barring the lost baggage\xe2\x80\x94a successful issue, he had forgotten the\r\nblows; denied that any blows had been dealt that unseemly first night\r\nunder the pines. He asked neither pension nor retaining fee, but, if\r\nthey deemed him worthy, would they write him a testimonial? It might be\r\nuseful to him later, if others, their friends, came over the Passes. He\r\nbegged them to remember him in their future greatnesses, for he \xe2\x80\x9copined\r\nsubtly\xe2\x80\x9d that he, even he, Mohendro Lal Dutt, MA of Calcutta, had \xe2\x80\x9cdone\r\nthe State some service\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\nThey gave him a certificate praising his courtesy, helpfulness, and\r\nunerring skill as a guide. He put it in his waist-belt and sobbed with\r\nemotion; they had endured so many dangers together. He led them at high\r\nnoon along crowded Simla Mall to the Alliance Bank of Simla, where they\r\nwished to establish their identity. Thence he vanished like a\r\ndawn-cloud on Jakko.\r\n\r\nBehold him, too fine-drawn to sweat, too pressed to vaunt the drugs in\r\nhis little brass-bound box, ascending Shamlegh slope, a just man made\r\nperfect. Watch him, all Babudom laid aside, smoking at noon on a cot,\r\nwhile a woman with turquoise-studded headgear points south-easterly\r\nacross the bare grass. Litters, she says, do not travel as fast as\r\nsingle men, but his birds should now be in the Plains. The holy man\r\nwould not stay though Lispeth pressed him. The Babu groans heavily,\r\ngirds up his huge loins, and is off again. He does not care to travel\r\nafter dusk; but his days\xe2\x80\x99 marches\xe2\x80\x94there is none to enter them in a\r\nbook\xe2\x80\x94would astonish folk who mock at his race. Kindly villagers,\r\nremembering the Dacca drug-vendor of two months ago, give him shelter\r\nagainst evil spirits of the wood. He dreams of Bengali Gods, University\r\ntext-books of education, and the Royal Society, London, England. Next\r\ndawn the bobbing blue-and-white umbrella goes forward.\r\n\r\nOn the edge of the Doon, Mussoorie well behind them and the Plains\r\nspread out in golden dust before, rests a worn litter in which\xe2\x80\x94all the\r\nHills know it\xe2\x80\x94lies a sick lama who seeks a River for his healing.\r\nVillages have almost come to blows over the honour of bearing it, for\r\nnot only has the lama given them blessings, but his disciple good\r\nmoney\xe2\x80\x94full one-third Sahibs\xe2\x80\x99 prices. Twelve miles a day has the _dooli_\r\ntravelled, as the greasy, rubbed pole-ends show, and by roads that few\r\nSahibs use. Over the Nilang Pass in storm when the driven snow-dust\r\nfilled every fold of the impassive lama\xe2\x80\x99s drapery; between the black\r\nhorns of Raieng where they heard the whistle of the wild goats through\r\nthe clouds; pitching and strained on the shale below; hard-held between\r\nshoulder and clenched jaw when they rounded the hideous curves of the\r\nCut Road under Bhagirati; swinging and creaking to the steady jog-trot\r\nof the descent into the Valley of the Waters; pressed along the steamy\r\nlevels of that locked valley; up, up and out again, to meet the roaring\r\ngusts off Kedarnath; set down of mid-days in the dun gloom of kindly\r\noak-forests; passed from village to village in dawn-chill, when even\r\ndevotees may be forgiven for swearing at impatient holy men; or by\r\ntorchlight, when the least fearful think of ghosts\xe2\x80\x94the _dooli_ has\r\nreached her last stage. The little hill-folk sweat in the modified heat\r\nof the lower Siwaliks, and gather round the priests for their blessing\r\nand their wage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe have acquired merit,\xe2\x80\x9d says the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cMerit greater than your\r\nknowing. And ye will return to the Hills,\xe2\x80\x9d he sighs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely. The high Hills as soon as may be.\xe2\x80\x9d The bearer rubs his\r\nshoulder, drinks water, spits it out again, and readjusts his grass\r\nsandal. Kim\xe2\x80\x94his face is drawn and tired\xe2\x80\x94pays very small silver from his\r\nbelt, heaves out the food-bag, crams an oilskin packet\xe2\x80\x94they are holy\r\nwritings\xe2\x80\x94into his bosom, and helps the lama to his feet. The peace has\r\ncome again into the old man\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, and he does not look for the hills\r\nto fall down and crush him as he did that terrible night when they were\r\ndelayed by the flooded river.\r\n\r\nThe men pick up the _dooli_ and swing out of sight between the scrub\r\nclumps.\r\n\r\nThe lama raises a hand toward the rampart of the Himalayas. \xe2\x80\x9cNot with\r\nyou, O blessed among all hills, fell the Arrow of Our Lord! And never\r\nshall I breathe your airs again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou art ten times the stronger man in this good air,\xe2\x80\x9d says Kim,\r\nfor to his wearied soul appeal the well-cropped, kindly Plains. \xe2\x80\x9cHere,\r\nor hereabouts, fell the Arrow, yes. We will go very softly, perhaps, a\r\n_kos_ a day, for the Search is sure. But the bag weighs heavy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, our Search is sure. I have come out of great temptation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was never more than a couple of miles a day now, and Kim\xe2\x80\x99s shoulders\r\nbore all the weight of it\xe2\x80\x94the burden of an old man, the burden of the\r\nheavy food-bag with the locked books, the load of the writings on his\r\nheart, and the details of the daily routine. He begged in the dawn, set\r\nblankets for the lama\xe2\x80\x99s meditation, held the weary head on his lap\r\nthrough the noonday heats, fanning away the flies till his wrists\r\nached, begged again in the evenings, and rubbed the lama\xe2\x80\x99s feet, who\r\nrewarded him with promise of Freedom\xe2\x80\x94today, tomorrow, or, at furthest,\r\nthe next day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever was such a _chela_. I doubt at times whether Ananda more\r\nfaithfully nursed Our Lord. And thou art a Sahib? When I was a man\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nlong time ago\xe2\x80\x94I forgot that. Now I look upon thee often, and every time\r\nI remember that thou art a Sahib. It is strange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast said there is neither black nor white. Why plague me with\r\nthis talk, Holy One? Let me rub the other foot. It vexes me. I am _not_\r\na Sahib. I am thy _chela_, and my head is heavy on my shoulders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPatience a little! We reach Freedom together. Then thou and I, upon\r\nthe far bank of the River, will look back upon our lives as in the\r\nHills we saw our days\xe2\x80\x99 marches laid out behind us. Perhaps I was once a\r\nSahib.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas never a Sahib like thee, I swear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am certain the Keeper of the Images in the Wonder House was in past\r\nlife a very wise Abbot. But even his spectacles do not make my eyes\r\nsee. There fall shadows when I would look steadily. No matter\xe2\x80\x94we know\r\nthe tricks of the poor stupid carcass\xe2\x80\x94shadow changing to another\r\nshadow. I am bound by the illusion of Time and Space. How far came we\r\ntoday in the flesh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps half a koss.\xe2\x80\x9d (Three quarters of a mile, and it was a weary\r\nmarch.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf a koss. Ha!
