I went ten thousand thousand in the spirit. How, we\r\nare all lapped and swathed and swaddled in these senseless things.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nlooked at his thin blue-veined hand that found the beads so heavy.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Chela_, hast thou never a wish to leave me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim thought of the oilskin packet and the books in the food-bag. If\r\nsomeone duly authorized would only take delivery of them the Great Game\r\nmight play itself for aught he then cared. He was tired and hot in his\r\nhead, and a cough that came from the stomach worried him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d he said almost sternly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not a dog or a snake to bite when I\r\nhave learned to love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art too tender towards me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot that either. I have moved in one matter without consulting thee. I\r\nhave sent a message to the Kulu woman by that woman who gave us the\r\ngoat\xe2\x80\x99s milk this morn, saying that thou wast a little feeble and\r\nwouldst need a litter. I beat myself in my mind that I did not do it\r\nwhen we entered the Doon. We stay in this place till the litter\r\nreturns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am content. She is a woman with a heart of gold, as thou sayest, but\r\na talker\xe2\x80\x94something of a talker.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will not weary thee. I have looked to that also. Holy One, my\r\nheart is very heavy for my many carelessnesses towards thee.\xe2\x80\x9d An\r\nhysterical catch rose in his throat. \xe2\x80\x9cI have walked thee too far: I\r\nhave not picked good food always for thee; I have not considered the\r\nheat; I have talked to people on the road and left thee alone ... I\r\nhave\xe2\x80\x94I have ... _Hai mai!_ But I love thee ... and it is all too late\r\n... I was a child ... Oh, why was I not a man? ...\xe2\x80\x9d Overborne by\r\nstrain, fatigue, and the weight beyond his years, Kim broke down and\r\nsobbed at the lama\xe2\x80\x99s feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a to-do is here!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man gently. \xe2\x80\x9cThou hast never\r\nstepped a hair\xe2\x80\x99s breadth from the Way of Obedience. Neglect me? Child,\r\nI have lived on thy strength as an old tree lives on the lime of a new\r\nwall. Day by day, since Shamlegh down, I have stolen strength from\r\nthee. _Therefore_, not through any sin of thine, art thou weakened. It\r\nis the Body\xe2\x80\x94the silly, stupid Body\xe2\x80\x94that speaks now. Not the assured\r\nSoul. Be comforted! Know at least the devils that thou fightest. They\r\nare earth-born\xe2\x80\x94children of illusion. We will go to the woman from Kulu.\r\nShe shall acquire merit in housing us, and specially in tending me.\r\nThou shalt run free till strength returns. I had forgotten the stupid\r\nBody. If there be any blame, I bear it. But we are too close to the\r\nGates of Deliverance to weigh blame. I could praise thee, but what\r\nneed? In a little\xe2\x80\x94in a very little\xe2\x80\x94we shall sit beyond all needs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so he petted and comforted Kim with wise saws and grave texts on\r\nthat little-understood beast, our Body, who, being but a delusion,\r\ninsists on posing as the Soul, to the darkening of the Way, and the\r\nimmense multiplication of unnecessary devils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHai! hai! Let us talk of the woman from Kulu. Think you she will ask\r\nanother charm for her grandsons? When I was a young man, a very long\r\ntime ago, I was plagued with these vapours\xe2\x80\x94and some others\xe2\x80\x94and I went\r\nto an Abbot\xe2\x80\x94a very holy man and a seeker after truth, though then I\r\nknew it not. Sit up and listen, child of my soul! My tale was told.\r\nSaid he to me, \xe2\x80\x98_Chela_, know this. There are many lies in the world,\r\nand not a few liars, but there are no liars like our bodies, except it\r\nbe the sensations of our bodies.\xe2\x80\x99 Considering this I was comforted, and\r\nof his great favour he suffered me to drink tea In his presence. Suffer\r\nme now to drink tea, for I am thirsty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a laugh across his tears, Kim kissed the lama\xe2\x80\x99s feet, and set\r\nabout the tea-making.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou leanest on me in the body, Holy One, but I lean on thee for some\r\nother things. Dost know it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have guessed maybe,\xe2\x80\x9d and the lama\xe2\x80\x99s eyes twinkled. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must change\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo, when with scufflings and scrapings and a hot air of importance,\r\npaddled up nothing less than the Sahiba\xe2\x80\x99s pet palanquin sent twenty\r\nmiles, with that same grizzled old Oorya servant in charge, and when\r\nthey reached the disorderly order of the long white rambling house\r\nbehind Saharunpore, the lama took his own measures.\r\n\r\nSaid the Sahiba cheerily from an upper window, after compliments: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\nis the good of an old woman\xe2\x80\x99s advice to an old man? I told thee\xe2\x80\x94I\r\n_told_ thee, Holy One, to keep an eye upon the _chela_. How didst thou\r\ndo it? Never answer me! _I_ know. He has been running among the women.\r\nLook at his eyes\xe2\x80\x94hollow and sunk\xe2\x80\x94and the Betraying Line from the nose\r\ndown! He has been sifted out! Fie! Fie! And a priest, too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim looked up, over-weary to smile, shaking his head in denial.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not jest,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama. \xe2\x80\x9cThat time is done. We are here upon\r\ngreat matters. A sickness of soul took me in the Hills, and him a\r\nsickness of the body. Since then I have lived upon his strength\xe2\x80\x94eating\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChildren together\xe2\x80\x94young and old,\xe2\x80\x9d she sniffed, but forbore to make any\r\nnew jokes. \xe2\x80\x9cMay this present hospitality restore ye! Hold awhile and I\r\nwill come to gossip of the high good Hills.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt evening time\xe2\x80\x94her son-in-law was returned, so she did not need to go\r\non inspection round the farm\xe2\x80\x94she won to the meat of the matter,\r\nexplained low-voicedly by the lama. The two old heads nodded wisely\r\ntogether. Kim had reeled to a room with a cot in it, and was dozing\r\nsoddenly. The lama had forbidden him to set blankets or get food.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know\xe2\x80\x94I know. Who but I?\xe2\x80\x9d she cackled. \xe2\x80\x9cWe who go down to the\r\nburning-ghats clutch at the hands of those coming up from the River of\r\nLife with full water-jars\xe2\x80\x94yes, brimming water-jars. I did the boy\r\nwrong. He lent thee his strength? It is true that the old eat the young\r\ndaily. Stands now we must restore him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast many times acquired merit\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_My_ merit. What is it? Old bag of bones making curries for men who do\r\nnot ask \xe2\x80\x98Who cooked this?\xe2\x80\x99 Now if it were stored up for my grandson\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe that had the belly-pain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo think the Holy One remembers _that!_ I must tell his mother. It is\r\nmost singular honour! \xe2\x80\x98He that had the belly-pain\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94straightway the Holy\r\nOne remembered. She will be proud.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy _chela_ is to me as is a son to the unenlightened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay grandson, rather. Mothers have not the wisdom of our years. If a\r\nchild cries they say the heavens are falling. Now a grandmother is far\r\nenough separated from the pain of bearing and the pleasure of giving\r\nthe breast to consider whether a cry is wickedness pure or the wind.\r\nAnd since thou speakest once again of wind, when last the Holy One was\r\nhere, maybe I offended in pressing for charms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSister,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, using that form of address a Buddhist monk may\r\nsometimes employ towards a nun, \xe2\x80\x9cif charms comfort thee\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are better than ten thousand doctors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, if they comfort thee, I who was Abbot of Such-zen, will make as\r\nmany as thou mayest desire. I have never seen thy face\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_That_ even the monkeys who steal our loquats count for again. Hee!\r\nhee!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut as he who sleeps there said,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he nodded at the shut door of the\r\nguest-chamber across the forecourt\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthou hast a heart of gold... And he\r\nis in the spirit my very \xe2\x80\x98grandson\xe2\x80\x99 to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood! I am the Holy One\xe2\x80\x99s cow.\xe2\x80\x9d This was pure Hinduism, but the lama\r\nnever heeded. \xe2\x80\x9cI am old. I have borne sons in the body. Oh, once I\r\ncould please men! Now I can cure them.\xe2\x80\x9d He heard her armlets tinkle as\r\nthough she bared arms for action. \xe2\x80\x9cI will take over the boy and dose\r\nhim, and stuff him, and make him all whole. Hai! hai! We old people\r\nknow something yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWherefore when Kim, aching in every bone, opened his eyes, and would go\r\nto the cook-house to get his master\xe2\x80\x99s food, he found strong coercion\r\nabout him, and a veiled old figure at the door, flanked by the grizzled\r\nmanservant, who told him very precisely the things that he was on no\r\naccount to do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou must have? Thou shalt have nothing. What? A locked box in which\r\nto keep holy books? Oh, that is another matter. Heavens forbid I should\r\ncome between a priest and his prayers! It shall be brought, and thou\r\nshalt keep the key.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey pushed the coffer under his cot, and Kim shut away Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npistol, the oilskin packet of letters, and the locked books and\r\ndiaries, with a groan of relief. For some absurd reason their weight on\r\nhis shoulders was nothing to their weight on his poor mind. His neck\r\nached under it of nights.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThine is a sickness uncommon in youth these days: since young folk\r\nhave given up tending their betters. The remedy is sleep, and certain\r\ndrugs,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Sahiba; and he was glad to give himself up to the\r\nblankness that half menaced and half soothed him.\r\n\r\nShe brewed drinks, in some mysterious Asiatic equivalent to the\r\nstill-room\xe2\x80\x94drenches that smelt pestilently and tasted worse. She stood\r\nover Kim till they went down, and inquired exhaustively after they had\r\ncome up. She laid a taboo upon the forecourt, and enforced it by means\r\nof an armed man. It is true he was seventy odd, that his scabbarded\r\nsword ceased at the hilt; but he represented the authority of the\r\nSahiba, and loaded wains, chattering servants, calves, dogs, hens, and\r\nthe like, fetched a wide compass by those parts. Best of all, when the\r\nbody was cleared, she cut out from the mass of poor relations that\r\ncrowded the back of the buildings\xe2\x80\x94house-hold dogs, we name them\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ncousin\xe2\x80\x99s widow, skilled in what Europeans, who know nothing about it,\r\ncall massage. And the two of them, laying him east and west, that the\r\nmysterious earth-currents which thrill the clay of our bodies might\r\nhelp and not hinder, took him to pieces all one long afternoon\xe2\x80\x94bone by\r\nbone, muscle by muscle, ligament by ligament, and lastly, nerve by\r\nnerve. Kneaded to irresponsible pulp, half hypnotized by the perpetual\r\nflick and readjustment of the uneasy _chudders_ that veiled their eyes,\r\nKim slid ten thousand miles into slumber\xe2\x80\x94thirty-six hours of it\xe2\x80\x94sleep\r\nthat soaked like rain after drought.\r\n\r\nThen she fed him, and the house spun to her clamour. She caused fowls\r\nto be slain; she sent for vegetables, and the sober, slow-thinking\r\ngardener, nigh as old as she, sweated for it; she took spices, and\r\nmilk, and onion, with little fish from the brooks\xe2\x80\x94anon limes for\r\nsherbets, fat quails from the pits, then chicken-livers upon a skewer,\r\nwith sliced ginger between.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen something of this world,\xe2\x80\x9d she said over the crowded trays,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand there are but two sorts of women in it\xe2\x80\x94those who take the strength\r\nout of a man and those who put it back. Once I was that one, and now I\r\nam this. Nay\xe2\x80\x94do not play the priestling with me. Mine was but a jest.\r\nIf it does not hold good now, it will when thou takest the road again.\r\nCousin,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94this to the poor relation, never wearied of extolling her\r\npatroness\xe2\x80\x99s charity\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9che is getting a bloom on the skin of a new-curried\r\nhorse. Our work is like polishing jewels to be thrown to a\r\ndance-girl\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim sat up and smiled. The terrible weakness had dropped from him like\r\nan old shoe. His tongue itched for free speech again, and but a week\r\nback the lightest word clogged it like ashes. The pain in his neck (he\r\nmust have caught it from the lama) had gone with the heavy dengue-aches\r\nand the evil taste in the mouth. The two old women, a little, but not\r\nmuch, more careful about their veils now, clucked as merrily as the\r\nhens that had entered pecking through the open door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is my Holy One?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear him! Thy Holy One is well,\xe2\x80\x9d she snapped viciously. \xe2\x80\x9cThough that\r\nis none of _his_ merit. Knew I a charm to make him wise, I\xe2\x80\x99d sell my\r\njewels and buy it. To refuse good food that I cooked myself\xe2\x80\x94and go\r\nroving into the fields for two nights on an empty belly\xe2\x80\x94and to tumble\r\ninto a brook at the end of it\xe2\x80\x94call you _that_ holiness? Then, when he\r\nhas nearly broken what thou hast left of my heart with anxiety, he\r\ntells me that he has acquired merit. Oh, how like are all men! No, that\r\nwas not it\xe2\x80\x94he tells me that he is freed from all sin. _I_ could have\r\ntold him that before he wetted himself all over. He is well now\xe2\x80\x94this\r\nhappened a week ago\xe2\x80\x94but burn me such holiness! A babe of three would do\r\nbetter. Do not fret thyself for the Holy One. He keeps both eyes on\r\nthee when he is not wading our brooks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not remember to have seen him. I remember that the days and\r\nnights passed like bars of white and black, opening and shutting. I was\r\nnot sick: I was but tired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA lethargy that comes by right some few score years later. But it is\r\ndone now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaharanee,\xe2\x80\x9d Kim began, but led by the look in her eye, changed it to\r\nthe title of plain love\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cMother, I owe my life to thee. How shall I\r\nmake thanks? Ten thousand blessings upon thy house and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe house be unblessed!\xe2\x80\x9d (It is impossible to give exactly the old\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x99s word.) \xe2\x80\x9cThank the Gods as a priest if thou wilt, but thank me,\r\nif thou carest, as a son. Heavens above! Have I shifted thee and lifted\r\nthee and slapped and twisted thy ten toes to find texts flung at my\r\nhead? Somewhere a mother must have borne thee to break her heart. What\r\nused thou to her\xe2\x80\x94son?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had no mother, my mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cShe died, they tell me, when I\r\nwas young.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Hai mai!_ Then none can say I have robbed her of any right if\xe2\x80\x94when\r\nthou takest the road again and this house is but one of a thousand used\r\nfor shelter and forgotten, after an easy-flung blessing. No matter. I\r\nneed no blessings, but\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She stamped her foot at the poor relation.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake up the trays to the house. What is the good of stale food in the\r\nroom, O woman of ill-omen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ha\xe2\x80\x94have borne a son in my time too, but he died,\xe2\x80\x9d whimpered the\r\nbowed sister-figure behind the _chudder_. \xe2\x80\x9cThou knowest he died! I only\r\nwaited for the order to take away the tray.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is I that am the woman of ill-omen,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old lady penitently.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe that go down to the _chattris_ (the big umbrellas above the\r\nburning-ghats where the priests take their last dues) clutch hard at\r\nthe bearers of the _chattis_ (water-jars\xe2\x80\x94young folk full of the pride\r\nof life, she meant; but the pun is clumsy). When one cannot dance in\r\nthe festival one must e\xe2\x80\x99en look out of the window, and grandmothering\r\ntakes all a woman\xe2\x80\x99s time. Thy master gives me all the charms I now\r\ndesire for my daughter\xe2\x80\x99s eldest, by reason\xe2\x80\x94is it?\xe2\x80\x94that he is wholly\r\nfree from sin. The _hakim_ is brought very low these days. He goes\r\nabout poisoning my servants for lack of their betters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _hakim_, mother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat very Dacca man who gave me the pill which rent me in three\r\npieces. He cast up like a strayed camel a week ago, vowing that he and\r\nthou had been blood-brothers together up Kulu-way, and feigning great\r\nanxiety for thy health. He was very thin and hungry, so I gave orders\r\nto have him stuffed too\xe2\x80\x94him and his anxiety!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would see him if he is here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe eats five times a day, and lances boils for my hinds to save\r\nhimself from an apoplexy. He is so full of anxiety for thy health that\r\nhe sticks to the cook-house door and stays himself with scraps. He will\r\nkeep. We shall never get rid of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSend him here, mother\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the twinkle returned to Kim\xe2\x80\x99s eye for a\r\nflash\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand I will try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll send him, but to chase him off is an ill turn. At least he had\r\nthe sense to fish the Holy One out of the brook; thus, as the Holy One\r\ndid _not_ say, acquiring merit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a very wise _hakim_. Send him, mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPriest praising priest? A miracle! If he is any friend of thine (ye\r\nsquabbled at your last meeting) I\xe2\x80\x99ll hale him here with horse-ropes\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94and give him a caste-dinner afterwards, my son ... Get up and see\r\nthe world! This lying abed is the mother of seventy devils ... my son!\r\nmy son!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe trotted forth to raise a typhoon off the cook-house, and almost on\r\nher shadow rolled in the Babu, robed as to the shoulders like a Roman\r\nemperor, jowled like Titus, bare-headed, with new patent-leather shoes,\r\nin highest condition of fat, exuding joy and salutations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove, Mister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, but I _am_ jolly glad to see you. I will\r\nkindly shut the door. It is a pity you are sick. Are you very sick?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe papers\xe2\x80\x94the papers from the _kilta_. The maps and the _murasla!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe held out the key impatiently; for the present need on his soul was\r\nto get rid of the loot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are quite right. That is correct Departmental view to take. You\r\nhave got everything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll that was handwritten in the _kilta_ I took. The rest I threw down\r\nthe hill.\xe2\x80\x9d He could hear the key\xe2\x80\x99s grate in the lock, the sticky pull\r\nof the slow-rending oilskin, and a quick shuffling of papers. He had\r\nbeen annoyed out of all reason by the knowledge that they lay below him\r\nthrough the sick idle days\xe2\x80\x94a burden incommunicable. For that reason the\r\nblood tingled through his body, when Hurree, skipping elephantinely,\r\nshook hands again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is fine! This is finest! Mister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara! you have\xe2\x80\x94ha! ha! swiped\r\nthe whole bag of tricks\xe2\x80\x94locks, stocks, and barrels. They told me it was\r\neight months\xe2\x80\x99 work gone up the spouts! By Jove, how they beat me! ...\r\nLook, here is the letter from Hil\xc3\xa1s!\xe2\x80\x9d He intoned a line or two of Court\r\nPersian, which is the language of authorized and unauthorized\r\ndiplomacy. \xe2\x80\x9cMister Rajah Sahib has just about put his foot in the\r\nholes. He will have to explain offeecially how the deuce-an\xe2\x80\x99-all he is\r\nwriting love-letters to the Czar. And they are very clever maps ... and\r\nthere is three or four Prime Ministers of these parts implicated by the\r\ncorrespondence. By Gad, sar! The British Government will change the\r\nsuccession in Hil\xc3\xa1s and Bun\xc3\xa1r, and nominate new heirs to the throne.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Trea-son most base\xe2\x80\x99 ... but you do not understand? Eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre they in thy hands?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. It was all he cared for.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust you jolly-well bet yourself they are.\xe2\x80\x9d He stowed the entire trove\r\nabout his body, as only Orientals can. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are going up to the\r\noffice, too. The old lady thinks I am permanent fixture here, but I\r\nshall go away with these straight off\xe2\x80\x94immediately. Mr Lurgan will be\r\nproud man. You are offeecially subordinate to me, but I shall embody\r\nyour name in my verbal report. It is a pity we are not allowed written\r\nreports. We Bengalis excel in thee exact science.\xe2\x80\x9d He tossed back the\r\nkey and showed the box empty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. That is good. I was very tired. My Holy One was sick, too. And\r\ndid he fall into\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah yess. I am his good friend, I tell you. He was behaving very\r\nstrange when I came down after you, and I thought perhaps he might have\r\nthe papers. I followed him on his meditations, and to discuss\r\nethnological points also. You see, I am verree small person here\r\nnowadays, in comparison with all his charms. By Jove, O\xe2\x80\x99Hara, do you\r\nknow, he is afflicted with infirmity of fits. Yess, I tell you.\r\nCataleptic, too, if not also epileptic. I found him in such a state\r\nunder a tree _in articulo mortem_, and he jumped up and walked into a\r\nbrook and he was nearly drowned but for me. I pulled him out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I was not there!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim. \xe2\x80\x9cHe might have died.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he might have died, but he is dry now, and asserts he has\r\nundergone transfiguration.\xe2\x80\x9d The Babu tapped his forehead knowingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ntook notes of his statements for Royal Society\xe2\x80\x94_in posse_. You must\r\nmake haste and be quite well and come back to Simla, and I will tell\r\nyou all my tale at Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s. It was splendid. The bottoms of their\r\ntrousers were _quite_ torn, and old Nahan Rajah, he thought they were\r\nEuropean soldiers deserting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the Russians? How long were they with thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne was a Frenchman. Oh, days and days and days! Now all the\r\nhill-people believe all Russians are all beggars. By Jove! they had not\r\none dam\xe2\x80\x99-thing that I did not get them. And I told the common\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x94oah, _such_ tales and anecdotes!\xe2\x80\x94I will tell you at old Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhen you come up. We will have\xe2\x80\x94ah\xe2\x80\x94a night out! It is feather in both\r\nour caps! Yess, and they gave me a certificate. That is creaming joke.\r\nYou should have seen them at the Alliance Bank identifying themselves!\r\nAnd thank Almighty God you got their papers so well! You do not laugh\r\nverree much, but you shall laugh when you are well. Now I will go\r\nstraight to the railway and get out. You shall have all sorts of\r\ncredits for your game. When do you come along? We are very proud of you\r\nthough you gave us great frights. And especially Mahbub.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, Mahbub. And where is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSelling horses in this vi-cinity, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere! Why? Speak slowly. There is a thickness in my head still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Babu looked shyly down his nose. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see, I am fearful man,\r\nand I do not like responsibility. You were sick, you see, and I did not\r\nknow where deuce-an\xe2\x80\x99-all the papers were, and if so, how many. So when\r\nI had come down here I slipped in private wire to Mahbub\xe2\x80\x94he was at\r\nMeerut for races\xe2\x80\x94and I tell him how case stands. He comes up with his\r\nmen and he consorts with the lama, and then he calls me a fool, and is\r\nvery rude\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut wherefore\xe2\x80\x94wherefore?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is what _I_ ask. I only suggest that if anyone steals the papers\r\nI should like some good strong, brave men to rob them back again. You\r\nsee, they are vitally important, and Mahbub Ali he did not know where\r\nyou were.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMahbub Ali to rob the Sahiba\xe2\x80\x99s house? You are not pleased, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA native proverb\xe2\x80\x94unquotable\xe2\x80\x94showed the blackness of Kim\xe2\x80\x99s disapproval.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Hurree shrugged his shoulders\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthere is no accounting for thee\r\ntaste. Mahbub was angry too. He has sold horses all about here, and he\r\nsays old lady is _pukka_ (thorough) old lady and would not condescend\r\nto such ungentlemanly things. _I_ do not care. I have got the papers,\r\nand I was very glad of moral support from Mahbub. I tell you, I am\r\nfearful man, but, somehow or other, the more fearful I am the more\r\ndam\xe2\x80\x99-tight places I get into. So I was glad you came with me to Chini,\r\nand I am glad Mahbub was close by. The old lady she is sometimes very\r\nrude to me and my beautiful pills.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah be merciful!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim on his elbow, rejoicing. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a beast of\r\nwonder is a Babu! And that man walked alone\xe2\x80\x94if he did walk\xe2\x80\x94with robbed\r\nand angry foreigners!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOah, thatt was nothing, after they had done beating me; but if I lost\r\nthe papers it was pretty-jolly serious. Mahbub he nearly beat me too,\r\nand he went and consorted with the lama no end. I shall stick to\r\nethnological investigations henceforwards. Now good-bye, Mister O\xe2\x80\x99Hara.\r\nI can catch 4.25 p.m. to Umballa if I am quick. It will be good times\r\nwhen we all tell thee tale up at Mr Lurgan\xe2\x80\x99s. I shall report you\r\noffeecially better. Good-bye, my dear fallow, and when next you are\r\nunder thee emotions please do not use the Mohammedan terms with the\r\nTibetan dress.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shook hands twice\xe2\x80\x94a Babu to his boot-heels\xe2\x80\x94and opened the door. With\r\nthe fall of the sunlight upon his still triumphant face he returned to\r\nthe humble Dacca quack.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe robbed them,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Kim, forgetting his own share in the game.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe tricked them. He lied to them like a Bengali. They give him a\r\n_chit_ (a testimonial). He makes them a mock at the risk of his\r\nlife\xe2\x80\x94_I_ never would have gone down to them after the pistol-shots\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nthen he says he is a fearful man ... And he _is_ a fearful man. I must\r\nget into the world again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt first his legs bent like bad pipe-stems, and the flood and rush of\r\nthe sunlit air dazzled him. He squatted by the white wall, the mind\r\nrummaging among the incidents of the long _dooli_ journey, the lama\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nweaknesses, and, now that the stimulus of talk was removed, his own\r\nself-pity, of which, like the sick, he had great store. The unnerved\r\nbrain edged away from all the outside, as a raw horse, once rowelled,\r\nsidles from the spur. It was enough, amply enough, that the spoil of\r\nthe _kilta_ was away\xe2\x80\x94off his hands\xe2\x80\x94out of his possession. He tried to\r\nthink of the lama\xe2\x80\x94to wonder why he had tumbled into a brook\xe2\x80\x94but the\r\nbigness of the world, seen between the forecourt gates, swept linked\r\nthought aside. Then he looked upon the trees and the broad fields, with\r\nthe thatched huts hidden among crops\xe2\x80\x94looked with strange eyes unable to\r\ntake up the size and proportion and use of things\xe2\x80\x94stared for a still\r\nhalf-hour. All that while he felt, though he could not put it into\r\nwords, that his soul was out of gear with its surroundings\xe2\x80\x94a cog-wheel\r\nunconnected with any machinery, just like the idle cog-wheel of a cheap\r\nBeheea sugar-crusher laid by in a corner. The breezes fanned over him,\r\nthe parrots shrieked at him, the noises of the populated house\r\nbehind\xe2\x80\x94squabbles, orders, and reproofs\xe2\x80\x94hit on dead ears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Kim. I am Kim. And what is Kim?\xe2\x80\x9d His soul repeated it again and\r\nagain.\r\n\r\nHe did not want to cry\xe2\x80\x94had never felt less like crying in his life\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nof a sudden easy, stupid tears trickled down his nose, and with an\r\nalmost audible click he felt the wheels of his being lock up anew on\r\nthe world without. Things that rode meaningless on the eyeball an\r\ninstant before slid into proper proportion. Roads were meant to be\r\nwalked upon, houses to be lived in, cattle to be driven, fields to be\r\ntilled, and men and women to be talked to. They were all real and\r\ntrue\xe2\x80\x94solidly planted upon the feet\xe2\x80\x94perfectly comprehensible\xe2\x80\x94clay of his\r\nclay, neither more nor less. He shook himself like a dog with a flea in\r\nhis ear, and rambled out of the gate. Said the Sahiba, to whom watchful\r\neyes reported this move: \xe2\x80\x9cLet him go. I have done my share. Mother\r\nEarth must do the rest. When the Holy One comes back from meditation,\r\ntell him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere stood an empty bullock-cart on a little knoll half a mile away,\r\nwith a young banyan tree behind\xe2\x80\x94a look-out, as it were, above some\r\nnew-ploughed levels; and his eyelids, bathed in soft air, grew heavy as\r\nhe neared it. The ground was good clean dust\xe2\x80\x94no new herbage that,\r\nliving, is half-way to death already, but the hopeful dust that holds\r\nthe seeds of all life. He felt it between his toes, patted it with his\r\npalms, and joint by joint, sighing luxuriously, laid him down full\r\nlength along in the shadow of the wooden-pinned cart. And Mother Earth\r\nwas as faithful as the Sahiba. She breathed through him to restore the\r\npoise he had lost lying so long on a cot cut off from her good\r\ncurrents. His head lay powerless upon her breast, and his opened hands\r\nsurrendered to her strength. The many-rooted tree above him, and even\r\nthe dead manhandled wood beside, knew what he sought, as he himself did\r\nnot know. Hour upon hour he lay deeper than sleep.\r\n\r\nTowards evening, when the dust of returning kine made all the horizons\r\nsmoke, came the lama and Mahbub Ali, both afoot, walking cautiously,\r\nfor the house had told them where he had gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah! What a fool\xe2\x80\x99s trick to play in open country!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the\r\nhorse-dealer. \xe2\x80\x9cHe could be shot a hundred times\xe2\x80\x94but this is not the\r\nBorder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, repeating a many-times-told tale, \xe2\x80\x9cnever was such\r\na _chela_. Temperate, kindly, wise, of ungrudging disposition, a merry\r\nheart upon the road, never forgetting, learned, truthful, courteous.\r\nGreat is his reward!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know the boy\xe2\x80\x94as I have said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he was all those things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome of them\xe2\x80\x94but I have not yet found a Red Hat\xe2\x80\x99s charm for making him\r\noverly truthful. He has certainly been well nursed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Sahiba is a heart of gold,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cShe looks\r\nupon him as her son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHmph! Half Hind seems that way disposed. I only wished to see that the\r\nboy had come to no harm and was a free agent. As thou knowest, he and I\r\nwere old friends in the first days of your pilgrimage together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a bond between us.\xe2\x80\x9d The lama sat down. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are at the end of\r\nthe pilgrimage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo thanks to thee thine was not cut off for good and all a week back.\r\nI heard what the Sahiba said to thee when we bore thee up on the cot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMahbub laughed, and tugged his newly dyed beard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was meditating upon other matters that tide. It was the _hakim_ from\r\nDacca broke my meditations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOtherwise\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94this was in Pushtu for decency\xe2\x80\x99s sake\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthou wouldst have\r\nended thy meditations upon the sultry side of Hell\xe2\x80\x94being an unbeliever\r\nand an idolater for all thy child\xe2\x80\x99s simplicity. But now, Red Hat, what\r\nis to be done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis very night,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the words came slowly, vibrating with triumph\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthis\r\nvery night he will be as free as I am from all taint of sin\xe2\x80\x94assured as\r\nI am, when he quits this body, of Freedom from the Wheel of Things. I\r\nhave a sign\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he laid his hand above the torn chart in his bosom\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nmy time is short; but I shall have safeguarded him throughout the\r\nyears. Remember, I have reached Knowledge, as I told thee only three\r\nnights back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be true, as the Tirah priest said when I stole his cousin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwife, that I am a _sufi_ (a free-thinker); for here I sit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mahbub\r\nto himself, \xe2\x80\x9cdrinking in blasphemy unthinkable ... I remember the tale.\r\nOn that, then, he goes to _Jannatu l\xe2\x80\x99Adn_ (the Gardens of Eden). But\r\nhow? Wilt thou slay him or drown him in that wonderful river from which\r\nthe Babu dragged thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was dragged from no river,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama simply. \xe2\x80\x9cThou hast\r\nforgotten what befell. I found it by Knowledge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, ay. True,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Mahbub, divided between high indignation and\r\nenormous mirth. \xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten the exact run of what happened. Thou\r\ndidst find it knowingly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to say that I would take life is\xe2\x80\x94not a sin, but a madness simple.\r\nMy _chela_ aided me to the River. It is his right to be cleansed from\r\nsin\xe2\x80\x94with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, he needs cleansing. But afterwards, old man\xe2\x80\x94afterwards?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat matter under all the Heavens? He is sure of Nibban\xe2\x80\x94enlightened\xe2\x80\x94as\r\nI am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell said. I had a fear he might mount Mohammed\xe2\x80\x99s Horse and fly away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay\xe2\x80\x94he must go forth as a teacher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha! Now I see! That is the right gait for the colt. Certainly he must\r\ngo forth as a teacher. He is somewhat urgently needed as a scribe by\r\nthe State, for instance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo that end he was prepared. I acquired merit in that I gave alms for\r\nhis sake. A good deed does not die. He aided me in my Search. I aided\r\nhim in his. Just is the Wheel, O horse-seller from the North. Let him\r\nbe a teacher; let him be a scribe\xe2\x80\x94what matter? He will have attained\r\nFreedom at the end. The rest is illusion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat matter? When I must have him with me beyond Balkh in six months!\r\nI come up with ten lame horses and three strong-backed men\xe2\x80\x94thanks to\r\nthat chicken of a Babu\xe2\x80\x94to break a sick boy by force out of an old\r\ntrot\xe2\x80\x99s house. It seems that I stand by while a young Sahib is hoisted\r\ninto Allah knows what of an idolater\xe2\x80\x99s Heaven by means of old Red Hat.\r\nAnd I am reckoned something of a player of the Game myself! But the\r\nmadman is fond of the boy; and I must be very reasonably mad too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the prayer?\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama, as the rough Pushtu rumbled into\r\nthe red beard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter at all; but now I understand that the boy, sure of Paradise,\r\ncan yet enter Government service, my mind is easier. I must get to my\r\nhorses. It grows dark. Do not wake him. I have no wish to hear him call\r\nthee master.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he _is_ my disciple. What else?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has told me.\xe2\x80\x9d Mahbub choked down his touch of spleen and rose\r\nlaughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not altogether of thy faith, Red Hat\xe2\x80\x94if so small a\r\nmatter concern thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lama.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought not. Therefore it will not move thee, sinless, new-washed\r\nand three parts drowned to boot, when I call thee a good man\xe2\x80\x94a very\r\ngood man. We have talked together some four or five evenings now, and\r\nfor all I am a horse-coper I can still, as the saying is, see holiness\r\nbeyond the legs of a horse. Yea, can see, too, how our Friend of all\r\nthe World put his hand in thine at the first. Use him well, and suffer\r\nhim to return to the world as a teacher, when thou hast\xe2\x80\x94bathed his\r\nlegs, if that be the proper medicine for the colt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not follow the Way thyself, and so accompany the boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMahbub stared stupefied at the magnificent insolence of the demand,\r\nwhich across the Border he would have paid with more than a blow. Then\r\nthe humour of it touched his worldly soul.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSoftly\xe2\x80\x94softly\xe2\x80\x94one foot at a time, as the lame gelding went over the\r\nUmballa jumps. I may come to Paradise later\xe2\x80\x94I have workings that\r\nway\xe2\x80\x94great motions\xe2\x80\x94and I owe them to thy simplicity. Thou hast never\r\nlied?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat need?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Allah, hear him! \xe2\x80\x98What need\xe2\x80\x99 in this Thy world! Nor ever harmed a\r\nman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce\xe2\x80\x94with a pencase\xe2\x80\x94before I was wise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo? I think the better of thee. Thy teachings are good. Thou hast\r\nturned one man that I know from the path of strife.\xe2\x80\x9d He laughed\r\nimmensely. \xe2\x80\x9cHe came here open-minded to commit a _dacoity_ (a\r\nhouse-robbery with violence). Yes, to cut, rob, kill, and carry off\r\nwhat he desired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA great foolishness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! black shame too. So he thought after he had seen thee\xe2\x80\x94and a few\r\nothers, male and female. So he abandoned it; and now he goes to beat a\r\nbig fat Babu man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah forbid it! Some men are strong in knowledge, Red Hat. Thy\r\nstrength is stronger still. Keep it\xe2\x80\x94I think thou wilt. If the boy be\r\nnot a good servant, pull his ears off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a hitch of his broad Bokhariot belt the Pathan swaggered off into\r\nthe gloaming, and the lama came down from his clouds so far as to look\r\nat the broad back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat person lacks courtesy, and is deceived by the shadow of\r\nappearances. But he spoke well of my _chela_, who now enters upon his\r\nreward. Let me make the prayer! ... Wake, O fortunate above all born of\r\nwomen. Wake! It is found!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKim came up from those deep wells, and the lama attended his yawning\r\npleasure; duly snapping fingers to head off evil spirits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have slept a hundred years. Where\xe2\x80\x94? Holy One, hast _thou_ been here\r\nlong? I went out to look for thee, but\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he laughed drowsily\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI slept by\r\nthe way. I am all well now. Hast thou eaten? Let us go to the house. It\r\nis many days since I tended thee. And the Sahiba fed thee well? Who\r\nshampooed thy legs? What of the weaknesses\xe2\x80\x94the belly and the neck, and\r\nthe beating in the ears?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGone\xe2\x80\x94all gone. Dost thou not know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know nothing, but that I have not seen thee in a monkey\xe2\x80\x99s age. Know\r\nwhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrange the knowledge did not reach out to thee, when all my thoughts\r\nwere theeward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot see the face, but the voice is like a gong. Has the Sahiba\r\nmade a young man of thee by her cookery?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe peered at the cross-legged figure, outlined jet-black against the\r\nlemon-coloured drift of light. So does the stone Bodhisat sit who looks\r\ndown upon the patent self-registering turnstiles of the Lahore Museum.\r\n\r\nThe lama held his peace. Except for the click of the rosary and a faint\r\n_clop-clop_ of Mahbub\xe2\x80\x99s retreating feet, the soft, smoky silence of\r\nevening in India wrapped them close.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear me! I bring news.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut let us\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOut shot the long yellow hand compelling silence. Kim tucked his feet\r\nunder his robe-edge obediently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear me! I bring news! The Search is finished. Comes now the Reward...\r\nThus. When we were among the Hills, I lived on thy strength till the\r\nyoung branch bowed and nigh broke. When we came out of the Hills, I was\r\ntroubled for thee and for other matters which I held in my heart. The\r\nboat of my soul lacked direction; I could not see into the Cause of\r\nThings. So I gave thee over to the virtuous woman altogether. I took no\r\nfood. I drank no water. Still I saw not the Way. They pressed food upon\r\nme and cried at my shut door. So I removed myself to a hollow under a\r\ntree. I took no food. I took no water. I sat in meditation two days and\r\ntwo nights, abstracting my mind; inbreathing and outbreathing in the\r\nrequired manner ... Upon the second night\xe2\x80\x94so great was my reward\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nwise Soul loosed itself from the silly Body and went free. This I have\r\nnever before attained, though I have stood on the threshold of it.\r\nConsider, for it is a marvel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA marvel indeed. Two days and two nights without food! Where was the\r\nSahiba?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kim under his breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYea, my Soul went free, and, wheeling like an eagle, saw indeed that\r\nthere was no Teshoo Lama nor any other soul. As a drop draws to water,\r\nso my Soul drew near to the Great Soul which is beyond all things. At\r\nthat point, exalted in contemplation, I saw all Hind, from Ceylon in\r\nthe sea to the Hills, and my own Painted Rocks at Such-zen; I saw every\r\ncamp and village, to the least, where we have ever rested. I saw them\r\nat one time and in one place; for they were within the Soul. By this I\r\nknew the Soul had passed beyond the illusion of Time and Space and of\r\nThings. By this I knew that I was free. I saw thee lying in thy cot,\r\nand I saw thee falling downhill under the idolater\xe2\x80\x94at one time, in one\r\nplace, in my Soul, which, as I say, had touched the Great Soul. Also I\r\nsaw the stupid body of Teshoo Lama lying down, and the _hakim_ from\r\nDacca kneeled beside, shouting in its ear. Then my Soul was all alone,\r\nand I saw nothing, for I was all things, having reached the Great Soul.\r\nAnd I meditated a thousand thousand years, passionless, well aware of\r\nthe Causes of all Things. Then a voice cried: \xe2\x80\x98What shall come to the\r\nboy if thou art dead?\xe2\x80\x99 and I was shaken back and forth in myself with\r\npity for thee; and I said: \xe2\x80\x98I will return to my _chela_, lest he miss\r\nthe Way.\xe2\x80\x99 Upon this my Soul, which is the Soul of Teshoo Lama, withdrew\r\nitself from the Great Soul with strivings and yearnings and retchings\r\nand agonies not to be told. As the egg from the fish, as the fish from\r\nthe water, as the water from the cloud, as the cloud from the thick\r\nair, so put forth, so leaped out, so drew away, so fumed up the Soul of\r\nTeshoo Lama from the Great Soul. Then a voice cried: \xe2\x80\x98The River! Take\r\nheed to the River!\xe2\x80\x99 and I looked down upon all the world, which was as\r\nI had seen it before\xe2\x80\x94one in time, one in place\xe2\x80\x94and I saw plainly the\r\nRiver of the Arrow at my feet. At that hour my Soul was hampered by\r\nsome evil or other whereof I was not wholly cleansed, and it lay upon\r\nmy arms and coiled round my waist; but I put it aside, and I cast forth\r\nas an eagle in my flight for the very place of the River. I pushed\r\naside world upon world for thy sake. I saw the River below me\xe2\x80\x94the River\r\nof the Arrow\xe2\x80\x94and, descending, the waters of it closed over me; and\r\nbehold I was again in the body of Teshoo Lama, but free from sin, and\r\nthe _hakim_ from Decca bore up my head in the waters of the River. It\r\nis here! It is behind the mango-tope here\xe2\x80\x94even here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah kerim! Oh, well that the Babu was by! Wast thou very wet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I regard? I remember the _hakim_ was concerned for the body\r\nof Teshoo Lama. He haled it out of the holy water in his hands, and\r\nthere came afterwards thy horse-seller from the North with a cot and\r\nmen, and they put the body on the cot and bore it up to the Sahiba\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat said the Sahiba?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was meditating in that body, and did not hear. So thus the Search is\r\nended. For the merit that I have acquired, the River of the Arrow is\r\nhere. It broke forth at our feet, as I have said. I have found it. Son\r\nof my Soul, I have wrenched my Soul back from the Threshold of Freedom\r\nto free thee from all sin\xe2\x80\x94as I am free, and sinless! Just is the Wheel!\r\nCertain is our deliverance! Come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe crossed his hands on his lap and smiled, as a man may who has won\r\nsalvation for himself and his beloved.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK KIM ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 2226-0.txt or 2226-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n    https://www.gutenberg.org/2/2/2/2226/\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the United\r\nStates without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
